Project Hail Mary

Chapter 1

“W hat’s two plus two?”

Something about the question bothers me. I’m tired. I fall back asleep.

A few minutes go by, then I hear it again.

“What’s two plus two?”

The soft, female voice has no feeling, and she says it exactly like before. It’s a computer. A computer is bothering me. I’m even more annoyed now.

“Lrmln,” I say. I’m surprised. I meant to say “Leave me alone”—a perfectly good response in my view—but I couldn’t speak.

“Incorrect,” says the computer. “What’s two plus two?”

Time for a test. I’ll try to say hello.

“Hlllch?” I say.

“Incorrect. What’s two plus two?”

What’s going on? I want to find out, but I don’t have much to go on. I can’t see. I can’t hear anything but the computer. I can’t even feel. No, that’s not true. I feel something. I’m lying down. I’m on something soft. A bed.

I think my eyes are closed. That’s not so bad. All I have to do is open them. I try, but nothing happens.

Why can’t I open my eyes?

Open.

Aaaand…open!

Open, dang it!

Ooh! I felt a wiggle that time. My eyelids moved. I felt it.

Open!

My eyelids creep up and blinding light burns my eyes.

“Glunn!” I say. I force myself to keep my eyes open. Everything is white mixed with pain.

“Eye movement detected,” my tormenter says. “What’s two plus two?”

The whiteness fades. My eyes are adjusting. I start to see shapes, but nothing clear yet. Let’s see…can I move my hands? No.

Feet? Also no.

But I can move my mouth, right? I’ve been saying things. Not things that make sense, but it’s something.

“Fffr.”

“Incorrect. What’s two plus two?”

The shapes start to become clear. I’m in a bed. It’s kind of…oval-shaped.

LED lights shine down on me. Cameras in the ceiling watch my every move. Creepy as that is, I’m much more worried about the robot arms.

The two brushed-steel arms hang from the ceiling. Each has a bunch of scary, pointy tools where hands should be. I don’t like the look of that at all.

“Ffff…oooh…rrrr,” I say. Is that right?

“Incorrect. What’s two plus two?”

Dang it. I gather all my willpower and inner strength. Also, I’m starting to panic a little. Good. I use that too.

“Fffoouurr,” I finally say.

“Correct.”

Thank God. I can talk. Kind of.

I breathe a sigh of relief. Wait—I just controlled my breathing. I take another breath. On purpose. My mouth is sore. My throat is sore. But it’s my soreness. I have control.

I’m wearing a breathing mask. It’s tight to my face and connected to a hose that goes behind my head.

Can I get up?

No. But I can move my head a little. I look down at my body. I’m naked and connected to more tubes than I can count. There’s one in each arm, one in each leg, one in my "private area," and two that disappear under my thigh. I’m guessing one of them is up where the sun doesn’t shine.

That can’t be good.

Also, I’m covered in electrodes. They’re sensor stickers, like for an EKG, but everywhere. At least they’re just on my skin and not stuck inside me.

“Wh—” I gasp. I try again. “Where…am…I?”

“What’s the cube root of eight?” the computer asks.

“Where am I?” I say again. This time it’s easier.

“Incorrect. What’s the cube root of eight?”

I take a deep breath and speak slowly. “Two times e to the two-i -pi.”

“Incorrect. What’s the cube root of eight?”

But I wasn’t wrong. I just wanted to see how smart the computer was. Answer: not very.

“Two,” I say.

“Correct.”

I wait for more questions, but the computer seems happy.

I’m tired. I fall back asleep.

—

I wake up. How long did I sleep? It must have been a while because I feel good. I open my eyes easily. That’s better.

I try to move my fingers. They wiggle like I want them to. Good. Now we’re moving forward.

“Hand movement detected,” says the computer. “Remain still.”

“What? Why—”

The robot arms come for me. They move fast. Before I know it, they’ve taken out most of the tubes from my body. I didn’t feel anything. But my skin is kind of numb anyway.

Only three tubes are left: an IV in my arm, a tube up my butt, and a catheter. I really wanted those last two gone, but whatever.

I lift my right arm and let it drop back onto the bed. I do the same with my left. They feel super heavy. I do this a few times. My arms are strong. That doesn’t make sense. I guess I must have had a major health issue and been in this bed for a while. Otherwise, why would I be hooked up to all this stuff? Shouldn’t my muscles be weaker?

And shouldn’t there be doctors? Or at least the sounds of a hospital? And what’s up with this bed? It’s not a rectangle, it’s an oval and I think it’s attached to the wall, not the floor.

“Take…” I stop talking. Still a little tired. “Take the tubes out….”

The computer doesn’t answer.

I lift my arms a few more times. I wiggle my toes. I’m definitely getting stronger.

I move my ankles back and forth. They work. I lift my knees up. My legs are also toned. Not super muscular, but still too healthy for someone about to die. Though, I’m not sure how muscular they should be.

I press my hands into the bed and push. My upper body lifts up. I’m actually getting up! It takes all my strength, but I keep going. The bed rocks a little as I move. It’s not a normal bed, that’s for sure. As I lift my head higher, I see the head and foot of the oval bed are connected to strong wall mounts. It’s like a firm hammock. Strange.

Soon, I’m sitting on the tube in my butt. Not very comfortable, but when is a tube up your butt ever comfortable?

I can see things better now. This is no regular hospital room. The walls look like plastic and the whole room is round. Bright white light comes from LED lights in the ceiling.

There are two more hammock-like beds attached to the walls, each with a patient. We're in a triangle, and the ceiling-mounted robot arms are in the center. I guess they're for all three of us. I can't see much of the others—they've settled into their beds like I did.

There's no door. Just a ladder on the wall to…a hatch? It's round with a wheel-handle in the middle. Yeah, it has to be some kind of hatch. Like on a submarine. Maybe we have a contagious disease? Maybe this is an airtight quarantine room? There are small vents here and there on the wall, and I feel a little airflow. It could be a controlled environment.

I move one leg over the side of my bed, and it wobbles. The robot arms rush toward me. I flinch, but they stop and hover nearby. I think they're ready to catch me if I fall.

“Full-body motion detected,” the computer says. “What’s your name?”

“Seriously?” I ask.

“Incorrect. Attempt number two: What’s your name?”

I open my mouth to answer.

“Uh…”

“Incorrect. Attempt number three: What’s your name?”

Only now I realize: I don't know who I am. I don't know what I do. I don't remember anything at all.

“Um,” I say.

“Incorrect.”

A wave of tiredness washes over me. It’s actually kind of nice. The computer must have calmed me down through the IV line.

“…waaaait…” I mumble.

The robot arms gently lay me back down on the bed.

—

I wake up again. One of the robot arms is on my face. What is it doing?!

I shudder, more surprised than anything else. The arm goes back up into the ceiling. I feel my face to see if it’s hurt. One side is rough, and the other is smooth.

“You were shaving me?”

“Consciousness detected,” the computer says. “What’s your name?”

“I still don’t know.”

“Incorrect. Second try: What’s your name?”

I’m white, I’m male, and I speak English. Let’s guess. “J–John?”

“Incorrect. Third try: What’s your name?”

I pull the IV out of my arm. “Screw you.”

“Incorrect.” The robot arms reach for me. I roll off the bed, which is a mistake. The other tubes are still connected.

The butt tube comes right out. Doesn’t even hurt. The still-inflated catheter yanks right out of my penis. And that does hurt. It’s like peeing a golf ball.

I scream and twist on the floor.

“Physical distress,” says the computer. The arms chase me. I crawl along the floor to escape. I get under one of the other beds. The arms stop, but they don’t give up. They wait. They’re run by a computer. They won’t get impatient.

I let my head fall back and gasp for breath. After a while, the pain lessens, and I wipe tears from my eyes.

I have no idea what’s going on.

“Hey!” I call out. “Someone, wake up!”

“What’s your name?” the computer asks.

“One of you humans, wake up, please.”

“Incorrect,” the computer says.

My crotch hurts so much I have to laugh. It’s just so ridiculous. Plus, the endorphins are kicking in and making me lightheaded. I look back at the catheter by my bunk. I shake my head in amazement. That thing went through my urethra. Wow.

And it did some damage on the way out. A little streak of blood sits on the ground. It’s just a thin red line of—

I sipped my coffee, ate the last bit of toast, and asked the waitress for my bill. I could have saved money making breakfast at home instead of eating at a diner every morning. Probably would have been a good idea, since I didn't make much money. But I hate cooking and I love eggs and bacon.

The waitress nodded and went to the register to ring me up. But another customer came in to get a table right then.

I checked my watch. Just after seven a.m. No hurry. I liked to get to work by seven-twenty so I could get ready for the day. But I didn’t have to be there until eight.

I took out my phone and checked my email.

TO: Astronomy Curiosities astrocurious@scilists.org

FROM: (Irina Petrova, PhD) ipetrova@gaoran.ru

SUBJECT: The Thin Red Line

I frowned at the screen. I thought I’d canceled that list. I left that life a long time ago. It didn’t have many emails, and if I remembered right, they were usually pretty interesting. Just a group of astronomers, astrophysicists, and other experts talking about anything that seemed strange to them.

I glanced at the waitress—the customers had a lot of questions about the menu. Probably asking if Sally’s Diner had gluten-free vegan grass clippings or something. The people of San Francisco could be difficult sometimes.

With nothing else to do, I read the email.

Hello, experts. My name is Doctor Irina Petrova, and I work at the Pulkovo Observatory in St. Petersburg, Russia.

I am writing to you to ask for assistance.

For the last two years, I have been working on a theory about infrared light from nebulae. I have made careful observations in a few specific IR light ranges. And I have found something strange—not in any nebula, but here in our own solar system.

There is a very weak, but noticeable line in the solar system that gives off infrared light at the 25.984 micron wavelength. It seems to be only that wavelength, with no changes.

Attached are Excel spreadsheets with my data. I have also included some images of the data as a 3-D model.

You will see on the model that the line is an uneven arc that goes straight up from the sun’s North Pole for 37 million kilometers. From there, it sharply angles down and away from the sun, toward Venus. After the arc’s peak, the cloud widens like a funnel. At Venus, the arc’s width is as wide as the planet itself.

The infrared glow is very weak. I could only detect it because I was using very sensitive equipment while looking for IR light from nebulae.

To be sure, I asked for a favor from the Atacama observatory in Chile—the best IR observatory in the world, in my opinion. They confirmed what I found.

There are many reasons for seeing IR light in space between planets. It could be space dust or other particles reflecting sunlight. Or some molecule could be absorbing energy and releasing it in the infrared range. That would even explain why it’s all the same wavelength.

The shape of the arc is especially interesting. My first thought was that it is a group of particles moving along magnetic field lines. But Venus has almost no magnetic field. No magnetosphere, no ionosphere, nothing. What forces would make particles arc toward it? And why would they glow?

Any suggestions or theories would be welcome.

—

What the heck was that?

I remembered it all at once. It just popped into my head without warning.

I didn’t learn much about myself. I live in San Francisco—I remember that. And I like breakfast. Also, I used to be into astronomy, but now I’m not?

Apparently, my brain thought it was important that I remember that email. Not simple things like my own name.

My subconscious is trying to tell me something. Seeing the line of blood must have reminded me of the “Thin Red Line” title of that email. But what does that have to do with me?

I wiggle out from under the bed and sit up against the wall. The arms point toward me but still can’t reach.

Time to check out the other patients. I don’t know who I am or why I’m here, but at least I’m not alone—and they’re dead.

Yes, definitely dead. The one closest to me was a woman, I think. At least, she had long hair. Other than that, she’s mostly a mummy. Dried skin hung over bones. There’s no smell. Nothing is rotting. She must have died a long time ago.

The person in the other bed was a man. I think he’s been dead even longer. His skin is not just dry and leathery but also falling apart.

Okay. So I’m here with two dead people. I should be disgusted and horrified, but I’m not. They’re so far gone they don’t even look human. They look like Halloween decorations. I hope I wasn’t close friends with either of them. Or, if I was, I hope I don’t remember it.

Dead people are a concern, but I’m more concerned that they’ve been here so long. Even a quarantine area would remove dead people, wouldn’t they? Whatever’s wrong must be really bad.

I get to my feet. It’s slow and takes a lot of effort. I steady myself at the edge of Ms. Mummy’s bed. It wobbles, and I wobble with it, but I stay upright.

The robot arms reach for me, but I flatten myself against the wall again.

I’m pretty sure I was in a coma. Yeah. The more I think of it, I was definitely in a coma.

I don’t know how long I’ve been here, but if I was put here at the same time as my roommates, it’s been a while. I rub my half-shaved face. Those arms are designed to manage long-term unconsciousness. More proof I was in a coma.

Maybe I can get to that hatch?

I take a step. Then another. Then I sink to the floor. It’s just too much for me. I have to rest.

Why am I so weak when I have these great muscles? And if I was in a coma, why do I even have muscles? I should be a shriveled, weak thing right now, not this buff.

I have no idea what I'm supposed to do. What should I do? Am I really sick? I mean, I feel like garbage of course, but I don’t feel “sick.” I’m not queasy. I don’t have a headache. I don’t think I have a fever. If I don’t have a disease, why was I in a coma? Some kind of injury?

I feel around my head. No bumps or scars or bandages. The rest of my body seems fine too. Better than fine. I’m ripped.

I want to fall asleep but I fight it.

Time to try this again. I push myself back up. It’s like lifting weights. But it’s a little easier this time. I’m getting better and better (I hope).

I shuffle along the wall, using my back for support as much as my feet. The arms keep reaching for me but I stay away.

I pant and gasp. I feel like I’ve run a marathon. Maybe I have a lung thing? Maybe I’m in isolation to keep me safe?

I finally make it to the ladder. I stumble forward and grab one of the steps. I’m just so weak. How am I going to climb a 10-foot ladder?

Ten-foot ladder.

I think in feet and inches. That’s a clue. I’m probably an American. Or English. Or maybe Canadian. Canadians use feet and inches for short distances.

I ask myself: How far is it from L.A. to New York? My first thought: 3,000 miles. A Canadian would have used kilometers. So I’m English or American. Or I’m from Liberia.

I know Liberia uses imperial units but I don’t know my own name. That’s annoying.

I take a deep breath. I hold onto the ladder with both hands and put my foot on the bottom step. I pull myself up. It’s wobbly, but I do it. Both feet are on the bottom step now. I reach up and grab the next step. Okay, I’m moving forward. I feel like my whole body is made of lead—everything takes so much effort. I try to pull myself up, but my hand just isn’t strong enough.

I fall backward off the ladder. This is going to hurt.

It doesn’t hurt. The robot arms catch me before I hit the ground because I fell where they could reach me. They don’t miss a beat. They return me to bed and settle me in like a mother putting her child to sleep.

You know what? This is okay. I’m really tired and lying down feels good. The gentle rocking of the bed is comforting. Something bothers me about how I fell off the ladder. I replay it in my head. I can’t say what it is, but something just feels…“wrong.”

Hmm.

I drift off.

“Eat.”

There’s a toothpaste tube on my chest.

“Huh?”

“Eat,” the computer says again.

I lift the tube. It’s white with black text that reads DAY 1—MEAL 1.

“What the heck is this?” I say.

“Eat.”

I unscrew the cap and smell something good. My mouth waters at the thought of it. I realize how hungry I am. I squeeze the tube and gross brown stuff comes out.

“Eat.”

Who am I to question a creepy robot computer boss? I carefully lick the stuff.

Oh my God, it’s good! It’s so good! It’s like thick gravy but not too heavy. I squeeze more straight into my mouth and enjoy it. I swear it’s better than sex.

I know what’s happening here. They say hunger makes everything taste better. When you’re starving, your brain really rewards you for finally eating. Good job, it says, we won't die for a little while!

It all makes sense. If I was in a coma for a long time, I must have been getting fed. I didn’t have a tube in my stomach when I woke up, so they probably fed me with a tube down my throat. It’s the easiest way to feed someone who can’t eat but can digest food. Plus, it keeps the digestive system working and healthy. And it explains why the tube was gone when I woke up. If they can, they take out the tube while the patient is still unconscious.

Why do I know that? Am I a doctor?

I squeeze another shot of gravy-goo into my mouth. Still delicious. I gulp it down. Soon the tube is empty. I hold it up. “More of this!”

“Meal complete.”

“I’m still hungry! Give me another tube!”

“You’ve had all your food for this meal.”

It makes sense. My digestive system is getting used to food again. It’s best to take it slow. If I eat as much as I want, I’ll probably get sick. The computer is doing the right thing.

“Give me more food!” No one cares about the right thing when they’re hungry.

"Food provided for this meal is complete."

"Bah."

Still, I feel much better than before. The food gave me energy right away, and I've had more rest.

I get out of bed, ready to run for the wall, but the arms don't chase me. I guess I'm allowed out of bed now that I've shown I can eat.

I look down at my naked body. This just feels wrong. I know the only other people around are dead, but still.

"Can I have some clothes?"

The computer says nothing.

"Fine. Be that way."

I pull the sheet off the bed and wrap it around my body a few times. I pull one corner over my shoulder from behind and tie it to another in the front. Instant toga.

"Movement detected," says the computer. "What's your name?"

“I am Emperor Comatose. Kneel before me.”

“Incorrect.”

Time to see what’s up that ladder.

I’m a little shaky, but I start walking across the room. This is a victory in itself—I don’t need wobbly beds or walls to hold on to. I’m on my own two feet.

I make it to the ladder and grab hold. I don’t need something to hang on to, but it sure makes things easier. The hatch above looks pretty solid. I assume it’s airtight. And it might be locked. But I have to at least try.

I climb up one rung. Hard, but I can do it. Another rung. Okay, I’m getting the hang of this. Slow and steady.

I make it to the hatch. I hold on to the ladder with one hand and turn the hatch’s circular handle with the other. It actually turns!

“Holy moly!” I say.

“Holy moly”? Is that what I say when I’m surprised? It’s okay, I guess. I would have expected something a little less old-fashioned. What kind of weirdo am I?

I turn the handle three full times and hear a click. The hatch tilts downward and I get out of the way. It falls open, held by its strong hinge. I’m free!

Sort of.

Beyond the hatch, there’s just darkness. A little scary, but at least it’s a step forward.

I reach into the new room and pull myself up. Lights turn on as soon as I enter. Probably the computer's doing it.

The room looks to be the same size and shape as the one I left—another round room.

One large table—a lab table, it seems—is fixed to the floor. Three lab stools are fixed nearby. All around the walls are pieces of lab equipment. All of it fixed to tables or benches that are bolted to the floor. It’s like the room is ready for a huge earthquake.

A ladder along the wall goes to another hatch in the ceiling.

I’m in a well-stocked laboratory. Since when do isolation wards let patients into the lab? And this doesn’t look like a medical lab, anyway. What the heck is going on?!

Heck? Seriously? Maybe I have young kids. Or I’m very religious.

I stand to get a better look at things.

The lab has smaller equipment fixed to the table. I see an 8000x microscope, an autoclave, a bank of test tubes, sets of supply drawers, a sample fridge, a furnace, pipettes—wait a minute. Why do I know all these terms?

I look at the large equipment along the walls. Scanning electron microscope, tiny 3-D printer, 11-axis milling machine, laser interferometer, 1-cubic-meter vacuum chamber—I know what everything is. And I know how to use it.

I’m a scientist! Now we’re getting somewhere! Time for me to use science. Okay, smart brain: think of something!

…I’m hungry.

You have disappointed me, brain.

Okay, well I have no idea why this lab is here or why I’m allowed in. But…let's go!

The hatch in the ceiling is 10 feet off the ground. It’s going to be another ladder climb. At least I’m stronger now.

I take a few deep breaths and start climbing the ladder. Same as before, this simple act is a huge effort. I may be getting better, but I’m not “well.”

Good lord I’m heavy. I make it to the top, but barely.

I sit on the uncomfortable bars and push on the hatch’s handle. It doesn’t move.

“To unlock hatch, say your name,” says the computer.

“But I don’t know my name!”

“Incorrect.”

I smack the handle with my palm. It doesn’t move, and now my palm hurts. So…yeah. No luck.

This will have to wait. Maybe I’ll remember my name soon. Or find it written somewhere.

I climb back down the ladder. At least, that’s the plan. You’d think going down would be easier and safer than going up. But no. Instead of gracefully climbing down, I put my foot on the next rung at a weird angle, lose my grip on the hatch handle, and fall like an idiot.

I flail like an angry cat, reaching for anything I can grab. Turns out that’s a terrible idea. I fall onto the table and smack a set of supply drawers with my shin. It hurts like crazy! I cry out, grab my shin in pain, accidentally roll off the table, and fall to the floor.

No robot arms to catch me this time. I land on my back, and it knocks the wind out of me. Then, to make things worse, the supply drawers fall over, the drawers open, and lab supplies rain down on me. The cotton swabs aren’t a problem. The test tubes just hurt a little (and surprisingly don’t break). But the tape measure smacks me right on the forehead.

More stuff clatters down, but I’m too busy holding the growing bump on my forehead to notice. How heavy is that tape measure? A 3-foot fall off a table left a bump on my head.

“That. Did not work,” I say to no one. That whole thing was just ridiculous. Like something out of a Charlie Chaplin movie.

Actually…it really was like that. A little too much like that.

That same feeling of unease hits me.

I grab a test tube and toss it up. It rises and falls as it should. But it bothers me. Something about falling objects irritates me right now. I want to know why.

What do I have to use? Well, I have a whole lab and I know how to use it. But what's close by? I look around at the mess on the floor. Test tubes, sample swabs, Popsicle sticks, a digital stopwatch, pipettes, some tape, a pen…

Okay, I might have what I need here.

I stand up and dust off my toga. There's no dust on it—my whole world seems very clean, but I do it anyway.

I pick up the tape measure and look at it. It's metric. Maybe I'm in Europe? Whatever. Then I grab the stopwatch. It's sturdy, like something for a hike. It has a plastic case with a rubber ring around it. Definitely waterproof. But also completely dead. The screen is blank.

I press some buttons, but nothing happens. I turn it over to look at the battery area. Maybe I can find a drawer with batteries if I know what kind it takes. I see a small red plastic ribbon sticking out of the back. I pull it, and it comes out. The stopwatch beeps on.

Like toys that say "batteries included." The plastic tab kept the battery from dying before the owner uses it. Okay, this is a new stopwatch. Everything in this lab looks new. Clean, neat, no wear. Not sure what that means.

I play with the stopwatch until I understand how it works. It’s simple.

I use the tape measure to see how high the table is. The table's underside is 91 centimeters from the floor.

I pick up a test tube. It’s not glass. It might be a strong plastic or something similar. It definitely didn’t break when it fell 3 feet onto the hard floor. Whatever it's made of, it's heavy enough that air resistance doesn't really matter.

I lay it on the table and get the stopwatch ready. I push the test tube off the table with one hand and start the stopwatch with the other. I time how long it takes to hit the ground. It takes about 0.37 seconds. That’s really fast. I hope my own reaction time isn’t messing up the results.

I write the time down on my arm with the pen—I still haven’t found any paper.

I put the test tube back and do the test again. This time I get 0.33. I do it a total of twenty times, writing down the results, to reduce the impact of my mistakes in starting and stopping the timer. In the end, I get an average of 0.348 seconds. My arm looks like a math teacher’s chalkboard, but that’s fine.

0.348 seconds. Distance equals one-half acceleration times time squared. So acceleration equals two times distance over time squared. These formulas come to me easily. It's like I was born knowing them. I’m definitely good at physics. Good to know.

I do the math and get an answer I don’t like. The gravity in this room is too strong. It’s 15 meters per second per second when it should be 9.8. That’s why things falling “feel” wrong to me. They’re falling too fast. And that’s why I’m so weak even though I have muscles. Everything weighs one and a half times as much as it should.

The thing is, nothing changes gravity. You can’t make it stronger or weaker. Earth’s gravity is 9.8 meters per second per second. That’s it. And I’m experiencing more than that. There’s only one explanation.

I’m not on Earth.

Chapter 2

Okay, breathe. Let's not freak out. Yes, the gravity is too strong. Start there and think of reasonable answers.

I could be in a centrifuge. It would have to be pretty big. But with Earth's gravity giving 1 g, you could have these rooms at an angle going around a track or on the end of a long arm or something. Spin that around, and the total force of the spin plus Earth's gravity could be 15 meters per second per second.

Why would someone build a huge centrifuge with hospital beds and a lab in it? I don't know. Could you even do that? How big would it have to be? And how fast would it spin?

I think I know how to figure it out. I need a good accelerometer. Dropping things off a table and timing them is okay for rough guesses, but it's only as good as my reaction time on the stopwatch. I need something better. And only one thing will work: a small piece of string.

I look through the lab drawers.

After a few minutes, I have half the drawers open and have found almost every kind of lab supply except string. I'm about to quit when I finally find a spool of nylon thread.

"Yes!" I pull off a few feet of thread and bite it off. I tie a loop on one end and tie the other end around the tape measure. The tape measure will be the "weight" in this test. Now I just need something to hang it from.

I look up at the hatch over my head. I climb up the ladder (easier now) and put the loop over the latch handle. Then I let the tape measure pull the string tight.

I have a pendulum.

The cool thing about pendulums is that the time it takes to swing back and forth—the period—stays the same, no matter how big the swing. If it has a lot of energy, it swings farther and faster, but the period is still the same. This is how mechanical clocks keep time. The period depends on only two things: the length of the pendulum and gravity.

I pull the pendulum to the side. I let it go and start the timer. I count each swing back and forth. It’s not exciting. I almost fall asleep, but I keep going.

When I reach ten minutes, the pendulum is hardly moving, so I decide that’s enough. In total, it swung 346 times in ten minutes.

Now for phase two.

I measure from the hatch handle to the floor. It’s a little over two and a half meters. I go back downstairs to the “bedroom.” Again, the ladder is easy. I feel much better now. That food really helped.

“What’s your name?” the computer asks.

I look at my sheet toga. “I am the great philosopher Pendulus!”

“Incorrect.”

I hang the pendulum on a robot hand near the ceiling. I hope it stays still for a while. I estimate the distance from the robot hand to the ceiling—about a meter. My pendulum is now four and a half meters lower than before.

I do the experiment again. Ten minutes on the stopwatch, and I count the swings. The result: 346 swings. Same as upstairs.

Gosh.

Here's the thing: in a centrifuge, the farther you are from the middle, the stronger the force. So, if I were in a centrifuge, the "gravity" down here would be stronger than upstairs. But it isn't. At least, not enough to change how many times the pendulum swings.

But what if I'm in a really big centrifuge? One so big that the force difference between here and the lab is so small it doesn't change the number of swings?

Let's see…the formula for a pendulum…and the formula for the force of a centrifuge…wait, I don't have the actual force, just a swing count, so there’s a one-over-x thing involved…this is actually a very helpful problem!

I have a pen, but no paper. That’s okay—I have a wall. After a lot of "crazy prisoner scribbling on a wall"–type stuff, I have my answer.

Let's say I'm on Earth and in a centrifuge. That would mean the centrifuge adds to the force already here from Earth. According to my math (and I showed all my work!), that centrifuge would need a 700-meter radius (which is almost half a mile) and would be spinning at 88 meters per second—almost 200 miles per hour!

Hmm. I mostly think in metric when doing science. Interesting. Most scientists do, though, right? Even scientists who grew up in America.

Anyway, that would be the biggest centrifuge ever…and why would anyone build it? Plus, it would be super loud. Zooming through the air at 200 miles per hour? At the very least there’d be some shaking here and there, not to mention a lot of wind noise. I don’t hear or feel anything like that.

This is getting strange. Okay, what if I'm in space? There wouldn't be shaking or wind resistance, but the centrifuge would have to be bigger and faster because there’s no gravity to help.

More math, more graffiti on the wall. The radius would have to be 1,280 meters—close to a mile. Nothing that big has ever been built for space.

So I’m not in a centrifuge. And I’m not on Earth.

Another planet? But there isn’t any planet, moon, or asteroid in the solar system with this much gravity. Earth is the biggest solid thing in the whole system. Sure, the gas giants are larger, but unless I’m in a balloon floating in Jupiter’s winds, there’s nowhere I could be to feel this force.

How do I know all that space stuff? I just know it. It feels natural—like information I always use. Maybe I’m an astronomer or a planetary scientist. Maybe I work for NASA or ESA or—

—

I met Marissa every Thursday night for steak and beer at Murphy’s on Gough Street. Always at six p.m., and because the staff knew us, always at the same table.

We’d met almost twenty years ago in grad school. She dated my roommate back then. Their relationship (like most in grad school) was a mess and they broke up within three months. But she and I became good friends.

When the host saw me, he smiled and pointed to our usual table. I walked through the corny decorations to Marissa. She had a couple of empty glasses in front of her and a full one in her hand. Looks like she’d started early.

“Pre-gaming, huh?” I said, sitting down.

She looked down and played with her glass.

“Hey, what’s wrong?”

She took a sip of whiskey. “Rough day at work.”

I signaled the waiter. He nodded but didn’t even come over. He knew I wanted a rib-eye, medium, mashed potatoes on the side, and a pint of Guinness. Same thing I ordered every week.

“How rough could it be?” I asked. “Easy government job with the DOE. You probably get, like, twenty days off a year? All you have to do is show up and you get paid, right?”

Again, no laugh. Nothing.

“Oh, come on!” I said. “What’s got you so down?”

She sighed. “You know about the Petrova line?”

“Sure. Interesting mystery. I’m guessing solar radiation. Venus doesn’t have a magnetic field, but positive particles might go there because it’s electrically neutral—”

“No,” she said. “It’s something else. We don’t know what. But it’s something…else. But whatever. Let’s eat steak.”

I snorted. “Come on, Marissa, tell me. What is wrong?”

She thought about it. “Why not? You’ll hear it from the president in about twelve hours anyway.”

“The president?” I said. “Of the United States?”

She took another sip of whiskey. “Have you heard of Amaterasu? It’s a Japanese solar probe.”

“Sure,” I said. “JAXA has been getting some great data from it. It’s really cool, actually. It’s in a solar orbit, about halfway between Mercury and Venus. It has twenty different instruments that—”

“Yeah, I know. Whatever,” she said. “According to their data, the sun’s output is decreasing.”

I shrugged. “So? Where are we in the solar cycle?”

She shook her head. “It’s not the eleven-year cycle. It’s something else. JAXA considered the cycle. There’s still a downward trend. They say the sun is 0.01 percent less bright than it should be.”

“Okay, interesting. But hardly worth three whiskeys before dinner.”

She pursed her lips. “That’s what I thought. But they’re saying that value is increasing. And the speed of the increase is increasing. It’s some sort of exponential loss that they caught very, very early thanks to their probe’s super sensitive instruments.”

I leaned back in the booth. “I don’t know, Marissa. Finding an exponential progression that early seems really unlikely. But okay, let’s say the JAXA scientists are right. Where’s the energy going?”

“The Petrova line.”

“Huh?”

“JAXA studied the Petrova line carefully and says it’s getting brighter as the sun dims. Somehow, the Petrova line is stealing energy from the sun.”

She took some papers from her purse and put them on the table. They were full of graphs and charts. She sorted through them, found the one she wanted, and slid it to me.

The x-axis said “time,” and the y-axis said “luminosity loss.” The line was definitely exponential.

“This can’t be right,” I said.

“It is,” she said. “The sun’s output will drop one percent in nine years. In twenty years, it’ll be five percent. This is bad. Really bad.”

I stared at the graph. “That means an ice age. Like…soon. An instant ice age.”

“Yeah, at least. And crop failures, mass starvation…I don’t know what else.”

I shook my head. “How can the sun change so fast? It’s a star. Stars don’t change this fast. Changes take millions of years, not dozens. You know that.”

“No, I don’t know that. I used to know that. Now I only know the sun’s dying,” she said. “I don’t know why and I don’t know what we could do about it. But I know it’s dying.”

“How…” I frowned.

She finished her drink. “President speaks to the nation tomorrow morning. I think they’re working with other world leaders to all announce at the same time.”

The waiter set down my Guinness. “Here you go, sir. The steaks should be out soon.”

“I need another whiskey,” Marissa said.

“Make it two,” I added.

—

I blink. Another memory.

Was it true? Or is that just a random memory of me talking to someone who believed in a fake doomsday theory?

No. It’s real. I’m terrified just thinking about it. And it’s not just a sudden terror. It’s a cozy, comfortable terror that’s always with me. I’ve felt it for a long time.

This is real. The sun is dying. And I’m caught up in it. Not just as someone who lives on Earth and will die with everyone else—I’m actively involved. I feel responsible.

I still don’t remember my own name, but I remember random bits of information about the Petrova problem. They call it the Petrova problem. I just remembered that.

My subconscious has priorities. And it’s desperately telling me about this. I think my job is to solve the Petrova problem.

…in a small lab, wearing a bedsheet toga, with no idea who I am, and no help other than a mindless computer and two mummified roommates.

My vision blurs. I wipe my eyes. Tears. I can’t…I can’t remember their names. But…they were my friends. My comrades.

Only now do I realize I’ve been facing away from them the whole time. I’ve done everything I can to keep them out of sight. Scrawling on the wall like a madman with the corpses of people I cared about right behind me.

But now the distraction is over. I turn to look at them.

I sob. It comes without warning. I remember bits and pieces all at once. She was funny—always ready with a joke. He was professional and never lost his nerve. I think he was military and he was definitely our leader.

I fall to the floor and put my head in my hands. I can’t hold anything back. I cry like a child. We were a lot more than friends. And “team” isn’t the right word either. It’s stronger than that. It’s…

It’s on the tip of my tongue…

Finally, the word pops into my head. It had to wait until I wasn’t paying attention to sneak in.

Crew. We were a crew. And I’m all that’s left.

This is a spacecraft. I know that now. I don’t know how it has gravity but it’s a spaceship.

Things start to make sense. We weren’t sick. We were in suspended animation.

But these beds aren’t magical “freeze chambers” like in the movies. There’s no special technology here. I think we were in medically induced comas. Feeding tubes, IVs, constant medical care. Everything a body needs. Those arms probably changed sheets, kept us rotated to prevent bedsores, and did everything ICU nurses normally do.

And we were kept fit. Electrodes all over our bodies to stimulate muscle movement. Lots of exercise.

But in the end, comas are dangerous. Very dangerous. Only I survived, and my brain is a mess.

I walk over to the woman. I actually feel better, looking at her. Maybe it’s a sense of closure, or maybe it’s just the calmness that comes after a crying fit.

The mummy has no tubes attached. No monitoring equipment at all. There’s a small hole in her leathery wrist. That’s where the IV was when she died, I guess. So the hole never healed.

The computer must have deleted everything when she died. Waste not, want not, I guess. No use wasting resources on dead people. More for the living.

More for me, in other words.

I take a deep breath and let it out. I have to be calm. I have to think clearly. I remembered a lot just then—my crew, some of what they were like, that I’m on a spaceship (I’ll freak out about that later). The point is I’m getting more memories back, and they’re coming sort of when I want them instead of randomly. I want to focus on that, but the sadness is just so strong.

“Eat,” says the computer.

A panel in the center of the ceiling opens up, and a food tube drops out. One of the robot arms catches it and places it on my bed. The label reads DAY 1—MEAL 2.

I’m not in the mood to eat, but my stomach growls as soon as I see the tube. Whatever I’m feeling, my body has needs.

I open the tube and squirt goop into my mouth.

I have to admit: It’s another amazing flavor. I think it’s chicken with a little vegetable. There’s no texture, of course—it’s basically baby food. And it’s a little thicker than my earlier meal. It’s all about getting my stomach used to solid food again.

“Water?” I say between mouthfuls.

The ceiling panel opens again, this time with a metal cylinder. An arm brings it to me. Text on the shiny container reads DRINKING WATER. I unscrew the top and, sure enough, there’s water in there.

I take a sip. It’s room temperature and tastes flat. It’s probably distilled and without minerals. But water’s water.

I finish the rest of my meal. I haven’t had to use a bathroom yet but I will eventually. I’d rather not pee on the floor.

“Toilet?” I say.

A wall panel spins around to show a metal toilet. It’s right there in the wall, like in a prison cell. I take a closer look. It has buttons and stuff on it. I think there’s a vacuum pipe in the bowl. And there’s no water. I think this might be a zero-g toilet changed to work in gravity. Why do that?

“Okay, uh…hide toilet.”

The wall swivels around again. The toilet is gone.

All right. I’m well fed. I’m feeling a little better about things. Food will do that.

I need to focus on good things. I’m alive. Whatever killed my friends, it didn’t kill me. I’m on a spaceship—I don’t know the details, but I know I’m on a ship and it seems to be working right.

And my mental state is getting better. I’m sure of it.

I sit cross-legged on the floor. It’s time to take action. I close my eyes and let my mind wander. I want to remember something—anything—on purpose. I don’t care what. But I want to start it. Let’s see what I get.

I start with what makes me happy. I like science. I know it well. I get excited by the little experiments I’ve been doing. And I’m in space. So maybe I can think about space and science and see what ideas come to me….

—

I took the very hot spaghetti TV dinner out of the microwave and hurried to my couch. I peeled off the plastic top to let the steam out.

I turned the TV sound on and listened to the live broadcast. Several coworkers and a few friends had asked me to watch this with them, but I didn’t want to spend the whole evening answering questions. I just wanted to watch quietly.

It was the most watched event ever. More than the moon landing. More than any World Cup Final. Every TV channel, streaming service, news website, and local station was showing the same thing: NASA’s live broadcast.

A reporter stood with an older man in the viewing area of a flight-control room. Behind them, men and women in blue shirts focused on their computer screens.

“This is Sandra Elias,” said the reporter. “I’m here at the Jet Propulsion Laboratory in Pasadena, California. I’m here with Dr. Browne, who is the head of Planetary Sciences for NASA.”

She turned to the scientist. “Doctor, what’s happening now?”

Browne cleared his throat. “We got confirmation about ninety minutes ago that ArcLight successfully entered orbit around Venus. Now we’re just waiting for the first set of data.”

It had been a crazy year since JAXA's announcement about the Petrova problem. But study after study backed up what they found. Time was running out, and the world needed to know what was happening. That's how Project ArcLight started.

The situation was scary, but the project itself was amazing. The science geek in me couldn’t help but be excited.

ArcLight was the most expensive unmanned spacecraft ever made. The world needed answers and couldn’t wait around. Usually, if you asked a space agency to send a probe to Venus in less than a year, they’d laugh at you. But it’s amazing what you can do when money is no object. The United States, European Union, Russia, China, India, and Japan all helped pay for it.

“Tell us about going to Venus,” the reporter said. “What makes it so difficult?”

“The main problem is fuel,” said Browne. “There are specific times when traveling between planets uses the least amount of fuel, but we were nowhere near a good time to go from Earth to Venus. So, we had to put a lot more fuel in orbit just to get ArcLight there in the first place.”

“So it’s just bad timing, then?” the reporter asked.

“I don’t think there’s ever a good time for the sun to get dimmer.”

“Good point. Please continue.”

“Venus moves very fast compared to Earth, which means it takes more fuel just to catch up. Even when things are ideal, it actually takes more fuel to get to Venus than it does to get to Mars.”

“Amazing. Amazing. Now, Doctor, some people have asked, ‘Why bother with the planet? The Petrova line is huge, going all the way from the sun to Venus. Why not somewhere in between?’ ”

“Because the Petrova line is thickest there—as thick as the whole planet. And we can use the planet’s gravity to help us. ArcLight will orbit Venus twelve times while gathering samples of whatever the Petrova line is made of.”

“And what do you think that material is?”

“We have no idea,” said Browne. “None at all. But we might know soon. After ArcLight finishes this first orbit, it should have enough material for its lab.”

“And what can we expect to learn tonight?”

“Not much. The lab is pretty simple. Just a strong microscope and an x-ray tool. The main goal here is to bring samples back. It’ll be another three months for ArcLight to return with those samples. The lab is there to get some data in case something goes wrong on the way back.”

“Good planning as always, Dr. Browne.”

“It’s what we do.”

A cheer came from behind the reporter.

“I’m hearing—” She paused for the sound to quiet down. “I’m hearing that the first orbit is done and the data is coming in now….”

The main screen in the control room changed to a black-and-white picture. It was mostly gray, with black dots here and there.

“What are we looking at, Doctor?” the reporter asked.

“This is from the internal microscope,” said Browne. “It’s magnified ten thousand times. Those black dots are about ten microns across.”

“Are those dots what we’ve been looking for?” she asked.

“We can’t be sure,” said Browne. “They could just be dust. Any large gravity source like a planet will have a cloud of dust surrounding—”

“What the fuck?!” someone shouted in the background. Several flight controllers gasped.

The reporter chuckled. “Everyone’s excited here at JPL. We’re live, so we apologize for any—”

“Oh my God!” said Browne.

More images appeared on the main screen. One after another. All almost the same.

Almost.

The reporter looked at the images on-screen. “Are those particles…moving?”

The images, playing one after another, showed the black dots changing shape and moving around in their space.

The reporter cleared her throat and said what many would call the understatement of the century: “They look a bit like microbes, don’t you think?”

“Telemetry!” Dr. Browne called out. “Is the probe shaking?”

“Already checked,” said someone. “No shaking.”

“Are they all moving in the same direction?” he asked. “Could something outside be causing it? Like magnetism? Static electricity?”

The room went silent.

“Anyone?!” said Browne.

I dropped my fork right into my spaghetti.

Is this really alien life? Am I really this lucky?! To be alive when humanity first finds life from another planet?!

Wow! I mean—the Petrova problem is still scary but…wow! Aliens! This could be aliens! I couldn’t wait to talk about this with the kids tomorrow—

—

“Angular anomaly,” the computer says.

“Darn it!” I say. “I was almost there! I almost remembered myself!”

“Angular anomaly,” the computer repeats.

I unfold myself and get to my feet. From what I can tell, the computer seems to understand some of what I say. Like Siri or Alexa. So I’ll talk to it like them.

“Computer, what is an angular anomaly?”

“Angular anomaly: a critical object is not at the expected angle by at least 0.01 radians.”

“What object is anomalous?”

“Angular anomaly.”

Not much help. I'm on a ship, so it must be a navigation problem. That can't be good. How would I even steer this thing? I don’t see anything that looks like spaceship controls—not that I really know what those look like. But all I’ve found so far is a “coma room” and a lab.

That other hatch in the lab—the one that goes farther up—that must be important. This is like being in a video game. Explore the area until you find a locked door, then look for the key. But instead of searching bookshelves and trash cans, I have to search my mind. Because the “key” is my own name.

The computer's not being unreasonable. If I can't remember my own name, I probably shouldn't be allowed into important areas of the ship.

I climb onto my bunk and lie on my back. I keep an eye on the robot arms above, but they don't move. I guess the computer thinks I can take care of myself for now.

I close my eyes and focus on that flash of memory. I can see bits and pieces of it in my mind. Like looking at an old, damaged photo.

I'm in my house…no…apartment. I have an apartment. It's tidy, but small. There's a picture of the San Francisco skyline on one wall. Not helpful. I already know I lived in San Francisco.

There's a Lean Cuisine microwave meal on the coffee table in front of me. Spaghetti. The heat isn't even yet, so there are pockets of nearly frozen noodles next to burning hot ones. But I'm taking bites anyway. I must be hungry.

I'm watching NASA on TV; I see all that stuff from my previous flash of memory. My first thought is…I'm thrilled! Could it be alien life? I can't wait to tell the kids!

I have kids? This is a single man's apartment with a single man eating a single man's meal. I don't see anything feminine at all. There's nothing to suggest a woman in my life. Am I divorced? Gay? Either way, there's no sign that children live here. No toys, no pictures of kids on the wall or mantel, nothing. And the place is way too clean. Kids make a mess of everything. Especially when they start chewing gum. They all go through a gum phase—at least, a lot of them do—and they leave it everywhere.

How do I know that?

I like kids. Huh. Just a feeling. But I like them. They’re cool. They’re fun to be around.

So I’m a single man in my thirties, who lives alone in a small apartment, I don’t have any kids, but I like kids a lot. I don’t like where this is headed…

A teacher! I’m a schoolteacher! I remember now!

Oh, thank God. I’m a teacher.

Chapter 3

“All right,” I said, looking at the clock. “We have one minute until the bell. You know what that means!”

“Lightning round!” yelled my students.

Life hadn't changed much since the Petrova line announcement, surprisingly.

The situation was terrible and deadly, but it was also normal. Londoners during the Blitz in World War II went about their day as usual, knowing that sometimes buildings get blown up. No matter how bad things were, someone still had to deliver milk. And if Mrs. McCreedy’s house was bombed at night, you just took it off the delivery list.

So, with the apocalypse coming—possibly caused by an alien—I stood in front of a bunch of kids and taught them basic science. Because what's the point of having a world if you're not going to pass it on to the next generation?

The kids sat in neat rows of desks, facing the front. Pretty standard stuff. But the rest of the room was like a mad scientist's lab. I’d spent years making it look that way. I had a Jacob’s ladder in one corner (I kept it unplugged so the kids didn’t kill themselves). Along another wall was a bookshelf full of jars with animal parts in formaldehyde. One of the jars was just spaghetti and a boiled egg. The kids guessed about that one a lot.

And in the center of the ceiling was my pride and joy—a huge mobile that was a model of the solar system. Jupiter was the size of a basketball, while tiny Mercury was as small as a marble.

It had taken me years to get a reputation as the “cool” teacher. Kids are smarter than most people think. And they can tell when a teacher actually cares about them, not just going through the motions. Anyway, it was time for the lightning round!

I grabbed a handful of beanbags off my desk. “What is the real name of the North Star?”

“Polaris!” said Jeff.

“Correct!” I threw a beanbag to him. Before he even caught it, I asked the next question. “What are the three basic kinds of rocks?”

“Igneous, sedentary, and metamorphic!” yelled Larry. He was easily excited, to say the least.

“So close!” I said.

“Igneous, sedimentary, and metamorphic,” said Abby with a sneer. She was a pain, that one, but really smart.

“Yes!” I threw her a beanbag. “What wave do you feel first during an earthquake?”

“The P-wave,” Abby said.

“You again?” I threw her a beanbag. “What’s the speed of light?”

“Three times ten to—” Abby began.

“C!” yelled Regina from the back. She rarely spoke. It was good to see her opening up.

“Tricky, but right!” I threw her a beanbag.

“I was answering first!” Abby complained.

“But she finished her answer first,” I said. “What’s the closest star to Earth?”

“Alpha Centauri!” Abby said quickly.

“Wrong!” I said.

“No, I’m not!”

“Yes, you are. Anyone else?”

“Oh!” Larry said. “It’s the sun!”

“Right!” I said. “Larry gets the beanbag! Watch out for what you assume, Abby.”

She folded her arms in a huff.

“Who knows the radius of Earth?”

Trang raised his hand. “Three thousand, nine hundre—”

“Trang!” Abby said. “The answer is Trang.”

Trang froze, confused.

“What?” I asked.

Abby smirked. “You asked who could tell you the radius of Earth. Trang can. I answered correctly.”

Outsmarted by a thirteen-year-old. Not the first time. I dropped a beanbag on her desk as the bell rang.

The kids jumped up and grabbed their books and backpacks. Abby, thrilled with her win, took her time.

“Remember to trade in your beanbags at the end of the week for toys and prizes!” I called to them as they left.

Soon, the classroom was empty. Only the sounds of kids in the hallway showed anyone had been there. I picked up their homework from my desk and put it in my bag. Sixth period was over.

Time to hit the teachers’ lounge for a cup of coffee. Maybe I’d grade some papers before I headed home. Anything to avoid the parking lot. A bunch of helicopter moms would be swarming the school to pick up their children. And if one of them saw me, they always had some complaint or suggestion. I can’t blame someone for loving their kids, and God knows we could use more parents involved in their kids’ educations, but there’s a limit.

“Ryland Grace?” said a woman’s voice.

I looked up, surprised. I hadn’t heard her come in.

She looked to be in her mid-forties, wearing a nice business suit. She carried a briefcase.

“Uh, yeah,” I said. “Can I help you?”

“I think you can,” she said. She had a slight accent. Something European—I couldn’t quite place it. “My name is Eva Stratt. I’m with the Petrova Taskforce.”

“The what?”

“The Petrova Taskforce. It’s an international group formed to handle the Petrova-line situation. I’ve been given the job of finding a solution. They’ve given me some power to get things done.”

“They? Who's they?”

“Every country in the UN.”

“Wait, what? How did—”

“Unanimous secret vote. It’s complicated. I want to talk to you about a scientific paper you wrote.”

“Secret vote? Never mind.” I shook my head. “I'm done writing papers. Academia didn’t suit me.”

“You’re a teacher. You’re still in academia.”

“Well, yeah,” I said. “But I mean, you know, academia. With scientists and peer review and—”

“And jerks who got you kicked out of your university?” She raised an eyebrow. “And who cut off all your funding and made sure you never got published again?”

“Yeah. That.”

She pulled a binder out of her briefcase.

She opened it and read the first page. “ ‘An Analysis of Water-Based Assumptions and Recalibration of Expectations for Evolutionary Models.’ ” She looked up at me. “You wrote this paper, right?”

“I’m sorry, how did you get—”

“A boring title, but the content is really exciting, I must say.”

I set my bag on my desk. “Look, I wasn't in a good place when I wrote that, okay? I was tired of the research world, and it was my way of saying goodbye. I’m much happier now as a teacher.”

She flipped through a few pages. “You spent years fighting the idea that life needs liquid water. You have a whole section here called ‘The Goldilocks Zone Is for Dummies.’ You call out many famous scientists by name and scold them for thinking a certain temperature range is necessary.”

“Yeah, but—”

“Your doctorate is in molecular biology, right? Don’t most scientists agree that life needs liquid water to develop?”

“They’re wrong!” I crossed my arms. “There’s nothing special about hydrogen and oxygen! They're needed for life on Earth, sure. But another planet could have completely different conditions. All life needs is a chemical reaction that makes copies of the original thing that started it. And you don’t need water for that!”

I closed my eyes, took a deep breath, and let it out. “Anyway, I got mad, and I wrote that paper. Then I got a teaching license, a new career, and started enjoying my life. So I’m glad no one believed me. I’m better off.”

“I believe you,” she said.

“Thanks,” I said. “But I have papers to grade. Can you tell me why you’re here?”

She put the binder back in her briefcase. “I assume you know about the ArcLight probe and the Petrova line.”

“I wouldn’t be a very good science teacher if I didn’t.”

“Do you think those dots are alive?” she asked.

“I don’t know—they might just be dust moving around in magnetic fields. I guess we’ll find out when ArcLight gets back to Earth. That’s soon, right? Just a few weeks?”

“It comes back on the twenty-third,” she said. “Roscosmos will get it from low-Earth orbit with a special Soyuz mission.”

I nodded. “Then we’ll know pretty soon. The smartest people in the world will look at them and figure out what they are. Who’s going to do that? Do you know?”

“You,” she said. “You’re going to do it.”

I stared blankly.

She waved her hand in front of my face. “Hello?”

“You want me to look at the dots?” I said.

“Yes.”

“The whole world asked you to solve this, and you came to a middle school science teacher?”

“Yes.”

I turned and walked out. “You’re lying, crazy, or both. I have to go now.”

“This isn’t a choice,” she said to my back.

“Looks like one to me!” I waved goodbye.

Yeah. It wasn’t a choice.

When I got back to my place, before I reached my door, four men in suits surrounded me. They showed me their FBI badges and rushed me into one of three black SUVs in the parking lot. After a twenty-minute drive where they wouldn't answer any of my questions or even talk to me, they parked and took me into a plain-looking building in a business park.

My feet barely hit the ground as they led me down an empty hall with doors every 30 feet. Finally, they opened double doors at the end of the hall and nudged me inside.

Unlike the rest of the empty building, this room was full of furniture and shiny, high-tech gadgets. It was the best biology lab I’d ever seen. And right in the middle was Eva Stratt.

“Hello, Dr. Grace,” she said. “This is your new lab.”

The FBI agents closed the doors behind me, leaving Stratt and me alone in the lab. I rubbed my shoulder where they had handled me a little too roughly.

I looked at the door behind me. “So…when you say ‘a certain amount of authority’…”

“I have all the authority.”

“You have an accent. Are you even from America?”

“I’m Dutch. I used to manage things at ESA. But that doesn’t matter. Now I’m in charge here. There is no time for slow, international groups. The sun is dying. We need a solution. It’s my job to find it.”

She pulled up a lab stool and sat down. “These ‘dots’ are probably alive. The sun dimming faster and faster matches how a living thing's population grows.”

“You think they’re… eating the sun?”

“They’re at least eating its energy,” she said.

“Okay, that’s—well, scary. But anyway: What do you want from me?”

“The ArcLight probe is bringing the samples back to Earth. Some of them might still be alive. I want you to study them and find out what you can.”

“Yeah, you said that before,” I replied. “But I still think there are people who can do this better than me.”

“Scientists everywhere will study them, but I want you to see them first.”

“Why?”

“It lives on or close to the sun. Does that sound like a life-form that needs water?”

She was right. Water can't exist at those temperatures. After about 3,000 degrees Celsius, hydrogen and oxygen break apart. The sun's surface was 5,500 degrees Celsius.

She went on. “The field of guessing about life on other planets is small—only about five hundred people in the world. And everyone I speak to—from Oxford professors to Tokyo University researchers—seems to think you could have been a leader if you hadn’t suddenly quit.”

“Wow,” I said. “I didn’t leave well. I’m surprised they said such good things about me.”

“Everyone knows how serious this is. There’s no time to hold grudges. But you can prove you were right. You don’t need water for life. That must be something you want.”

“Yeah,” I said. “I mean…yes. But not like this.”

She jumped off her stool and walked to the door. “It is what it is. Be here on the twenty-third at seven p.m. I’ll have the sample for you.”

“Wha—” I said. “It’ll be in Russia, won’t it?”

“I told Roscosmos to land their Soyuz in Saskatchewan. The Royal Canadian Air Force will get the sample and bring it straight here to San Francisco by fighter jet. The U.S. will let the Canadians use the airspace.”

“Saskatchewan?”

“Soyuz capsules launch from Baikonur Cosmodrome, which is at a high latitude. The safest landing spots are at that same latitude. Saskatchewan is the closest big, flat area to San Francisco that works.”

I held up my hand. “Wait. Do the Russians, Canadians, and Americans all just do what you say?”

“Yes. No questions asked.”

“Are you kidding me with all this?!”

“Get settled in your new lab, Dr. Grace. I have other things to do.”

She walked out the door without another word.

“Yes!” I pump my fist.

I jump up and climb the ladder to the lab. Then I climb that ladder and grab the Mystery Hatch.

Like before, when I touch the handle, the computer says, “To unlock hatch, state your name.”

“Ryland Grace,” I say, smiling. “ Dr. Ryland Grace.”

A small click from the hatch is the only answer. After all the thinking I did to remember my name, I hoped for something more exciting. Confetti, maybe.

I grab the handle and twist. It turns. My area is about to get bigger by at least one room. I push the hatch up. Unlike the door between the bedroom and the lab, this one slides to the side. This next room is pretty small, so I guess there wasn’t space for a swinging door. And that next room is…um…?

LED lights turn on. The room is round, like the other two, but the walls slant inward toward the ceiling. It’s like the top of a cone.

I haven’t had much information lately. Now it’s hitting me from everywhere. Computer monitors and touchscreens cover every surface. The number of blinking lights and colors is amazing. Some screens show numbers, others have diagrams, and others are just black.

On the edge of the slanted walls is another hatch. This one is less mysterious. It says AIRLOCK across the top, and it has a round window. Through the window, I see a small chamber—just big enough for one person—with a spacesuit inside. The far wall has another hatch. Yup. It’s an airlock.

And in the center of everything is a chair. It's placed just right so I can easily reach all the screens and touch panels.

I climb into the room and sit in the chair. It’s comfortable, like a bucket seat.

“Pilot detected,” the computer says. “Angular anomaly.”

Pilot. Okay.

“Where is the anomaly?” I ask.

“Angular anomaly.”

This computer is no HAL 9000. I look around at the many screens for a clue. The chair turns easily, which is good in this 360-degree computer pit. I see one screen with a blinking red border. I lean in to get a better look.

ANGULAR ANOMALY: RELATIVE MOTION ERROR

PREDICTED VELOCITY: 11,423 KPS

MEASURED VELOCITY: 11,872 KPS

STATUS: AUTO-CORRECTING TRAJECTORY. NO ACTION REQUIRED.

Well. That doesn’t mean anything to me. Except “kps.” That might mean “kilometers per second.”

Above the text is a picture of the sun. It’s jiggling slightly. Maybe it’s a video? Like a live feed? Or am I just imagining it? On a whim, I touch the screen with two fingers and spread them apart.

The image zooms in, just like on a smartphone. There are a couple of sunspots on the left side of the image. I zoom in on those until they fill the screen. The image stays amazingly clear. It’s either a very high-resolution photo or a very high-resolution solar telescope.

I guess the group of sunspots is about 1 percent the width of the circle. Pretty normal for sunspots. That means I’m now looking at half a degree of the sun’s edge (very rough math here). The sun rotates about once every twenty-five days (science teachers know this stuff). So it should take an hour for the spots to move off the screen. I’ll check back later and see if they have. If so, it’s a live image. If not, it’s a picture.

Hmm…11,872 kilometers per second.

Speed is relative. It only makes sense if you compare two things. A car on the freeway might be going 70 miles per hour compared to the ground , but compared to the car next to it, it’s moving almost 0. So what is that “measured velocity” measuring the speed of? I think I know.

I’m in a spaceship, right? I must be. So that value is probably my speed. But compared to what? Judging by the big picture of the sun above the text, I’m guessing it’s the sun. So I’m going 11,872 kilometers per second compared to the sun.

I see a flicker from the text below. Did something change?

ANGULAR ANOMALY: RELATIVE MOTION ERROR

PREDICTED VELOCITY: 11,422 KPS

MEASURED VELOCITY: 11,871 KPS

STATUS: AUTO-CORRECTING TRAJECTORY. NO ACTION REQUIRED.

Those numbers are different! They both went down by one. Oh wow. Wait a second. I pull the stopwatch from my toga (the best ancient Greek philosophers always carried stopwatches in their togas). Then I stare at the screen for what feels like forever. Just before I’m about to quit, the numbers both drop by one again. I start the timer.

This time, I’m ready for the wait. Again, it feels endless, but I stand still. Finally, the numbers both drop again and I stop the timer.

Sixty-six seconds.

“Measured velocity” is going down by one every sixty-six seconds. Some quick math tells me that’s an acceleration of…15 meters per second per second. That’s the same “gravity” acceleration I figured out earlier.

The force I’m feeling isn’t gravity. And it’s not a centrifuge. I’m in a spaceship that is constantly speeding up in a line. Well, actually it’s slowing down—the values are going down.

And that velocity…it’s a lot of velocity. Yes, it’s going down, but wow! To reach Earth orbit you only need to go 8 kps. I’m going over 11,000. That’s faster than anything in the solar system. Anything that fast will escape the sun’s gravity and fly off into interstellar space.

The readout doesn’t have anything to show what direction I’m going. Just a relative velocity. So now my question is: Am I heading toward the sun, or away from it?

It’s almost a given. I’m either going to crash into the sun or head out into deep space with no chance of coming back. Or, I might be going generally toward the sun, but not directly at it. If that’s the case, I’ll miss the sun…and then fly off into deep space with no hope of returning.

Well, if the sun image is live, the sunspot will get bigger or smaller on-screen as I move. So I just have to wait to see if it’s live. That’ll take about an hour. I start the stopwatch.

I look at the million other screens in the small room. Most of them are active, but one just shows a picture of a round emblem. I think it’s probably just a screen saver or something. If I touch it, that computer will turn on. But that screen saver might be the most important thing in here.

It’s a mission emblem. I’ve seen enough NASA documentaries to recognize it. The round emblem has a blue outer ring with white letters. The letters say HAIL MARY at the top and EARTH at the bottom. The name and “home port” for this ship.

I didn’t think the ship came from somewhere besides Earth, but okay. Anyway, I guess I finally know the name of the ship I’m on.

I’m on the Hail Mary.

Not sure what to do with that information.

But that’s not all the emblem tells me. Inside the blue ring, there’s a black circle with strange symbols inside: a yellow circle with a dot in the middle, a blue circle with a white cross, and a smaller yellow circle with a lowercase t. No idea what any of that is supposed to mean. Around the edge of the black area it says: “姚,” “ИЛЮХИНА,” and “GRACE.”

The crew.

I’m “Grace,” so the other two must be the names of the mummies in the bunks downstairs. A Chinese person and a Russian person. The memory of them is almost there, but I can’t quite grasp it. I think some internal protection is blocking it. When I remember them, it’s going to hurt, so my brain won’t let me remember them. Maybe. I don’t know—I’m a science teacher, not a trauma expert.

I wipe my eyes. Maybe I won’t try so hard to remember that just yet.

I have an hour to spare. I let my mind drift to see what else I can recall. It’s getting easier and easier.

—

“I’m not completely comfortable with all this,” I said. My voice was muffled by the hazmat suit. My breath fogged up the clear plastic face shield.

“You’ll be fine,” Stratt’s voice said over the intercom. She watched from behind thick, double-layered glass.

They’d improved the lab a bit. The equipment was the same, but now the whole room was sealed off from the air. The walls were covered in thick plastic sheets, held together with special tape. I saw CDC logos everywhere. Quarantine rules. Not very comforting.

The only way in was through a big plastic airlock. And they made me put on the hazmat suit before entering. An air hose connected my suit to a spool in the ceiling.

All the best equipment was ready for anything I wanted to do. I’d never seen a lab so well supplied. And in the middle was a cart with wheels holding a cylinder. The word образец was stenciled on the cylinder. Not very helpful.

Stratt wasn’t alone in the observation room. About twenty people in military uniforms stood with her, all watching with interest. There were Americans, Russians, a few Chinese officers, and many more uniforms I didn’t recognize. A large international group. None of them spoke, and they all stayed a few feet behind Stratt, without a word.

I grabbed the air hose with my gloved hand and pointed it at Stratt. “Is this really necessary?”

She pressed the intercom button. “There’s a good chance the sample in that cylinder is an alien life-form. We’re not taking any risks.”

“Wait… you’re not taking any risks. But I am!”

“It’s not like that.”

“How is it not like that?”

She paused. “Okay, it’s exactly like that.”

I walked to the cylinder. “Did everyone else have to go through all this?”

She looked at the military people and they shrugged at her. “What do you mean by ‘everyone else’?”

“You know,” I said. “The people who moved it to this container.”

“That’s the sample container from the capsule. It’s three centimeters of lead around a shell of steel that’s a centimeter thick. It’s been sealed since it left Venus. It has fourteen latches you’ll need to open to get to the sample itself.”

I looked at the cylinder, back to her, back to the cylinder, and back to her. “This is nonsense.”

“Look at the bright side,” she said. “You’ll be forever known as the man who made first contact with extraterrestrial life.”

“If it even is life,” I mumbled.

I got the fourteen latches open with some effort. Those things were tight. I vaguely wondered how the ArcLight probe closed them in the first place. Must have been some kind of cool automated system.

The inside wasn’t impressive. I didn’t expect it to be. Just a small, clear, plastic ball that appeared to be empty. The mysterious dots were microscopic and there weren’t very many of them.

“No radiation detected,” Stratt said through the intercom.

I glanced over at her. She watched her tablet intently.

I took a good long look at the ball. “Is this a vacuum?”

“No,” she said. “It’s full of argon gas at normal pressure. The dots have been moving around the whole time the probe was returning from Venus. So it looks like the argon doesn’t affect them.”

I looked all around the lab. “There’s no glove box here. I can’t just expose unknown samples to normal air.”

“The whole room is full of argon,” she said. “Don’t kink your air line or tear your suit. If you breathe argon—”

“I’ll suffocate without even knowing. Yeah, okay.”

I took the ball to a tray and carefully twisted it open. I put one half in a sealed plastic container and wiped the other half with a dry cotton swab. I scraped the swab on a slide and took it to a microscope.

I thought they’d be harder to find, but there they were. Dozens of little black dots, all wriggling.

“You recording all this?”

“From thirty-six different angles,” she said.

“Sample has many round objects,” I said. “Almost the same size—each about ten microns across…”

I adjusted the focus and tried different backlighting. “Samples are solid…I can’t see inside, even with the brightest light….”

“Are they alive?” Stratt asked.

I glared at her. “I can’t tell that instantly. What do you think will happen?”

“I want you to find out if they’re alive. And if they are, find out how they work.”

“That’s a big ask.”

“Why? Biologists figured out how bacteria work. Just do what they did.”

“That took thousands of scientists two centuries!”

“Well…do it faster than that.”

“Here’s the thing”—I pointed back to the microscope—“I’m going back to work. I’ll tell you what I find out when I find it out. Until then, you can all enjoy some quiet time to study.”

I spent the next six hours doing small tests. During that time, the military people left, until only Stratt was left. I had to admire her patience. She sat in the back of the room and worked on her tablet, sometimes looking up to see what I was doing.

She perked up when I went through the airlock and into the room. “Got something?” she asked.

I unzipped the suit and stepped out. “Yeah, I need to pee.”

She typed on her tablet. “I didn’t think of that. I’ll have a bathroom put in the quarantine area tonight. It’ll have to be a portable toilet. We can’t have pipes going in and out.”

“Fine, whatever,” I said. I hurried off to the restroom to take care of things.

When I came back, Stratt had moved a small table and two chairs to the middle of the observation room. She was sitting in one of the chairs and pointed to the other. “Have a seat.”

“I’m in the middle of—”

“Have a seat.”

I sat down. She definitely had a powerful presence. Maybe it was her tone of voice or just how confident she seemed? Either way, when she spoke, you just felt like you should do what she said.

“What have you found so far?” she asked.

“It’s only been one afternoon,” I said.

“I didn’t ask how long it’s been. I asked what you’ve found out so far.”

I scratched my head. After being in that suit for hours, I was sweaty and probably smelled bad. “It’s…weird. I don’t know what those dots are made of. And I really want to know.”

“Is there some equipment you need that you don’t have?” she asked.

“No, no. It has everything a guy could want. It just…doesn’t work on these dots.” I sat back in the chair. I’d been standing most of the day and it was nice to rest for a moment. “The first thing I tried was the x-ray spectrometer. It shoots x-rays into a sample, which makes it send out photons. You can then tell what elements are there from the photons' wavelengths.”

“And what did that tell you?”

“Nothing. As far as I can tell, these dots just soak up x-rays. The x-rays go in, but they never come out. Nothing comes out. That’s very strange. I can’t think of anything that does that.”

“Okay.” She wrote some notes on her tablet. “What else can you tell me?”

“Next, I tried gas chromatography. That’s where you turn the sample into a gas and then figure out what elements or compounds are in the gas. That didn’t work either.”

“Why not?”

I threw my hands up. “Because the darn things just won’t turn into gas. That made me try all sorts of burners, ovens, and crucible furnaces, but nothing happened. The dots don’t change even at two thousand degrees Celsius. Nothing.”

“And that’s odd?”

“It’s really odd,” I said. “But these things live on the sun. At least sometimes. So, I guess being very heat-resistant makes sense.”

“They live on the sun?” she said. “So they’re alive?”

“I’m pretty sure they are, yeah.”

“Explain more.”

“Well, they move around. You can easily see it through the microscope. That alone doesn’t prove they’re alive—lifeless things move all the time because of static electricity or magnetic fields or whatever. But I noticed something else. Something weird. And it made everything make sense.”

“Okay.”

“I put a few dots in a vacuum and ran a spectrograph. Just a simple test to see if they give off light. And they do, of course. They give off infrared light at the 25.984 micron wavelength. That’s the Petrova frequency—the light that makes the Petrova line. I expected that. But then I noticed they only give off light when they’re moving. And they really give off a lot of it. I mean, not a lot from our point of view, but for a tiny single-celled organism it’s a ton.”

“And why is that important?”

“I did some quick calculations. And I’m pretty sure that light is how they move around.”

Stratt raised an eyebrow. “I don’t understand.”

“Believe it or not, light has momentum,” I said. “It creates a force. If you were out in space and you turned on a flashlight, it would give you a tiny bit of thrust.”

“I didn’t know that.”

“Now you do. And even a tiny push on a tiny object can move it. I figured out the dots’ average mass was about twenty picograms. That took a while, but the lab equipment is great. Anyway, the movement I see matches the force of the light they send out.”

She set her tablet down. I had, it seemed, actually gotten her full attention. “Does that happen naturally?”

I shook my head. “No. Nothing in nature stores that much energy. You don’t get how much energy these dots are giving off. It’s like…reaching the point where mass turns into energy. E = mc 2 stuff. These tiny dots have way more energy stored inside them than they should.”

“Well,” she said. “They did just come from the sun. And the sun is losing energy.”

“Yeah. That’s why I think it’s alive,” I said. “It takes in energy, stores it somehow, then uses it to move. That’s not just physics or chemistry. That’s complicated and on purpose. It must have evolved.”

“So the Petrova line is…tiny rocket flames?”

“Probably. And I bet we’re only seeing a bit of the light from that area. They use it to push themselves to Venus or the sun. Or both. I don’t know. The light will go away from where they’re going. Earth isn’t in that line, so we only see light bouncing off space dust nearby.”

“Why go to Venus?” she asked. “And how do they make more of themselves?”
