“And as we get more Astrophage, we’ll go further and further out to sea. Anyway. Head down to the hangar deck. I have carpenters building rooms and offices right now. Pick some you like and take them.”

“This is our life now,” said Dimitri. “Welcome.”

Chapter 6

Okay, if I’m going to die, it has to matter. I need to figure out how to stop Astrophage. Then I’ll send my findings to Earth. And then…I’ll die. There are many ways to die without pain here—from taking too many pills to lowering the oxygen until I just fall asleep and die.

Nice thought.

I eat a tasty tube of “Day 4—Meal 2.” I think it tastes like beef. The food is getting thicker now. There are actually some solid pieces in it. I think I’m chewing on a small piece of carrot. It’s nice to feel some texture in the food for a change.

“More water!” I say.

The NannyBot (as I now call it) quickly takes my plastic cup and gives me a full one. It’s funny. Three days ago, those arms on the ceiling were a scary mechanical monster. Now they’re just…there. Part of life.

I like to think in the dormitory. Now that the dead bodies are gone, anyway. The lab doesn’t have anywhere comfortable to relax. The control room has a nice chair, but it’s small and has lights flashing everywhere. But the dormitory has my nice, comfortable bed where I can lie back and think about what to do next. Also, the bedroom is where all the food comes from.

I’ve remembered a lot over the past few days. It seems Project Hail Mary worked, because I’m here, in another star system. I guess it’s Tau Ceti. It makes sense that I thought it was the sun. Tau Ceti is a lot like the sun as stars go. Same type, color, and so on.

And I know why I’m here! Not just in general terms like “Hey, the world’s ending. Stop that.” But very specifically: Find out why Astrophage didn’t affect Tau Ceti.

Easy to say. Hard to do. I hope I remember more details later.

A million questions run through my mind. Some of the most important are:

  1. How do I search an entire solar system for information about Astrophage?
  2. What am I supposed to do? Throw some of my Astrophage fuel at Tau Ceti to see what happens?
  3. How do I steer this ship anyway?
  4. If I do find useful information, how do I tell Earth about it? I think that’s what the beetles are for, but how do I upload data to them? How do I aim them? How do I launch them?
  5. Why would I, of all people, be part of this mission? Yes, I figured out a bunch of stuff about Astrophage, but so what? I’m a lab guy, not an astronaut. It’s not like they sent Wernher von Braun into space. Surely there were people better suited for this.

I decide to start small. First I have to figure out what this ship can do and how to control it. They put the crew in comas. They must have known it might mess with our minds. There has to be an instruction manual somewhere.

“Flight manual,” I say out loud.

“Ship information can be found in the control room,” says the NannyBot.

“Where?”

“Ship information can be found in the control room.”

“No. Where in the control room can ship information be found?”

“Ship information can be found in the control room.”

“You kind of suck,” I say.

I head up to the control room and really examine every screen. I spend an hour noting what each area seems to show and guessing what the functions are. I’m really hoping to find something like “Information” or “Here to save humanity? Press this button to learn more!”

No such luck. After hours of messing with screens, I’ve found nothing. I guess they figured if the crew is so brain-dead that they don’t remember how to use the ship, they’re probably not useful as scientists anyway.

I did find out that any screen can show any instrument panel. They’re pretty much the same. Just tap the upper-left corner, and a menu appears. Pick whatever panel you want.

That’s nice. You can change what you’re looking at. And the screen right in front of the pilot’s seat is the biggest.

I decide to try a more hands-on approach: I’m gonna start pushing buttons!

Hopefully there’s no “Blow Up the Ship” button. I think Stratt would have stopped that from happening.

Stratt. I wonder what she’s doing right now. Probably in a control room somewhere with the pope making her a cup of coffee. She was (is?) a really bossy person. But gosh darn it, I’m glad she was in charge of making this ship happen. Now that I’m aboard it and all. Her attention to detail and insistence on perfection are nice to have all around me.

Anyway, I bring up the “Scientific Instrumentation” panel on the main screen. It’s the same panel I spent a lot of time with earlier—the one that currently shows an image of Tau Ceti. It has the word “Helioscope” in the upper-left corner. I hadn’t noticed that before. The left side of the screen has a bunch of icons. Other equipment, I assume. I press one at random.

Tau Ceti disappears. The top-left corner changes to read “External Collection Unit.” The screen shows a diagram of a plain rectangle. There are some controls here and there to change the angle and to “open bow side” and “open stern side.” Okay. Noted. Not sure what to do with that information. I press another icon at random.

This time it changes to “Petrovascope.” Beyond that, there’s just a black screen with an error message: PETROVASCOPE CANNOT BE USED WHILE SPIN DRIVE IS ACTIVE.

“Hmph,” I say.

Okay, what’s a Petrovascope? Best guess: a telescope and/or camera that looks specifically for the IR light that Astrophage emit. It looks for the Petrova line via the Petrova wavelength so it’s a Petrovascope and we really need to stop putting “Petrova” in front of everything.

Why can’t I use it when the spin drive is active?

I don’t how a spin drive works, or why it’s called a spin drive, but I do know I have one in the back of the ship and it’s consuming Astrophage as fuel. So it’s my engine. It probably activates enriched Astrophage to use them as thrust.

Ah…that would mean there’s a ridiculous amount of IR light coming out the back of the ship right now. Like…enough to vaporize a battleship or something. I’d have to do the math to know for sure but—I can’t help it, I want to do the math right now.

The engines consume 6 grams of Astrophage per second. Astrophage stores energy as mass. So basically, the spin drive converts 6 grams of mass into pure energy every second and spits it out the back. Well, it’s the Astrophage doing the work, but whatever.

I open the “Utility” panel on a smaller screen to my right. It has a bunch of familiar apps, all ready to go. One of them is a calculator. I use it to calculate the energy from converting that 6 grams…good Lord. It’s 540 trillion Joules. And the ship is sending out that much energy every second. So it’s 540 trillion watts. I can’t even imagine that much energy. It’s way more than the surface of the sun. Seriously. Like…you’d get less energy if you were on the surface of the sun than if you were standing behind the Hail Mary at full power.

I’m slowing down now. I have to be. The plan is to stop in the Tau Ceti system. So I’m probably pointed away from the star and slowing down—after spending a really long time at almost light speed during the trip.

Okay, so all that light energy will hit dust particles, ions, and anything else between me and Tau Ceti as I move along. Those poor little particles will be completely vaporized. And that’ll send some IR light back at the ship. Not much compared to the engine's power, but it would blind the Petrovascope, which is carefully designed to look for tiny amounts of that exact frequency.

So I can’t use the Petrovascope with the engine on.

But man. I would love to know if Tau Ceti has a Petrova line.

In theory, any star infected with Astrophage should have one, right? The little pests need carbon dioxide to breed. They can’t get that from the star (unless you go way into the core, and I don’t know if even Astrophage could survive those temperatures).

If I see a Petrova line, it means that Tau Ceti has an active Astrophage population that, for some reason, hasn’t grown out of control like it has everywhere else. And that line will lead to a planet that has carbon dioxide. Maybe there’s some other chemical in that atmosphere that stops the Astrophage? Maybe the planet has a strange magnetic field that messes with their ability to navigate? Maybe the planet has a bunch of moons that the Astrophage physically crashes into?

Maybe Tau Ceti just doesn’t have any planets with carbon dioxide in their atmospheres. That would be awful. It would mean this whole trip was pointless and Earth is doomed.

I could guess all day. Without data, it’s just a shot in the dark. And without the Petrovascope, I don’t have data. At least, not the data I want.

I turn my attention to the Navigation screen. Should I mess with it? I mean—I don’t know how to fly this ship. The ship does, but I don’t. If I push the wrong button, I’ll be dead in space.

Actually, it would be worse. I’d be speeding toward Tau Ceti at—I check the screen—7,595 kilometers per second. Wow! A few days ago, it was over 11,000. That’s what constantly speeding up at 1.5 g’s does. Or "slowing down," I guess. It’s the same in physics. The point is, I’m slowing down compared to the star.

There’s a button on the screen that says “Course.” Seems okay to tap, right? Famous last words. I should just wait for the computer to think the trip is done. But I can’t help it.

I tap the button. The screen shows the Tau Ceti solar system. Tau Ceti is in the center, shown with the Greek letter tau.

Ohhhh…that’s what the lowercase t is on the Hail Mary symbol. It’s a tau, for “Tau Ceti.” Okay.

Anyway, four planet paths are shown as thin white lines around the star. The planets are shown as circles with error bars. We don’t have perfect info on exoplanets. If I could get the science tools working, I could get better info on where the planets are. I’m twelve light-years closer to them than astronomers on Earth.

A yellow line goes almost straight into the system from off-screen. It curves toward the star between the third and fourth planets and into a circle. There’s a yellow triangle on the line, far from the four planets. That’s me, I think. And the yellow line is my path. Above the map is the text:

TIME TO ENGINE CUTOFF: 0005:20:39:06

The last number counts down every second. Okay, I learned a few things. First, I have about five days left (almost six) before the engine stops. Second, the timer shows days with four numbers. That means this trip took at least one thousand days. Over three years. Well, it takes light twelve years to get here, so it should take me a long time too.

Oh, right. Relativity.

I don’t know how long it took. Or, I don’t know how long it felt for me. When you go close to the speed of light, time changes for you. More time will have passed on Earth than I felt since I left Earth.

Relativity is strange.

Time is crucial. And sadly, while I slept, Earth aged at least thirteen years. Even if I solve the Astrophage problem now, it will take at least thirteen years for that news to reach Earth. So, Earth will suffer at least twenty-six years of Astrophage damage. I hope they are finding ways to cope or at least lessen the harm. They wouldn't have sent the Hail Mary if they didn't think they could last at least twenty-six years, right?

Anyway, the trip took at least three years (from my view). Is that why we were put in comas? Was it a problem for us to be awake the whole time?

I only feel the tears when one falls off my face. The decision to put us in comas killed two close friends. They're gone. I don't recall a single moment with them, but the loss is huge. I'll join them soon. There's no way home. I'll die out here too. But unlike them, I'll die alone.

I wipe my eyes and try to think of other things. My whole species is at stake.

Based on the map, the ship will automatically put me in a stable orbit around Tau Ceti, between the third and fourth planets. I'd guess that's about 1 AU, the same distance Earth is from the sun. A nice, safe distance from the star. A slow orbit that takes about a year. Maybe longer, because Tau Ceti is smaller than the sun, so it has less mass. Less mass means less gravity and a slower orbit at the same distance.

Okay, I have five days to wait until the engine stops. I'll just wait it out instead of messing around. Once the engines are off, I’ll use the Petrovascope to see what's out there. Until then, I’ll learn as much about the ship as I can.

I’ll do anything to avoid thinking about Yáo and Ilyukhina.

Officially, the ship was called the People’s Liberation Army Navy Gansu. Why their navy includes "Army" in its name is beyond me. But people stopped using that name and started calling it Stratt’s Vat. Despite the sailors' protests, the name remained. We sailed around the South China Sea, always keeping a distance from land.

I had spent a wonderful week focused solely on science.

No meetings. No interruptions. Just experiments and building. I had forgotten how enjoyable it was to become completely absorbed in a project.

My first breeder prototype had successfully completed another test. It wasn’t much to look at—mostly a 30-foot metal pipe with various pieces of control equipment attached randomly. But it worked. It could only produce a few micrograms of Astrophage per hour, but the idea was sound.

I had a team of twelve people—engineers from different countries. Two Mongolian brothers were my best engineers. When Stratt called me to meet her in the conference room, I left them in charge.

I found her alone in the meeting room. The table was covered with papers and charts, as usual. Graphs and diagrams were displayed on all the walls—some new, some old.

Stratt was sitting at one end of the long table, with a bottle of Dutch gin and a short glass. I had never seen her drink before.

“You wanted to see me?” I asked.

She looked up. Her eyes were puffy. She hadn’t slept. “Yes. Sit down.”

I sat in the chair next to her. “You look awful. What’s happening?”

“I have to make a decision. And it’s not easy.”

“How can I help?”

She offered me the gin. I shook my head. She filled her own glass. “The Hail Mary will have a very small crew area—about 125 cubic meters.”

I tilted my head. “That’s actually pretty big for spaceships, right?”

She waved her hand back and forth. “Big for a capsule like Soyuz or Orion. But tiny for a space station. It’s about one-tenth the size of the International Space Station’s crew area.”

“Okay,” I said. “What’s the problem?”

“The problem”—she grabbed a manila folder and dropped it in front of me—“is that the crew will kill each other.”

“Huh?” I opened the folder. Inside were many typed pages. Actually, they were scans of typed pages. Some were in English, some in Russian. “What is all this?”

“During the Space Race, the Soviets briefly wanted to go to Mars. They thought if they put people on Mars, the U.S. moon landing would seem unimportant.”

I closed the folder. The Cyrillic writing meant nothing to me. But I guessed Stratt could read it. She always seemed to know whatever language was being spoken.

She rested her chin on her hands. “Getting to Mars with 1970s tech means using a Hohmann transfer trajectory, so the crew would spend over eight months on a ship. The Soviets tested what happens when people are in a small, isolated space for months.”

“And?”

“After seventy-one days, the men were fighting daily. They stopped the test on day ninety-four because one person tried to stab another with broken glass.”

“How big will the crew be?”

“The current plan is three,” she said.

“Okay,” I said. “So you’re worried about what happens when we send three astronauts on a four-year trip in a 125-cubic-meter space?”

“It’s not just if they get along. Each person will know they’re going to die in a few years. And that the ship is the only world they’ll know for their short lives. The psychiatrists I spoke to think crushing depression is likely. And suicide is a real risk.”

“Yeah, that’s heavy,” I said. “But what else can we do?”

She picked up a stack of papers and slid it to me. I picked it up and read the title: “A Study of Long-Term Primate and Human Coma Patients and Detrimental Aftereffects—Srisuk et al.”

“Okay. What am I looking at?”

“That’s a study by a failed company in Thailand.” She swirled the gin in her glass. “Their idea was to put cancer patients in comas for their chemotherapy. The patient gets the chemo, but doesn’t have to be awake to suffer through it. Wake them up when the cancer goes away. Or when it can’t be treated anymore and it’s time for hospice. Either way, they avoid a lot of suffering.”

“That…sounds like a great idea,” I said.

She nodded. “It would be, if it wasn’t so deadly. It turns out the human body isn’t meant to be in a coma for a long time. Chemo lasts months, and often needs more rounds after that. They tried different ways to induce comas in primates, and the primates either died during the coma or woke up with brain damage.”

“So why are we talking about it?”

“Because they did more studies—this time on old data from human coma patients. They looked at humans who had come out of long comas okay and tried to see what they had in common. They found something.”

Old Russian space-agency documents were a mystery to me, but scientific papers were my thing for a long time. I flipped through the paper and skimmed to the findings. “Gene markers?” I said.

“Yes,” she said. “They found a group of genes that give a human ‘coma resistance.’ That’s what they’re calling it. The sequences are in what scientists used to think was useless DNA. But apparently it’s something we developed a long time ago for some unknown reason and still exists in some people’s DNA.”

“Are they sure these genes cause coma resistance?” I said. “They’re related, but do they cause it?”

“Yes, they’re sure. The genes are also found in lower primates. Whatever it is, it’s very old. Some think it might go all the way back to our water ancestors that used to hibernate. Anyway, they ran tests on primates with those genes and they survived long comas with no side effects. Every single one of them.”

“Okay. I see what you’re getting at.” I put the paper down. “Do DNA tests on all applicants, and use only the people who have those coma-resistance genes. During the trip, put the crew in comas. They don’t have to experience four years of getting on each other’s nerves or thinking about their deaths.”

She raised her glass to me. “It gets better. Having the crew in comas makes getting food much easier. We just pump powdered, balanced nutrients into their stomachs. No need for tons of different meals. Just powder and a water system that cleans itself.”

I smiled. “This sounds amazing. Like being frozen in sci-fi stories. Why are you drinking and stressed?”

“There are a few problems,” she said. “First, we'd need a fully automatic system to watch and care for the coma patients. If it failed, everyone would die. It’s not just about checking vitals and giving drugs through an IV. It would have to move and clean them, handle bedsores, and treat problems like infections around the IVs. Things like that.”

“Okay, but it seems like the world's doctors could figure that out,” I said. “Use your Stratt power to make them do it.”

She took another sip. “That’s not the big issue. The big issue is this: Only one in seven thousand people has the right genes for it.”

I sat back. “Wow.”

“Yeah. We couldn’t send the best people. We’d be sending the seven-thousandth best.”

“More like the three-thousand-five-hundredth best, on average,” I said.

She rolled her eyes.

“Still,” I said. “One in seven thousand is a million people worldwide. Think of it that way. You’d have a million people to choose from. You only need three.”

“Six,” she said. “We need a main crew and a backup crew. Can’t have the mission fail because someone gets hit by a car crossing the street the day before launch.”

“Okay, then six.”

“Yeah. Six people who are astronaut material, who have the scientific skills to figure out what’s going on with Astrophage at Tau Ceti, and who are willing to go on a suicide mission.”

“Out of a population of a million,” I said. “A million.”

She fell silent and took another sip of gin.

I cleared my throat. “So you either take your chances with picking the best people and maybe they kill each other, or you take your chances on medical technology that we still have to develop to automatically care for less talented people.”

“More or less. Either way, it’s a terrible risk. It’s the hardest decision I’ve ever had to make.”

“Good thing you already made up your mind, then,” I said.

She raised an eyebrow. “Huh?”

“Sure,” I said. “You just wanted someone to tell you what you already know. If you leave the crew awake, there’s nothing you can do about the psychosis risk. But we’ve got years to perfect the automated-coma-bed technology.”

She frowned a little but didn’t speak.

I softened my voice. “Besides, we’re already asking these people to die. We shouldn’t ask them to suffer emotionally for four years, too. Science and morality both agree, and you know it.”

She nodded, barely noticeably. Then, she finished her gin. “All right, you can go.” She slid her laptop over and began typing.

I left without another word. She had her own problems, and I had mine.

—

The memories are coming back easier now. I still can’t remember everything, but it’s not a surprise when they happen. It’s just sort of…“Oh hey, I know that. Always knew it, really.”

I guess I’m one of those people who can resist comas. That explains why I’m here instead of the much better people who should have been sent.

But Yáo and Ilyukhina probably had those genes, too, and they didn’t make it. I think the medical robot wasn’t perfect. They must have had some medical problem it couldn’t solve.

I shake off their memory.

The next few days test my patience. I distract myself by learning more about the ship.

I take inventory of the entire lab. One of the first things I find is a touchscreen computer in a pull-out drawer in the center table. It's a great find, because it has many research-related programs, unlike the panels in the control room, which focus on the ship or its instruments.

I see many math and science apps—mostly common ones I recognize. But the real treasure is the library!

As far as I can tell, this panel can access almost any scientific textbook ever written, every scientific paper ever published, and much more. One directory is simply called "Library of Congress," and it seems to be the complete digital record of everything copyrighted in the United States. Sadly, no books about the Hail Mary.

And the reference manuals. So many reference manuals. Data piled on data. I guess they figured solid-state hard drives are light, so they didn't need to limit information. They may have even burned the data into ROMs.

They included reference material on things that are probably useless. But it's good to know that if I ever need the average rectal temperature of a healthy goat, I can find it! (It's 103.4°F / 39.7°C.)

Playing with the panel leads to my next discovery: I know how I'll send information back to Earth using the beetles.

I knew they'd be involved, but now I know the details. Besides the ship's huge data storage, the panel also has four smaller external drives: John, Paul, George, and Ringo. Each shows 5 terabytes free. It's easy to guess that's the beetle's data.

So how do I launch them when the time comes? To find out, I go to the control room.

I have to search through a few menus on the Beetles panel to find the launch command, but I find it. It seems to be just a button labeled "Launch." I guess they use stars to orient themselves and fly toward Earth on their own. The Hail Mary did the same thing to get here, so they know how. No need to add human error when choosing their course.

While I’m here, I check out the Scientific Instrumentation screen. The first few sections are for the helioscope, the Petrovascope, and a telescope that can see visible light, infrared light, and other types of light.

I play with the visible-light telescope. It’s kind of fun. I can look at the stars. I mean, there’s not much else out there. Even Tau Ceti’s planets would just be tiny dots from here. But it’s still nice to see the outside from my small, enclosed space.

I also found a screen for spacewalks (EVAs). It has what I expected. There are controls for the spacesuit, so someone in the control room can handle any suit problems during a spacewalk. That way, the person in the suit doesn’t have to. Also, the ship has a complicated tether system on the outside. It’s basically tracks that the tether hook can slide along. They really thought spacewalks would be important. Probably to gather local Astrophage.

If there is any.

If Tau Ceti has a Petrova line, then there’s Astrophage to collect. Getting some would be the first step: bringing it to the lab and seeing if it’s different from the Astrophage on Earth. Maybe it’s a weaker type?

The next two days are mostly me worrying about what will happen. I know what’s going to happen, but I still worry.

I fidget in the control room and watch the seconds go by.

“You’re going to be in zero gravity,” I say. “You’re not going to fall. You won’t be in danger. The ship's acceleration will stop. But that’s okay.”

I don’t like roller coasters or water slides. That falling feeling terrifies me. And soon I’m going to feel that exact feeling because the “gravity” I’ve been feeling will stop completely.

The seconds tick by. “Four…three…two…”

“Here we go,” I said.

“One…zero.”

Right on time, the engines stopped. The 1.5 g’s I’ve been feeling are gone. There’s no gravity.

I panic. No amount of thinking about it could have helped. I just panic.

I scream and wave my arms. I make myself curl up like a baby—it feels good and keeps me from bumping into anything.

I shiver and shake as I float in the control room. I should have strapped myself in, but I didn’t think of it. Idiot.

“I’m not falling!” I yell. “I’m not falling! This is just space! Everything is fine!”

It’s not fine. I feel sick to my stomach. I’m going to throw up. Throwing up in zero g is bad. I don’t have a bag. I really wasn’t ready for this. I was dumb to think I could just talk myself out of a basic fear.

I pull open my jumpsuit collar and lean my head down. Just in time. I throw up all of “Day 9—Meal 3” into my shirt. I hold the collar tight to my chest after. It’s gross, but it’s contained. Better than it floating around and choking me.

“Oh gosh…” I whine. “Gosh…this is…”

Can I do this? Will I be completely useless from now on? Will humanity die because I can’t handle zero g?

No.

I clench my teeth. I clench my fists. I clench my butt. I clench every part of me that I know how to clench. It makes me feel in control. I’m doing something by aggressively doing nothing.

After forever, the panic starts to fade. Human brains are amazing. We can get used to almost anything. I’m getting used to it.

The slight lessening of fear helps me even more. I know I will be less afraid now. And knowing that makes the fear go away even faster. Soon, the panic turns into fear, which fades into general worry.

I look around the control room and nothing feels right. Nothing changed, but now there’s no down. I still feel sick. I grab my collar in case I need to throw up again but I don’t. I hold it in.

The feeling of warm vomit squishing between my chest and jumpsuit is disgusting. I need to change.

I aim myself at the hatchway to the lab and kick off the wall behind me. I float down and into the lab. The whole room is full of floating stuff. I left things on the table when I listed them. Now all that stuff is floating around freely, pushed by air from the vents.

“Dummy,” I say to myself. I should have known that would happen.

I go on to the bedroom. It’s also got junk floating everywhere. I opened most of the bins in the storage area to see what was inside. Now the bins and everything in them drift around.

“Clean me!” I say to the arms.

The arms don’t do anything.

I strip and use the jumpsuit to wipe gunk off my body. I found the sponge-bath area a few days ago—just a sink with sponges that pop out of the wall. No room for a shower, I guess. Anyway, I clean up with that stuff.

I’m not sure what to do with the dirty gunk.

“Laundry?” I say.

The arms reach down and take the dirty jumpsuit. A panel in the ceiling opens and they put it in there. What happens when it’s full? No idea.

I find another jumpsuit in the junk and put it on. Getting dressed in zero g is interesting. Not harder, but different. I get the new jumpsuit on. It’s a little tight. I check the name tag. It says 姚. It’s one of Yáo’s jumpsuits. Well, it’s not too tight. And I don’t want to float around the bedroom all day looking for one of mine. I’ll sort it out later.

For now, I’m too excited to see what’s out there. I mean, come on! I’m the first human to explore another star system! And I’m here!

I push off the floor toward the hatch…and miss. I crash into the ceiling. At least I get my arms up to protect my face. I bounce off the ceiling and back to the floor.

“Ow,” I mumble. I try again, a little more slowly, and I make it. I float up through the lab, and into the control room. Getting around is easier with no gravity. I still feel sick but I have to admit: This is pretty fun.

I pull myself into the pilot’s chair and strap myself in to keep from floating away.

The Navigation screen reads PRIMARY TRANSIT COMPLETE. The Spin Drive screen says THRUST: 0. But most important, the Petrovascope screen says READY.

I rub my hands together, then reach for the screen. The interface is simple enough. The corner has an icon that is a toggle switch with two states: “Visible” and “Petrova.” It’s currently set to “Visible.” The rest of the screen shows a visible-light view from the ship. Seems like an ordinary camera. I poke at the screen and quickly realize I can pan, zoom in or out, rotate, and so on.

All I see is stars in the distance. I guess I should pan around until I find Tau Ceti. I swipe my finger left, left, left…just generally trying to see where the star is. I don’t have a point of reference to work with. Every few left swipes I throw in a down swipe. Just to cover all angles over time. I do finally find Tau Ceti, but it doesn’t look like it should.

A few days ago, when I looked at it with the helioscope, it looked like any other star. But now it’s a solid black circle with a hazy ring of light around it. I realize why immediately.

The Petrovascope is a pretty sensitive piece of equipment. It’s fine-tuned to spot even the smallest amounts of the Petrova wavelength. A star will give off absolutely obscene amounts of light at all wavelengths. It’d be like staring at the sun with binoculars. The equipment has to protect itself from the star. It probably has a physical metal plate that it keeps between its sensors and the star at all times. So I’m looking at the back of that plate.

Good design.

I reach up to the toggle switch. This is it. If there’s no Petrova line here, I don’t know what to do. I mean, I’ll try to figure out something. But I’ll be kind of lost.

I flip the toggle.

The stars disappear. The hazy ring surrounding Tau Ceti remains. That’s to be expected. It’s the star’s corona, which will be emitting plenty of light, so some of it’s bound to be the Petrova wavelength.

I search the image desperately. At first, nothing, but then I see it. A beautiful dark-red arch coming out of the bottom-left of Tau Ceti.

I clap my hands. “Yes!”

The shape is clear. It’s a Petrova line! Tau Ceti has a Petrova line! I do a little wiggly dance in my chair. It’s not easy in zero g but I try my best. Now we’re getting somewhere!

There are so many experiments I need to do, I don’t even know where to start. I should see where the line goes, to begin with. One of the planets, of course, but which one and what’s interesting about it? And I should get a sample of local Astrophage to see if it’s the same as what we have back on Earth. I could do that by flying into the Petrova line and then scraping the dust off the hull during a spacewalk.

I could spend a week just writing a list of experiments I want to do!

I see a flash on the screen. Just a quick blink of light.

“What’s that?” I say. “Another clue?”

The flash happens again. I move and zoom in on that part of space. It’s nowhere near the Petrova line or Tau Ceti. Maybe light reflecting off a planet or asteroid?

I can see how that could happen. A very shiny asteroid could be bouncing enough light from Tau Ceti around that I see it on the Petrovascope, but it’s not constant, so maybe it’s an odd shape that’s rotating and—

The flash becomes a steady light. It’s just…“on” now. All the time.

I stare at the screen. “What…what’s going on here?…”

The light gets brighter. Not suddenly. Just slowly over time. I watch for a minute. It seems to be getting brighter faster now.

Is it something coming toward me?

A quick idea comes to mind: Maybe Astrophage are drawn to other Astrophage? Maybe some of them saw the flare from my engines, which would be the wavelength they use, and they came toward me. Maybe this is how they find the main group? So this could be a bunch of Astrophage headed my way, thinking I can lead them to the planet with the carbon dioxide?

Interesting idea. But I have no proof.

The steady light grows brighter, brighter, brighter, and then vanishes.

“Huh,” I say. I wait a few minutes, but the light doesn’t come back.

“Hmm….” I remember the strange event. But there’s nothing I can do about it right now. Whatever it was, it’s gone.

Back to the Petrova line. First, I want to find out which planet the line goes to. I guess I’ll have to figure out how to navigate the ship, but that’s another problem.

I look back at the Petrova line. Something’s wrong. Half of it is just…gone.

It’s coming from Tau Ceti, just like before, but then it stops suddenly at a random spot.

“What is going on?”

Did I mess up their path, maybe? If it’s that easy, wouldn’t we have figured it out when the Hail Mary was wandering around our solar system?

I zoom in on where it stops. It’s just a straight line. Like someone cut the Petrova line with a knife and tossed the scraps.

A huge line of moving Astrophage doesn’t just vanish. I have an easier idea: Something’s on the camera lens. Some junk. Maybe a clump of excited Astrophage. That would be great. I’d have a sample to check out right away!

Maybe a regular view will help me see what’s happening. I hit the button.

And that’s when I see it.

Something is blocking my view of the Petrova line. It’s right by my ship. Maybe a few hundred meters away. It’s sort of triangle-shaped and has pointy bits along its side.

Yes. I said side. It’s not an asteroid—the lines are too smooth; too straight. This thing was made. Built. Put together. Shapes like that don’t happen naturally.

It’s a ship.

Another ship.

There’s another ship in this system with me. Those flashes of light—those were its engines. It’s powered by Astrophage. Just like the Hail Mary. But the design, the shape—it’s unlike any spacecraft I’ve ever seen. It's made of huge, flat surfaces—the worst way to make a container that holds pressure. No one would make a ship like that.

No one on Earth would, anyway.

I blink a few times at what I’m seeing. I gulp.

This…this is an alien spacecraft. Made by aliens. Aliens smart enough to make a spacecraft.

Humanity isn’t alone in the universe. And I’ve just met our neighbors.

“Holy fucking shit!”

Chapter 7

A flood of thoughts hit me all at once: We’re not alone. This is an alien. That ship is weird, how does that work? Do they live here? Is this their star? Am I starting an interplanetary problem by wandering into alien territory?!

“Breathe,” I tell myself.

Okay, one thing at a time. What if this is another ship from Earth? One I don’t remember? Heck, it took me a few days to remember my name. Maybe Earth sent multiple ships with different designs? Like, for backup or to make it more likely that at least one of them works. Maybe that ship is the Praise Allah or the Blessings of Vishnu or something.

I look all around the control room. There are screens and controls for everything, but there’s nothing for a radio. The EVA panel has some radio controls, but that’s just for talking to crewmates when they’re outside.

If they’d sent multiple ships, surely they would have had some radio system so we could talk to each other.

Also, that ship…it’s crazy.

I go through the navigation console screens until I find the Radar panel. I’d noticed it earlier, but didn’t think much of it. I assume it’s there so I can get near asteroids or other objects and not hit them.

After a few tries, I manage to turn it on. It immediately spots the other ship and sounds an alarm. The loud noise hurts my ears.

“Whoa, whoa, whoa!” I say. I quickly look around the panel until I see a button labeled “Mute Proximity Alert.” I press it and the noise stops.

I scan the rest of the screen. There's a lot of data here, all in a window called "BLIP-A." I guess if there were multiple contacts, I'd get multiple windows. Whatever. It's all just raw numbers about the reading. Nothing cool like a 3D Star Trek scan or anything.

"Velocity" is zero. They have matched my velocity exactly. That can't be an accident.

"Range" is 217 meters. I'm guessing that's the distance to the closest part of the other ship. Or maybe the average. No, it would be the closest part. The point of this system is probably to avoid crashes.

Speaking of crashes—217 meters is a ridiculously small distance compared to the size of a solar system. There's no way this is an accident. That ship put itself here on purpose because I'm here.

Another reading, "Angular width," is 35.44 degrees. Okay, some basic math should handle this.

I bring up the Utility panel on the main screen and open the calculator app. Something 217 meters away is taking up 35.44 degrees of the view. Assuming the radar can see in all 360 degrees (it would be a pretty bad radar if it couldn't)...I type some numbers into the calculator to do an ARCTAN operation, and:

The ship is 139 meters long. Roughly.

I bring the Astrophage panel up on another screen. The little map there shows that the Hail Mary is just 47 meters long. So yeah. The alien ship is three times the size of mine. There's just no way Earth sent something that big.

And the shape. What is up with that shape? I turn my attention back to the Petrovascope (which is now just acting as a camera).

The center of the ship is diamond-shaped—a rhombus. Well, I guess it's an octahedron, really. Looks like it has eight faces, each triangular. That part alone is about the size of my ship.

The diamond is linked by three thick rods (I'm not sure what else to call them) to a wide, trapezoid-shaped base. That seems like it could be the back. And in front of the diamond is a thin stalk (I'm just making up names now) with four flat panels attached parallel to the main ship's direction. Maybe they're solar panels? The stalk goes on to a pyramid-shaped nose. A nose pyramid, I suppose.

Every part of the hull is flat. Even the "rods" have flat sides.

Why would anyone do that? Flat panels are a bad idea. I don't know who made this, but I assume they need some kind of air inside, right? Huge, flat panels are terrible for that.

Maybe this is just a probe and not a real ship. Maybe there's no air inside because there's nothing alive inside. I might be looking at an alien object instead of a ship.

Still, this is the most exciting moment in human history.

So it runs on Astrophage. That was the steady Petrova-frequency light I saw before. It's interesting that they use the same propulsion as we do. But since it's the best way to store energy, it's not surprising. When European sailors first met Asian sailors, no one was surprised they both used sails.

But the "why" is what I wonder about. Something on board (either a computer or a crew) decided to come to my ship. How did they even know I was here?

I guess they saw me the same way I saw them: the huge IR light from my engines. And since the back of my ship was facing Tau Ceti, I was shining a 540-trillion-watt flashlight at them. Depending on where they were, I might have looked even brighter than Tau Ceti itself, at least in the Petrova frequency.

So they can see the Petrova frequency. And so can I.

I go through the Spin Drive console screens until I find one called "Manual Control." When I click it, a warning box appears:

MANUAL CONTROL IS RECOMMENDED ONLY FOR EMERGENCIES. ARE YOU SURE YOU WANT TO ENTER MANUAL CONTROL MODE?

I tap “Yes.”

It brings up another box.

SECOND CONFIRMATION: TYPE “Y-E-S” TO ENTER MANUAL CONTROL MODE.

I groan and type Y-E-S.

The panel finally takes me to the Manual Control screen. It’s a bit scary. Not because it’s complex, but because it’s so simple.

There are three sliders labeled “Drive 1,” “Drive 2,” and “Drive 3,” each presently at zero. The top of each slider is labeled “107 N.” The N must mean “Newtons”—a unit of force. I guess if I put all three drives to maximum, it would give me 30 million Newtons. That’s about sixty times the thrust a jumbo jet’s engines produce during takeoff.

Science teachers know a lot of random facts.

There are a bunch more little sliders. In groups labeled “Yaw,” “Pitch,” and “Roll.” There must be little spin drives on the sides of the ship to adjust its direction. I can definitely see why it’s a bad idea to mess with this panel. One mistake and I’ll put the ship into a spin that tears it apart.

But at least they thought of that. There’s a button in the middle of the screen labeled “Zero All Rotation.” Good.

I check the Petrovascope again. Blip-A hasn’t moved. It’s on my left, a little bit ahead.

I switch the Petrovascope back to Petrova-frequency mode, and the screen turns mostly black. Like before, I can see the Petrova line in the background, blocked by Blip-A.

“Let’s see if you have anything to say…” I mutter. Spin drive 2 is in the middle of the ship. Its thrust will be straight ahead and hopefully won’t change my direction. We’ll see.

I set it to 0.1% power for one second, then back to 0.

Even with just one engine, at one one-thousandth power, for one second, the ship drifts a bit. The “Velocity” value for Blip-A on the Radar panel shows 0.086 m/s. That tiny thrust moved my ship about 8 centimeters per second.

But I don’t care about that. I care about the other ship.

I watch the Petrovascope. A drop of sweat falls from my forehead and floats away. I feel like my heart is going to jump out of my chest.

Then, the back of the ship lights up in the Petrova frequency for one second. Just like I did.

“Wow!”

I flick the drive on and off several times: three short bursts, a long one, and one more short one. There’s no message there. I just want to see what they do with it.

They were more ready this time. In seconds, the other ship repeats the pattern.

I gasp. And I smile. Then I wince. Then I smile again. This is a lot to process.

That was too fast for any probe to react. If it had remote control, the controllers would have to be at least a few light-minutes away—there’s just nothing around here that could be hiding them.

There is an intelligent life-form on that ship. I am about 200 meters from a real alien!

I mean…my ship is powered by aliens. But this new one is intelligent!

Oh my gosh! This is it! First Contact! I’m the one! I’m the one who meets aliens for the first time!

The Blip-A (that’s what I’m calling their ship for now) briefly starts its engines again. I watch closely to memorize the sequence, but it’s just a single dim light. They’re not signaling. They’re maneuvering.

I check the Radar panel. As expected, the Blip-A moves alongside the Hail Mary and holds position at 217 meters.

I switch through the Scientific panel to bring up the normal telescopic cameras again. The Petrovascope’s normal-light camera is just to orient things for the main scope itself. The telescope has much better resolution and clarity. I guess I’m too excited to think straight because it took me until now to think of it.

The image is much clearer through the main telescope. I guess it’s just an incredibly high-resolution camera, because I can still zoom in and out with no loss of clarity. I have a very good view of the Blip-A now.

The ship’s hull is a mix of gray and tan. The pattern looks random and smooth, like someone started mixing paint but stopped too soon.

I see movement in the corner of the screen. An odd-shaped thing slides along a track in the hull. It’s a stalk sticking up with five jointed “arms” coming out of the top. Each arm has a clamp-like “hand” on the end.

Only now do I see a network of tracks all over the hull.

It’s a robot. Something controlled from inside. At least, I think so. It doesn’t look like a little green man, and it definitely doesn’t look like an alien spacesuit.

Not that I know what either of those things would look like.

Yeah, I’m pretty sure it’s a hull-mounted robot. Space stations back on Earth have them. They’re a good way to do things outside your ship without having to put on a suit.

The robot moves along the hull until it gets to the spot closest to the Hail Mary. One of its little clamp hands holds a cylinder. I don’t really know how big it is, but the robot is tiny compared to the ship. I think it’s about my size or maybe smaller, but that’s just a guess.

The robot stops, reaches toward my ship, and gently lets go of the cylinder into space.

The cylinder moves slowly toward me. It’s rotating slightly, end-over-end. Not perfectly, but still very smooth.

I check the Radar panel. The Blip-A is stopped. And there’s a “Blip-B” screen now. It shows the much smaller cylinder coming closer at 8.6 centimeters per second.

Interesting. That’s the exact same speed I used for the Hail Mary a little while ago when I flashed the engine as a greeting. That can’t be an accident. They want me to have that cylinder, and they want to send it to me at a speed they know I’m used to.

“Very thoughtful of you…” I say.

These aliens are smart.

I have to think they mean well at this point. I mean, they’re really trying to say hi and be helpful. Besides, if they don't mean well, what could I do about it? Die. That’s what I’d do. I’m a scientist, not Buck Rogers.

Well, I guess I could aim the spin drives at their ship and fire them at full power, which would vaporize—you know what? I’m just not going to think about that right now.

Some quick math tells me the cylinder will take over forty minutes to get to me. I have that long to get into an EVA suit, go outside, and get ready on the hull for humanity’s first touchdown catch with an alien quarterback.

I learned a lot about the airlock when I was giving my crewmates a burial in space and—

Ilyukhina would have loved this. She would have been jumping up and down with excitement. Yáo would have been calm and steady, but he would have smiled when he thought we weren’t looking.

The tears blur my vision. With no gravity, they cover my eyes. It’s like trying to see underwater. I wipe them off and throw them across the control room. They splatter on the opposite wall. I don’t have time for this. I have an alien thingy to catch.

I unhook my belt and float over to the airlock. My mind is racing with ideas and questions. And I’m jumping to crazy, unsupported conclusions. Maybe this smart alien species invented Astrophage. Maybe they genetically engineered it to “grow” spaceship fuel. The ultimate solar power. Maybe once I explain what’s happening to Earth, they’ll have a solution.

Or maybe they’ll get on my ship and lay eggs in my brain. You never know.

I open the inner airlock door and pull out the EVA suit. Do I even know how to get into this thing? Or how to use it safely?

I unlock the Orlan-MKS2 EVA suit and open the back hatch. I turn on the main power with a switch on the belt. The suit starts up right away, and the panel on the chest says ALL SYSTEMS FUNCTIONAL—what the heck? I know exactly what to do.

We probably trained with this a lot. I know it like I know physics. It’s in my head, but I don’t remember learning it.

The Russian suit is one sealed unit. Unlike American suits where you put on the top and bottom, then deal with the helmet and gloves, the Orlan is like a onesie with a hatch in the back. You step in, close the hatch, and you’re done. It’s like an insect shedding its skin in reverse.

I open the back and wiggle into the suit. Zero gravity really helps here. I don’t have to struggle with the suit as much as I usually would. Weird. I know this is easier than before, but I don’t remember doing it before. I think I have brain damage from that coma.

I’m good enough for now. I keep going.

I get my arms and legs in the right spots. The jumpsuit is uncomfortable in the Orlan. I’m supposed to wear special underwear. I even know what it looks like, but it’s just for temperature and monitoring. I don’t have time to find it in storage. I have a date with a cylinder.

Now in the suit, I push against the airlock wall with my legs to close the back flap. When it’s close enough, a light turns green on the chest panel. I reach up with my thick gloves and press the Autoseal button.

The suit closes the opening with loud clicks. With a final “clunk,” the outer seal locks. My status board is green, and I have seven hours of air. The pressure inside is 400 hectopascals—about 40 percent of Earth’s air at sea level. That’s normal for spacesuits.

The whole process took only five minutes. I’m ready to go outside.

Interesting. I didn’t have to go through a decompression step. On space stations back home, astronauts have to spend hours in an airlock slowly getting used to the low pressure needed for the EVA suit before they can go out. I don’t have that problem. Apparently, the entire Hail Mary is at that 40 percent pressure.

Good design. The only reason space stations around Earth have a full atmosphere of pressure is in case the astronauts have to abort and return to Earth quickly. But for the Hail Mary crew…where would we go? Might as well use the low pressure all the time. It makes things easier on the hull and lets you do quick EVAs.

I take a deep breath and let it out. A soft whir comes from somewhere behind me and cool air flows along my back and shoulders. Air conditioning. It feels nice.

I grab a handhold and spin myself around. I pull the inner airlock door closed and then turn the main lever to start the cycling sequence. A pump starts up. It’s louder than I would have thought. It sounds like a motorcycle idling. I keep my hand on the lever. Pushing it back to the original position will stop the cycle and repressurize. If I see even a hint of a red light on my suit panel, I’m going to throw that lever so fast it’ll make my head spin.

After a minute, the pump grows quieter. Then quieter still. It’s probably as loud as it ever was. But with the air leaving the chamber, there’s no way for the noise to get to me other than through my feet touching the Velcro pads on the floor.

Finally, the pump stops. I’m in total silence aside from the fans inside the suit. The airlock controls show that the pressure inside is zero, and a yellow light turns green. I’m clear to open the outer door.

I grab the hatch crank, then hesitate.

“What am I doing?” I say.

Is this really a good idea? I want that cylinder so badly I’m just moving ahead without any sort of plan. Is this worth risking my life over?

Yes. Absolutely.

Okay, but is it worth risking everyone on Earth? If I mess up and die, the whole Hail Mary Project will be pointless.

Hmm.

Yes. It’s still worth it. I don’t know what these aliens are like, what they want, or what they’re planning. But they will have information. Any information, even stuff I’d rather not know, is better than nothing.

I spin the handle and open the door. Empty black space is outside. The light of Tau Ceti shines on the door. I peek out and see Tau Ceti myself. From here, it’s a little less bright than the sun on Earth.

I double-check my tether to be sure I’m attached, then I step into space.

—

I’m good at this.

I must have practiced a lot. Maybe in a water tank or something. But it feels natural to me.

I leave the airlock and clip one of my tethers to a rail on the outer hull. Always have two tethers. And always have at least one attached. That way you're never in danger of floating away from the ship. The Orlan-MKS2 is probably the best EVA suit ever, but it doesn't have a SAFER unit like NASA's EMU suit. At least with a SAFER unit you have a little thrust to get back to the ship if you drift away.

All that info rushes into my head at once. I guess I’ve spent a lot of time thinking about spacesuits. Maybe I’m the crew’s EVA expert? I don’t know.

I flip up the sun visor and look toward the Blip-A. I wish I could learn something special by seeing it up close, but it’s pretty far away. The Hail Mary’s telescope gave me a much better view. Still, there’s something…different about staring right at an alien spacecraft.

I see a flash of the cylinder. Now and then the flat ends of the slowly spinning cylinder reflect Taulight.

I’ve decided “Taulight” is a word, by the way. Light from Tau Ceti. It’s not “sunlight.” Tau Ceti isn’t the sun. So…Taulight.

I still have a good twenty minutes before the cylinder gets to the ship. I watch it for a while to guess where it’ll hit. It’d be nice to have a crewmate inside at the radar station.

It’d be nice to have a crewmate at all.

After five minutes, I have a good idea where the cylinder will hit. It’s headed for about the middle of the ship. That’s as good a place as any for aliens to aim for.

I move across the hull. The Hail Mary is pretty big. My little pressurized area is only half its length and the back half widens out to be three times as wide. Most of that will be empty now, I guess. It used to be full of Astrophage for my one-way trip here.

The hull has rails and latch points all over for EVA tethers. Tether by tether, rail by rail, I make my way toward the middle of the ship.

I have to step over a thick ring. It circles the crew area of the ship. It’s a good 2 feet thick. I don’t know what it is, but it must be pretty heavy. Weight is everything when designing spaceships, so it must be important. I’ll guess about that later.

I continue along, one hull latch at a time, until I’m roughly in the center of the hull. The cylinder gets closer. I adjust my position a bit to keep up with it. After a painfully long wait, it’s almost within reach.

I wait. No need to rush. If I grab at it too early, I might knock it off course and into space. I’d have no way to get it back. I don’t want to look dumb in front of the aliens.

Because they’re surely watching me right now. Probably counting my arms and legs, noting my size, figuring out what part they should eat first, whatever.

I let the cylinder get closer and closer. It’s moving less than 1 mile per hour. Not exactly a fast pass.

Now that it’s so close, I can guess its size. It’s not big at all. About the size and shape of a coffee can. It’s a dull gray color with spots of slightly darker gray randomly here and there. Similar to the Blip-A ’s hull, kind of. Different color but same spots. Maybe it’s a style thing. Random spots are “in” this season or something.

The cylinder floats into my arms and I grab it with both hands.

It weighs less than I expected. It’s probably hollow. It’s a container. There’s something inside they want me to see.

I hold the cylinder under one arm and use the other to deal with tethers. I hurry back to the airlock. It’s a stupid thing to do. There’s no reason to hurry and it literally puts my life in danger. One slip-up and I’d be lost in space. But I just can’t wait.

I get back into the ship, cycle the airlock, and float into the control room with my prize in hand. I open the Orlan suit, already thinking about what tests I’ll run on the cylinder. I have a whole lab to work with!

The smell hits me right away. I gasp and cough. The cylinder is bad!

No, not bad. But it smells bad. I can hardly breathe. The chemical smell is familiar. What is it? Cat pee?

Ammonia. It’s ammonia.

“Okay,” I wheeze. “Okay. Think.”

My first thought is to close the suit again. But that would just trap me in a small space with the ammonia that’s already in here. Better to let the cylinder air out in the bigger space of the ship.

Ammonia isn’t poisonous—at least, not in small amounts. And the fact that I can still breathe at all tells me it’s a small amount. If it weren’t, my lungs would have chemical burns and I’d be out cold or dead now.

As it is, there’s just a bad smell. I can handle a bad smell.

I climb out the back of the suit while the cylinder floats in the middle of the console room. Now that it’s not a surprise anymore, I can handle the ammonia. It’s no worse than using a lot of Windex in a small room. Unpleasant but not dangerous.

I grab the cylinder—and it’s super hot!

I yelp and pull my hands away. I blow on them for a moment and check for burns. It wasn’t too bad. Not stovetop hot. But hot.

Grabbing it with my bare hands was dumb. Bad thinking. I figured that since I’d held it before, it was safe to do now. But earlier I had thick spacesuit gloves on.

“You’ve been a bad alien cylinder,” I tell it. “You need a time-out.”

I pull my arm back into my sleeve and wrap my hand in the cuff. I use my protected knuckles to push the cylinder into the airlock. Once it’s in, I shut the door.

I’ll leave it alone for now. It’ll cool down to the room temperature eventually. And while it does, I don’t want it floating around my ship. I don’t think there’s anything in the airlock that can be damaged by some heat.

How hot was it?

Well, I had both hands on it (like an idiot) for a split second. My own reaction time was enough to keep me from getting burned. So it’s probably less than 100 degrees Celsius.

I open and close my hands a few times. They don’t hurt anymore, but I still remember the pain.

“Where’d the heat come from?” I mumbled.

The cylinder was out in space for a good forty minutes. It should have lost heat through radiation. It should be cold , not hot. I’m about 1 AU from Tau Ceti, and Tau Ceti has half the brightness of the sun. So I don’t think the Taulight could have heated the cylinder up much. Definitely not more than radiation would cool it down.

So either it has a heater inside or it was very hot when it started. I guess I’ll find out soon. It’s not very heavy, so it’s probably thin. If there’s no internal heat, it’ll cool off very fast in the air here.

The room still smells like ammonia. Ugh.

I float down to the lab. I don’t know where to start. So many things I want to do. Maybe I should begin by figuring out what the cylinder is made of? Something safe for the Blip-A ’s crew might be very poisonous to me, and neither of us would know.

Maybe I should check for radiation.

I drift to the lab table and reach out to steady myself. I’m getting better at dealing with zero-g. I think I remember an astronaut documentary saying some people are fine with it, while others really struggle. Looks like I’m one of the fortunate ones.

I’m using “fortunate” loosely here. I’m on a suicide mission. So…yeah.

The lab is a puzzle. It has been for a while. It’s clearly designed as if there will be gravity. It has tables, chairs, test-tube trays, and so on. There’s none of the usual stuff you’d expect in a weightless place. No Velcro on the walls, no computer screens at all angles. No smart use of space. Everything assumes there will be a “floor.”

The ship can speed up just fine. For a long time, too. It kept me at 1.5 g’s for probably a few years. But they can’t expect me to just keep the engines on and fly in circles to keep gravity in the lab, right?

I look around at each piece of lab equipment and try to clear my mind. There has to be a reason for this. And it’s in my memory somewhere. The trick is to think about what I want to know, but not worry about it too much. It’s like falling asleep. You can’t really do it if you focus on it too hard.

So many high-quality pieces of equipment. I let my mind wander as I look across them all….

Chapter 8

By the time we got to Geneva, I had no idea what day it was.

The computer predictions for the Astrophage breeder didn’t match what was actually happening. But I had managed to grow almost six grams of Astrophage. The aircraft carrier’s reactor couldn’t make enough heat to speed up the reaction any more. Stratt kept hinting they’d get a heat source that could keep up, but nothing had happened yet.

I typed on my computer even as the fancy private jet stopped at the gate. Stratt had to tell me to stop working.

Three hours later, we waited in a conference room.

Always a conference room. My life was just going from one conference room to another. This one was nicer than most, at least. With nice wood walls and a cool mahogany table. It was really something.

Stratt and I didn’t talk. I worked on heat stuff while she typed on her laptop doing who-knows-what. We were around each other enough.

Finally, a serious-looking woman came in and sat across from Stratt.

“Thank you for meeting me, Ms. Stratt,” she said with a Norwegian accent.

“No need to thank me, Dr. Lokken,” she said. “I don’t want to be here.”

I looked up from my laptop. “You are? I thought you scheduled this.”

She didn’t take her eyes off the Norwegian. “I scheduled it because I had six different world leaders on the phone at the same time bothering me to do it. I finally gave in.”

“And you are…?” Lokken asked me.

“Ryland Grace.”

She actually backed away. “ The Ryland Grace? Author of ‘An Analysis of Water-Based Assumptions and Recalibration of Expectations for Evolutionary Models’?”

“Yeah, got a problem with that?” I said.

Stratt half smiled at me. “You’re famous.”

“Infamous,” said Lokken. “His silly paper was an insult to the entire scientific community. This man works for you? Ridiculous. All his ideas about alien life were proven wrong.”

I scowled. “Hey. My point is life doesn’t have to have water to evolve. Just because we found some life that does use water, that doesn’t mean I’m wrong.”

“Of course it does. Two life-forms separately evolved to need water—”

“Independently?!” I scoffed. “Are you crazy? Do you really think something as complex as mitochondria would evolve the same way twice? This is clearly a panspermia event.”

She dismissed my statement like a fly. “Astrophage mitochondria is very different from Earth mitochondria. They clearly evolved on their own.”

“They’re ninety-eight percent the same!”

“Ahem,” said Stratt. “I don’t really understand what you’re arguing about, but can we—”

I pointed at Lokken. “This idiot thinks Astrophage evolved on its own, but it’s obvious Astrophage and Earth life are related!”

“That’s interesting, but—”

Lokken slammed the table. “How could a common ancestor have crossed interstellar space?”

“The same way Astrophage does it!”

She leaned toward me. “Then why haven’t we seen interstellar life all this time?”

I leaned toward her. “No idea. Maybe it was a one-time thing.”

“How do you explain the differences in mitochondria?”

“Four billion years of evolution going in different directions.”

“Stop,” Stratt said calmly. “I don’t know what this is…some kind of science argument? That’s not what we’re here for. Dr. Grace, Dr. Lokken, please sit down.”

I plopped into my seat and folded my arms. Lokken sat as well.

Stratt fiddled with a pen. “Dr. Lokken, you’ve been bothering governments to bother me. Over and over. Day in and day out. I know you want to be involved in Project Hail Mary, but I won’t make it a huge international mess. We don’t have time for the politics and power plays that always happen on big projects.”

“I’m not happy to be here either,” Lokken said. “I’m here, even though it’s a big problem for both of us, because this was the only way to tell you about a critical design flaw in the Hail Mary.”

Stratt sighed. “We sent out those early designs for general feedback. Not required meetings in Geneva.”

“Then consider this ‘general feedback.’ ”

“Could have been an email.”

“You would have deleted it. You have to listen to me, Stratt. This is important.”

Stratt twirled the pen around a few more times. “Well, I’m here. Go ahead.”

Lokken cleared her throat. “Tell me if I’m wrong, but the whole point of the Hail Mary is to be a lab. One we can send to Tau Ceti to find out why that star—and only that star—is safe from Astrophage.”

“That’s right.”

She nodded. “Then do you also agree that the lab on the ship is the most important thing?”

“Yes,” Stratt said. “Without it, the mission means nothing.”

“Then we have a big problem.” Lokken pulled some papers from her purse. “I have a list of the lab equipment you want on board. Spectrometers, DNA sequencers, microscopes, chemistry lab glassware—”

“I know the list,” Stratt said. “I approved it.”

Lokken dropped the papers on the table. “Most of this stuff won’t work in zero g.”

Stratt rolled her eyes. “We’ve already thought of that, of course. Companies everywhere are working on zero-g versions of this equipment right now.”

Lokken shook her head. “Do you know how much research went into making electron microscopes? Gas chromatographs? Everything else on this list? A century of science built on many failures. You just assume that making these things work in zero-g will happen right away?”

“I don’t see any way around it, unless you invented artificial gravity.”

“We have invented artificial gravity,” Lokken insisted. “A long time ago.”

Stratt looked at me. Clearly, that surprised her.

“I think she means a centrifuge,” I said.

“I know she means a centrifuge,” Stratt said. “What do you think?”

“I hadn’t thought about it before. I guess…it could work….”

Stratt shook her head. “No. That won’t work. We need to keep things simple. As simple as we can. Big, strong ship, few moving parts. The more complicated we make it, the more things can go wrong.”

“It’s worth the risk,” said Lokken.

“We’d have to add a giant weight to the Hail Mary just to make it work.” Stratt frowned. “I’m sorry, but we barely have enough energy to make the Astrophage for the current weight limit. We can’t just double it.”

“Wait. We have enough energy to make all the fuel? When did that happen—?” I said.

“You don’t need to add weight,” Lokken said. She pulled another paper from her purse and slapped it down on the table. “If you take the current design, cut it in half between the crew area and the fuel tanks, the two sides will have a good weight balance for a centrifuge.”

Stratt peered at the diagram. “You put all the fuel on the same side. That’s two million kilograms.”

“No.” I shook my head. “The fuel would be used up.”

They both looked at me.

“It’s a one-way trip,” I said. “The fuel will be gone when they get to Tau Ceti. Lokken picked a cut point where the back of the ship will weigh three times as much as the front. It’s a good weight balance for a centrifuge. It could work.”

“Thank you,” said Lokken.

“How do you cut a ship in half?” asked Stratt. “How does it become a centrifuge?”

Lokken flipped the diagram over to reveal a detailed image showing a connection between the two ship halves. “Spools of Zylon cable between the crew area and the rest of the ship. We could simulate one g of gravity with a hundred meters of separation.”

Stratt pinched her chin. Had someone actually changed her mind on something?

“I don’t like complicated things…” she said. “I don’t like risk.”

“This makes things simpler and safer,” Lokken said. “The ship, the crew, the Astrophage…it’s all just there to support the lab equipment. You need equipment you can count on. Stuff that’s been used for years with tons of use in the real world. Every possible problem has already been fixed. If you have one g of gravity—to make sure they’re in the environment they were made for—you get all that reliability.”

“Hmm,” said Stratt. “Grace? What do you think?”

“I…I think it’s a good idea.”

“Really?”

“Yeah,” I said. “I mean, we already have to build the ship to handle four years of constant speeding up at one and a half g’s or so. It’s going to be pretty strong.”

She looked at Lokken’s drawing for a while. “Wouldn’t this make the artificial gravity in the crew area backward?”

And she was right. The Hail Mary was designed so that “down” was “toward the engines.” As the ship speeds up, the crew is pushed “down” to the floor. But inside a centrifuge, “down” is always “away from the center of the spin.” So the crew would all be pushed toward the front of the ship.

“Yes, that would be a problem.” Lokken pointed to the diagram. The cables didn’t connect right to the crew area. They connected to two big discs on each side. “The cables connect to these big hinges. The whole front half of the ship can spin 180 degrees. So when they’re using the centrifuge, the front will face in toward the other half of the ship. Inside the crew area, the force of gravity will be away from the front—same as when the engines are firing.”

Stratt thought about it. “This is a pretty complex machine and you’ll be splitting the ship in two. You really think this is less risky?”

“Less risky than using brand-new equipment that hasn’t been tested enough. Trust me, I’ve used sensitive equipment most of my career,” I said. “It’s picky and fragile even when things are perfect.”

Stratt picked up her pen and tapped it on the table several times. “Okay. We’ll do it.”

Lokken smiled. “Excellent. I’ll write up a paper and send it along to the UN. We can form a committee—”

“No, I said we’ll do it.” Stratt stood up. “You’re with us now, Dr. Lokken. Pack a bag and meet us at Genève Aéroport. Terminal 3, private plane called Stratt.”

“What? I work for ESA. I can’t just—”

“Yeah, don’t worry,” I said. “She’s going to call your boss or your boss’s boss or whatever and have you assigned to her. You just got drafted.”

“I…I didn’t mean to design it myself,” Lokken argued. “I only meant to point out—”

“I never said you volunteered,” Stratt said. “It’s not voluntary at all.”

“You can’t just force me to work for you.”

But Stratt was already walking out of the room. “Meet us at the airport in one hour or I’ll have the Swiss Gendarmerie drag you there in two hours. Your call.”

Lokken stared at the door, stunned, then back to me.

“You get used to it,” I said.

—

The ship is a centrifuge! I remember now!

That’s why there’s a strange area called “Cable Faring.” That’s where the spools and Zylon cables are. The ship can break in half, turn the crew area around, and spin.

That turning-around part—that’s the odd ring I saw on the hull during my spacewalk! I remember the design now. It has two big hinges, letting the crew area turn around before the centrifuge starts.

It’s a lot like Apollo spacecraft. The lunar lander was attached below the command module at launch, but they’d separate, turn the command module around, and reconnect with the lander on their way to the moon. It’s one of those things that seems silly but is the best way to fix a problem.

I float back to the cockpit and check screens on different consoles. When each one isn’t what I want, I go to the next. Finally, I find it. The “Centrifuge” screen. It was hidden as a subpanel in the Life Support screen.

It looks easy enough. There are yaw, pitch, and roll readouts, showing the ship’s current state, like the Navigation panel. Another readout is labeled “Crew Compartment Angle”—that must be the turning-around part. Each one reads “0° per second.”

Below those is a button labeled “Engage Centrifuge Sequence.” Underneath that are numbers for rotational acceleration rate, final speed, spooling rate, estimated g-force at the lab floor, four screens for spool status (I guess there are four spools, two on each side), which emergency plans to use if there’s a problem, and more stuff I won’t pretend to understand. The important thing is all those readouts already have values.

Gotta love computers. They do all the thinking so you don’t have to.

I check out the emergency mode. It just says “Spin Down.” I tap it, and a menu pops up. The choices are: “Spin Down,” “Halt All Spools,” and one in red called “Separate.” I don’t want to do that one. I think “Spin Down” will slowly slow the ship’s spin if something’s wrong. Sounds good, so I’ll leave it on that.

I’m about to turn on the centrifuge, but I stop. Is everything secure? Is it okay to suddenly have a lot of force on the ship? I ignore the thought. The ship sped up constantly for years. It should be fine with a little centrifuge, right?

Right?

Like many astronauts before me, I trust the engineers who built this. Dr. Lokken, I guess. Hope she knew what she was doing.

I hit the button.

At first, nothing. I wonder if I even hit it right, or if I just missed the screen like I do on my phone sometimes.

Then, the alert goes off all over the ship. The loud triple beep repeats every few seconds. No one on the crew could miss that. A last warning, I guess, in case they weren't paying attention.

Above me, the Petrovascope screen locks. That confirms the ship's engines use Astrophage. It makes sense, but I wasn’t sure until now.

The beeping stops, and nothing happens. Then I see I’m closer to the Nav panel than before. I floated to the edge of the room. I put my arm out to stop myself and get back to where I was. Then I float toward the Nav panel again.

“Ohhh,” I say.

It’s begun. I’m not moving toward the Nav panel. The whole cockpit is moving toward me. The ship is starting to spin.

Everything shifts and changes direction. That's because as the ship spins, the crew area is also turning. This could get tricky.

“Uh…right!” I push off the wall and into the pilot seat.

I tilt. Or, rather, the room tilts. No, that doesn’t make sense. Nothing tilts. The ship is spinning faster and faster. It’s also speeding up the acceleration. Also, the front half of the ship has separated from the back, and it’s turning around those two big hinges. When it’s done, the nose will be pointing in toward the back half of the ship. All of this is happening at the same time, so the forces I’m feeling are really strange. Very complicated stuff, but also not my problem. It’s up to the computer to handle.

I watch the Centrifuge panel. The pitch rate reads 0.17° per second. Another readout labeled “Component Separation” reads 2.4 meters. There’s a little beep and the “Crew Compartment Angle” readout blinks. It shows 180°. I assume this whole process was planned out to reduce shock to the system and/or crew.

I feel a slight pressure on my butt as the seat pushes against me. The change is very smooth. I just…feel more and more gravity in what feels like a tilting room. It’s a strange feeling.

I know, logically, that I’m in a ship spinning around. But there are no windows to see out of. Only screens. I check the telescope screen that’s still pointed at the Blip-A. The stars in the background don’t move. It’s adjusting for my rotation somehow and canceling it out. That bit of software was probably hard to do, considering the camera probably isn’t at the exact center of rotation.

My arms grow heavy, so I put them on the armrests. I have to start using my neck muscles again for the first time in a while.

Five minutes after the process began, I feel a little less than normal Earth gravity. A quadruple beep announces the end of the process.

I check the Centrifuge screen. It shows a spin rate of 20.71° per second, a total distance of 104 meters, and a “Lab Gravity” of 1.00 g.

The ship diagram shows the Hail Mary split in two, the crew compartment facing the other half. The two halves are ridiculously far apart, and the whole thing spins slowly. Well, actually pretty fast, but it looks slow from that distance.

I unstrap from the chair, walk to the airlock, and open the hatch. The ammonia smell comes into the cockpit again, but not as strong as before. The alien thing is on the floor. I quickly touch it to check the temperature. It’s still warm, but not burning hot anymore. Good. It doesn’t have a heater or anything weird like that. It was just really hot at first.

I pick it up. Time to see what this thing is made of. And what’s inside.

Before leaving the cockpit, I look at the Telescope screen one last time. I don’t know why—I guess I just like to see what alien ships near me are doing.

The Blip-A spins in space. It rotates, probably at the same rate as the Hail Mary. I guess they saw me spin the centrifuge and thought it was another way to talk.

Humanity’s first misunderstanding with an intelligent alien race. Glad I could be a part of it.

—

I put the cylinder on the lab table. Where do I start? Everywhere!

I check if it's radioactive with a Geiger counter. It's not. That’s good.

I poke it with different things to feel how hard it is. It’s hard.

It looks like metal but doesn’t feel exactly like metal. I use a multimeter to see if it conducts electricity. It doesn’t. Interesting.

I get a hammer and chisel. I want a small piece of the cylinder material for the gas chromatograph—that way I’ll know what elements are in it. After a few hits with the hammer, the chisel breaks. The cylinder isn’t even scratched.

“Hm.”

The cylinder is too big for the gas chromatograph. But I find a handheld x-ray spectrometer. It looks like a UPC scanner gun. Easy to use, and it’ll give me some idea of what this thing is made of. It’s not as precise as the chromatograph, but it's better than nothing.

After a quick scan, it tells me the cylinder is made of xenon.

“What…?”

I use the spectrometer on the steel lab table to make sure it’s working right. It reports iron, nickel, chromium, and so on. Just what it should say. So I check the cylinder again and get the same weird results as before. I test it four more times but keep getting the same answer.

Why did I run the test so many times? Because those results don't make any sense. Xenon is a noble gas. It doesn’t react with anything. It doesn’t connect to anything. And it’s a gas at room temperature. But somehow it’s part of this solid thing?

And no, it’s not a tube full of xenon or anything like that. A spectrometer doesn't scan deeply. It can only tell you what's on the surface. If I pointed it at gold-plated nickel, it would say “100% gold,” because that’s all it can see. It can only tell me what the molecules on the cylinder's surface are. Apparently, they’re made of xenon.

This handheld spectrometer can’t detect elements lighter than aluminum. So there could be carbon, hydrogen, nitrogen, or anything else in there. But for elements it can detect…I’m looking at pure xenon.

“How?!”

I sit down on a stool and stare at the cylinder. What a strange object. What do I even call noble gases that react with things? Un-nobles?

But being stumped has one good effect. It makes me stop frantically messing with the cylinder and just look at it. For the first time, I see a thin line going around it, about an inch from the top. I feel it with my fingernail. It’s definitely a groove of some kind. Is that a lid? Maybe it just opens.

I pick up the cylinder and try to pull off the top. It doesn’t move. On a whim, I try to unscrew it. It also doesn’t move.

But there’s no reason aliens would use the same tightening and loosening rules we do, right?

I turn the lid to the right and it turns. My heart jumps!

I keep turning. After 90 degrees I feel it unlock. I pull the two pieces apart.
