Both halves have complex things inside. They look like…models of some kind? They both have very thin poles sticking up from their bases, leading to spheres of different sizes. I don’t see any moving parts, and everything seems to be made of the same strange material as the case.

I look at the bottom half first. Got to start somewhere.

A single whisker holds up…an abstract sculpture? It’s a marble-sized ball and a BB-sized ball, each held in place by thinner whiskers branching off the main vertical “trunk.” There’s also a strange curved shape connecting the tops of the two balls. This whole thing looks familiar…why…?

“Petrova line!” I say suddenly.

I’ve seen that curved shape so many times I know it by heart. My heart races.

I point to the large ball. “So you must be a star. And the little one must be a planet.”

These aliens know about Astrophage. Or, at least, they know about the Petrova line. But that doesn’t really tell me anything. They’re in a ship powered by Astrophage, so of course they know about it. And we’re chatting in a solar system that has a Petrova line, so that’s not surprising either. This could be their home system for all I know.

This is a good start, though. We were “talking” by flashing our engines. So they know I use Astrophage and that I can “see” (with help from the ship) the Petrova frequency. From that, they figured I must be able to see the Petrova line. They’re smart.

I look at the other half of the thing. Dozens of whiskers rise from the base. They’re all different lengths and each one ends in a ball less than a millimeter across. I poke a whisker with my finger and it doesn’t bend. I press harder and harder. Eventually the whole thing slides on the table. Those whiskers are stronger than something that thin should be.

I guess xenon makes pretty strong material when you get it to react with things. It bugs my sensitive scientist's heart! I try to ignore it and get back to what I’m doing.

I count thirty-one whiskers, each with its little ball at the end. While counting, I notice something special. There’s one whisker sticking up from the exact center of the disc, but unlike the others, it’s not connected to a ball. I squint to get a good look.

Instead of one ball, it’s two balls of different sizes and a curve—okay, I see. It’s a very small copy of the Petrova-line model on the other half of the thingamajig. Maybe one-twentieth the size.

And that little Petrova-line model has an even thinner line connecting it to another ball at the end of a different line. No, not quite a ball. It’s another Petrova-line model. I check the rest of the thingamajig for any more of them, but I don’t see any. Just the one in the middle and the one off to the side.

“Wait a minute…waaaaait a minute…”

I pull out the drawer with the lab computer panel. Time to use that huge amount of reference info. I find a big spreadsheet with the info I need, open it in Excel (Stratt likes products that are proven and easy to get), and do some math on it. Soon, I have the data chart I wanted. And it matches.

Stars. The little balls on the end of the lines are stars. Of course they are. What else would have a Petrova line?

But they’re not just any stars. These are specific stars. They’re all in the right places compared to each other, with Tau Ceti right in the center. The map looks a little strange. To make the balls match my data chart of star locations, I have to hold the thingamajig at an angle and turn it a bit.

But of course, all of Earth’s data is based on Earth’s orbit as the reference point. People from another planet would have a different system. But however you look at it, it means the same thing: The thingamajig is a map of the nearby stars.

Then I’m suddenly very interested in that little line connecting the center ball (Tau Ceti) to another ball. I check the matching star in my catalog: It’s called 40 Eridani. But I bet the crew of the Blip-A call it home.

That’s the message. “We’re from the 40 Eridani system. And now we’re here at Tau Ceti.”

But there’s even more to it. They’re also saying “40 Eridani has a Petrova line, just like Tau Ceti.”

I pause, letting that settle.

“Are you going through the same thing?!” I say.

Of course, they are! Astrophage is affecting all the nearby stars. These people are from a planet circling 40 Eridani, and 40 Eridani is infected, just like Earth’s sun! They have some advanced science, so they did what we did. They built a ship and came to Tau Ceti to see why it’s not dying!

“Wow!” I say.

Okay, I’m jumping to conclusions. Maybe they collect Astrophage from their Petrova lines and see it as a good thing. Maybe they created Astrophage. Maybe they just think Petrova lines are beautiful. This could mean many things. But the most likely, in my opinion, is that they’re here to find a cure.

Aliens.

Real aliens.

Aliens from the 40 Eridani system. So, I guess that makes them Eridanians? It’s hard to say, even harder to remember. Eridans? No. How about Eridians? It sounds like “iridium,” which is one of the coolest elements on the periodic table. Yeah, I’m going to call them Eridians.

And I think it’s pretty clear how I should react.

I searched the lab carefully a few days ago. There’s an electronics kit in one of the drawers. The challenge is remembering which one.

I don’t remember, of course. It takes me a while to search, and I almost swear a few times, but I find it eventually.

I don’t have any xenonite (that’s what I’m calling this weird alien stuff, and no one can stop me). But I do have solder and a soldering iron. I break off a small piece of solder, melt one end, and stick it to the Tau Ceti sphere. It sticks pretty well, which is good. You never know with xenonite.

I check, double-check, and triple-check to make sure I know which little star in the model is Sol (Earth’s sun). I solder the other side of the wire to Sol.

I look around the lab until I find some hard wax. With some poking, fire, and a little swearing, I make a really bad copy of the Petrova-line icon they sent me. I stick it onto Sol in the model. It looks okay. At least, good enough that they should understand.

I look at it. The sleek, thin lines of the xenonite whiskers are ruined by my crooked, blob-ended solder and bad wax model. It’s like someone drew with crayons on a Da Vinci, but it will have to do.

I try to screw the top and bottom of the thing back together. They don’t fit. I try again. Still doesn’t work. I remember that Eridians use left-handed screws. So I do what feels like unscrewing. The two pieces connect perfectly.

Time to give it back to them. Nicely.

Except I can’t. Not with the ship spinning like this. If I tried to go out the airlock, I’d fly off into space.

I grab the thing and go up to the control room. I strap myself into the chair and tell the ship to stop spinning.

Like before, I feel the room tilt, but this time it tilts the other way. And again, I know it’s not really tilting, it’s just how I feel the sideways movement, but whatever.

I feel the gravity lessen and the room straighten until I’m floating again. This time I’m not confused. I guess my instincts have accepted that gravity comes and goes. The operation finishes with a final “clunk” as the crew compartment realigns with the back of the ship.

I get back in the EVA suit, grab the gadget, and go out into space again. I don’t need to use tethers to cross the hull this time. I just clip my tether in the airlock.

The Blip-A has stopped spinning—probably when the Hail Mary did. And it’s still 217 meters away.

I don’t need to be a great quarterback to make this throw. I just need to launch the gadget toward the Blip-A. It’s over a hundred meters wide. I should be able to hit it.

I give the gadget a push. It floats away from me at a good speed. Maybe 2 meters per second—about a jogging pace. This is a kind of message too. I’m telling my new friends that I can handle slightly faster deliveries.

The gadget floats toward the Eridian ship and I go back into mine.

“Okay, guys,” I say. “The enemy of my enemy is my friend. If Astrophage is your enemy, I’m my friend.”

—

I watch the Telescope screen. Sometimes I look away. Sometimes I play Klondike solitaire on the Nav panel. But I always check the telescope every few seconds. A thick pair of gloves, taken from the lab earlier, tries to float away. I grab them and stuff them behind the pilot’s seat.

It’s been two hours and my alien friends haven’t said a word. Are they waiting for me to speak again? I just told them what star I came from. It’s their turn to talk, right?

Do they even understand the idea of taking turns? Or is that just a human thing?

What if Eridians live for 2 million years and waiting a century to answer is normal for them?

How am I going to get rid of this red 7 on the far-right pile? I don’t have any black 8s in my deck and—

Movement!

I turn to the Telescope screen so fast my legs swing out into the middle of the control room. There’s another cylinder coming toward me. I guess the many-armed robot threw it just now. I check the Radar screen. Blip-B is moving at over a meter per second. I only have a few minutes to get ready!

I get back into the EVA suit and use the airlock. When I open the outside door, I see the cylinder spinning. It might be the same one as before, or it might be new. And this time, it’s going straight for the airlock. I guess they saw that’s where I left and came back into the ship and decided to make it easier for me.

How thoughtful of them.

They’re accurate too. A minute later, the cylinder floats right through the middle of the open doorway. I catch it. I wave to the Blip-A and close the door. They probably don’t know what a wave is, but I felt like I should do it.

I go back to the control room and take off the EVA suit, leaving the cylinder to float near the airlock. The ammonia smell is strong, but this time I’m prepared for it.

I put the thick lab gloves on and grab the cylinder. Even through the fireproof gloves, I can feel the warmth. I know I should wait for it to cool down but I don’t want to.

It looks the same as before. I unscrew it the same left-handed way. This time, there’s no star map. Instead, it’s a model. What am I looking at here?

A single post from the base holds up an odd shape. No, two odd shapes connected by a tube. Hey, wait. One of the shapes is the Hail Mary. Oh, and the other one is the Blip-A.

The models have no detail or texture. But they’re good enough for me to recognize what they are, so they did their job. The Hail Mary is only 3 inches long, while the Blip-A is closer to 8 inches. Man, that ship is huge.

And that tube connecting them? It connects to the Hail Mary’s airlock and leads to the center of the Blip-A’s diamond-shaped segment. The tunnel is just wide enough to cover my airlock door.

They want to meet.

Chapter 9

I let the model float in the middle of the room. The xenonite is almost unbreakable, so I don’t need to worry about it hitting anything.

Is this a good idea? I have a planet to save. As amazing as it is to meet smart aliens, is this risk worth it?

The Eridians clearly understand Astrophage. At least enough to make engines from it. And—I think—they’re trying to tell me they’re here for the same reason I am. They might have information I don’t. They might even have the solution I’m looking for. And they seem nice enough.

But this is like a stranger offering me candy in space. I want the candy (information), but I don’t know the stranger.

What else can I do? Ignore them?

I could keep going as if I never saw them. They’re probably as surprised to see me as I am to see them. They might keep trying to talk, but I don’t think they’d get mean.

Or would they? I can’t know.

No, this is obvious. I have to talk to them. If they have any information about Astrophage, even a little bit, I have to talk to them. It’s a risk, yes, but this whole mission is a risk.

Okay. So what would I do if I were them?

I’m an Eridian. I want to build a tunnel to connect to the strange human ship. But I don’t know what the human ship is made of. How can I ensure they'll connect or seal? My xenonite knowledge is unmatched, but how do I join it to “humanium” or whatever that ship is made of? I’ve sent the human my xenonite designs, so he knows what I have. But I still don’t know what he has.

They’ll need a piece of my hull. And they’ll need to know it’s a piece of my hull.

“Right,” I say to no one.

I don’t know if this is a good idea or a terrible one. But I’m going to break off a piece of my hull.

I grab some EVA tools from Drawer 17E in the lab. I found them a while ago. They’re on a tool belt that clips onto the EVA suit. Stratt and the others made sure we had all the tools we'd need for hull repairs. Ilyukhina would normally fix things, but she’s gone.

Huh. Random thought. Ilyukhina was our engineer—the one who fixed everything. Okay. Well, now it’s me.

I get back in the EVA suit, and back outside. Again. Going in and out is getting annoying. I hope this tunnel works.

I move along the hull, adjusting the tether each time. And I start thinking…

What good is a tunnel, really? Our environments probably aren't compatible. We can’t just connect the ships with a tunnel and shake hands. I think there’s a lot of ammonia over there.

And the temperature. Those cylinders are hot when I get them.

Some quick calculations tell me that first cylinder they sent should have lost 100 degrees Celsius or more during that forty-minute trip (depending on its starting temperature). And it was still hot when I got it. So it was really hot when it left their ship. Like…way hotter than the boiling point of water.

I try not to guess too much, but come on. I’m a scientist and these are aliens. I’m going to guess.

Do Eridians live in a place hotter than boiling water? If so, it proves I was right! The goldilocks zone is nonsense! You don’t need liquid water for life!

I should be more focused on the “first contact with smart aliens” thing or the “save all of humanity” thing, but darn it, I can spend a moment being happy about being right when everyone said I was wrong!

I finally reach a spot on the ship that seems right for the job. I’m behind the entire pressurized part of the ship, past the part where it gets wider. If I’m right, I’m standing on a big empty tank that used to be full of Astrophage. If I break the ship here, it shouldn’t matter.

I pull out a hammer and chisel. Not the best way to do this, but I can’t think of anything better. I start by putting one corner of the chisel on the ship and tapping it lightly. It makes a noticeable dent. It doesn’t take much to get through this outer layer.

I use the hammer and chisel to cut out a 6-inch circle of ship material. There’s a layer of something underneath. I can feel it with the chisel. Probably insulation.

I have to pry the circle out with the chisel. The layer underneath holds strong, but then gives way suddenly. The ship sample flies off into space.

“Shoot!”

I jump off the ship. I grab the circle right before my tether pulls tight. I breathe for a second, thinking about how dumb I am, then pull myself back to the ship. Looking at the circle, it seems like there’s a light, foamy stuff attached to the underside. Styrofoam, maybe. Probably something more complex than that.

“I hope you guys watched all that,” I say. “Because I’m not doing it again.”

I throw the hull chunk at the Blip-A.

Doing this right in front of them will make them sure I’m sending them a sample of the hull. I hope it’s enough for what they want to do. I don’t even know if they wanted it or needed it. They might be looking at their screens right now and saying, “What is this idiot doing? Is he poking a hole in his own ship? Why?”

I stay on the hull and watch as the chunk tumbles in the Taulight. The multi-armed robot on the Blip-A’s hull slides along its rails to catch it. Once it’s in position, it waits for the hull chunk to arrive and catches it perfectly.

And then, I swear to God, it waves at me! One of its little arms waves at me!

I wave back.

It waves again.

Okay, this could go on all day. I head back toward the airlock.

Your move, guys.

Their move is taking forever, and I’m getting bored.

Wow. Here I am in a spaceship in the Tau Ceti system, waiting for the smart aliens I just met to continue our talk…and I’m bored. Humans sure can get used to weird stuff and make it seem normal.

I look through the Radar panel controls to see what else it can do. After poking around in the settings, I find what I want: the close-range warning settings. Right now, it’s set to 100 kilometers. Seems okay. You’d think things would be millions of kilometers away, or at least tens of thousands. So, a rock within 100 kilometers would be a big deal.

I change the setting to 0.26 kilometers. I hope it doesn’t say it’s too low, but it works.

I stretch and float out of the pilot seat. The Blip-A is 271 meters away. If they get closer than 260 meters, or send another gift that gets that close, the warning will sound. I don’t have to keep staring at the screen. The control room will yell a warning when the Blip-A does something interesting.

I float down to the sleeping area.

“Food,” I say.

The arms grab a box from their little stash in the ceiling and stick it to my bunk. I should check out what’s in there sometime. For now, I push off the ceiling and float down to the food. The box, marked DAY 10—MEAL 1, has a Velcro-like strip on the bottom to keep it on the bed. I open it up and see a burrito.

Not sure what I expected, but okay. A burrito it is.

Turns out it’s a room-temperature burrito. Beans, cheese, some red sauce…all pretty tasty, really. But room temperature. Either the crew doesn’t get hot meals here or the machine doesn’t trust a recent coma patient not to burn himself on hot food. Probably the latter.

I float up to the lab and put the burrito in the sample furnace. I leave it there for a few minutes before pulling it out with tongs. The cheese bubbles and a cloud of steam slowly floats out in all directions.

I leave the burrito to float in the air and cool.

I snicker. If I really wanted a hot burrito, I’d turn on the spin drives, go outside, and hold the burrito in the light from it. That’d get it hot really quick. As in: It would get vaporized along with my arm and whatever else was in the blast range, because—

—

“Welcome to Little Russia!” said Dimitri. He waved dramatically at the aircraft carrier’s lower hangar deck. The whole space had been changed into labs full of high-tech equipment. Dozens of scientists in lab coats worked at their tasks, sometimes speaking Russian to each other. Dimitri’s Denizens, we called them.

We probably spent too much time naming stuff.

I held my little sample container like Scrooge with a bag of coins. “I’m not happy about this.”

“Oh, hush,” said Stratt.

“I’ve only made eight grams of Astrophage so far, and I’m supposed to just give away two grams of it? Two grams may not seem like much, but it’s ninety-five billion Astrophage cells.”

“It is for a good cause, my friend!” said Dimitri. “I promise you will like it. Come, come!”

He led Stratt and me through to the main lab. The center was taken up by a huge cylindrical vacuum chamber. The chamber was open and three technicians were attaching something to a table inside.

Dimitri said something in Russian to them. They answered him. He said something else and pointed to me. They smiled and made happy Russian sounds.

Then Stratt said something stern in Russian.

“Sorry,” said Dimitri. “English only for now, my friends! For the American!”

“Hello, American!” said one of the technicians. “I speak English for you! You have fuel?”

I gripped my sample container tighter. “I have some fuel….”

Stratt looked at me the way I look at stubborn students in my class. “Hand it over, Dr. Grace.”

“You know, my breeder doubles Astrophage population over time, right? Taking away two grams now is like taking away four grams next month.”

She took the container from me and gave it to Dimitri.

He held up the small metal vial and looked at it with pleasure. “This is a good day. I have been waiting for this day. Dr. Grace, let me show you my spin drive!”

He motioned for me to follow and hurried up the stairs into the vacuum chamber. The technicians left, one by one, to give us space.

“Everything is connected,” one of them said. “Checklist complete. Ready for test.”

“Good, good,” Dimitri said. “Dr. Grace, Ms. Stratt. Come!”

He led Stratt and me into the vacuum chamber. A thick, shiny metal plate was against one wall. In the middle of the chamber was a round table with a device on it.

“This is spin drive.” Dimitri grinned.

It wasn’t impressive to look at. It was about two feet wide, mostly round, but with one side cut flat. Sensors and wires stuck out from openings all over it.

Dimitri lifted the top cover to show the inside. It was more complex. Inside was a clear triangle on a rotor. Dimitri spun it a little. “See? Spin. Spin drive.”

“How does it work?” I asked.

He pointed to the triangle. “This is the revolver—made of strong, clear plastic. And this”—he pointed to a space between the revolver and the outer shell—“is where fuel goes in. A light inside that part of the revolver shines a little light with wavelengths of 4.26 and 18.31 microns—wavelengths that attract Astrophage. The Astrophage move to that revolver face. But not too fast. The Astrophage's push is based on the strength of the light. A dim light makes a weak push, but it’s enough to make the Astrophage stick to the surface.”

He turned the triangle and lined up an edge with the flat side of the shell. “Rotate 120 degrees, and this face of the revolver with Astrophage stuck to it now faces the back of the ship. Increase the light inside. The Astrophage are now very excited and push very hard toward the light! Their thrust—Petrova-frequency light—leaves the back of the ship. This pushes the ship forward. Millions of little Astrophages pushing on the back of the ship make it go, yes?”

I bent down to look. “I see…this way no part of the ship has to be in the path of the light.”

“Yes, yes!” said Dimitri. “Astrophage force only limited by the brightness of light attracting it. I did much math and decided best is to make Astrophage use all energy in four seconds. Any faster and force will break revolver.”

He turned the revolver another 120 degrees and pointed to the remaining third of the shell. “This is cleaning area. A squeegee wipes dead Astrophage off the revolver.”

He pointed to the cleaning area, then the fueling area, and then the open face. “All three areas active at same time. So while this area cleans dead Astrophage off this face, the fueling area adds Astrophage to that face, and the other face is pointed out back of ship, providing thrust. This pipelining means the part of triangle pointed out back of ship is always thrusting.”

Dimitri opened my vial of Astrophage and put it in the fueling chamber. I guess since the Astrophage will find their way to the triangle face, no special handling was needed. He could just…let the fuel see the light.

“Come, come,” he said. “Experiment time!”

We left the vacuum chamber, and Dimitri sealed it off. He yelled something in Russian, and all the Russians started repeating it. Everyone made their way to the far side of the hangar deck, including us.

They’d set up a folding table. It had a laptop on it with Russian writing on the screen.

“Ms. Stratt. How far is the carrier from the nearest land?” Dimitri asked.

“About three hundred kilometers,” she said.

“This is good.”

“Wait, why?” I said. “Why is that good?”

Dimitri pursed his lips. “It is…good. Time for science!”

He pushed a button. There was a muffled whump from the far side of the bay, followed by a hum, and then nothing.

“Experiment done.” He leaned forward to read the screen. “Sixty thousand Newtons of force!”

He turned to the other Russians. “60,000 ньютонов!”

They all cheered.

Stratt turned to me. “That’s a lot, right?”

I was too busy staring at Dimitri to answer her. “Did you say sixty thousand Newtons?”

He pumped his fist. “Yes! Sixty thousand Newtons! Held for one hundred microseconds!”

“Oh my God. From that little thing?!” I started to walk forward. I had to see it myself.

Dimitri grabbed my arm. “No. You stay here, friend. We all stay here. One point eight billion Joules of light energy came out. That’s why we needed the vacuum chamber and one thousand kilograms of silicon. No air to turn into plasma. Light goes straight to silicon block. Energy is absorbed by melting the metal. See?”

He turned the laptop toward me. A camera inside the vacuum chamber showed the glowing blob that used to be a thick metal plate.

“Whoa…” I said.

“Yes, yes,” Dimitri said. “That Mr. Einstein with his E = mc 2. Very powerful stuff. We let the cooling system work on it for a few hours. It uses seawater. It will be fine.”

I just shook my head in amazement. In just 100 microseconds—that’s one ten-thousandth of a second—Dimitri’s spin drive melted a metric ton of metal. All that energy had been stored in my little Astrophages. Slowly collected from the carrier’s nuclear reactor heat over time by my breeder. The math made sense, but seeing it happen was different.

“Wait…how much Astrophage did you use?”

Dimitri smiled. “I can only guess based on thrust. But it was close to twenty micrograms.”

“I gave you two whole grams! Can I have the rest back, please?”

“Don’t be greedy,” Stratt said. “Dimitri needs it for more tests.”

She turned to him. “Good work. How big will the real drive be?”

Dimitri pointed to the video feed. “That big. That is real drive.”

“No, I mean the one on the ship.”

“That,” he said, pointing again. “You want backup, safety, and it to work all the time, yes? So we don’t make just one big engine. We make a thousand little ones. One thousand and nine, actually. Enough for all the thrust we need, with plenty extra. If some break during the trip? Not a problem. More thrust from the others makes up for it.”

“Ah.” Stratt nodded. “Lots of little spin drives. I like it. Keep up the good work.”

She headed to the stairwell.

I stared at Dimitri. “If you’d set off all two grams of that sample at once…”

He shrugged. “Fwoosh! We turn to vapor. All of us. The ship too. The explosion would make a small tsunami. But we're three hundred kilometers away from land, so it is okay.”

He slapped me on the back. “And I’d owe you a drink even after death, yes?! Ha-ha-ha-ha!”

—

“Huh,” I say to myself. “So that’s how the spin drive works.”

I munch on my burrito.

So I guess I have a thousand of those (“A thousand and nine!” I hear Dimitri’s voice in my head). At least—that’s how many I started with. Some probably broke down during the trip. There’s probably a panel on the Spin Drive console that’ll tell me how each one is doing.

The proximity alert interrupts my thoughts.

“Finally!”

I “drop” the burrito (it floats where I leave it) and push myself up to the control room. The hatch from the dormitory to the lab doesn’t line up with the hatch from the lab to the control room, but there’s a diagonal path that will send me through both if I do it just right.

I don’t get it right this time. I have to push off a lab wall on the way. Still, I’m getting better at it.

I check the Radar panel and, sure enough, the Blip-A is coming! Not just a piece of it this time. The whole ship is headed my way. Nice and slow. Maybe they’re trying to seem friendly? Either way, it’s almost here.

It looks like its hull has something new. On that diamond part that’s as big as the Hail Mary itself, there’s a tube sticking straight up. The hull robot is sitting next to it, looking proud. Maybe I’m imagining things a little.

The tube looks like it’s made of xenonite. It’s patchy gray and tan with lines like wood grain. Hard to see from here, but it also looks like it’s hollow.

I think I know what’s going to happen. If they do what they showed me with the model, they’ll put the other end of it against my airlock.

How will they connect their tunnel? My airlock can connect to other ships—probably the ship that brought me and my crew to the Hail Mary—but I don’t expect Eridians to know all the details of a universal airlock.

The Blip-A gets closer and closer. What if they mess up? What if they don’t get the measurements right? What if they accidentally poke a hole in my ship? I’m the only thing standing between humanity and extinction. Could a mistake in alien math destroy my entire species?

I rush to the airlock and put on the EVA suit. I’m in it faster than ever. Better to be safe than sorry.

The Blip-A is so close now that the Telescope screen just shows a blurry patch of hull. I switch to the external cameras. There are cameras all over my ship. They’re all controlled from a screen on the EVA panel. I guess it’s good to know where your astronaut is when you’re telling them what to do out there.

The tunnel is about 20 feet long. Or 7 meters. Man, being an American scientist is annoying sometimes. You think in random units depending on the situation.

The hull robot reaches out with some seriously long arms. I didn’t know it could do that. It stretches way past the tunnel toward my airlock. Not creepy at all. Five alien robot arms reaching for my front door. Nothing to worry about.

Each arm’s three-fingered “hand” is holding…something. A curved bar with a flat plate on each end. Like a coffee-mug handle. Three of the arms reach the Hail Mary and stick the flat parts of their devices to the hull. Soon after, the other two arms do the same. Then, all five pull back, dragging the Hail Mary toward the tunnel.

Okay. So those flat things are handles. How are they stuck on? Good question! My hull is smooth and made of aluminum that isn't magnetic (why do I remember that now?). The handles definitely aren’t connected with screws or bolts. Must be glue.

And it starts to click.

Of course they aren’t going to figure out how the docking thing works. They’re going to glue one end of the tunnel to my ship. Why not? Much easier.

My ship groans. It’s a 100,000-kilogram machine that was never meant to be pulled by its airlock. Can the hull handle this?

I check the seals on my spacesuit again.

The control room moves around me. It’s not fast—just a few centimeters per second. Hey, for small spaceship speeds I think in metric! Much better than “cubits per fortnight” or whatever.

I let the wall come to me. Without really thinking about it, I like being a bit farther from the airlock. Something scary is happening over there.

Clunk.

The Eridian tunnel has bumped the hull. Clicks and scrapes come next. I watch the hull camera views.

The tunnel mouth, now stuck to the airlock opening, is bigger than the whole airlock door. I guess this is it. If the glue holds the pressure. They don't even know my air pressure. What's the glue made of? So many questions.

I can't use the control-room panels with my EVA suit gloves. I wish I could zoom in somehow. I squint at a video feed showing the tunnel. It looks tight against the hull to me. The hull curves around that spot. It's a hard shape to make, but the Eridians copied it perfectly.

After another minute, the robot arms let go of the handles, leaving them on the hull.

A soft sound comes from the airlock. A whooshing sound. Is that air flowing? They're filling the tunnel with air!

My heart races. Can my ship handle this? What if their air eats aluminum? What if aluminum kills Eridians instantly? This is a terrible idea!

The whooshing stops.

I gulp.

They're done. Nothing's dissolved yet. I float to the airlock to take a look.

I had both airlock doors sealed, of course. More protection in case of a leak. I open the inner door and float inside. I look out the small window.

The blackness of space is gone, replaced by the blackness of a dark tunnel. I turn on the helmet lights and angle my head to shine light through the window.

The end of the tunnel is very close. I don’t mean I’m worried about it. I mean the end is not 20 feet away. It’s more like 10 feet. And while the rest of the tunnel is made of gray and tan spotted xenonite, the wall at the end has a hexagonal pattern of random colors.

They didn’t just put in a tunnel. They connected my airlock to theirs, with a wall in between.

Smart.

I close the inner airlock door with me inside and remove the air. I turn the outer door’s handle and push. It opens easily. The tunnel has no air—at least, on my side of the divider.

I think I understand. This is a test. They had the same worries I had. Connect it, let me fill my half with air, and see what happens. Either it works or it doesn’t. If it works, great! If not, they’ll try something else. Or maybe ask me to try something.

Okay. Let’s see.

I tell the airlock to add air. It won’t—the outer door is open. Good to know that safety feature is there, but I’ll have to get around it.

It’s not hard—there’s a manual valve that will let air from the ship into the airlock. It ignores all computer controls. You don’t want someone to die because of a software problem, right?

I open the valve. Air rushes in from the Hail Mary and, with the airlock wide open, into the tunnel. After three minutes, the airflow slows and then stops. My suit readings say there’s 400 hectopascals of pressure outside. The Hail Mary has the same pressure as my part of the tunnel.

I close the valve and wait. I watch the pressure gauge on my suit. The pressure stays at 400 hectopascals. We have a good seal.

Eridians know how to stick xenonite to aluminum. Of course they do. Aluminum's an element, and any species that could invent xenonite probably knows the periodic table a thousand times better than we do.

Time for a leap of faith. I open the EVA suit and climb out. The strong smell of ammonia fills the air but it’s otherwise breathable. It’s my own air, after all. I push the suit back toward the airlock. The helmet lamps are my only light, so I position the suit so the lights point down the tunnel.

I float to the mystery wall and reach out, but stop. I can feel the heat even from a few inches away. Eridians like it hot.

In fact, I’m starting to sweat. The tunnel walls are heating my air. It’s uncomfortable, but not too bad. I can open the Hail Mary ’s inner airlock door if I want the climate control to take over. Then our systems can fight it out. They’ll keep the hot side hot and I’ll keep the cold side cold.

Even with sweat on my brow and the ammonia making my eyes water, I keep going. I’m too curious not to. Who could blame me?

There are at least twenty hexagons on this wall. They’re all different colors and textures and some might be see-through. I should list each one and see if I can figure out what they're made of. Looking closer, I see a seam along the edges of the hexes.

That’s when I hear a sound from the other side:

Knock, knock, knock.

Chapter 10

They knocked, so I should knock back. I know the wall will be hot, so I tap it quickly.

I knock three times, like they did.

No answer right away. I study the hex wall. About forty hexes, and each looks different. Maybe different materials? I feel like I should do something, but what?

Are they watching? I don’t see a camera.

I point to my airlock. I don’t know if they can see me or understand. I push off the wall and go back to the airlock, then open the inner door. Why not? The pressure is the same. It’s okay to leave it open. If the tunnel loses pressure, the air will slam the door shut and I’ll live.

I go to the lab and pack a bag with some important things, then go back to the tunnel.

First, I tape LED lamps along the tunnel, pointing at the hex wall. Now I can see better. I use my handheld x-ray spectrometer to scan a hex. It’s xenonite, like the cylinders they sent.

Almost.

The trace elements are a bit different. Interesting. Maybe xenonite is like steel—many recipes? I check the next hex. Another unique mix.

Best guess: Different types of xenonite work best in different situations. They didn't know what my air was like, so they want to test different compounds against it. When I leave the tunnel, they’ll check the hexes to see which one does best.

That means I should leave the tunnel. Should I lower the pressure on my side for them? Seems polite. I could do it easily—I'd just tell the airlock to cycle. It would think, "Wow, there's a lot of air in me today!" but would just keep pumping until it was empty.

But then again, maybe they can sample the air on this side. If so, I should leave it here, right?

I decide to leave it as is. They probably have a way to sample it. If I were building this tunnel, that’s what I’d do, and they seem smart.

I turn back toward the airlock, but something catches my eye. Movement!

I quickly look back at the hex wall. Nothing's changed. But I could have sworn I saw something move. Some of the hexes are shiny—I probably just saw my reflection.

Wait…

One hex looks different. Why?

It’s near the tunnel wall. Not very noticeable. I float over to get a better look.

“Holy cow!” I say.

This hex is clear! All the others are cloudy, but this one is like glass! I grab a lamp from the wall and hold it up to the hex. I press my head against the hot wall to get a better look.

Light shines through to the other side. I can see the tunnel walls beyond. Either their side has no air, or their air is clear. Either way, nothing is blocking or blurring my view.

Suddenly, a rock hits the other side of the hex. It stays there, just inches from me. It’s roughly triangular, a dark brown color, with rough, jagged edges, like a spear tip from a caveman.

Have I found cavemen in space?

Stop being stupid, Ryland.

Why did they put a rock there? Is it sticky? Are they trying to block my view? If so, they’re failing. The little triangle is only a couple of inches wide at its thickest, and the hex is a good 8 inches across.

And it gets weirder. Now the rock is bending at joints, and two similar rocks do the same. Then there's a longer rock attached to them that—

That’s not a rock. It’s a claw! A claw with three fingers!

I need to see more! I press my face against the hex. It burns, but I don’t pull away. It hurts, and it’ll probably leave a mark. I should get a camera from the lab, but no one would think of that now.

I groan as my face hurts, but I get a better view.

The alien’s claw—er…I’ll call it a hand. That’s less scary. The alien’s hand has three triangular fingers, each with joints. Knuckles, I guess. They can close up into a raindrop shape or widen out to a sort of three-legged starfish.

The skin is weird. It looks like brownish-black rock. It’s uneven and bumpy, like someone carved the hand out of granite and hasn’t smoothed it yet. Natural armor, maybe? Like a turtle shell but less neat?

There’s an arm too. I can barely see it from this angle, no matter how hard I press my face into the Hot Wall of Pain. But there’s an arm leading away from the hand. I mean, there’d have to be, right? Not just a magic floating hand.

I can’t take the pain anymore. I pull my head away. I feel my face. It’s pretty raw, but there aren’t any blisters.

Tap-tap-tap.

The alien is tapping the clear hex with a finger. So I flick it with my finger three times.

It taps the hex again, three times. So I tap again as well.

Then comes something creepy. The cla— hand— retreats and returns with an object and holds it against the clear hexagon. Whatever it is, it’s small. I drift closer to the wall for a better look. The heat warms my face.

The object is xenonite, of course. It’s about a half-inch high and finely detailed. It looks like a doll. But it has an oversized head and really thick arms and legs—

“Oh!”

It’s me. I’m a tiny Russian Orlan-MKS2 EVA suit. That’s all they’ve seen of me so far.

Another hand appears. I have two hands, so it makes sense they do too. The second hand holds a model of the Hail Mary. It seems to be the same size as the little me. The hands then push the little me into the little Hail Mary’s airlock.

It’s clear. It’s saying, Go back into your ship.

I give a thumbs-up. The alien lets Mini-Me and the Hail Mary model float away. Then it twists a hand into something like a thumbs-up. It’s just two fingers curled into a ball with the third pointing up. At least it’s not the middle one.

I go back to the Hail Mary and close the airlock door.

I’m panting and excited. I can’t believe that just happened.

That’s an alien. I just saw an alien. Not just an alien ship, but an alien being. I mean—just his claw—er…hand. But still.

I say “his hand,” but maybe it’s her hand. Or some other pronoun I don’t know. They might have seventeen sexes, for all I know. Or none. No one talks about the hard parts of first contact: pronouns. I’ll use “he” for now, because it seems rude to call a thinking being “it.”

Also, until I hear otherwise, his name is Rocky.

Okay, now what? Rocky told me to go back into my ship. So I did.

I feel kind of dumb. There’s a lot of science I should be doing, right?

I peek through the airlock window. My lamps are still taped to the walls in the tunnel, and I can see some…changes.

The hex wall is gone. Just plain gone. I can see all the way to the Blip-A’s hull. And there’s a hull robot attached to it, reaching out and doing stuff with its little robot hands.

And yeah, its hands look like Rocky’s hands, generally. Three fingers. About the same size as Rocky’s hands. Probably controlled with something like a Nintendo Power Glove inside the ship.

Man, I’m old.

The robot is very interested in my lamps. Heck, I would be too. Those are alien artifacts with alien technology. Sure, they’re just lights, but they’re alien lights to my Eridian friends over there. Probably the most exciting scientific find of their history. The robot arm puts them in a little space on the Blip-A hull, and a latch closes. I bet those are going to be the most studied lamps ever.

I’m glad they got to have that moment of discovery, but they took my light source away. I can hear the occasional clunk, but it’s pitch-dark in there.

That’s interesting itself. I’m not an alien from 40 Eridani, but if I were working with a remote-controlled robot, I’d have a camera on it somewhere and a light to see what I was doing. But they don’t need that. They don’t need light.

Wait a minute. What they see might be totally different from what we see. Humans only see a small part of all the light out there. We changed to see the light that's most common on Earth. Maybe Eridians changed to see different light. The room could be full of infrared or ultraviolet light, and I wouldn't see anything.

Hmm. A robot. Why a robot? They had a living thing there a few minutes ago—my boy Rocky. Why switch him out with a robot?

Vacuum.

They probably took all the air out of the tunnel. They have a piece of my hull—they know it's made of aluminum and about how thick it is. Maybe they aren't sure if my ship can handle outside pressure. Or maybe their air messes with aluminum.

So they keep the tunnel empty, which means they have to use a robot.

I feel like Sherlock Holmes. All I saw was "nothing," and I figured out a bunch of stuff! Stuff that's just a guess and with nothing to prove it, but stuff anyway!

I could get another lamp—the lab has a few more. I could shine it in there to see what Robo-Rocky is doing. But I'll find out soon enough. And I don't want to be in some other part of the ship if something cool happens.

Just as I'm thinking that, something cool happens.

Knock-knock-knock.

No, that’s not creepy at all. Being in a spaceship twelve light-years from home and having someone knock on the door is totally normal.

Okay, now I need another lamp. I rush down to the lab to grab one, then back up to the control room. I cycle the airlock without putting on the EVA suit. I turn the manual vent valves on both doors of the airlock to repressurize the tunnel. It works as expected. The seal is still good out there.

I open the outer door and float in, lamp in hand.

The hex wall is gone—replaced by a solid wall of clear material. And on the other side of that wall is Rocky.

He’s a spider. A big spider.

I turn to run. But my rational brain takes over.

“Easy…easy…they’re friendly,” I say to myself. I turn back and look at the scene.

Rocky is smaller than a human. He’s about the size of a Labrador. He has five legs coming out from a central shell-like thing. The shell, roughly a pentagon, is 18 inches across and half as thick. I don’t see eyes or a face anywhere.

Each leg has a joint in the middle—I’ll call it an elbow. Each leg (or arm?) ends in a hand. So he’s got five hands. Each hand has the triangular fingers I saw closely last time. All five hands look the same. I don’t see any “front” or “back” to him. He seems to be symmetrical with five sides.

He wears clothing. The legs are bare, showing the rocklike skin, but there’s cloth on the shell. Like a shirt with five armholes. I don’t know what the shirt is made of, but it looks thicker than human clothing. It’s a dull greenish-brown, with uneven shading.

The top of the shirt has a large open hole. Like the neck of a human’s T-shirt. That hole is smaller than the shell. So he must put the shirt on by pulling it down and sliding the arms through the holes. Like a human’s shirt.

But there’s no neck or head to fit through that hole on top. Just a hard, rocky pentagon that sticks up a little from the rough skin.

On his side of the tunnel, there are handles and latticework on the walls. He casually holds onto a couple of bars with two of his hands. I guess when you have five hands, zero g isn’t that hard. Just use a hand or two to stay in place and use the other three to do stuff.

For me, the tunnel is kind of small. But for him, it’s absolutely spacious.

He waves to me with a free arm. He knows one human greeting and he plans to use it.

I wave back. He waves again. I shake my head. No more waving.

He pivots his “shoulders” to rotate his shell back and forth. He “shook his head” as much as he could. I wonder how we’re going to stop this game of “Eridian See, Eridian Do,” but he takes care of that for me.

He taps the clear wall three times with a finger, then keeps the finger pointing. Is he…pointing?

I follow the line and wow, there’s stuff in the tunnel with me! They left me a present!

I can be forgiven for not noticing. Seeing an alien kind of distracted me from the small collection of objects on the tunnel wall.

“All right,” I say. “Let’s see what you left me.”

“♩♫♪♪♫,” says Rocky.

My jaw drops. Yes, I’m in zero g. It still drops.

There was no clear way he said the sounds. Just notes. Like whale song. Except not really like whale song, because there were several at once. Whale chords, I guess. And he was responding to me. That means he can hear too.

And importantly, the sounds were in my hearing range. Some of the notes were low, some of them high. But definitely able to be heard. That alone is amazing when I think about it. He’s from a different planet, and a totally different family tree of life, but we ended up with sound ranges that work together.

On top of all that, he thought my noises deserved a response.

“You have a language!” I say. “How do you have a language?! You don’t have a mouth!”

“♫♫♩,” Rocky explains.

Thinking clearly, you can’t make spaceships without a civilization, and you can’t have a civilization without being able to talk to each other. So of course they have language. It’s interesting that they talk with sound, like humans do. Coincidence? Maybe not. Maybe that’s just the easiest way for that to happen through evolution.

“♪.” Rocky points to the objects they left me.

“Right, right,” I say. The whole language thing is way more interesting to me, and I’d rather look into that. But for now, Rocky wants to know what I think of his gifts.

I float over to the objects. They're attached to the wall with my own tape.

The objects are two spheres. Each has a raised image on it. One has the Hail Mary and the other has the Blip-A.

I pull the Hail Mary ball off the tape. It’s not warm. In fact, the tunnel isn’t warm anymore. Interesting. Maybe they saw I like things cooler and made it more comfortable for me.

There’s a rattle from inside the ball. I shake it and listen. More rattling.

I find a seam. I twist the top and bottom of the ball against each other and they turn. Left-handed screw, of course.

I look to Rocky to see if he approves. He has no face, so no expression. He just floats there, watching me. Well, not watching…no eyes. Actually, wait. How does he know what I'm doing? He clearly knows—he waved and stuff. He must have eyes somewhere. I probably just don’t see them.

I turn back to the sphere. I pull the two halves apart and inside is…a bunch more little spheres.

I sigh. This makes even more questions.

The little beads float out and drift across my view. They’re not separate. They’re connected by little strings. Like a complicated necklace. I spread it out as much as I can.

They look like—for lack of a better word—beaded handcuffs. Two circles of beads connected by a small thread. Each circle has eight beads on it. The connecting thread has none. This seems very planned. But I have no idea what it means.

Maybe the other ball—the one with the Blip-A picture on it—will tell me more. I let the handcuffs float and take the Blip-A ball off the wall. I shake it and hear a lot of rattling inside. I unscrew the two halves and another set of beads comes out.

Unlike the handcuffs, there’s only one ring in this thing. And it has seven beads, not eight. Also, it has three strings sticking out of the circle, each going to a single bead. It's like a necklace with decorations hanging off it.

There’s more inside. I shake the model and another necklace floats out. I look at it and it’s just like the one I just looked at. I keep shaking and more necklaces come out. They’re all the same. I gather them up and put them in my pockets.

“This reminds me of something…” I hit my head. “What does this remind me of…?”

Rocky taps his shell with a claw. I know he’s just copying me, but it feels like he’s saying, Think, dummy!

What would I tell my students now?

Why did I suddenly think of my students? I saw a picture of my classroom. A quick memory. I’m holding a model of a molecule and explaining—

“Molecules!” I grab the handcuffs and show them to Rocky. “These are molecules! You’re trying to tell me something about chemistry!”

“♫♪♫♫♪.”

But wait. These are strange molecules. They don’t make sense. I look at the handcuffs. Nothing makes a molecule like this. Eight atoms on one side, eight on the other, and connected by…what? Nothing? The string isn’t even coming off a bead. It’s just connecting the two circles.

“Atoms!” I say. “The beads are protons. So the circles of beads are atoms. And the little connectors are chemical bonds!”

“Okay, if that’s the case…” I hold up the handcuffs and count everything again. “Then this is two atoms, each with eight protons, connected to each other. Element number eight is oxygen. Two oxygens. O2! And it was in the Hail Mary ball.”

I hold it toward Rocky. “You clever fellow, this is my atmosphere!”

I grab the other set of beads. “So your atmosphere is…seven protons connected to three individual atoms with one proton each. A nitrogen attached to three hydrogens. Ammonia! Of course it’s ammonia! You breathe ammonia!”

That explains the strong smell on all of the little presents they left me. Remaining traces of their air.

My smile fades. “Yikes. You breathe ammonia?”

I count all the little ammonia necklaces they gave me. I only got one O2 molecule, but he gave me twenty-nine ammonias.

I think about it for a moment.

“Oh,” I say. “I get it. I see what you’re saying.”

I look to my alien counterpart. “You have twenty-nine times more atmosphere than I do.”

Wow. Two things I notice right away: First, Eridians live under immense pressure. Like—similar to being a thousand feet underwater on Earth. Secondly, xenonite is incredible. I’m not sure how thick that wall is—half an inch, maybe? Less? But it’s holding back about 28 times the pressure of Earth's atmosphere. And it’s just a big, flat panel with no extra support (the worst shape for holding pressure). Their whole ship is made of big flat panels. That material must be incredibly strong. No wonder I couldn’t bend or break the pieces they sent before.

Our environments are not remotely compatible. I’d die in seconds on his side of the tunnel. And I bet he wouldn’t survive in one twenty-ninth of his normal pressure with no ammonia.

Okay, that's fine. We have sound and we can use gestures. That’s a good way to start talking.

I pause to think about all this. It’s amazing. I have an alien friend, and we’re chatting! I’m so excited! But I’m also exhausted. I’m so tired I can barely think. I haven’t slept in two days. There’s always been some big event happening. I can’t stay awake forever. I need sleep.

I hold up one finger. The “wait a minute” sign. Hopefully he remembers it from last time. He holds up one of his fingers too.

I hurry back to the ship and down to the lab. There’s an analog clock on the wall. Because every lab needs one. It’s hard, but I get it off the wall and hold it. I also grab a dry-erase marker from the workstation.

I go back through the control room to the Tunnel of Aliens. Rocky is still there. He seems happier to see me. How do I know? I don’t. He just moved a bit and seems more alert.

I show him the clock. I turn the time-set dial on the back, to show him how the hands move. He makes a circle with his hand. He understands!

I set the clock to 12:00. Then I use the marker to draw a line from the center to the twelve and another from the center to the two. I’d rather sleep eight hours, but I don’t want to make Rocky wait too long. I’ll take a two-hour nap. “I’ll come back when the clock looks like this,” I say. As if he’d understand.

“♩♪♫.” He gestures. He reaches out with two hands and grabs…nothing. Then he pulls the nothing toward him.

“What?”

He taps the wall and points to the clock, then does it again. Does he want the clock closer to the wall?

I push the clock closer. This seems to excite him. He does the gesture faster. I move it further forward. The clock is almost touching the wall now. He does the gesture one more time, but slower this time.

Now, I have no idea what he wants. So I just push the clock against the wall. It’s touching now. He raises his hands and shakes them a little. Weird jazz hands. Is that good?

Okay, I hope he knows I’ll be back in two hours. I turn to leave but hear tap-tap-tap right away.

“Whaaat?” I say.

“♪♪♫♪,” he says, pointing to the clock. It moved a little away from the wall. He doesn’t like that.

“Um, okay,” I say. I pull a loop of tape off the wall, unloop it, and rip it in half. I use the two halves to tape the left and right sides of the clock to the clear wall.

Rocky gives me the jazz hands signal again. I think it means “yes” or “I like this.” Like nodding.

I turn to leave again, but tap-tap-tap!

I spin around once more. “Dude, I just want a darn nap!”

He holds up a finger, using my own sign language against me. Now I have to wait! I guess that’s fair. I hold up my finger to show I understand.

He opens a round door leading into his ship. It’s the right size for an Eridian—I would have a hard time squeezing through if I tried. He disappears inside, leaving the door open. I’d love to know what’s beyond the door, but I can’t see anything. It’s totally dark in there.

Hmm. Interesting. It is completely dark in his ship. That door probably leads to an airlock. But even an airlock would have some lights in it, wouldn’t it?

Rocky didn’t have any problem getting around. But I know he can see—he responds to my gestures. This makes my earlier idea about Eridian vision seem more likely: I think they see a different part of the light spectrum than humans do. Maybe they see only infrared or only ultraviolet. That airlock might be perfectly lit up as far as Rocky’s concerned, but I can’t see a thing. Likewise, my lights are completely useless to him.

I wonder if we see any of the same light. Maybe red (the color with the longest wavelength that humans can see) is “♪♫♩,” the shortest wavelength they can see. Or something. Might be worth checking. I should bring a rainbow of lights and find out if he can—oh, he’s back.

Rocky bounces into the tunnel and spider-walks along the rails to the dividing wall. He’s incredibly graceful. Either he’s very experienced in zero g, or Eridians are just really good at climbing around. They have five hands with opposable fingers, and he’s an interstellar traveler, so it’s probably a little bit of both.

With one hand, he holds up a device for me to see. It’s…I don’t know what it is.

It’s a cylinder (man, these people like cylinders), a foot long and maybe 6 inches wide. I can see that his grip squishes the casing a little bit. It’s made of a soft material, like foam rubber. The cylinder has five square windows in a row. Inside each window is a shape. I think they might be letters. But they’re not just ink on paper. They’re on a flat surface, but the symbols stick out about an eighth of an inch.

“Huh,” I say.

The symbol on the right spins away to be replaced by another. A couple of seconds later, it happens again. Then again.

“It’s a clock!” I say. “I showed you a clock, so you showed me a clock!”

I point to my clock, still taped to the wall, and then to his. He does the jazz hands with two of the hands he’s not using. I do jazz hands back.

I watch the Eridian clock for a while. Rocky holds it still for me to see. The symbols—numbers, probably—change in the right window. They’re on a rotor, like an old digital clock from home. After a bit, the rotor to the left of it changes one position. Aha!

It seems the right rotor changes every two seconds, or a little more. It shows six different symbols before repeating: “ℓ,” “I,” “V,” “λ,” “+,” and “V,” in that order. When it reaches “ℓ,” the next rotor to the left moves one step. After about a minute, that second rotor goes through all the symbols, and when it reaches “ℓ,” the third rotor from the right moves.

They seem to read left to right—like English. A nice coincidence, but not that strange. There are only four options: left to right, right to left, top to bottom, or bottom to top. So there was a 1 in 4 chance we’d be the same.

So his clock is easy for me to read. And it works like a car odometer. “ℓ” is clearly their 0. So “I” is 1, “V” is 2, “λ” is 3, “+” is 4, and “V” is 5. What about 6 through 9? They don’t exist. After “V” we go back to “ℓ.” Eridians use base six.

Of all the things I teach, number bases are the hardest for students to really get. There’s nothing special about the number 10. We have ten digits because we have ten fingers. It's simple. Rockies have three fingers per hand and only use two hands when counting (they keep the other three feet/hands on the ground to stay steady). So they have six fingers to work with.

“I like you, Rocky! You’re a genius!”

And he is! With this, Rocky showed me:

  • How Eridian numbers work (base six)
  • How Eridian numbers are written (ℓ, I, V, λ,+, V)
  • How Eridians read information (left to right)
  • How long an Eridian second is

I hold up a finger and hurry back into the ship for my stopwatch. I come back and time Rocky’s clock. I start the timer just as the third rotor changes. The right rotor keeps clicking over about every two seconds, and every six clicks, the next rotor moves one. This will take a while, but I want the count to be as correct as possible. It takes about a minute and a half for the third rotor to move just one click. I expect to be here for ten minutes or so. But I plan to watch the whole time.

Rocky gets bored. At least, I think that’s what’s happening. He starts fidgeting, then lets the clock float near the divider wall. Then he wanders around his side of the tunnel. I’m not sure if he’s doing anything special. He opens a door to his ship, starts to climb through, then stops. He seems to think about it, then changes his mind. He closes the door. He doesn’t want to leave while I’m still here. Maybe I’ll do or say something interesting.

“♪♪♩,” he says.

“I know, I know,” I say. I hold up a finger.

He holds up his finger, then goes back to slowly bouncing from wall to wall. Zero-g pacing.

Finally, the third rotor completes a full lap and I stop my timer. Total time: 511.0 seconds. I don’t have a calculator, and I’m too excited to go back to the ship to get one. I pull out a pen and do long division on the palm of my hand. One Eridian second is 2.366 Earth seconds.

I circle the answer on my palm and stare at it. I add a few exclamation points nearby because I think they fit.

I know it doesn’t seem like much, but this is a huge deal. Rocky and I are astronauts. If we’re going to talk, we’re going to talk science. And just like that, Rocky and I have set up a basic unit of time. Next up: length and mass!

No, actually. Next up—a nap. I’m so tired. I pull my clock off the wall, circle the “2” with my dry-erase marker—just to be as clear as possible, then tape it back in place. I wave. He waves back. Then I go back for a nap.

—

This is ridiculous. How can I expect to sleep? How could anyone in this situation? I’m still trying to understand what’s happening. There’s an alien out there.

And it’s killing me that I can’t find out what he knows about Astrophage. But you can’t talk about complex science with someone using only gestures. We need a shared language, even if it's basic.

I just need to keep doing what I’m doing. Work on science communication. The verbs and nouns of physics. It’s the one set of ideas we’re sure to share—physical laws are the same everywhere. And once we have enough words to actually talk about science, we’ll start talking about Astrophage.

And in “VVℓλI” Eridian seconds I’ll be talking to him again. How the heck can a guy sleep at a time like this? There’s no way I can just—

Chapter 11

My timer beeps. I set it for two hours. It just hit zero. I blink a few times. I’m floating in a fetal position in the control room. I didn’t even make it to the dormitory.

I am not rested at all. Every part of me yells at me to go back to sleep, but I told Rocky I’d be back in two hours and I don’t want him to think humans are untrustworthy.

I mean…we’re pretty untrustworthy, but I don’t want him to know that.

I trudge (can you trudge in zero g? I say yes) through the airlock. Rocky is there waiting for me in the tunnel. He’s been busy while I was gone. There’s all sorts of stuff in there now.

The Eridian clock is still ticking—now attached to one of the lattice poles. But more interesting to me is the box that’s been added to the dividing wall. It’s a 1-foot cube and it sticks out into my side of the tunnel. It’s made of the same clear xenonite as the rest of the wall.

On Rocky’s side, the box has a flat panel door with a solid xenonite border. Also, there’s a square hole with a perfectly fitted square pipe leading away.

There are some…controls?…on the pipe near the box. Buttons, maybe? A wire from the control box runs along the pipe, disappearing into the hull where the pipe does.

Meanwhile, on my side of the cube is a crank, about the same shape as my own airlock door’s crank. And that’s attached to a square panel like the one on Rocky’s side and—

“It’s an airlock!” I said. “You made an airlock in our airlock tunnel!”

Brilliant. Just brilliant. Rocky and I can both use it. He can control the air in that little room with the mystery pipe, which I guess goes to some pumps or something in the Blip-A. And those buttons are the controls. Just like that, we can move stuff back and forth.

I do jazz hands. He does them back.

Hmm. More square, flat panels. Who makes a square airlock? Especially for Eridian air pressure. Even the pipe for the mini-airlock is square. I know they can make round xenonite—the cylinders he sent me when we met were round. This tunnel is round.

Maybe I’m thinking too much about it. Xenonite is so strong you don’t need to shape it carefully for pressure. Flat panels are probably easier to make.

This is awesome. I hold up a finger—he does the same. I fly to the lab and grab a tape measure. He showed me a time unit, so I’ll show him a length unit. The tape measure is metric, thank God. Using base-6 Eridian seconds will be confusing enough. I don’t want to add imperial units—even if they’re what I'm used to.

Back in the tunnel, I hold up the tape measure. I pull it out a little, then let it retract. I do this a few times. He does jazz hands. I point to the “squarelock” (what else would I call it?) and he does jazz hands again.

I hope that means it doesn’t have 29 atmospheres of ammonia in it right now. I guess we’ll see….

I turn the crank and open my door. It opens toward me easily.

Nothing explodes. I don’t even smell ammonia. And it wasn’t a vacuum in there. I couldn’t have opened the door if it was. Rocky made it exactly like my atmosphere. That's nice of him.

I put the tape measure in the middle of the box and let it float. I close the door and turn the crank.

Rocky presses a button and I hear a soft fwump then a steady hiss. A foggy gas pours in from the pipe. Probably ammonia. The tape measure bounces inside—pushed around like a leaf. Soon, the hiss slows to a trickle.

And then I realize my mistake.

The tape measure is a strong, metal kind used on construction sites, with tough rubber grips. But Eridians like it hot. How hot? I don’t know exactly, but it’s hotter than the temperature at which the rubber on the tape measure melts.

The blob of liquid rubber moves on the tape measure, held to it by surface tension. Rocky opens his door and carefully picks up my bad gift by the metal. At least that’s still solid. I think it’s aluminum. It’s good to know Eridian air isn’t hot enough to melt that too.

As Rocky pulls the tape measure toward him, the rubber blob breaks off and floats to his side of the tube.

He pokes the rubber blob and it sticks to his claw. He shakes it off easily. The temperature clearly doesn’t bother him. It’s like a human shaking water off his hand.

In my air, rubber that hot would burn. It would give off nasty, toxic gases. But there’s no oxygen on Rocky’s side of the wall. So the rubber just…stays a liquid. It floats to the tunnel wall and sticks there.

I shrug at him. Maybe he’ll know that means “I’m sorry.”

He shrugs back a little. But he does it with all five shoulders. It looks strange, and I don’t know if he understood me.

He pulls the tape out a little, then lets it snap back. He’s surprised, even though he must have known it would happen. He lets it go completely and lets it spin. He grabs it and does it again. Then again.

And again.

“Yeah, it’s fun,” I say. “But look at the markings. Those are centimeters. CEN-TI-ME-TERS.”

The next time he pulls the tape out, I point to the tape. “Look!”

He just keeps pulling it out and back again. I don’t see any sign that he cares about what’s written there.

“Ugh!” I hold up a finger. I go back to the lab and get another tape measure. It’s a well-stocked lab, and every space mission needs backups. I come back to the tunnel.

Rocky is still playing with the tape measure. Now he’s really enjoying himself. He pulls the tape out as far as he can, about a meter, then lets go of both the tape and the tape measure. The snap back makes the tape measure spin wildly in front of him.

“♩♪♫♪!!!” he says. I’m pretty sure that was a happy squeal.

“Look. Look,” I say. “Rocky. Rocky! Hey!”

He finally stops playing with the makeshift toy.

I pull some tape out on my tape measure, then point to the markings. “Look! Here! See these?”

He pulls his own out to about the same length. I can see the markings on his are still there—they didn’t get burned off in the intense Eridian heat or anything. What’s the problem?

I point at the 1-centimeter line. “Look. One centimeter. This line. Here.” I tap the line again and again.

He holds the tape out with two hands and taps it with a third. He matches my speed, but he’s nowhere near the 1-centimeter mark.

“Here!” I tap the mark harder. “Are you blind?!”

I pause.

“Wait. Are you blind?”

Rocky taps the tape some more.

I’ve always thought he had eyes somewhere that I didn’t recognize. But what if he doesn’t have eyes at all?

The airlock of the Blip-A was dark, and Rocky didn’t have any problem with it. So I thought he saw in light frequencies I can’t see. But the tape measure has white tape with black markings on it. Any vision in any spectrum should be able to see black on white. Black is the absence of light, and white is all frequencies reflected equally.

Wait—this doesn’t make sense. He knows what I’m doing. He copies my gestures. If he doesn’t have vision, how can he read my clock? How can he read his own clock?

Hmm…his clock has thick numbers. About an eighth of an inch. And, thinking back, he did have some trouble with my clock. He needed me to tape it to the wall. When it floated an inch away he got upset. Just being near the wall wasn’t enough. The clock had to be touching it.

“Sound?” I say. “Do you ‘see’ with sound?”

It would make sense. Humans use electromagnetic waves to understand our three-dimensional world. So why couldn’t another species use sound waves? Same idea—and we even have it on Earth. Bats and dolphins use echolocation to “see” with sound. Maybe Eridians have that ability, but amplified. Unlike bats and dolphins, Eridians have passive sonar. They use surrounding sound waves to figure out their environment instead of making a specific noise to track prey.

Just a theory. But it makes sense with what I know.

That’s why his clock numbers are thick. Because his sonar can’t perceive things that are too thin. My clock was hard for him. He can’t “see” the ink, but the hands are solid objects. So he knew about them. But the whole thing is covered in plastic….

I slapped my forehead. “That’s why you needed the clock pressed against the wall. You needed the sound waves bouncing around inside it to get to you more easily. And the tape measure I just handed you is useless. You can’t see the ink at all!”

He plays with the tape measure some more.

I hold up a finger. He’s more focused on the tape measure toy, but he absently returns the gesture with one of his spare hands.

I fly back into the ship, through the control room, and into the lab. I grab a screwdriver and head farther down to the dormitory. I detach a storage panel from the floor. Simple aluminum sheet. Maybe one-sixteenth of an inch thick, with the edges rounded so we don’t cut ourselves. Strong, durable, and light. Perfect for space travel. I fly back to the tunnel.

Rocky has wrapped one end of the tape around one of his tunnel’s grab-handles and tied it in a somewhat clumsy knot. He hangs on to the dispenser with one hand and uses the other four to climb backward along the bars.

“Hey,” I say. I hold up my hand. “Hey!”

He stops playing with the tape measure for a moment. “♩♪♩?”

I hold up two fingers.

Rocky holds up two fingers.

“Yeah. Okay. We’re copying each other again.” I hold up one finger, then two, then back to one, and then three.

Rocky repeats the same order, just as I hoped.

Now I put the aluminum panel between my hand and Rocky. Behind the panel, I hold up two fingers, then one, then three, then five.

Rocky holds up two fingers, then one, then three. He uses a second hand to hold up two more fingers for a total of five.

“Wow!” I say.

One-sixteenth-inch aluminum will block almost all light. Some very high frequencies can get through, but those frequencies would also pass right through me. So he wouldn’t see my hands. But sound travels through metals easily.

That’s proof. He’s not using light to see. It has to be sound. To Rocky, that metal plate is like a glass window. Maybe it blurs the image a little, but not much. He probably knows what the Hail Mary ’s control room looks like. Why not? The hull is just more aluminum.

How did he see me in space? No air in space. So no sound.

Wait. No. That’s a dumb question. He’s not a caveman in space. He’s an advanced space traveler. He has technology. He probably has cameras and radar that turn data into something he can understand. Like my Petrovascope. I can’t see IR light, but it can and shows it to me on a monitor with light I can see.

The Blip-A control room probably has awesome Braille-like readouts. Well, I’m sure it’s way more advanced than that.

“Wow…” I stare at him. “Humans spent thousands of years looking at the stars and wondering what was out there. You guys never saw stars but still worked out space travel. You Eridians must be amazing. Scientific geniuses.”

The knot in the tape comes loose, snaps back, and hits Rocky’s hand. He shakes his hand in pain for a moment, then keeps messing with the tape measure.

“Yeah. You’re definitely a scientist.”

—

“All rise,” said the bailiff, “the United States District Court for the Western District of Washington is now in session. Judge Meredith Spencer is presiding.”

The entire courtroom stood as the judge sat down.

“Be seated,” the bailiff said. He gave the justice a folder. “Your Honor, today’s case is Intellectual Property Alliance v. Project Hail Mary.”

The judge nodded. “Plaintiff, are you ready for trial?”

The plaintiff’s table was full of well-dressed men and women. The oldest of them, a man in his sixties, stood to answer. “We are, Your Honor.”

“Defense, are you ready for trial?”

Stratt sat alone at the defense table, typing on her tablet.

The justice cleared her throat. “Defense?”

Stratt finished typing and stood. “I’m ready.”

Justice Spencer pointed to Stratt’s table. “Counselor, where is the rest of your team?”

“Just me,” she said. “And I’m not a counselor—I’m the defendant.”

“Ms. Stratt.” Spencer took off her glasses and glared. “The defendant in this case is a rather famous group of international scientists.”

“Led by me,” said Stratt. “I ask that you dismiss the case.”

“You can’t ask that yet, Ms. Stratt,” said Spencer. “Just tell me if you’re ready to start.”

“I’m ready,”

“All right. Plaintiff, you may begin your opening statement.”

The man stood. “Thank you, your Honor, ladies and gentlemen of the jury, my name is Theodore Canton, lawyer for the Intellectual Property Alliance in this case.

“During this trial, we will show that Project Hail Mary has gone too far in how it gets and uses digital data. They have a huge storage device with copies of every single piece of copyrighted software, as well as every single book and written work available digitally. They did all this without paying or getting permission from the correct copyright holders or intellectual property owners. Also, many of their technical designs break patents held by—”

“Your Honor,” Stratt interrupted. “Can I ask that you dismiss the case now?”

“Technically,” said the justice, “but it’s not standard—”

“I ask that you dismiss the case.”

“Your Honor!” Canton argued.

“On what basis, Ms. Stratt?” said the justice.

“Because I don’t have time for this nonsense,” she said. “We are building a ship to literally save our species. And we have very little time to finish it. It will have three astronauts—just three—to do experiments we can’t even imagine now. We need them to be ready for any possible study they think is necessary. So we are giving them everything. All of human knowledge, along with all software. Some of it is silly. They probably won’t need Minesweeper for Windows 3.1, and they probably don’t need a complete Sanskrit-to-English dictionary, but they’re going to have them.”

Canton shook his head. “Your Honor, my clients don’t deny the importance of the Hail Mary Project. The issue is the illegal use of copyrighted material and patented technology.”

Stratt shook her head. “It would take a crazy amount of time and effort to get permission from every company. So we’re not doing it.”

“I assure you, Ms. Stratt, you will follow the law,” said the justice.

“Only when I want to.” Stratt held up a sheet of paper. “According to this international agreement, I am personally protected from being charged with any crime anywhere on Earth. The United States Senate approved that agreement two months ago.”

She held up a second piece of paper. “And to make situations like this easier, I also have a pardon in advance from the president of the United States for any and all crimes I am accused of within U.S. territories.”

The bailiff took the papers and handed them to the justice.

“This…” said the justice, “this is exactly what you say it is.”

“I’m only here as a favor,” said Stratt. “I didn’t have to come at all. But since the software business, patent abusers, and everyone tied to creative rights joined together in one lawsuit, I thought it would be quickest to settle this right away.”

She grabbed her bag and put the tablet inside. “I’m leaving now.”

“Wait, Ms. Stratt,” said Justice Spencer. “This is still a court, and you will stay until this is over!”

“No, I won’t,” said Stratt.

The officer walked forward. “Ma’am. I’ll have to stop you if you don’t listen.”

“Who’s going to make me?” Stratt asked.

Five armed men in military uniforms came into the courtroom and stood around her. “Because I have the U.S. Army,” she said. “And it’s a really good army.”

—

I look through my software while eating a peanut-butter tortilla. I know that doesn’t sound good, but it is.

I’ve learned how to grip the lab chair with my legs so I don’t float off while using the laptop. Turns out I have a lot of laptops. At least six that I’ve found in the storage area so far. And they’re all connected to a shipwide Wi-Fi network. Useful.

If I remember right, I should have almost all the software somewhere on the ship. The hard part is finding the one I need. I wouldn’t even know its name. Luckily, one of the books in the digital library is a list of software. That helped.

Finally I find something that will work: “Tympanum Labs Waveform Analyzer.” There are many waveform-analysis programs in my library. This one just has the best reviews according to a 2017 computer magazine that reviewed waveform analyzers.

I install the software on one of the laptops. It’s easy to use and has many features. But I’m most interested in the Fourier transform. It’s the most basic tool in sound-wave analysis and probably the most important. The math behind it is complicated, but the result is this: if you run a sound wave through a Fourier transform, it will give you a list of the notes being played at the same time. So if I played a C-major chord and let this app listen, it would tell me there’s a C, an E, and a G. It’s incredibly useful.

No more acting. It’s time to learn Eridianese. Yes, I just made up that word. No, I don’t regret it. I’m doing many things for the first time in human history out here and a lot needs naming. Just be glad I don’t name things after myself.

I launch Microsoft Excel on another laptop and tape the two laptops together back to back. Yes, I could just run both programs on one laptop, but I don’t want to switch back and forth.

I fly up through the ship and back into the tunnel. Rocky isn’t there.

Hmph.

Rocky can’t spend all day waiting for me, but why isn’t there someone in the tunnel all the time? If my crewmates were still here, we would take turns watching. Ilyukhina would probably be camped out here nonstop and only leave to sleep.
