What if they are having different people in the tunnel? How do I know Rocky is just one person? I don’t know how to tell Eridians apart. Maybe I’ve been talking to six different people. That’s a disturbing thought.

No…that’s not it. I’m pretty sure Rocky is just Rocky. The ridges on his shell and rocky bumps on his hands are unique. I remember there’s a jagged bit sticking up out of one of his fingers…yeah. It’s the same guy.

If you looked at a rock for hours, and someone replaced it with a very similar, but slightly different rock, you would know.

Okay, so where is the rest of the crew? I’m alone because my crewmates didn’t make it. But Eridians have better space technology. Bigger ship, almost indestructible hull material. There has to be a crew in there.

Ah! I bet Rocky’s the captain! He puts himself at risk by talking to the scary alien. Everyone else stays back on the ship. That’s what Captain Kirk would do. So why not Captain Rocky?

Anyway, I have cool stuff I want to do and I’m impatient.

“Yo! Rocky!” I yell. “Come here!”

I listen for any sounds of movement. “Come on, man! Your entire way of sensing things is sound—I bet you can hear a pin drop a mile away! You know I’m calling you! Move your…whatever you sit on! I want to talk!”

I wait and wait, but no Rocky.

My guess is I’m pretty high priority to him. So whatever he’s doing must be really important. After all, he’s got a ship to deal with. He probably needs to eat and sleep. Well, he has to eat, anyway—all living things need energy somehow. I don’t know if Eridians sleep.

Come to think of it…sleep might not be such a bad idea. Out of the past forty-eight hours I’ve had a two-hour nap and nothing else. Rocky’s clock is still there, stuck between a grab bar and the wall. It’s ticking away as normal. It’s interesting that his clock only has five digits. By my math, it’ll roll over back to ℓℓℓℓℓ every five hours or so. Maybe that’s the length of an Eridian day?

Think about it later. Sleep first. I made a simple chart on my laptop to change Rocky's time to mine and back. I want to sleep eight hours. I type in the time on Rocky’s clock, which is IℓIVλ, and the chart tells me what the clock will say eight hours from now. The answer: Iλ+VVλ.

I rush back to the lab for Popsicle sticks and tape. Rocky can’t see ink, so I have to make do.

I tape the sticks to the wall to tell Rocky when I’ll be back: Iλ+VVλ. Luckily, the symbols are mostly straight lines, so my little project should be good enough for him.

It's strange that my return time has six digits, one more than Rocky’s clock. But he’ll figure it out. If Rocky said “I’ll be back at thirty-seven o’clock,” I’d know what he meant.

Before bed, I grab a small camera from the lab’s vacuum chamber. It’s a wireless camera that connects to a screen attached to the chamber. I tape the camera in the tunnel, aimed at the wall. I take the screen to my bunk.

Now I have a baby monitor in the tunnel. It has no sound, since the camera is for experiments, not talking. But it’s better than nothing.

I tuck the sheets and blankets tightly around the oval mattress. I squeeze in between the bedding, so I won’t float around while I sleep.

My big plans for talking to Rocky will have to wait. I’m a little annoyed, but not for long. I fall asleep fast.

Chapter 12

T ap-tap-tap.

The sound barely gets through to me. It’s so distant.

Tap-tap-tap.

I wake up from a sleep without dreams. “Huh?”

Tap-tap-tap.

“Breakfast,” I mumble.

The robot arms reach into a space and take out a wrapped meal. It’s like Christmas every morning here. I pull the lid off and steam rises up. There’s a breakfast burrito inside.

“Nice,” I say. “Coffee?”

“Preparing…”

I take a bite of the breakfast burrito. It’s good. All the food is good. I guess they figured if we’re going to die, we may as well eat good stuff.

“Coffee,” says the computer. A mechanical arm hands me a pouch with a straw. Like a Capri Sun for adults. Made for zero gravity.

I let the burrito float nearby and take a sip of coffee. It’s delicious, of course. It even has just the right amount of cream and sugar. That’s a very personal thing that everyone likes differently.

Tap-tap-tap.

What is that?

I check the LCD screen taped near my bunk. Rocky is in the tunnel tapping on the divider wall.

“Computer! How long was I asleep?”

“Patient was unconscious for ten hours and seventeen minutes.”

“Oh crud!”

I wriggle out of my bedding and bounce up through the ship toward the control room. I carry the burrito and coffee with me because I’m starving.

I bounce into the tunnel. “Sorry! Sorry!”

Rocky taps the divider louder than before, now that I’m here. He points to the Popsicle-stick numbers I taped to the divider and then to his clock. He makes a fist with one hand.

“I’m sorry!” I clasp my hands together as if praying. I don’t know what else to do. There’s no space symbol for begging. I don’t know if he understands, but he opens his fist.

Maybe it was a small scolding. I mean, he could have made five fists, but he only made one.

Anyway, I kept him waiting over two hours. He’s understandably upset. Hopefully this next trick will make up for it.

I hold up a finger. He does the same.

I grab my duct-taped laptops and start the sound-analysis program on one and Excel on the other. I press them against the tunnel wall and tape them there.

I pull the Popsicle-stick numbers off the divider wall. They’re as good a place to start as any. I hold up the “I” and point to it. “One,” I say. “One.”

I point to my mouth, then back to the Eridian number. “One.” Then I point to Rocky.

He points to the “I” and says “♪.”

I pause the waveform analyzer and scroll back a few seconds. “There we go…” Rocky’s word for “one” is just two notes played together. There are also many other tones and echoes in there, but the main sounds are just two notes.

I type “one” into the spreadsheet on the other computer and write down the frequencies.

“Okay…” I go back to the divider and hold up the “V” symbol. “Two,” I say.

“♪,” he says. Another one-syllable word. The oldest words in a language are usually the shortest.

This time, it’s a chord made of four different notes. I enter “two” and record the frequencies for that word.

He starts to get excited. I think he knows what I’m doing, and it makes him happy.

I hold up the “λ” and before I can even speak, he points to it and says, “♫♪.”

Great. Our first two-syllable word. I have to scroll back and forth a bit in the waveform data to get the chords right. The first syllable has two notes, and the second has five! Rocky can make at least five different notes at the same time. He must have multiple sets of vocal cords or something. Well, he has five arms and five hands. So why not five sets of vocal cords?

I don’t see a mouth anywhere. The notes are just coming from somewhere inside him. When I first heard him speak, I thought it sounded like whale song. That may have been more accurate than I thought. Whales sound like they do because they move air back and forth across their vocal cords without letting it out. Rocky may be doing the same thing.

Tap-tap-tap-tap!

“What?” I look back at him.

He points to the “λ” symbol still in my hand and then at me. Then back to the “λ” and back at me. He’s almost frantic.

“Oh, sorry,” I say. I hold the number up properly and say, “Three.”

He does jazz hands. I throw some jazz hands back.

Huh. Now that we’re talking about it…

I stand still for a moment so he’ll know there was a break in the conversation. Then I do jazz hands and say, “Yes.”

I repeat the gesture. “Yes.”

He does it back to me and says, “♫♩.”

I write down the frequencies in my laptop.

“Okay, we have ‘yes’ in our vocabulary now,” I say.

Tap-tap-tap.

I look over. Once he has my attention, he does jazz hands again and says, “♫♩.” Same chord as before.

“Yes,” I say. “We talked about this.”

He holds up a finger for a moment. Then he makes two fists and taps them together. “♪♪.”

…What?

“Ohhh,” I say. I’m a teacher. What would I teach someone who just learned ‘yes’?

“That’s ‘no.’ ”

At least I hope so.

I make fists and tap them together. “No.”

“♫♩,” he says. I check the laptop. He just said yes.

Wait. Does that mean it's not no? Is that another yes? Now I’m confused.

“No?” I ask.

“No,” he says in Eridian.

“So, ‘yes’?”

“No, yes.”

“Yes?”

“No. No.”

“Yes, yes?”

“No!” He makes a fist at me, clearly frustrated.

Enough of this back-and-forth routine. I hold up a finger.

He unclenches his fist and returns the gesture.

I type what I think is “no” into my spreadsheet. If I’m wrong, I’m wrong, and we'll figure it out later.

I hold up the “+” symbol. “Four.”

He holds up three fingers on one hand and one finger on another. “♩♩.”

I write down the frequencies.

—

Over the next several hours, we grow our shared vocabulary to several thousand words. Language is an accelerating system. The more words you know, the easier it is to explain new ones.

Talking is hard because my system for listening to Rocky is slow and awkward. I check the frequencies he makes with one laptop, then find them in my spreadsheet on the other laptop. It's not a good system. I’ve had enough.

I take an hour to write some software. I'm not a computer expert, but I know some basic programming. I write a program that takes the audio-analysis software's output and finds the words in my table. It's barely a program—more like a script. It's not efficient, but computers are fast.

Luckily, Rocky speaks with musical notes. It’s hard to make a computer turn human speech into text, but it’s easy to make a computer recognize musical notes and find them in a chart.

After that, my laptop screen shows me the English translation of what Rocky is saying as it happens. When a new word appears, I add it to my database, and the computer recognizes it from then on.

Rocky, however, doesn’t use anything to record what I’m saying or doing. No computer, no pen, no microphone. Nothing. He just listens carefully. And as far as I can tell, he remembers everything I told him. Every word. Even if I only told it to him once, hours ago. I wish my students paid attention that well!

I think Eridians have much better memories than humans.

In general, the human brain is like a bunch of software fixes put together into one unit that somehow works. Each "feature" was added as a random change that solved a specific problem to help us survive.

Basically, the human brain is a mess. Everything about evolution is messy. So, I assume Eridians are also a mix of random changes. But whatever made their brains the way they are, it gave them what we humans would call "photographic memory."

It's probably even more complex than that. Humans have a whole part of our brains for sight, and it even has its own memory storage. Maybe Eridians are just really good at remembering sounds. After all, it's their main sense.

I know it's too soon, but I can't wait. I get a vial of Astrophage from the lab supplies and take it to the tunnel. I hold it up. "Astrophage," I say.

Rocky's whole body changes. He lowers his shell a little. He grips the bars he uses to stay in place a bit tighter. "♫♪♫," he says, his voice quieter than usual.

I check the computer. It's not a word I've saved yet. It must be his word for Astrophage. I write it down in the database.

I point to the vial. “Astrophage on my star. Bad.”

“♫♩♪♫ ♫♪♫♩ ♫♪♫,” Rocky says.

The computer translates: Astrophage on my star. Bad bad bad.

Okay! Theory confirmed. He’s here for the same reason I am. I want to ask so many more questions. But we just don’t have the words. It’s so frustrating!

“♫♫ ♫♩♪♪♫ ♫♪♫,” Rocky says.

My computer shows the text: You come from where, question?

Rocky has learned the basic word order of English. I think he realized early on that I can’t easily remember things, so he uses my system instead of trying to teach me his. I probably seem pretty dumb, honestly. But some of his own grammar slips in now and then. He always ends a question with the word “question.”

“No understand,” I say.

“You star is what name, question?”

“Oh!” I say. He wants the name of my star. “Sol. My star is called ‘Sol.’ ”

“Understand. Eridian name for your star is_ ♫♪♫♪♩♩.”

I write down the new word. It’s Rocky’s word for “Sol.” Unlike two humans struggling to talk, Rocky and I can’t even say each other’s names right.

“My name for your star is ‘Eridani,’ ” I say. We really call it “40 Eridani,” but I want to keep it easy.

“Eridian name for my star is ♫♩♪♪♪.”

I add the word to the list. “Understand.”

“Good.”

I don’t need the computer to tell me that one. I’m starting to know some common words like “you,” “me,” “good,” “bad,” and so on. I’m not artistic and have a terrible ear for music. But after hearing a chord a lot, you start to remember it.

I check my watch—yes, I have a watch now. The stopwatch also tells time. It took me a while to notice. I was busy with other things.

We’ve been doing this all day and I’m tired. Do Eridians even sleep? I guess I’ll find out.

“Human bodies must sleep. Sleep is this.” I curl up and close my eyes to show what sleep is like. I fake a snore because I’m a bad actor.

I return to normal and point to his clock. “Humans sleep for twenty-nine thousand seconds.”

Besides having perfect memory, Eridians are extremely good at math. At least, Rocky is. As we worked with scientific units, it became clear that he could convert from his units to mine instantly. And he easily understands base ten.

“Many seconds…” he says. “Why be still so many seconds, question…Understand!”

He relaxes his limbs until they are limp. He curls up like a dead bug and stays still for a while. “Eridians same! ♪♫♫♪ !”

Thank goodness. I can’t imagine explaining “sleep” to someone who had never heard of it. Hey, I’m going to become unconscious and hallucinate for a while. Also, I spend a third of my time doing this. And if I can’t do it for a while, I go insane and eventually die. Don't worry.

I add his word for “sleep” to the dictionary.

I turn to leave. “I’m going to sleep now. I’ll come back in twenty-nine thousand seconds.”

“I observe,” he says.

“You observe?”

“I observe.”

“Uh…”

He wants to watch me sleep? That would be strange usually, but it's okay when you’re studying a new life-form, I guess.

“I will be still for twenty-nine thousand seconds,” I warn him. “A lot of seconds. I won’t do anything.”

“I observe. Wait.”

He goes back to his ship. Is he finally going to get something to write with? After a few minutes, he comes back with a device in one of his hands and a bag held in two more.

“I observe.”

I point to the device. “What is that?”

“♫♪♩♫.” He pulls some kind of tool out of the bag. “♫♪♩♫ not work.” He pokes the device with the tool a few times. “I change. ♫♪♩♫ work.”

I don’t bother to write down the new word. What would I call it? “Thing Rocky was holding that one time”? Whatever it is, it has a couple of wires sticking out and a hole that shows some complicated parts inside.

The object itself doesn't matter. The important thing is he’s fixing it. New word for us.

“Fix.” I say. “You fix.”

“♫♪♫♪,” he says.

I add “fix” to the dictionary. I think it’ll come up a lot.

He wants to watch me sleep. He knows it won’t be exciting, but he wants to anyway. So he brought work to keep busy.

Okay. Whatever makes him happy.

“Wait,” I say.

I go back to the ship and head to the dormitory.

I pull the mattress pad, sheets, and blanket from my bunk. I could use one of the other two bunks but…my dead friends were in them, so I don’t want to.

I bring the pad and sheets through the lab, awkwardly through the control room, and into the tunnel. I use a lot of duct tape to stick the mattress pad to the wall, then arrange the sheets and blanket.

“I sleep now,” I say.

“Sleep.”

I turn off the lights in the tunnel. It’s completely dark for me, but it doesn’t bother Rocky, who wants to watch me. It's the best of both worlds.

I wiggle into bed and try not to say good night. That would just cause more questions.

I fall asleep to the occasional clink and scrape of Rocky working on his device.

—

The next few days are the same, but not boring at all. We learn a lot more words and some grammar. Tenses, plurals, "what if" statements…language is complicated. But we’re learning it bit by bit.

Even though it’s slow, I’m learning more of his language. I don’t need the computer as much. But I still need it sometimes—it’ll take a long time to be able to do without it.

I spend an hour every day learning Eridian words. I made a simple program to pick words randomly from my spreadsheet and play the sounds with a music app. It’s a basic program, not written well, but computers are fast. I want to stop using the spreadsheet as soon as possible. I still need it all the time for now. But sometimes I understand a whole sentence without using the computer. Small steps.

Every night, I sleep in the tunnel. He watches. I don’t know why. We haven’t talked about it yet. We’ve been too busy with other things. But he really wants to watch me sleep. Even if I just want a short nap.

Today I want to tackle a very important science topic that’s been tricky for us. Mostly because we're in zero gravity.

“We need to talk about mass.”

“Yes. Kilogram.”

“Right. How do I explain a kilogram to you?” I ask.

Rocky pulls out a small ball from his bag. It’s about the size of a ping-pong ball. “I know mass of this ball. You measure. You tell me how many kilograms ball is. Then I know kilogram.”

He figured it out!

“Yes! Give me the ball.”

He grabs several support bars with different hands and puts it in the mini-airlock. After a few minutes of waiting for it to cool, I have it in my hands. It’s smooth and made of a metal. Seems pretty dense to me.

“How will I measure this?” I mutter.

“Twenty-six,” Rocky says suddenly.

“What about twenty-six?”

He points to the ball in my hand. “Ball is twenty-six.”

Oh, I get it. The ball weighs twenty-six of something. Whatever he's measuring in. Okay. All I have to do is figure out how heavy this ball is, divide by twenty-six, and tell him the answer.

“I understand. The ball weighs twenty-six.”

“No. Is not.”

I pause. “It doesn’t?”

“Is not. Ball is twenty-six.”

“I don’t understand.”

He thinks for a moment, then says, “Wait.”

He disappears into his ship.

While he’s gone, I wonder how to weigh something in zero g. It still has mass, of course. But I can’t just put it on a scale. There’s no gravity. And I can’t use the Hail Mary’s spinning gravity. The tunnel is connected to her nose.

I could make a small spinner. Big enough for the smallest lab scale I have. Spin it at a steady rate with the scale inside. Measure something I know the mass of and then measure the ball. I could figure out the ball's mass from the comparison of the two measurements.

But I’d have to build a steady spinner. How would I do that? I can spin something in the lab’s zero-g easily, but how do I spin it at a steady rate for multiple tries?

Oooh! I don’t need a steady rate! I just need a string with a mark in the middle!

I fly into the Hail Mary. Rocky will forgive me for taking off. He can probably “watch” me from wherever he is on his ship anyway.

I bring the ball to the lab. I get a piece of nylon string and tie each end to a plastic sample container. Now I have a string with little buckets at each end. I put the containers together and pull the folded string tight. I use a pen to mark the end point. That’s the exact middle of this thing.

I wave the ball back and forth to get a feel for its mass. Probably less than a pound. Less than half a kilogram.

I leave everything floating in the lab and kick my way to the dormitory.

“Water,” I say.

“Water requested,” says the computer. The metal arms give me a zero-g “sipper” of water. Just a plastic pouch with a straw that only lets water through if you open a clip. And inside is 1 liter of water. The arms always give me water a liter at a time. You have to stay hydrated if you want to save the world.

I return to the lab. I pour about half the water into a sample box and seal it. I put the half-empty sipper into one bucket and the metal ball into the other. I set the whole thing spinning in the air.

The two weights clearly aren’t equal. The uneven spin of the two connected containers shows the water side is much heavier. Good. That’s what I wanted.

I pluck it out of the air and take a sip of water. I start it spinning again. Still off-center, but not as bad.

I take more sips, do more spins, take more sips, and so on until my little device spins perfectly around the marked center point.

That means the weight of the water is equal to the weight of the ball.

I pull out the sippy. I know how dense water is—it’s 1 kilogram per liter. So all I need to know is how much water is here to know its weight and therefore the weight of the metal ball.

I get a large plastic syringe from the supplies. It can pull out 100 cc of liquid at most.

I attach the syringe to the sippy and unclip the straw. I draw out 100 cc of water, then squirt it into my “wastewater box.” I repeat this a few more times. The last syringe is only about a quarter full when I empty the bag.

Result: 325 ccs of water, which weighs 325 grams! Therefore, Rocky’s ball also weighs 325 grams.

I return to the tunnel to tell Rocky all about how smart I am.

He makes a fist at me as I enter. “You left! Bad!”

“I measured the mass! I did a very smart experiment.”

He holds up a string with beads on it. “Twenty-six.”

The beaded string is just like the ones he sent me when we talked about our atmospheres—

“Oh,” I say. It’s an atom. That’s how he talks about atoms. I count the beads. There are twenty-six in all.

He’s talking about element 26—one of the most common elements on Earth. “Iron,” I say. I point at the necklace. “Iron.”

He points at the necklace and says, “♫♩♪♫♫.” I write the word in my dictionary.

“Iron,” he says again, pointing at the necklace.

“Iron.”

He points to the ball in my hand. “Iron.”

It takes a second to sink in. Then I slap my forehead.

“You are bad.”

It was a fun experiment, but a total waste of time. Rocky was giving me all the information I needed. Or trying to, at least. I know how dense iron is, and I know how to figure out the volume of a sphere. Getting to mass from there is just a little math.

I pull a pair of calipers out of the toolkit I keep in the tunnel and measure the sphere’s diameter. It’s 4.3 centimeters. From that I figure out the volume, multiply by the density of iron, and get a much more exact mass of 328.25 grams.

“I was only off by one percent,” I grumble.

“You talk to you, question?”

“Yes! I’m talking to me.”

“Humans are unusual.”

“Yes,” I say.

Rocky stretches his legs. “I sleep now.”

“Wow,” I say. This is the first time he’s had to sleep since we met. Good. This will give me some time for lab work. But how much time?

“How long do Eridians sleep?”

“I not know.”

“You don’t know? You’re Eridian. How can you not know how long Eridians sleep?”

“Eridians not know how long sleep last. Maybe short time. Maybe long time.”

They sleep for random amounts of time. I guess there’s no rule that says sleep has to become regular. Does he at least know a possible range?

“Is there a shortest time? A longest time?”

“ Minimum is 12,265 seconds. Maximum is 42,928 seconds.”

Rocky often gives me oddly specific numbers for things that should be estimates. It took me a while to figure it out, but I finally did. He is giving me estimates. But they’re in his units and in base six. It’s easier for him to change those values to Earth seconds than to think in Earth seconds.

If I changed those values back to Eridian seconds and looked at the numbers in base six, I bet they’d be round numbers. But I’m too lazy. Why change data he already changed? He’s never been wrong about math.

Meanwhile, I have to divide by 60 twice on a calculator just to convert from one of my planet’s units to another. He’ll sleep for at least three and a half hours and at most almost twelve hours.

“I understand,” I say. I head back toward the airlock.

“You watch, ask questions?” Rocky asks.

He watched me sleep, so it’s only fair he offers to let me watch him. I’m sure Earth scientists would be thrilled to learn anything about what an Eridian sleeping looks like. But I finally have time to deeply analyze xenonite, and I’m just dying to know how xenon bonds with other elements. If I can get any of my lab equipment to work in zero g, that is.

“Not necessary.”

“You watch, ask questions?” he asks again.

“No.”

“Watch.”

“You want me to watch you sleep?”

“Yes. Want want want.”

Through unspoken agreement, a repeated word three times means great emphasis.

“Why?”

“I sleep better if you watch.”

“Why?”

He waves his arms, trying to find a way to say it. “Eridians do that.”

Eridians watch each other sleep. It’s a custom. I should be more understanding, but he made fun of me when I talked to myself. “Eridians are strange.”

“Watch. I sleep better.”

I don’t want to watch a dog-sized spider not move for hours. There’s a crew in there, right? Have one of them do it. I point to his ship. “Have another Eridian watch you.”

“No.”

“Why not?”

“I am the only Eridian here.”

My mouth hangs open. “You’re the only person on that huge ship?!”

He’s quiet for a moment, then says, “♫♩♪♫♩♪♫ ♫♪ ♩♪♫ ♫♪♫♪♩ ♫♪♩♪ ♫♩ ♪ ♫♩♪ ♫ ♩♪♫♩♪ ♫♩♪ ♫.”

Complete nonsense. Did my patched-together translation software break? I check it. No, it’s working fine. I look at the waveforms. They look like the ones I’d seen before. But they’re lower. Now that I think about it, that whole sentence sounded lower than anything Rocky has ever said before. I select the whole part in the software’s recording history and raise it an octave. The octave is a universal thing, not just for humans. It means doubling the frequency of every note.

The computer immediately translates it. “Original crew was twenty-three. Now it is only me.”

That octave-drop…I think it’s emotion.

“They…did they die?”

“Yes.”

I rub my eyes. Wow. The Blip-A had a crew of twenty-three. Rocky is the only one left, and he’s upset about it.

“Wh…er…” I stammer. “Bad.”

“Bad bad bad.”

I sigh. “I started with three crew members. Now it’s just me.” I put my hand up against the divider.

Rocky puts a claw on the divider opposite my hand. “Bad.”

“Bad bad bad,” I say.

We stay like that for a moment. “I’ll watch you sleep.”

“Good. Me sleep,” he says.

His arms relax, and he looks completely like a dead bug. He floats freely in his side of the tunnel, no longer holding on to any support bars.

“Well, you’re not alone anymore, buddy,” I say. “Neither of us are.”

Chapter 13

“Mr. Easton, I don’t think we need to be searched,” said Stratt.

“I think you do,” said the head prison guard. His thick New Zealand accent sounded friendly, but there was an edge to it. This man had built his career on not tolerating nonsense.

“We’re exempt from all—”

“Stop,” Easton said. “No one gets in or out of Pare without a full search.”

Auckland Prison, which the locals called “Pare” for some reason, was New Zealand’s only maximum-security prison. The only way in was covered with security cameras and a micro-scanner for all visitors. Even the guards went through the detector on their way in.

Easton’s assistant and I stood to the side while our bosses argued. He and I looked at each other and shrugged. A small group of underlings with stubborn bosses.

“I’m not turning over my Taser. I can call your prime minister if you like,” Stratt said.

“Sure,” said Easton. “She’ll tell you the same thing I’m about to tell you now: We don’t allow weapons anywhere near those animals in there. Even my own guards only have batons. There are some rules we don’t change. I’m fully aware of your power, but it has limits. You’re not magical.”

“Mr. E—”

“Torch!” Easton said, holding out his hand.

His assistant handed over a small flashlight. He clicked it on. “Please open your mouth wide, Ms. Stratt. I need to check for anything hidden.”

Whoa boy. I stepped forward before this got any worse. “I’ll go first!” I opened my mouth wide.

Easton shined the light into my mouth and looked this way and that. “You’re clear.”

Stratt just glared at him.

He held the flashlight at the ready. “I can get a female guard in here and order a much more detailed search if you like.”

For a few seconds, she did nothing. Then she pulled her Taser from its holster and handed it over.

She must have been tired. I’d never seen her give up on a power trip before. Though, I also hadn’t seen her get into a pointless argument before either. She had a lot of authority and wasn’t afraid to use it when needed, but she usually wasn’t one to argue when a simple solution was available.

Soon, guards escorted Stratt and me through the cold, gray walls of the prison.

“What the heck is wrong with you?” I said.

“I don’t like little dictators in their little kingdoms,” she said. “It drives me crazy.”

“You can give a little sometimes.”

“I’m out of patience, and the world is running out of time.”

I held up a finger. “No, no, no! You can’t just say ‘I’m saving the world’ every time you’re a jerk.”

She thought about it. “Yeah, okay. You might be right.”

We followed the guards down a long hallway to the Maximum Security Unit.

“Maximum security seems like too much,” she said.

“Seven people died,” I reminded her. “Because of him.”

“It was an accident.”

“It was criminal carelessness. He deserves what he got.”

The guards led us around a corner. We followed. The place was a maze.

“Why bring me here?”

“Science.”

“Of course.” I sighed. “I don’t like this.”

“Noted.”

We entered a bare room with only a metal table. A prisoner in an orange jumpsuit sat on one side. He was balding, maybe in his late forties or early fifties. He was handcuffed to the table. He didn’t look dangerous.

Stratt and I sat across from him. The guards closed the door.

The man looked at us. He tilted his head, waiting for someone to talk.

“Dr. Robert Redell,” Stratt said.

“Call me Bob,” he said.

“I’ll call you Dr. Redell.” She took a file from her briefcase and read it. “You’re serving a life sentence for seven counts of manslaughter.”

“That’s why they say I’m here, yes,” he said.

I spoke up. “Seven people died on your rig because you were careless. Seems like a good reason for you to be here.”

He shook his head. “Seven people died because the control room didn’t follow the rules and turned on a main pumping station while workers were still in the reflector tower. It was a terrible accident, but it was an accident.”

“Explain it to us,” I said. “If the deaths at your solar farm weren’t your fault, why are you here?”

“Because the government thinks I stole millions of dollars.”

“And why do they think that?” I asked.

“Because I stole millions of dollars.” He moved his chained wrists to a better position. “But that had nothing to do with the deaths. Nothing!”

“Tell me about your blackpanel power idea,” Stratt said.

“Blackpanel?” He frowned. “It was just an idea. I emailed it without my name.”

Stratt rolled her eyes. “Do you really think email sent from a prison computer lab is anonymous?”

He looked away. “I don’t know computers. I’m an engineer.”

“I want to hear more about blackpanel,” she said. “And if I like what I hear, it could shorten your jail time. So start talking.”

He perked up. “Well…I mean…okay. What do you know about solar thermal power?”

Stratt looked at me.

“Uh,” I said. “It’s when you have a lot of mirrors that reflect sunlight to the top of a tower. If you get a few hundred square meters of mirror focusing all that sunlight onto a single point, you can heat up water, boil it, and run a turbine.”

I turned to Stratt. “But that’s not new. There’s a working solar thermal power plant in Spain right now. If you want to know about it, talk to them.”

She silenced me with a hand motion. “And that’s what you were making for New Zealand?”

“Well,” he said. “New Zealand paid for it. But the idea was to power Africa.”

“Why would New Zealand pay a lot of money to help Africa?” I asked.

“Because we’re nice,” Redell said.

“Wow,” I said. “I know New Zealand is pretty cool but—”

“And it was going to be a New Zealand company making money from the power,” Redell said.

“There it is.”

He leaned forward. “Africa needs roads, power, and things like that. To do that, they need power. And they have nine million square kilometers of land no one uses that gets sunlight all day long. The Sahara Desert is just there , ready to give them everything. All we had to do was build the power plants!”

He flopped back in his chair. “But every local government wanted money. Bribes, payoffs, you name it. You think I stole a lot? That’s nothing compared to the bribes I had to pay just to build a solar plant in the middle of nowhere.”

“And then?” Stratt said.

He looked at his shoes. “We built a small plant—one square kilometer of mirrors. All of it focused on a big metal drum full of water on top of a tower. Boil the water, run a turbine, you know how it works. I had a crew checking the drum for leaks. When anyone’s in the tower, the mirrors are supposed to be pointed away. But someone in the control room turned on the whole system when they thought they were starting a fake test.”

He sighed. “Seven people. All dead right away. At least they didn’t suffer. Much. Someone had to pay. The people who died were all New Zealanders, and so am I. So the government came after me. It was a joke of a trial.”

“And the stealing?” I said.

He nodded. “Yeah, that came up in the trial too. But I would have gotten away with it if the project had worked. I’m not to blame here. I mean, yeah, stealing money, okay, I’m guilty of that. But I didn’t kill those people. Not by being careless or anything else.”

“Where were you when the accident happened?” Stratt said.

He paused.

“Where were you?” she repeated.

“I was in Monaco. On a vacation.”

“You’d been there for three months on that vacation. Gambling away your stolen money.”

“I…have a gambling problem,” he said. “I admit that. I mean, gambling debt made me steal in the first place. It’s a sickness.”

“And what if you had been doing your job instead of going on a spree for three months? What if you’d been there the day the accident happened? Would the accident still have happened?”

His expression was answer enough.

“Okay,” Stratt said. “Now we’re past the excuses and lies. You’re not going to convince me you’re an innocent victim. And now you know that. So let’s move on: Tell me about blackpanels.”

“Yeah, okay.” He calmed down. “I’ve worked in energy my whole life, so Astrophage is really interesting to me. A storage method like that—man, if it weren’t hurting the sun, it would be the best thing to ever happen to us.”

He moved in his seat. “Nuclear reactors, coal plants, solar thermal plants…they all do the same thing: Heat water to make steam, then use the steam to power a turbine. But with Astrophage, we don’t need any of that. It turns heat directly into stored energy. And it doesn’t need much heat. Just anything above 96.415 degrees.”

“We know that,” I said. “I’ve been using heat from a nuclear reactor to grow Astrophage for months.”

“How much did you get? A few grams? My idea can get you a thousand kilograms a day. In a few years, you’ll have enough for the whole Hail Mary mission. It’ll take you longer to build the ship anyway.”

“Okay, I’m listening,” I said. Of course, Stratt hadn’t told me anything about this "blackpanel" thing.

“Get a square of metal foil. Any metal will work. Anodize it until it’s black. Don’t paint it—anodize it. Put clear glass over it with a one-centimeter gap. Seal the edges with brick, foam, or something that insulates well. Then put it in the sun.”

“Okay, what will that do?”

“The black foil will absorb sunlight and get hot. The glass will keep the heat in—heat can only escape through the glass, which is slow. It’ll get to a temperature over one hundred degrees Celsius.”

I nod. “And at that temperature, you can grow Astrophage.”

“Yes.”

“But it would be ridiculously slow,” I said. “If you had a one-square-meter box and perfect weather…say, one thousand watts of sunlight per square meter…”

“It’s about half a microgram per day,” he said. “More or less.”

“That’s nowhere near ‘a thousand kilograms’ per day.”

He smiled. “It just depends on how many square meters you make.”

“You’d need two trillion square meters to get a thousand kilograms per day.”

“The Sahara Desert is nine trillion square meters.”

My jaw dropped.

“That was quick,” said Stratt. “Explain.”

“Well,” I said. “He wants to cover part of the Sahara Desert with black panels. Like…a quarter of the whole Sahara Desert!”

“It’d be the biggest thing humans ever made,” he said. “You could easily see it from space.”

I glared at him. “And it would destroy the environment of Africa and probably Europe.”

“Not as much as the coming ice age will.”

Stratt held up her hand. “Dr. Grace. Would it work?”

I fidgeted. “Well, I mean…it seems like a good idea. But I don’t know if we can actually do it. This isn’t like building a building or a road. We’re talking about literally trillions of these things.”

Redell leaned in. “That’s why I designed the blackpanels to be made entirely out of foil, glass, and ceramics. All materials we have plenty of here on Earth.”

“Wait,” I said. “How do the Astrophage reproduce in this situation? Your blackpanels will help them, sure, and they’ll be ready to reproduce. But there are a lot of steps they need to take when they reproduce.”

“Oh, I know.” He smirked. “We’ll have a static magnet in there to give them a magnetic field to follow—they need that to start their migration. Then we’ll have a small IR filter on one part of the glass. It’ll only let the CO2 IR light through. The Astrophage will go there to reproduce. Then, after dividing, they’ll head toward the glass because that’s where the sun is. We’ll have a small pinhole somewhere in the side of the panel for air to get in and out. It’ll be slow enough that it doesn’t cool down the panel, but fast enough to replace the CO2 used by Astrophage while reproducing.”

I opened my mouth to protest, but I couldn’t find anything wrong with it. He’d thought it all through.

“Well?” said Stratt.

“As a reproduction system it’s horrible,” I said. “Way less efficient and far lower yield than my system on the carrier’s reactor. But he didn’t design it to be efficient. He designed it to be easily expanded.”

“That’s right,” he said. He pointed to Stratt. “I hear you have total power over almost the whole world right now.”

“That’s an overstatement,” she said.

“Not by much, though,” I said.

Redell went on. “Can you get China to focus their factories on making blackpanels? Not just them but nearly every industrial country on Earth? That’s what it would take.”

She pressed her lips together. After a moment, she said, “Yes.”

“And can you tell the damn crooked government leaders in North Africa to stay out of the way?”

“That part will be simple,” she said. “When this is all done, those governments will keep the blackpanels. They’ll be the industrial-energy superpower of the world.”

“See, there we go,” he said. “Save the world and permanently raise Africa out of poverty while we’re at it. Of course, this is all just a theory. I have to develop the blackpanel and make sure we can make a lot of them. I’d need to be in a lab instead of prison.”

Stratt thought it over. Then she stood.

“Okay. You’re with us.”

He pumped his fist.

—

I wake up in my bed, which is stuck to the tunnel wall. The first night was a mess with duct tape. After that, I learned that epoxy glue works well on xenonite, so I could add a couple of anchor points and put the mattress up right.

I sleep in the tunnel every night now. Rocky wants me to. And, about every eighty-six hours, Rocky sleeps in the tunnel and wants me to watch. Well, he’s only slept three times so far, so I don't know much about when he's awake. But he’s been pretty regular about it.

I stretch my arms and yawn.

“Good Morning,” Rocky says.

It’s completely dark. I turn on the lamp next to the bed.

Rocky has a whole workshop on his side of the tunnel. He’s always changing or fixing something. It seems like his ship always needs work. Right now, he's holding a long metal thing with two of his hands and using two more to poke at the insides with thin tools. His last hand holds a handle on the wall.

“Mornin’,” I say. “I’m going to eat. I’ll be back.”

Rocky waves without really paying attention. “Eat.”

I float down to the dorm for my morning routine. I eat a prepackaged breakfast (scrambled eggs with pork sausage) and drink a bag of hot coffee.

It’s been a few days since I last cleaned up, and I can smell myself. Not good. So I wash myself at the sponge-bath station and get clean coveralls. With all this technology, I haven’t seen a way to clean clothes. So I soak them in water and put them in the lab freezer for a while. It kills the germs, and those cause the smell. Fresh, but not really clean, clothes.

I put on the coveralls. I’ve decided today is the day. After a week of improving our language skills, Rocky and I are ready to have real conversations. I can even understand him without looking at the translation about a third of the time now.

I float back to the tunnel, finishing my coffee.

Okay. Finally I think we have the right words for this. Here goes.

I clear my throat. “Rocky. I am here because Astrophage makes Sol sick but doesn’t make Tau Ceti sick. Are you here for the same reason?”

Rocky puts the device and his tools on his chest strap and climbs along the support rails to the divider. Good. He understands this is serious.

“Yes. No understand why Tau not sick but Eridani is sick. If Astrophage no leave Eridani, my people die.”

“Same!” I say. “Same same same! If Astrophage keeps infecting Sol, all the humans will die.”

“Good. Same. You and I will save Eridani and Sol.”

“Yes yes yes!”

“Why did other humans on your ship die, question?” Rocky asks.

Oh. So we’re going to talk about that?

I rub the back of my head. “We, uh…we slept all the way here. Not a normal sleep. A special sleep. A dangerous sleep, but we had to. My crewmates died, but I didn’t. Just luck.”

“Bad,” he says.

“Bad. Why did the other Eridians die?”

“I don’t know. Everyone got sick. Then everyone died.” His voice shakes. “I’m not sick. I don’t know why.”

“Bad,” I say with a sigh. “What kind of sick?”

He thinks for a moment. “I need a word. Small life. One thing. Like Astrophage. Eridian body made of many many of these.”

“Cell,” I say. “My body has many cells too.”

He says the Eridian word for “cell,” and I add the sounds to my growing dictionary.

“Cell,” he says. “My crew have trouble with cells. Many cells die. Not infection. Not hurt. No reason. But not me. Never me. Why, I wonder? I don't know.”

Each cell in the sick Eridians died? That sounds awful. It also sounds like radiation sickness. How can I explain that? I shouldn’t have to. If they travel in space, they should know about radiation. We don’t have a word for it yet. Let’s fix that.

“I need a word: hydrogen atoms moving very fast. Very, very fast.”

“Hot gas.”

“No. Faster than that. Very, very, very fast.”

He wiggles his shell. He’s confused.

I try something else. “Space has very, very, very fast hydrogen atoms. They move almost as fast as light. Stars made them a long, long, long time ago.”

“No. Space has no mass. Space is empty.”

Oh boy. “No, that’s wrong. There are hydrogen atoms in space. Very, very fast hydrogen atoms.”

“Understood.”

“You didn’t know that?”

“No.”

I stare in shock.

How can a civilization develop space travel without ever discovering radiation?

—

“Dr. Grace,” she said.

“Dr. Lokken,” I said.

We sat across from each other at a small steel table. It was a tiny room, but big for an aircraft carrier. I wasn’t sure what it was for, and its name was in Chinese. But I thought it was where the navigator looked at maps…?

“Thank you for seeing me,” she said.

“No problem.”

We usually avoided each other. We went from “annoyed” to “very annoyed.” I was as much to blame as she was. We started badly in Geneva and never got better.

“I don’t think this is needed.”

“Me neither,” I said. “But Stratt said you had to show me this. So, here we are.”

“I have an idea. I want your opinion.” She took out a file and gave it to me. “CERN will release this paper next week. This is a draft. But I know people there, so they let me see it early.”

I opened the folder. “Okay, what’s it about?”

“They learned how Astrophage stores energy.”

“Really?!” I gasped. Then I cleared my throat. “Really?”

“Yes, and frankly it’s amazing.” She pointed to a graph on the first page. “Basically, it’s neutrinos.”

“Neutrinos?” I shook my head. “How the heck…”

“I know. It doesn’t make a lot of sense. But there’s a big burst of neutrinos every time they kill an Astrophage. They even took samples to the IceCube Neutrino Observatory and poked them in the main detector pool. They got a ton of hits. Astrophage can only hold neutrinos if it’s alive, and there are a lot inside.”

“How does it make neutrinos?”

She flipped a few pages in the paper and pointed to another chart. “This is more your thing than mine, but microbiologists have confirmed Astrophage has a lot of free hydrogen ions—just raw protons with no electron—zooming around just inside the cell membrane.”

“Yeah, I remember reading about that. It was a group in Russia that figured that out.”

She nodded. “CERN is pretty sure that, somehow, when those protons crash into each other really fast, their energy turns into two neutrinos that go in opposite directions.”

I leaned back, confused. “That is really strange. Mass doesn't usually just 'happen' like that.”

She wiggled her hand. “Not exactly. Sometimes gamma rays, when they get close to an atom's nucleus, will suddenly become an electron and a positron. It’s called ‘pair production.’ So it happens. But we’ve never seen neutrinos made that way.”

“That’s kind of cool. I never got too into atomic physics. I’ve never heard of pair production before.”

“It’s a thing.”

“Okay.”

“Anyway,” she said, “there’s a lot of complex stuff about neutrinos I won’t get into—there are different kinds and they can even change from one kind to another. But the main point is this: They’re an extremely tiny particle. Their mass is like one twenty-billionth the mass of a proton.”

“Waaaaaait,” I said. “We know Astrophage is always 96.415 degrees Celsius. Temperature is just how fast the particles inside are moving. So we should be able to calcu—”

“Calculate how fast the particles inside are moving,” she said. “Yes. We know how fast the protons move on average. And we know their mass, which means we know their energy. I know what you’re thinking and the answer is yes. It matches up.”

“Wow!” I put my hand on my forehead. “That’s amazing!”

“Yes. It is.”

That was the answer to the question we'd been asking for so long: Why is Astrophage’s critical temperature what it is? Why not hotter? Why not colder?

Astrophage makes neutrinos in pairs by smashing protons together. For the reaction to work, the protons need to hit each other with more energy than the energy of two neutrinos. If you start with the mass of a neutrino and work backward, you know how fast those protons have to hit each other. And when you know how fast the particles in an object are moving, you know its temperature. To have enough energy to make neutrinos, the protons have to be 96.415 degrees Celsius.

“Oh man,” I said. “So any heat energy above the critical temperature will just make the protons hit harder.”

“Yes. They’ll make neutrinos and have extra energy. Then they bump into other protons, and so on. Any heat energy above the critical temperature quickly turns into neutrinos. But if it drops below critical temperature, the protons are too slow, and neutrino production stops. The result: You can’t get it hotter than 96.415 degrees. Not for long, anyway. And if it gets too cold, the Astrophage uses stored energy to heat back up to that temperature—just like any other warm-blooded animal.”

She gave me a moment to let that all sink in. CERN really delivered. But a couple of things still bothered me.

“Okay, so it makes neutrinos,” I said. “How does it turn them back into energy?”

“That’s the easy part,” she said. “Neutrinos are what we call Majorana particles. This means a neutrino is its own antiparticle. Basically, every time two neutrinos collide, it’s a matter-antimatter interaction. They cancel each other out and become photons. Two photons, actually, with the same wavelength and going opposite directions. And since a photon’s wavelength is based on its energy…”

“The Petrova wavelength!” I exclaimed.

She nodded. “Yes. A neutrino’s mass energy is exactly the same as the energy in one photon of Petrova-wavelength light. This paper is truly groundbreaking.”

I rested my chin on my hands. “Wow…just wow. I guess the only question left is how does an Astrophage keep neutrinos inside?”

“We don’t know. Neutrinos routinely pass through the entire planet Earth without hitting a single atom—they’re just that small. Well, it’s more about quantum wavelengths and the chances of collision. But basically, neutrinos are famously hard to interact with. But for some reason, Astrophage has what we call ‘super cross-sectionality.’ That’s just a fancy term meaning nothing can quantum-tunnel through it. It goes against every law of particle physics we thought we knew, but it’s been proven again and again.”

“Yeah.” I tapped my finger on the table. “It absorbs all wavelengths of light—even wavelengths that should be too large to interact with it.”

“Yes,” she said. “It also collides with all matter that tries to get by, no matter how unlikely that collision should be. Anyway, as long as an Astrophage is alive, it shows this super cross-sectionality. And that leads nicely to what I wanted to talk to you about.”

“Oh?” I said. “There’s more?”

“Yes.” She took a drawing of the Hail Mary hull from her bag. “This is what I need you for: I’m trying to protect the Hail Mary from radiation.”

I perked up. “Of course! Astrophage will block it all!”

“Maybe,” she said. “But I need to know how space radiation works to be sure. I know the basics, but not the specifics. Please explain it to me.”

I folded my arms. “Well, there’s two types, really. High-energy particles from the sun, and GCRs that are just always around.”

“Start with the solar particles,” she said.

“Sure. Solar particles are just hydrogen atoms from the sun. Sometimes a magnetic storm on the sun can make it shoot out a lot of them. Other times it’s calmer. And lately, the Astrophage infection has been taking so much energy from the sun that magnetic storms are less common.”

“Horrifying,” she said.

“I know. Did you hear that global warming has been almost reversed?”

She nodded. “Humanity’s carelessness with our environment accidentally gave us an extra month by pre-heating the planet.”

“We fell in poop and came out smelling like roses,” I said.

She laughed. “I haven’t heard that one. We don’t have that saying in Norwegian.”

“You do now.” I smiled.

She looked down at the ship’s design—a little faster than I thought was needed, but oh well.

“How fast do these solar particles travel?” she asked.

“About four hundred kilometers per second.”

“Good. We can ignore them.” She scribbled a note on the paper. “The Hail Mary will be moving away faster than that within eight hours. They won’t be able to catch up, much less do any damage.”

I whistled. “It’s really amazing what we’re doing. I mean…jeez. Astrophage would be the best thing ever if it weren’t, you know, destroying the sun.”

“I know,” she said. “Now, tell me about GCRs.”

“Those are harder,” I said. “It means—”

“Galactic cosmic rays,” she said. “But they're not really cosmic rays, are they?”

“Right. They’re just hydrogen ions—protons. But they’re going much faster. Almost the speed of light.”

“Why call them cosmic rays if they aren't electromagnetic at all?”

“People used to think they were. The name stayed.”

“Do they come from one place?”

“No, they come from all directions. Supernovas make them, and they've happened everywhere. We're constantly surrounded by GCRs from all directions. They're a big problem for space travel. But not anymore!”

I leaned in to see her drawing again. It showed a cut-away of a ship's side. There was a tiny gap between two layers. “Will you fill that space with Astrophage?”

“That’s the idea.”

I studied the drawing. “You want fuel in the hull? Isn’t that risky?”

“Only if it sees CO2 light. Without CO2, it’s harmless. And it’ll be dark between the walls. Dimitri wants to mix Astrophage with thin oil to move it to the engines easily. I want to cover the hull with that mix.”

I pinched my chin. “It could work. But Astrophage can be killed by physical damage. You can kill one by poking it with a sharp nanostick.”

“Yes, that’s why I asked CERN to do some secret experiments for me as a favor.”

“Wow. CERN will just do whatever you want? Are you, like, a tiny Stratt or something?”

She chuckled. “Old friends and contacts. Anyway, they found that even particles moving close to light speed can’t get past Astrophage. And none of them seem to kill it either.”

“That actually makes a lot of sense,” I said. “It evolved to live on the surface of stars. They must get hit by energy and very fast-moving particles all the time.”

She pointed to a close-up picture of Astrophage canals. “The entire radiation load will be stopped. All we need is a layer of Astrophage liquid thick enough to make sure there’s always an Astrophage cell in the way of any incoming particles. One millimeter should be more than enough. Plus, we don’t waste any mass. We’ll be using the fuel itself as insulation. And if the crew needs that last little bit of Astrophage, well, consider it a backup.”

“Hmm…a ‘backup’ that could power New York City for twenty thousand years.”

She looked at the diagram, then back to me. “You did all that math in your head?”

“Eh, I had some shortcuts. We’re dealing with such huge amounts of energy here, I tend to think in ‘New York City years’ of energy, which is about one-half of one gram of Astrophage.”

She rubbed her temples. “And we need to make two million kilograms of it. If we make a mistake along the way…”

“We’ll save Astrophage the trouble of destroying humanity by destroying it ourselves,” I say. “Yeah. I think about that a lot.”

“So, what do you think?” she said. “Is this a terrible idea, or could it work?”

“I think it’s brilliant.”

She smiled and looked away.

Chapter 14

Another day, another staff meeting. Who knew saving the world could be so dull?

The science team sat around the meeting-room table. Me, Dimitri, and Lokken. Despite her talk about cutting out red tape, Stratt ended up with a bunch of unofficial department heads and daily staff meetings.

Sometimes, the things we all hate are the only way to get things done.

Stratt sat at the head of the table, of course. And next to her was a man I’d never seen before.

“Everyone,” Stratt said. “I want you to meet Dr. François Leclerc.”

The Frenchman to her left waved weakly. “Hello.”

“Leclerc is a world-famous climate scientist from Paris. I’ve put him in charge of watching, understanding, and—if possible—improving the climate effects of Astrophage.”

“Oh, is that all?” I said.

Leclerc smiled, but it disappeared fast.

“So, Dr. Leclerc,” Stratt said. “We’ve been getting a lot of conflicting reports on what to expect from the decrease in solar energy. It’s hard to find any two climate scientists who agree.”

He shrugged. “It’s hard to find two climate scientists who agree on anything. It is, sadly, not a precise science. There’s a lot we don't know and—if I’m being honest—a lot of guessing. Climate science is still very new.”

“You’re being too modest. Of all the experts, you’re the only one I could find whose climate predictions have been proven right again and again for the last twenty years.”

He nodded.

She pointed to a messy pile of papers on the table. “I’ve been sent every possible prediction, from small crop failures to the collapse of the world’s ecosystems. I want to hear what you think. You’ve seen the predicted solar-output numbers. What do you make of it?”

“Disaster, of course,” he said. “We’re looking at the extinction of many species, complete disruption of ecosystems everywhere, major changes in weather—”

“Humans,” Stratt said. “I want to know how this affects humans, and when. I don’t care about where some strange animal mates or any other random ecosystem.”

“We’re part of the environment, Ms. Stratt. We’re not separate from it. The plants we eat, the animals we raise, the air we breathe—it’s all connected. As the ecosystems collapse, it’ll directly affect humanity.”

“Okay, then: numbers,” Stratt said. “I want numbers. Real things, not vague guesses.”

He frowned at her. “Okay. Nineteen years.”

“Nineteen years?”

“You wanted a number,” he said. “Here’s a number. Nineteen years.”

“Okay, what does nineteen years mean?”

“That’s my guess for when half the people alive now will be dead. Nineteen years from now.”

The silence after that was like nothing I’d ever felt. Even Stratt was surprised. Lokken and I looked at each other. I don’t know why, but we did. Dimitri’s mouth dropped open.

“Half?” Stratt said. “Three point five billion people? Dead?”

“Yes,” he said. “Is that real enough for you?”

“How can you possibly know that?” she said.

He frowned. “And just like that, another person who doesn't believe in climate change is born. See how easy it is? I just have to tell you something you don’t want to hear.”

“Don’t talk down to me, Dr. Leclerc. Just answer my questions.”

He crossed his arms. “We’re already seeing big changes in the weather.”

Lokken cleared her throat. “I heard there were tornadoes in Europe?”

“Yes,” he said. “And they’re happening more and more. European languages didn’t even have a word for tornado until Spanish conquerors saw them in North America. Now they’re happening in Italy, Spain, and Greece.”

He tilted his head. “Partly, it’s because the weather is changing. And partly it’s because some crazy person decided to cover the Sahara Desert with black rectangles. As if messing up how heat moves near the Mediterranean Sea wouldn’t cause problems.”

Stratt rolled her eyes. “I knew the weather would be affected. We just don’t have a choice.”

He kept talking. “Ignoring what you did to the Sahara, we’re seeing strange things all over the world. The hurricane season is two months late. It snowed in Vietnam last week. The jet stream is a complicated mess that changes every day. Cold air from the Arctic is going to places it’s never been, and hot air from the tropics is going far north and south. It’s a chaotic mess.”

“Get back to the three and a half billion dead people,” Stratt said.

“Sure,” he said. “Figuring out famine is actually pretty simple. Take all the calories the world makes from farming each day, and divide by about fifteen hundred. The human population can’t be bigger than that number. Not for long, anyway.”

He played with a pen on the table. “I’ve used my best models. Crops are going to fail. The world’s main crops are wheat, barley, millet, potatoes, soy, and most important: rice. They all need specific temperatures. If your rice field freezes, the rice dies. If your potato farm floods, the potatoes die. And if your wheat farm gets too much humidity, it gets fungus and dies.”

He looked at Stratt again. “If only we had a steady supply of three-anused mud sloths, maybe we’d survive.”

Stratt pinched her chin. “Nineteen years isn’t enough. It’ll take thirteen years for the Hail Mary to reach Tau Ceti, and another thirteen for any results to return. We need at least twenty-six years. Twenty-seven would be better.”

He looked at her like she was crazy. “What are you saying? This isn’t optional. It’s happening. And we can’t stop it.”

“Nonsense,” she said. “Humans have been accidentally causing global warming for a century. Let’s see what we can do when we really try.”

He recoiled. “What? Are you serious?”

“A good layer of greenhouse gases would buy us time, right? It would insulate Earth like a coat and make the energy we have last longer. Am I wrong?”

“Wha—” he stammered. “You aren’t wrong, but the size…and the morality of purposefully causing greenhouse gases…”

“I don’t care about morality,” Stratt said.

“She really doesn’t,” I said.

“I care about saving humanity. So get me some greenhouse effect. You’re a climatologist. Think of something to make us last at least twenty-seven years. I’m not willing to lose half of humanity.”

Leclerc gulped.

She waved her hand dismissively. “Get to work!”

—

It takes three hours and adding fifty words to our shared vocabulary, but I can finally explain radiation—and how it affects living things—to Rocky.

“Thank,” he says, sounding lower than usual. Sad. “Now I know how my friends died.”

“Bad bad bad,” I say.

“Yes,” he answers.

During our talk, I learned the Blip-A has no protection from radiation at all. And I know why Eridians never found out about radiation. It took some time to put all this together, but here’s what I know:

The Eridian home planet is the first planet in the 40 Eridani system. Humans actually saw it a while back, but didn't know there was a whole civilization there. It’s listed as “40 Eridani A b.” That’s hard to say. The planet’s real name, from the Eridians, is a group of musical notes like any other Eridian word. So I’ll just call it “Erid.”

Erid is very close to its star—about one-fifth as far as Earth is from our sun. Their “year” is a little over forty-two Earth days long.

It’s what we call a “super-Earth,” weighing eight and a half times what Earth does. It’s about twice Earth’s diameter, and its surface gravity is a little over twice as strong. Also, it spins very fast. Ridiculously fast. Their day is only 5.1 hours long.

That’s when things started to make sense.

Planets get magnetic fields if the conditions are right. You need a molten-iron core, you need to be in the magnetic field of a star, and you need to be spinning. If all three are true, you get a magnetic field. Earth has one—that’s why compasses work.

Erid has all of those things in overdrive. They are bigger than Earth, with a bigger iron core. They are close to their star, so they have a much stronger magnetic field powering their own, and they spin extremely fast. In total, Erid’s magnetic field is at least twenty-five times as strong as Earth’s.

Plus, their atmosphere is extremely thick. Twenty-nine times as thick.

You know what strong magnetic fields and thick atmospheres are really good at? Blocking radiation.

All life on Earth evolved to handle radiation. Our DNA has error-correction built in because we’re constantly hit with radiation from the sun and from space. Our magnetic field and atmosphere protect us somewhat, but not completely.

For Erid, it’s complete. Radiation just doesn’t reach the ground. Light doesn’t even reach the ground—that’s why they never evolved eyes. The surface is totally dark. How can life exist in total darkness? I haven’t asked Rocky how that works yet, but there is plenty of life deep in Earth’s oceans where the sun doesn’t shine. So it’s definitely possible.

Eridians are extremely sensitive to radiation, and they never even knew it existed.

The next conversation took another hour and added a few dozen more words to the vocabulary.

Eridians invented space travel a long time ago. And with their amazing material (xenonite) they even built a space elevator. It's basically a cable from Erid’s equator up to where it stays in the same spot in orbit, with a weight on the end. They actually take elevators to get to space. We could do that on Earth if we knew how to make xenonite.

But, they never left orbit. They didn't need to. Erid has no moon. Planets that close to a star usually don't. Gravity tends to pull any potential moons apart. Rocky and his crew were the first Eridians to ever leave orbit.

So they never realized that Erid’s magnetic field, which goes far beyond where they orbit, had been protecting them all along.

One question was still unanswered.

“Why didn’t I die, question?” Rocky asks.

“I don’t know,” I say. “What’s different about you? What did you do that the others didn’t?”

“I fix things. I repair things that are broken, make things that are needed, and keep engines working.”

Sounds like an engineer to me. “Where were you most of the time?”

“I have a room on the ship. A workshop.”

I’m starting to understand. “Where is the workshop?”

“In back of ship near engines.”

That’s a smart place for your ship’s engineer. Near the engines, where things will probably need fixing.

“Where do you keep the Astrophage fuel?”

He gestures vaguely toward the ship's rear. “Lots of Astrophage containers. All in back. Close to engines. Easy to refuel.”

That’s it.

I sigh. He won’t like this. The answer was so obvious. They just didn't know. They didn’t even know the problem until it was too late.

“Astrophage blocks radiation,” I say. “You were around Astrophage most of the time. Your crew wasn’t. So they got the radiation.”

He doesn’t answer. He needs to think about it.

“Understand,” he says quietly. “Thank. I now know why I didn't die.”

I try to picture his people’s desperation. Their space program was way behind Earth’s, they didn't know what was out there, and they were still building an interstellar ship to save their species.

No different from my situation, I guess. I just have a little more technology.

“Radiation is here too,” I say. “Stay in your workshop as much as you can.”

“Yes.”

“Bring Astrophage to this tunnel and put it on the wall.”

“Yes. You do same.”

“I don’t need to.”

“Why not, question?”

Because it doesn’t matter if I get cancer. I’m going to die here anyway. But I don’t want to explain that I’m on a suicide mission right now. The conversation’s been pretty heavy already. So I’ll tell him a half-truth.

“Earth’s atmosphere is thin and our magnetic field is weak. Radiation reaches the surface. So Earth life learned to survive radiation.”

“Understand,” he says.

He keeps fixing things while I float in the tunnel. A random thought pops into my head. “Hey, I have a question.”

“Ask.”

“Why are Eridian science and human science so alike? Billions of years apart, but almost the same progress.”

It’s been bothering me for a while. Humans and Eridians evolved separately in different star systems. We had no contact until now. So why do we have almost the same technology? I mean, Eridians are a little behind us in space tech, but not by much. Why aren’t they in the stone age? Or some super-advanced age that makes Earth look old?

“Has to be, or you and I would not meet,” Rocky says. “If planet has less science, it can’t make spaceship. If planet has more science it can understand and destroy Astrophage without leaving their system. Eridian and human science both in special range: Can make ship, but can’t solve Astrophage problem.”

Huh. I hadn’t thought of that. But it’s clear now that Rocky says it. If this happened when Earth was in the Stone Age, we would have just died. And if it happened a thousand years from now, we’d probably figure out how to deal with Astrophage without any trouble. There’s a pretty small range of technology that would make a species send a ship to Tau Ceti for answers. Eridians and humans both fit into that range.

“I get it. Good point.” But it still bothers me. “Still weird. Humans and Eridians are close in space. Earth and Erid are only sixteen light-years apart. The galaxy is one hundred thousand light-years wide! Life must be rare. But we’re so close together.”

“Possible we are family.”

We’re related? How could—

“Oh! You mean…wow!” I need to think about this.

“Not sure. Guess.”

“That’s a great guess!” I say.

The panspermia theory. Lokken and I argued about it all the time.

Earth life and Astrophage are too alike to be random. I thought Earth was “seeded” by an Astrophage ancestor. Some spacefaring first life form that infected my planet. But it never hit me until now that the same might have happened to Erid.

Life could be everywhere! Anywhere it can grow from an Astrophage-like ancestor into the cells I have now. I don’t know what this “pre-Astrophage” thing would be, but Astrophage is super tough. So any planet that can support life would likely get it.

Rocky might be a distant relative. Very distant. The trees back home are closer relatives than Rocky. But still.

Wow.

“Great guess!” I say again.

“Thanks,” Rocky says. He must have figured that out a while ago. But I still had to think about it.

For once, an aircraft carrier was the perfect place to be.

The Chinese Navy didn’t even question Stratt’s orders anymore. The higher-ups got tired of approving every action and finally just issued a general order to do whatever she said as long as it didn’t involve firing weapons.

We anchored off the coast of western Antarctica in the dead of night. The coastline was far in the distance, visible only by moonlight. The entire continent had been evacuated of humans. Probably an overreaction—the Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station was 1,500 kilometers away. The people there would have been just fine. Still, no reason to take chances.

It was the largest naval exclusion zone in history. So big, even the U.S. Navy had to spread itself thin to make sure no commercial ships entered the area.

Stratt spoke into a walkie-talkie. “Destroyer One, confirm observation status.”

“Ready,” came an American accent.

“Destroyer Two, confirm observation status.”

“Ready,” came a different American’s voice.

The scientific team stood together on the carrier’s flight deck, staring toward land. Dimitri and Lokken hung back away from the edge. Redell was off in Africa running the blackpanel farm.

And of course, Stratt was standing a bit in front of everyone else.

Leclerc looked like he was being led to his death. “We’re almost ready,” he said with a sigh.

Stratt clicked her walkie-talkie again. “Submarine One, confirm you can see us.”

“Ready,” came the answer.

Leclerc checked his tablet. “Three minutes…get ready.”

“All ships: We are at Condition Yellow,” Stratt said into her radio. “Repeat: Condition Yellow. Submarine Two, confirm you can see us.”

“Ready.”

I stood next to Leclerc. “This is unbelievable,” I said.

He shook his head. “I wish this wasn’t my responsibility.” He played with his tablet. “You know, Dr. Grace, I’ve always been a hippie. From my childhood in Lyon to my university days in Paris. I’m a tree-hugging antiwar reminder of old protests.”

I didn’t say anything. He was having a terrible day. If I could help by just listening, I would.

“I became a climatologist to help save the world. To stop the terrible environmental disaster we were creating. And now…this. It’s necessary, but awful. As a scientist, I’m sure you understand.”

“Not really,” I said. “I spent my whole career looking away from Earth, not at it. I don’t know much about climate science.”

“Mm,” he said. “Western Antarctica is a churning mass of ice and snow. This whole area is a giant glacier, slowly moving to the sea. There are hundreds of thousands of square kilometers of ice here.”

“And we’re going to melt it?”

“The sea will melt it for us, but yes. The thing is, Antarctica used to be a jungle. For millions of years it was as green as Africa. But continental drift and natural climate change froze it over. All those plants died and rotted. The gases from that rotting—mostly methane—got trapped in the ice.”

“And methane’s a very strong greenhouse gas,” I said.

He nodded. “Much stronger than carbon dioxide.”

He checked his tablet again. “Two minutes!” he called out.

“All ships: Condition Red,” Stratt radioed. “Repeat: Condition Red.”

He turned back to me. “So here I am. Environmental activist. Climatologist. Antiwar campaigner.” He looked out to sea. “And I’m ordering a nuclear strike on Antarctica. Two hundred and forty-one nuclear weapons, from the United States, buried fifty meters deep along a crack every three kilometers. All going off at the same time.”

I nodded slowly.

“They say the radiation will be small,” he said.

“Yeah. If it helps, they’re fusion bombs.” I pulled my jacket tighter. “There’s a little fission reaction with uranium that starts the bigger fusion reaction. And the big explosion is just hydrogen and helium. No radiation from that.”

“Well, that’s good.”

“And this was the only way?” I asked. “Why can’t we have factories make lots of sulfur hexafluoride, or some other greenhouse gas?”

He shook his head. “We’d need thousands of times more than we could ever make. Remember, it took us a hundred years of burning coal and oil everywhere to even see it was changing the climate at all.”

He checked his tablet. “The ice will break off at the explosions and slowly go into the sea and melt. Sea levels will rise about a centimeter in the next month, the ocean temperature will drop a degree—which is a disaster, but never mind that now. Lots of methane will go into the air. And now, methane is our friend. Our best friend. And not just because it’ll keep us warm for a while.”

“Oh?”

“Methane breaks down in the air after ten years. We can knock pieces of Antarctica into the sea every few years to control the methane levels. And if Hail Mary finds a solution, we just have to wait ten years for the methane to go away. You can’t do that with carbon dioxide.”

Stratt came over to us. “Time?”

“Sixty seconds,” he said.

She nodded.

“So this fixes everything?” I asked. “Can we just keep digging in Antarctica for more methane to keep Earth’s temperature right?”

“No,” he said. “It’s a temporary fix at best. Putting this stuff into our atmosphere will keep the warmth in the air, but the damage to our ecosystem will still be huge. We’ll still have terrible and unpredictable weather, crop failures, and biomes destroyed. But maybe, just maybe, it won’t be quite as bad as it would have been without the methane.”

I looked at Stratt and Leclerc standing next to each other. Never in human history had so much power been given to so few people. These two people—just these two—were going to literally change the world.

“I’m curious,” I say to Stratt. “Once we launch Hail Mary, what will you do then?”

“Me?” she said. “It doesn’t matter. Once the Hail Mary launches, my power ends. I’ll probably be put on trial by a bunch of angry governments for using too much power. Might spend the rest of my life in jail.”

“I’ll be in the cell next to you,” said Leclerc.

“Are you at all worried about that?”

She shrugged. “We all have to give up something. If I have to be the world’s punching bag to save us, then that’s what I’ll do.”

“You think in odd ways,” I said.

“Not really. When it's either death or survival for everyone, things are simple. No tough choices about right and wrong, no worrying about who benefits most. Just one goal: make this work.”

“That’s what I tell myself,” Leclerc said. “Three…two…one…detonation.”

Nothing happened. The coast stayed the same. No explosion. No flash. Not even a pop.

He looked at his tablet. “The nukes went off. The shockwave should be here in about ten minutes. It’ll just sound like distant thunder, though.”

He looked down at the carrier deck.

Stratt put her hand on his shoulder. “You did what you had to do. We’re all doing what we have to do.”

He buried his face in his hands and cried.

Rocky and I talk about biology for hours. We're both really interested in how the other's body works. We wouldn't be good scientists if we weren't.

Eridian bodies are, honestly, amazing.

Erid is so close to its star that the amount of energy going into its living world is unbelievable. And Eridians, being at the top of the food chain, have much more energy to use than human bodies do. How much more? They have sacs in their body that just hold ATP—the main way DNA-based life stores energy. Usually, it's kept in cells, but they have so much they had to develop better ways to store it.

We're talking about crazy amounts of energy here. They take oxygen from minerals to get metals. Eridians are basically living smelters.

Humans have hair, fingernails, tooth enamel, and other "dead" stuff on our bodies that do important things. Eridians take that idea to the extreme. Rocky's shell is made of oxidized minerals. His bones are like honeycombs of metallic alloys. His blood is mostly liquid mercury. Even his nerves are inorganic silicates that send light-based signals.

In total, Rocky only has a few kilograms of biological material. Single-celled organisms travel through his blood, building up or fixing the body as needed. They also handle digestion and take care of the brain, which is safely in the center of his shell.

If bees evolved to make hives that could walk, and the queen was as smart as a human, that life-form would be like an Eridian. Except the Eridian’s "bees" are single-celled organisms.

Eridian muscles are inorganic. They're made of porous, sponge-like material sealed in flexible sacs. Most of the body's water is in those sacs. And the air pressure is so high that the 210°C water is still liquid.

They have two separate blood systems: the "ambient" system and the "hot" system. The ambient blood is 210 degrees Celsius. But the hot blood is kept at 305 degrees, which is hot enough to boil water even at Erid's air pressure. Both blood systems have blood vessels that expand or contract around the muscles as needed to set their temperature. Want to expand? Make it hot. Want to contract? Make it cold.

In short: Eridians are steam-powered.

Because of this, the body's circulation acts like a heat sink when muscles cool down. It always needs to be cooled back to normal temperature, which is why there's a radiator. Rocky "breathes" only to pass ammonia outside through capillaries in a radiator-like organ on his carapace. Five slits on top let air in and out, but it never enters his bloodstream.

Eridians don’t "breathe," but they use oxygen. They're more self-contained than humans, with plant-like and animal-like cells inside. Oxygen turns into CO2, CO2 back to oxygen, always balanced. Rocky’s body is a small biosphere needing only energy from food and airflow to release heat.

The hot blood is too hot for any biological material to survive; it boils the water inside. This sterilizes incoming food, killing pathogens.

For worker cells to service the hot-blood system, it must cool to normal levels. When that happens, the Eridian can’t use muscles, which is why they sleep.

They don’t "sleep" like humans; they’re truly paralyzed. The brain, also being maintained, has no conscious function. A sleeping Eridian can’t wake up.

That's why they watch over each other while sleeping. Someone must keep you safe. This likely began in caveman (cave-Eridian?) times and is now a social norm.

As amazed as I am, Rocky finds it boring. He’s utterly shocked and amazed by humanity.

“You hear light, question?” Rocky says, his voice wavering when surprised or impressed.

“Yes. I hear light.”

As we talk, he assembles a complicated piece of equipment, almost as big as he is. I recognize parts he's been repairing. He can talk and work on delicate machinery at once. Eridians are better at multitasking than humans.

“How, question?” he asks. “How can you hear light, question?”

I point to my eyes. “These are special parts of my body that focus and detect light. They send the information to my brain.”
