“Light gives you information, question? Enough information to understand room, question?”

“Yes. Light gives information to humans like sound gives information to Eridians.”

A thought occurs to him. He stops working on his device completely. “You hear light from space, question? You hear stars, planets, asteroids, question?”

“Yes.”

“Amaze. What about sound, question? You can hear sound.”

I point to my ears. “I hear sound with these. How do you hear sound?”

He gestures all over his shell and arms. “Everywhere. Tiny sensors on outer shell. All report back to brain. Like touch.”

So his whole body is a microphone. His brain must be doing some serious work. It has to know the exact position of his body, sense the time difference between sound hitting different parts of it…man, that’s interesting. But hey, my brain gives me a complete 3-D picture of my surroundings just from two eyeballs. Sensory input is really impressive for everyone.

“I can’t hear as well as you,” I say. “Without light, I can’t understand the room. I can hear you talk, but that’s it.”

He points to the divider. “This is wall.”

“This is a special wall. Light goes through this wall.”

“Amazing. I gave you many choices for wall when first build. You choose this because light goes through, right?”

It seems like so long ago—back when the divider was a mosaic of hexagons of different textures and colors. I’d picked the clear one, of course.

“Yes. I chose this because light goes through.”

“Amazing. I gave choices for different ♫♩♪♫ of sound. Never thought of light.”

I glance at the laptop to check what that mystery word was. I almost never have to look at the laptop now. Still, once in a while there’s a chord I just don’t remember. The computer says that word was “qualities.” Okay, I can’t blame myself for not knowing it. That one doesn’t come up very often.

“Just good luck,” I say.

“Good luck,” he agrees. He makes a few more adjustments to the device, puts his tools back in his bandolier, then says, “I am done.”

“What is it?”

“Device keeps me alive in small room.” He looks happy. I think. He’s holding his shell just a little higher than usual. “Wait.”

He disappears back into his ship, leaving the device behind. He returns with several sheets of clear xenonite. Each sheet is a pentagon about a centimeter thick and a foot across. I hate myself for thinking in mixed units like that. But that’s what my brain thought of.

“I make room now,” he says.

He puts the pentagons together edge to edge, using some kind of thick liquid glue from a tube to hold them together. Soon, he has two halves of a dodecahedron put together. He holds them toward me proudly and places them together . “Room.”

The “room” is a geodesic sphere made of pentagons. The total diameter is about a meter. Easily big enough to hold Rocky.

“What’s the purpose of that room?” I ask.

“Room and device keep me alive in you ship.”

I raise my eyebrows. “You’re coming into my ship?”

“Want to see human technology. Is allowed, question?”

“Yes! Okay! What do you want to see?”

“Everything! Human science better than Eridian science.” He points to the laptop floating beside me. “Machine that thinks. Eridians don’t have that.” He points to my toolkit. “Many machines there Eridians don’t have.”

“Yes. Come look at anything you want!” I point to the small airlock drawer in the divider wall. “How will you get it through that?”

“You leave tunnel. I make new divider wall. Bigger airlock.”

He pulls the finished device—which I now realize is a life-support system—onto his back and straps it on. It covers the radiator slits at the top of his shell.

“Is that blocking your radiator? Isn’t that dangerous?”

“No. This makes hot air into cold air,” he says.

Air conditioning. Not what I think of when I see a species that lives comfortably at over 200 degrees Celsius. But we all have our limits.

He seals the globe around himself with glue. “I test.”

He just floats there for a minute. Then, he says, “Works! Good!”

“Great!” I say. “But how does it work? Where does the heat go?”

“Easy,” he says. He taps a small part of the device. “Astrophage here. Astrophage take all heat hotter than ninety-six degrees.”

Right. Astrophage is hot to us, but cold to Eridians. It's perfect for air-conditioning. Rocky just has to run the air over Astrophage-filled cooling fins or something.

“Clever,” I say.

“Thank. You leave now. I make large airlock for tunnel.”

“Yes yes yes!” I say.

I gather all my stuff in the tunnel, even the mattress on the wall, and shove it into the control room. Then I go into the control room and seal both airlock doors.

I spend the next hour cleaning up. I wasn’t expecting anyone.

Chapter 15

It’s been a few hours. But I have to know. How does he change the tunnel?

He needs huge air pressure to live. My ship can’t take that. And he can’t be in a vacuum. So how does he make changes?

I hear clinks and clanks from the other side of the airlock. This time I’ll find out!

I enter the airlock and look through the window. The Blip-A’s hull robot has taken off the old tunnel and is putting on a new one.

Oh. Well. That’s a letdown.

The old tunnel floats off into space—its use is over, it seems. The robot puts the new tunnel in place and uses xenonite glue along the edge of the Blip-A’s hull.

How did Eridians fly a ship that went near the speed of light without computers? Guessed their way? They’re good at doing math in their heads. Maybe they never had to invent computers. But still. No matter how good they are at math, there are limits.

The clunking stops. I peek out the window again. The tunnel is fully installed.

It looks like the old tunnel, but it has a much bigger airlock part. Almost the whole divider wall is a cabinet big enough to hold Rocky with room left. But it’s not big enough to hold me. I guess I won’t be visiting the Blip-A anytime soon.

“Hmph,” I say. I try not to let it bother me, but come on. He gets to see an alien spaceship. Why don’t I get to see one?

Rocky’s side of the tunnel no longer has the network of gripping bars. Instead, there is a metal strip running along the length of the tunnel. It goes into the divider airlock and further onto my side of the tunnel. It leads right up to my airlock door.

Across from the metal strip is something that looks like a pipe. It’s made of the same dull alien browns and tans as the tunnel wall. And it’s square. It also runs the length of the tunnel.

With a whoosh , Rocky’s side of the tunnel fills with fog. Then a second whoosh fills my side. That’s what the pipe was for, I guess. Sending the right air to both sides. I’m glad Rocky has oxygen to work with.

The Blip-A door opens, and Rocky comes out, inside his geodesic ball. He’s wearing something like overalls with a bandolier across the bottom of his shell. The AC unit is on his back. Two of his hands hold metal blocks. The other three are free. One of them waves to me. I wave back.

The spaceball (what else should I call it?) floats into the airlock and then sticks to the metal plate.

“What?” I say. “How…”

Then I see it. The ball didn’t magically move. Those blocks Rocky is holding are magnets. Pretty strong ones, I guess. And the metal strip is magnetic. Probably iron. He rolls the ball along the metal line and into the divider airlock. He moves metal controls through the alien shell with his magnets. It’s amazing to watch.

After some hissing and the sound of pumps, he pushes a plate away, which opens the door on my side of the airlock. From there, he rolls along the metal line to my door. I open it.

“Hello!”

“Hello!”

“So…do I carry you around? Is that the plan?”

“Yes. Carry. Thank.”

I carefully grab the ball, worried it might be hot. But it isn’t. Among other things, xenonite is a great insulator. I pull him through and into the ship.

Rocky is heavy. Much heavier than I thought he would be. If there were gravity, I probably couldn’t lift him at all. As it is, he has a lot of inertia. It takes a lot of effort to pull him along. It’s like pushing a motorcycle that won't start. Seriously—he’s as heavy as a motorcycle.

I shouldn’t be surprised. He told me all about his biology and how it uses metals. Heck, his blood is mercury. Of course he’s heavy.

“You are very heavy,” I say. I hope he doesn’t think I mean, “Hey, fatty! Go on a diet!”

“My mass is one hundred sixty-eight kilograms,” he says.

Rocky weighs over 300 pounds!

“Wow,” I say. “You weigh a lot more than me.”

“What is your weight, question?”

“Maybe eighty kilograms.”

“Humans have very little weight!” he says.

“I’m mostly water,” I say. “Anyway. This is the control room. I operate the ship from here.”

“I understand.”

I push him ahead of me down the tunnel to the lab. He skitters around within his ball. He tends to shift around when he’s looking at something new. I think it helps him get a better “view” of things with his sonar. Kind of like a dog tilting its head to get more information about a sound.

“This is my lab,” I say. “All the science happens here.”

“Good good good room!” he squeals. His voice is a full octave higher than normal. “Want to understand everything!”

“I’ll answer any questions you have,” I say.

“Later. More rooms!”

“More rooms!” I say dramatically.

I push him into the dormitory. I move us slowly so he can see everything from the middle of the room. “I sleep here. Well, I used to. Then you made me sleep in the tunnel.”

“You sleep alone, really?”

“Yes.”

“I also sleep alone a lot. Very sad.”

He just doesn’t get it. Being afraid to sleep alone is probably natural for him. Interesting…that might have started their pack behavior. And a pack behavior is needed for a species to become smart. That strange (to me) sleep habit could be why I’m talking to Rocky right now!

Yeah, that wasn't very scientific. Lots of things probably made them intelligent. The sleep thing is likely just one part. But hey, I’m a scientist. I have to make theories!

I open a panel to the storage area and push his ball partly inside. “This is a small room for storage.”

“I understand.”

I pull him back out. “That’s all the rooms. My ship is much smaller than yours.”

“Your ship has much science!” he says. “Show me things in the science room, okay?”

“Sure.”

I take him back up to the lab. He moves around in the ball, looking at everything. I float us to the center of the room and hold onto the edge of the table.

I push the ball against the lab table. I think it’s steel, but I’m not sure. Most lab tables are. Let’s check.

“Use your magnets,” I say.

He pushes one of his magnets against the pentagon side touching the table. With a clunk the magnet sticks. He’s now held in place.

“Good!” he says. He uses his magnets on each side to roll across the table and back. It’s not pretty, but it works. At least I don’t have to hold him still.

I move away from the table and float to the edge of the room. “There’s a lot here. What do you want to know about first?”

He starts to point one way, then stops. Then he picks something else, but stops there too. Like a kid in a candy shop. Finally, he picks the 3-D printer. “That. What is that, okay?”

“It makes small things. I tell the computer what shape to make, and it tells this machine how to do it.”

“I see it make small thing, question?”

“It needs gravity.”

“That’s why your ship rotates, question?”

“Yes!” I say. Wow, he’s fast. “The rotation makes gravity for science stuff.”

“Your ship can’t rotate with the tunnel attached.”

“Right.”

He thinks it over.

“Your ship has more science than mine. Better science. I bring my things into your ship. Release tunnel. You spin your ship for science. You and I will study how to kill Astrophage together. Save Earth. Save Erid. This is a good plan, question?”

“Uh…yes! Good plan! But what about your ship?” I tap his xenonite bubble. “Human science can’t make xenonite. Xenonite is stronger than anything humans have.”

“I’ll bring materials to make xenonite. Can make any shape.”

“Okay,” I say. “Do you want to get your stuff now?”

“Yes!”

I went from being the “only space explorer left” to “a guy with a weird new roommate.” This should be interesting.

—

“Have you met Dr. Lamai?” Stratt asked.

I shrugged. “I’ve met so many people lately, I really can’t remember.”

The ship had a sick bay for the crew. This was a special medical center in the second hangar.

Dr. Lamai put her hands together and slightly bowed her head. “It’s nice to meet you, Dr. Grace.”

“Thanks,” I said. “Um, you too.”

“I’ve put Dr. Lamai in charge of all the medical stuff for the Hail Mary, ” Stratt said. “She was the main scientist for the company that made the coma technology we’re going to use.”

“Nice to meet you,” I said. “So you’re from Thailand, I guess?”

“Yes,” she said. “The company didn’t make it, sadly. Because the technology only works on one in every seven thousand people, so it’s not very useful for business. I’m very glad that my research might still help humanity.”

“That’s putting it lightly,” said Stratt. “Your technology might save humanity.”

Lamai looked away. “You’re too kind.”

She led us into her lab. A dozen areas each had slightly different experiment setups, each connected to a monkey that was unconscious.

I looked away. “Do I have to be here?”

“You’ll have to excuse Dr. Grace,” Stratt said. “He’s a bit…sensitive about some things.”

“I’m fine,” I said. “I know animal testing is needed. I just don’t like to watch it.”

Lamai said nothing.

“Dr. Grace,” Stratt said. “Stop being rude. Dr. Lamai, please update us.”

Lamai pointed to metal arms above the closest test monkey. “We created these automated stations to monitor and care for coma patients when we thought we'd have tens of thousands. That didn't happen.”

“Do they work?” Stratt asked.

“Our first design wasn't meant to be totally on its own. It would handle the basics, but if it faced a problem it couldn't fix, a doctor would be called.”

She walked by the line of sleeping monkeys. “We're making good progress on the fully automated version. This device is run by advanced software from Bangkok. It will care for a coma patient. It watches their health, gives them needed medical care, feeds them, checks their fluids, and so on. It’s still better to have a real doctor, but this is a good backup.”

“Are they a type of artificial intelligence?” Stratt asked.

“No,” Lamai said. “We don't have time to build a complex AI. This is just a set of rules. Very complicated, but not AI. We need to test it in many ways and know exactly how it acts and why. We can’t do that with an AI.”

“I see.”

She pointed to diagrams on the wall. “Our biggest discovery also destroyed our company. We found the genes that show who can resist long-term comas. We can find them with a simple blood test. And, as you know, when we tested this on people, we found that very few actually have these genes.”

“But couldn’t you still help those people?” I asked. “I know it’s only one in seven thousand, but it’s a start, right?”

Lamai shook her head. “Sadly, no. This isn’t a necessary treatment. There’s no urgent medical reason to be unconscious during chemotherapy. It even adds a bit of risk. So there wouldn't be enough people wanting it to keep a company going.”

Stratt rolled up her sleeve. “Check my blood for the genes. I want to know.”

Lamai was briefly surprised. “A-Alright, Ms. Stratt.” She went to a rolling cart and got a blood-draw kit. Someone this important wasn’t used to doing basic medical tasks. But Stratt was Stratt.

Still, Lamai was good at her job. She got the needle into Stratt quickly and on the first try. The blood went into the tube. When the blood draw was done, Stratt rolled down her sleeve. “Grace, you’re next.”

“Why?” I asked. “I’m not offering.”

“To show others,” she said. “I want everyone on this project, even those barely involved, to get tested. Astronauts are rare, and only one in seven thousand will be coma-resistant. We might not have enough good candidates. We need to be ready to include more people.”

“It’s a suicide mission,” I said. “It’s not like we’ll have people begging, ‘Pick me! Please pick me!’ ”

“Actually, we do have that,” Stratt said.

Lamai poked my arm. I looked away. I get a little sick when I see my blood going into a tube. “What do you mean, we have that?”

“We’ve already had tens of thousands of people volunteer. All knowing it’s a one-way trip.”

“Wow,” I said. “How many of them are crazy or want to die?”

“Probably a lot. But there are hundreds of astronauts with experience on the list too. Astronauts are brave people, ready to risk their lives for science. Many of them are willing to give their lives for humanity. I admire them.”

“Hundreds,” I say. “Not thousands. We’ll be lucky if even one of those astronauts is good enough.”

“We’re already counting on a lot of luck,” said Stratt. “Might as well hope for more.”

—

Soon after college, my girlfriend Linda moved in with me. The relationship only lasted eight more months and was a complete failure. But that’s not important right now.

When she moved in, I was shocked by how much random junk she felt she needed to bring into our small apartment. Box after box of stuff she had collected over decades of never throwing anything away.

Linda had very little compared to Rocky.

He’s brought in so much stuff we don’t have places to put it all.

Almost the whole dormitory is filled with duffel bags made of a canvas-like stuff. They're in random, muddy colors. When how things look doesn't matter, you just get whatever colors come out of the factory. I don't even know what's in all of them. He doesn't explain. Every time I think we might be done, he brings more bags in.

Well, I say "he" brings them in, but it's me. He hangs out in his ball, stuck to the wall with magnets, while I do all the work. Again, this is a lot like Linda.

"This is a lot of stuff," I say.

“Yes yes,” he says. “I need these things.”

“A lot of stuff.”

“Yes yes. Understand. Things in tunnel is last things.”

“Okay,” I grumble. I float back to the tunnel and grab the last few soft boxes. I move them through the cockpit and lab down to the dormitory. I find a place to cram them. There's very little space left. I vaguely wonder how much weight we just added to my ship.

I manage to keep the area near my bunk clear. And there's a spot on the floor that Rocky picked out as his sleeping place. The rest of the room is a crazy mess of soft boxes taped to each other, the wall, the other bunks, and anything else that will keep them from floating around.

“Are we done?”

“Yes. Now detach tunnel.”

I groan. “You made the tunnel. You take it off.”

“How I take off tunnel, question? Me inside ball.”

“Well, how do I do it? I don’t understand xenonite.”

He made a turning motion with two of his arms. “Turn tunnel.”

“Okay, okay.” I grab my EVA suit. “I’ll do it. Jerk.”

“No understand last word.”

“Not important.” I climb into the suit and close the rear flap.

—

Rocky is surprisingly good at doing things with a couple of magnets from inside a ball.

Each of his duffel bags has a metal plate on it. He can climb along the pile and move things around as needed. Sometimes, a bag he’s using to hold on to comes loose, and he floats away. When that happens, he calls me, and I put him back.

I hold on to my bunk and watch him do his thing. “Okay, step one. Taking astrophage samples.”

“Yes, yes.” He holds his hands out and moves one around the other. “Planet moves around Tau. Astrophage goes there from Tau. Same at Eridani. Astrophage uses carbon dioxide to make more astrophage there.”

“Yes,” I say. “Did you get a sample?”

“No. My ship had a tool for this. But the tool broke.”

“You couldn’t fix it?”

“The tool didn’t just stop working. It broke. It fell off the ship during the trip. The tool is gone.”

“Oh! Wow. Why did it fall off?”

He wiggles his shell. “Don’t know. Many things break. My people made the ship very quickly. No time to make sure everything worked well.”

Problems with quality because of deadlines: a problem all over the galaxy.

“I tried to replace it. Failed. Tried. Failed. Tried. Failed. I put the ship in the path of the Astrophage. Maybe some will stick to the hull. But the robot on the hull can’t find any. Astrophage is very small.”

His shell slumps down. His elbows are higher than his breathing holes. He sometimes lowers his shell when sad, but I’ve never seen him lower it this much.

His voice gets deeper. “Fail fail fail. I repair things, not do science. The smart science people died.”

“Hey…don’t think like that…” I say.

“Don’t understand.”

“Uh…” I pull myself over to his bags. “You’re alive. And you’re here. And you haven’t quit.”

But his voice is still low. “I try so many times. Fail so many times. Not good at science.”

“I am,” I say. “I’m a science human. You’re good at making and fixing things. We’ll figure this out together.”

He raises his shell a bit. “Yes. Together. You have thing to get Astrophage, yes?”

The External Collection Unit. I remember it from my first day in the control room. I didn’t think about it much then, but that must be it. “Yes. I have a thing for this.”

"Relief! I tried for so long. So many times. Failed." He's quiet for a moment. "I spent much time here. Much time alone."

"How long were you here alone?"

He pauses. "I need new words."

I pull my laptop off the wall. We run into new words every day, but it's happening less and less. That's something.

I start the frequency analyzer and bring up my dictionary. "Ready."

"Seven thousand seven hundred and seventy-six seconds is ♩♫♩♪♪. Erid rotates once in one ♩♫♩♪♪."

I immediately know the number. I figured it out when I was studying Rocky's clock. 7,776 is six to the fifth power. It's exactly how many Eridian seconds it takes for an Eridian clock to reset to all zeroes. They divided their day into a very convenient (to them) number of seconds. I can follow that.

"Eridian day." I enter it into my dictionary. "A planet rotating once is a 'day.'"

"I understand," he says.

"Erid circles Eridani once every 198.8 Eridian days. 198.8 Eridian days is ♫♩♪♫♪."

“Year,” I say, and say it again as I understand. “A planet circling a star once is one year. So, that's an Eridian year.”

“We use Earth units, or you'll get confused. How long is an Earth day? And how many Earth days in an Earth year?”

“One Earth day is 86,400 seconds. One Earth year is 365.25 Earth days.”

“Understood,” he says. “I have been here forty-six years.”

“Forty-six years?!” I gasp. “Earth years?!”

“I have been here forty-six Earth years, yes.”

He’s been stuck in this system longer than I’ve been alive.

“How…how long do Eridians live?”

He wiggled a claw. “Average is six hundred eighty-nine years.”

“Earth years?”

“Yes,” he says a little sharply. “Always Earth units. You're not good at math, so always Earth units.”

I can’t even speak for a moment.

“How many years have you been alive?”

“Two hundred ninety-one years.” He pauses. “Yes. Earth years.”

Wow. Rocky is older than the United States. He was born around the same time as George Washington.

He’s not even that old for his species. There are old Eridians who were alive when Columbus discovered North America (which was already inhabited).

“Why are you so surprised?” Rocky asks. “How long do humans live?”

Chapter 16

“T his is Earth gravity, right?” Rocky asks. His ball rests on the control-room floor next to the pilot seat.

I check the Centrifuge control screen. We are at full speed and extension. The crew area has turned 180 degrees correctly. The diagram shows the two halves of the ship fully apart. We are spinning smoothly in space. The “Lab Gravity” reads “1.00 g.”

“Yes. This is Earth gravity.”

He steps side to side, rolling his dome one face back and forth. “Not much gravity. What is it?”

“Nine point eight meters per second per second.”

“Not much gravity,” he repeats. “Erid gravity is 20.48.”

“That’s a lot of gravity,” I say. But that’s expected. He’d told me all about Erid before, including its size and mass. I knew their gravity had to be about double Earth’s. Good to have my math confirmed, though.

And wow. Rocky’s mass is 168 kilograms. That means on his home planet he weighs almost 800 pounds. And that’s normal for him, so I guess he can move around just fine.

Eight hundred pounds and can skitter around easily. Remember: Don’t arm-wrestle an Eridian.

“So,” I say, leaning back in the pilot’s seat. “What’s the plan? Fly into the Petrova line and get some Astrophage?”

“Yes! But first I make xenonite space for me.” He points down the hatchway toward the rest of the crew area. “Mostly in sleep room. But tunnels in lab and small area in control room. Is okay, question?”

Well, he can’t just stay in a ball forever. “Yes, that’s fine. Where is the xenonite?”

“Xenonite parts in bags in dormitory. Liquids. Mix. Become xenonite.”

Like epoxy. But really, really strong epoxy.

“Interesting! Someday I want to know all about xenonite.”

“I not understand science. I just use. Sorry.”

“That’s okay. I can’t explain how to make a thinking machine. I just use it.”

“Good. You understand.”

“How long will it take to build your xenonite structure?”

“Four days. Maybe five days. Why do you ask?”

“I want to work quickly.”

“Why so fast? Slower is safer. Fewer mistakes.”

I shift in my chair. “Earth is in a bad state. It’s getting worse all the time. I have to hurry.”

“I don’t understand,” says Rocky. “Why is Earth so bad so fast? Erid got bad slower. It had at least seventy-two years before big problems.”

Seventy-two years? Man, I wish Earth had that kind of time. But seventy-two years from now Earth will be a frozen wasteland and 99 percent of the human population will be dead.

Why isn’t Erid as badly affected? I furrow my brow. I only have to think for a moment before I have my answer: It’s all about thermal energy storage.

“Erid is much hotter than Earth,” I say. “And Erid is much larger with a much thicker atmosphere. So Erid has a lot more heat stored in its air. Earth is getting cold fast. Very fast. In fourteen more years, most humans will be dead.”

His voice becomes monotone. It’s a very serious tone. “I understand. Stress. Concern.”

“Yes.”

He clicks two claws together. “Then we work. We work now! Learn how to kill Astrophage. You return to Earth. You explain. Save Earth!”

I sigh. I’ll have to explain this sooner or later. Might as well be now. “I’m not going back. I’m going to die here.”

His shell shudders. “Why, question?”

“My ship only had enough fuel to get here. I don’t have enough to go home. I have tiny probes that will return to Earth with my findings. But I will stay here.”

“Why is mission like this, question?”

“This was all the fuel my planet could make in time.”

“You knew this when you left Earth, question?”

“Yes.”

“You are good human.”

“Thanks.” I try not to think about what’s coming. “So, let’s collect Astrophage. I have ideas for how we can get some samples. My equipment is very good at detecting small amounts—”

“Wait.” He holds up a claw. “How much Astrophage does your ship need to return to Earth, question?”

“Uh…just over two million kilograms,” I say.

“I can give it,” he says.

I sit up in my chair. “What?!”

“I can give it. I have extra. I can give that much and still have plenty to return to Erid. You can have it.”

My heart skips a beat. “Seriously?! That’s a lot of fuel! Let me say it again: two million kilograms. Two times ten to the sixth power!”

“Yes. I have much Astrophage. My ship used less than planned on the trip here. You can have two million kilograms.”

I fall back into my seat. I pant. I almost can’t breathe. My eyes fill with tears. “Oh my God…”

“Don’t understand.”

I wipe away tears.

“You okay, right?”

“Yes!” I sob. “Yes, I’m okay. Thank you! Thank you thank you!”

“I am happy. You not die. Let’s save planets!”

I break down, crying tears of joy. I’m going to live!

—

Half the Chinese crew stood on the flight deck. Some were actually doing their jobs, but most were there to see humanity’s saviors. The whole science team was there as well. The same people we had at our weekly meetings. Stratt, me, Dimitri, Lokken, and our newest scientist, Dr. Lamai. Oh, and no science team would be complete without a gambling swindler, so Bob Redell was there too.

To be fair, Bob had done his job well. He had managed the Sahara Astrophage Farm wonderfully. It’s rare to find a scientist who is also a good manager. It was not easy, but the farm was making Astrophage at the levels he’d promised.

The helicopter came in low and slow, then landed perfectly on the helipad. A ground crew rushed up to secure it. The rotors kept spinning and the cargo door opened.

Three people walked out, each in blue jumpsuits, each with their country’s flag on the shoulder. A Chinese man, a Russian woman, and an American man.

The ground crew led them away, and the chopper lifted off again. Shortly after, another helicopter came down. Like before, this helicopter had three astronauts on board. This time, a Russian man, a Russian woman, and an American woman.

These six were the main and reserve crews for the Hail Mary. Either of the helicopters could have easily fit all six astronauts, but Stratt had a firm rule: No crewmember and their backup could share a plane, helicopter, or car, no matter what. Each role was unique and took years of training. We couldn’t let one car accident wipe out humanity's hope.

There weren't many choices. Not many people could resist comas, had "the right stuff," and would agree to a suicide mission.

Even so, picking the team was long, hard, and full of fighting between the governments involved. Stratt stood her ground and demanded only the best, but she had to give in sometimes.

"Women," I said.

"Yes," Stratt said, annoyed.

"Even though you didn't want them."

"Yes."

"Good."

"No, it's not." She frowned. "The Americans and Russians made me do it."

I folded my arms. “I never thought a woman would be so sexist towards women.”

“It’s not sexism. It’s realism.” She brushed a strand of hair from her face. “My rules were that all candidates must be straight men.”

“Why not all straight women?”

“Most scientists and astronaut candidates are men. That’s just how it is. Don’t like it? Tell your female students to study science and math. I’m not here to make things equal. I’m here to do what it takes to save humanity.”

“Still seems sexist.”

“Call it what you want. There’s no room on this mission for sexual tension. What if someone falls in love? Or there’s a fight? People kill for less.”

I looked across the deck at the candidates. Captain Yang welcomed them aboard. He seemed especially interested in his countryman—they were smiling and shaking hands.

“You didn’t want a Chinese guy either. You thought their space program was too new. But I hear you chose him to lead the crew.”

“He’s the best person for the job. So he’s the commander.”

“Maybe the Russians and Americans are good enough too. Maybe the people saving the world will act like adults. Maybe ignoring half the people who could help because you’re afraid astronauts can’t control themselves isn’t smart.”

“We’ll have to hope so. The Russian woman—Ilyukhina—is on the main crew as well. She’s a materials expert and the best person for the job. The science expert is Martin DuBois—the American man. Two men and one woman. Sounds like trouble.”

I put my hand to my chest, pretending to be shocked. “Oh my! DuBois is black! I can’t believe you let that happen! Are you worried he’ll mess up the mission by talking about rap music and basketball?”

“Oh, be quiet,” she said.

We watched the astronauts get swarmed by the deck crew. They were completely in awe—especially of Yáo.

“DuBois has three PhDs—physics, chemistry, and biology.” Stratt pointed to the American woman. “That’s Annie Shapiro. She created a new way to splice DNA that’s now called the Shapiro method.”

“Really?” I said. “ The Annie Shapiro? She made three whole enzymes from nothing to splice DNA using—”

“Yes, yes. She’s very smart.”

“She did it for her PhD project. Her project. Do you know how many people might win a Nobel Prize for research they did in grad school? Not many, I can tell you. And she’s your second choice for the science expert?”

“She’s the best DNA splicing expert alive. But DuBois knows a lot about many different things, and that’s more important. We don’t know what they’ll find out there. We need someone who knows a little about everything.”

“Amazing people,” I said. “The very best.”

“I’m glad you’re impressed. Because you’ll be training DuBois and Shapiro.”

“Me?” I asked. “I don’t know how to train astronauts!”

“NASA and Roscosmos will teach them the astronaut stuff,” she said. “You’re going to teach them the science stuff.”

“Are you kidding? They’re way smarter than me. What would I teach them?”

“Don’t underestimate yourself,” said Stratt. “You’re the world’s leading expert on Astrophage biology. You’re going to share everything you know about it with both of them. Here comes the main crew.”

Yáo, Ilyukhina, and DuBois walked over to Stratt.

Yáo bowed. He spoke with a very slight accent, but his English was perfect. “Ms. Stratt. It is an honor to finally meet you. Please accept my deepest thanks for choosing me as the commander for this important mission.”

“Nice to meet you too,” she said. “You were the most qualified. No thanks needed.”

“Hello!” Ilyukhina rushed forward and hugged Stratt. “I’m here to die for Earth! Pretty awesome, yes?!”

I leaned to Dimitri. “Are all Russians crazy?”

“Yes,” he said with a smile. “It’s the only way to be both Russian and happy.”

“That’s…dark.”

“That’s Russian!”

DuBois shook Stratt’s hand and spoke almost too quietly to hear. “Ms. Stratt. Thank you for this chance. I won’t disappoint you.”

I and the other science heads all shook hands with the three astronauts. It was messy, more like a casual party than a serious meeting.

In the middle of it all, DuBois turned to me. “You must be Ryland Grace?”

“Yeah,” I said. “It’s an honor to meet you. What you’re doing is just… I can’t even imagine the sacrifice you’re making. Or should I not mention it? I don’t know. Maybe we shouldn’t talk about it?”

He smiled. “I think about it a lot. We don’t have to avoid it. Besides, you and I are alike, it seems.”

I shrugged. “I guess so. I mean, you’re way ahead of me, but I do love cellular biology.”

“Well, yes, that too,” he said. “But I meant coma resistance. I hear you have the coma-resistance markers, like me and the rest of the crew.”

“I do?”

He raised his eyebrow. “They didn’t tell you?”

“No!” I glanced at Stratt. She was talking to Embezzler Bob and Commander Yáo. “This is the first I’m hearing of it.”

“That’s strange,” he said.

“Why wouldn’t she tell me?”

“You’re asking the wrong person, Dr. Grace. But I’m guessing they only told Stratt and she only told people who had to know.”

“It’s my DNA,” I muttered. “Someone should have told me.”

DuBois smoothly changed the subject. “Anyway, I’m looking forward to learning all about the Astrophage life-cycle. Dr. Shapiro—my partner on the backup crew—is also very excited. It’ll be a class of two, I guess. Do you have any experience teaching?”

“Actually, yes,” I said. “A lot.”

“Excellent.”

I’m all smiles. It’s been three days since I found out I won’t die and I’m still all smiles.

Well, actually, I could still easily die. The trip home is long and dangerous. Just because I survived my coma on the way here, that doesn’t mean I’ll survive it on the way home. Maybe I can stay awake and just eat the feeding-tube slurry when my normal food runs out? I can do four years all alone, right? We were in comas to keep from killing one another. But being alone is a whole different kind of mental problem. I should read up on it.

But not now. Right now I have to save Earth. My own survival is a problem for later. But it’s a problem , not a hopeless guarantee of death.

The light on the Centrifuge screen blinks green.

“Gravity at full,” I say with a smile.

We were back in zero g for a short time, but now I have the centrifuge going again. I had to stop it because I needed to use the engines. We can’t have fake gravity and the engines on at the same time. Just imagine firing up the spin drives while the ship is in two pieces connected by a hundred meters of cable. It’s not a pleasant thought.

During the decades (gasp!) that Rocky’s been here, he studied the system very well. He gave me all the information he’d gathered. He listed six planets, noting their size, weight, positions, orbits, and general atmosphere. He didn’t have to travel around to do it. He just did astronomical observations from the Blip-A. Turns out Eridians are as curious as humans are.

And it’s a good thing too. This isn’t Star Trek. I can’t just turn on a scanner and get all that information about a star system. It took Rocky months of watching to get things this detailed.

And more important, Rocky knows all about the local Petrova line. As expected, it leads to one specific planet—probably the one with the most carbon dioxide. In this case, it’s the third planet from the star, “Tau Ceti e.” At least, that’s what Earth calls it.

So that’ll be our first stop.

Sure, we could fly the Hail Mary through any part of the Petrova line and get some Astrophage that way. But we’d only cross the line for a few seconds. A solar system isn’t still. We have to keep moving fast enough to orbit the star.

But Tau Ceti e is a nice, big planet in the widest part of the Petrova line. We can park the Hail Mary in orbit and be surrounded by local Astrophage for half of every orbit. And we can stay there as long as we want, getting all the data we need about the Astrophage here and how the Petrova line works.

So we’re on our way to the mysterious planet.

I can’t just ask Mr. Sulu to plot a course. I spent two days doing math, checking my work, and checking it again before I figured out the exact angle and power needed.

Sure, I have 20,000 kilograms of Astrophage left. And yes, that’s a lot of fuel considering I can get 1.5 g’s by using 6 grams per second. And yes, Rocky’s ship seems to have tons of Astrophage (I still don’t understand how he has so much extra fuel). But I’m saving fuel anyway.

I got us going fast and we’re on course for Tau Ceti e. I’ll slow down to orbit in about eleven days. While we wait, we may as well have gravity. So we’re back to using the centrifuge.

Eleven days. Amazing. The total distance we’ll travel is over 150 million kilometers. That’s about the same as the distance from Earth to the sun. And we’re doing it in eleven days. How? By going incredibly fast.

I used three hours of thrust to get us going, and I’ll use another three when we get to Tau Ceti e to slow down. Right now, we’re moving at 162 kilometers per second. It’s crazy. If you left Earth at that speed, you’d get to the moon in forty minutes.

This whole trip, including the slowdown burn at the end, will use up 130 kilograms of fuel.

Astrophage. Wild stuff.

Rocky's in a clear bubble of xenon gas in the control room floor.

“Dull name,” Rocky says.

“What? What name's dull?” I ask.

He’s spent days building up the Eridian Zone all over the ship. He even put in his own new tunnels between decks. It’s like having giant hamster tunnels everywhere.

He moves from one handhold to another. “Tau Ceti e. Dull name.”

“Then name it.”

“Me name? No. You name.”

“You got here first.” I undo my seatbelts and stretch. “You found it. You figured out its orbit and location. You name it.”

“This is you ship. You name.”

I shake my head. “Earth rule. If you find a place first, you name it.”

He thinks about it.

Xenonite is amazing. Just a centimeter of this clear stuff keeps my thin air with oxygen separate from Rocky’s thick air with ammonia. And my 20 degrees Celsius separate from Rocky’s 210 degrees Celsius.

He’s taken over more of some rooms than others. The dormitory is mostly his now. I made him move all his junk into his room, so we agreed he could have most of the space in there.

He also put a big airlock in the dormitory. He made it the size of the Hail Mary’s airlock, thinking anything important would fit through it. I can’t go into his area. My spacesuit wouldn’t work there. I’d be crushed. The airlock is so we can pass things back and forth.

The lab is mostly mine. He has a tunnel up the side and another going along the ceiling and into the control room. He can watch my science stuff. But Earth equipment won’t work in his air, so it has to be in mine.

The control room is small. Rocky put the xenonite bulb in the floor by the hatch. He tried to not take up much space. He says the holes he added won’t hurt the ship.

“Okay,” he says. “Name is ♫♩♪♫ .”

I don’t need the frequency analyzer now. It was an A-below-middle-C major fifth, then an E-flat octave, and then a G-minor seventh. I put it in my spreadsheet, but I don’t know why. I haven’t looked at it in days. “What does it mean?”

"It is name of my mate."

I widen my eyes. That little devil! He never told me he had a mate! I guess Eridians don’t share everything.

We’d talked about some basic biology during our travels. I explained how humans reproduce, and he told me where baby Eridians come from. They’re hermaphrodites and reproduce by laying eggs next to each other. Things happen between the eggs and one of them absorbs the other, leaving one good egg that will hatch in one Eridian year—forty-two Earth days.

Laying eggs together is, basically, the Eridian version of sex. And they mate for life. But this is the first I’ve heard of Rocky doing it.

“You have a mate?”

"Unknown," Rocky says. "Mate possibly has new mate. I gone a long time."

“Sad,” I say.

"Yes, sad. But necessary. Must save Erid. You pick human word for ♫♩♪♫ ."

Proper nouns are annoying. If you’re learning German from a guy named Hans, you just call him Hans. But I literally can’t make the sounds Rocky makes and vice versa. So when one of us tells the other about a name, the other one has to pick or make up a word to represent that name in their own language. Rocky’s actual name is a sequence of notes—he told it to me once but it has no meaning in his language, so I stuck with “Rocky.”

But my name is actually an English word. So Rocky just calls me the Eridian word for "grace."

Anyway, now I need to think of an English word for “Rocky’s partner.”

“Adrian,” I say. Why not? “Human word is ‘Adrian.’ ”

“Understood,” he says. He goes down his tunnel into the lab.

I put my hands on my hips and look up to watch him leave. “Where are you going?”

“Eat.”

“Eat?! Wait!”

I’ve never seen him eat. I’ve never even seen a hole other than the radiator vents on top of his shell. How does he get food in? Also, how does he lay eggs? He’s been pretty secretive about it. He ate in his ship when we were connected. And I think he grabbed a few meals now and then while I slept.

I hurry down the ladder into the lab. He’s already halfway down his tunnel, climbing the many handholds. I keep up, climbing my own ladder. “Hey, I want to watch!”

Rocky reaches the lab’s floor and stops. “Is private. I sleep after eat. You watch me sleep, ask questions?”

“I want to watch you eat!”

“Why ask?”

“Science,” I say.

Rocky moves his shell back and forth a few times. Eridian way of showing he’s a little annoyed. “Is about living things. Is icky.”

“Science.”

He wiggles his shell again. “Okay. You watch.” He keeps going down.

“Yes!” I follow him down.

I squeeze into my small space in the dorm. All I have now is my bed, the toilet, and the robot arms.

To be fair, he doesn’t have much space either. He takes up most of the room, but it’s filled with all his stuff. Plus, he made a makeshift workshop in there and a system to keep him alive using parts from his ship.

He opens one of his many soft bags and takes out a sealed package. He tears it open with his claws and there are different shapes I don’t know. Mostly rocky stuff like his shell. He starts tearing them apart into smaller and smaller pieces with his claws.

“That’s your food?” I ask.

“Socially awkward,” he says. “No talk.”

“Sorry.”

I guess eating for them is something gross that is to be done in private.

He tears the rough chunks off the food and reveals meat underneath. It’s definitely meat—it looks just like Earth meat. Since we almost certainly come from the same basic building blocks of life, I bet we use the same proteins and have the same general solutions to evolutionary problems.

Once again, I feel sad. I want to spend the rest of my life studying Eridian biology! But I have to save humanity first. Stupid humanity. Getting in the way of my hobbies.

He pulls all of the rough chunks off the meat and sets them aside. Then he tears the meat into small pieces. He always keeps the food on the packaging it came in. It never touches the floor. I wouldn’t want my food touching the floor either.

After a while, he has shredded the edible parts of his meal as much as he can with his hands. Much more than any human would with their food.

Then he steps to the other side of his compartment, leaving his food where it was. He pulls a flat, round container from a sealed box and places it under his thorax.

Then things…get gross. He did warn me. I can’t complain.

The rough armor on his abdomen splits, and I see something fleshy rip open underneath. A few drops of shiny silver liquid dribble out. Blood?

Then a gray blob falls out of his body into the pan. It lands with a wet splat.

He closes the pan and puts it back in its box.

He goes back to the food and rolls onto his back. The big hole in his stomach is still open. I can see inside. There’s soft flesh in there.

He reaches over with some of his hands and grabs some good pieces of food. He brings them to his opening and drops them in. He does this again and again, slowly and carefully, until all the food is in his…mouth? Stomach?

There is no chewing. There are no teeth. As far as I can tell, nothing is moving inside.

He finishes the last of his food, then lets his arms drop. He lies flat on the floor, not moving.

I try not to ask if he’s okay. I mean, he looks dead. But this is probably just how Eridians eat. And poop. Yeah. I’m guessing that blob that came out earlier was what’s left of his last meal. He’s a monostome—that is, waste comes out the same opening that food goes in.

The opening in his stomach closes slowly. Something like a scab forms where the skin was broken. But I don’t see it for long. The rocky stomach covering folds back into place soon after.

“I…sleep…” he mumbled. “You…watch…question?”

A food coma for Rocky is a big deal. This doesn’t look like he's doing it on purpose at all. This is a nap his body forces him to take after eating.

“Yes, I watch. Sleep.”

“Sle…ee…p…” he mumbles. Then he conks out, still belly-up on the floor.

His breathing speeds up. It always does when he first falls asleep. His body has to get rid of all the heat in his hot blood.

After a few minutes, he stops panting. Now I know he’s really asleep. Once he stops panting, I’ve never seen him wake up again in less than two hours. I can sneak off to do my own thing. In this case, I’ll write down everything I just saw about his digestion.

Step 1: Subject poops from mouth.

“Yup,” I say to myself. “That was pretty freaking gross.”

Chapter 17

I wake up with Rocky looking at me.

It happens every morning now. But it never stops being weird.

How do I know that a five-sided creature with no eyes is “looking” at me? I just know. Something in the way he is.

“You awake,” he says.

“Yeah.” I get out of bed and stretch. “Food!”

The arms reach up and give me a hot box. I open it and take a look. Looks like eggs and sausage.

“Coffee.”

The arms hand me a cup of coffee like they’re supposed to. It’s kind of cool that the arms will hand me a cup when there’s gravity, but a pouch when there isn’t. I’ll remember this when I write the Hail Mary ’s review.

I look at Rocky. “You don’t have to watch me sleep. It’s okay.”

He turns to a worktable in his part of the dorm. “Eridian culture rule. Must watch.” He picks up a device and fiddles with it.

Ah, the c-word. “Culture.” We have an unspoken agreement that cultural things just have to be accepted. It ends any small argument. “Do it my way because that’s how I was raised,” basically. We haven’t had any cultural clashes…yet.

I eat my breakfast and drink my coffee. Rocky doesn’t say anything to me during that time. He never does. Eridian courtesy.

“Trash,” I say.

The arms collect my empty cup and meal container.

I go up to the control room and sit in the pilot’s seat. I bring up the telescope view on the main screen. Planet Adrian is in the center. I’ve been watching it get bigger for the past ten days. The closer we get, the more I respect Rocky’s astronomy skills. All of his observations on its motion and mass have been perfect.

Hopefully his gravity calculation is right too. Or we’ll have a very short and painful attempt to orbit.

Adrian is a pale-green planet with thin white clouds in the upper atmosphere. I can’t see the ground at all. Again, I’m amazed at the software that must be in this ship’s computers. We are spinning around as we fly through space. But the image on-screen is perfectly still.

“We’re getting close,” I say. Rocky is two floors below me, but I speak normally. I know he can hear it just fine.

“You know air yet, question?” Rocky calls out. Just as I know his hearing ability, he knows my hearing limitations.

“I’ll try again now,” I say.

I switch to the Spectrometer screen. The Hail Mary has been very reliable in almost every way, but you can’t expect perfection. The spectrometer has been acting up. I think it’s the digitizer. I’ve been trying it every day, and it keeps saying it can’t get enough data to analyze.

I focus on Adrian and try again. The closer we get, the more reflected light we’ll get, and maybe it’ll be enough for the spectrometer to tell me what Adrian’s atmosphere is made of.

ANALYZING…

ANALYZING…

ANALYZING…

ANALYSIS COMPLETE.

“It worked!” I say.

“Worked, question?!” Rocky says, his voice an octave higher than normal. He hurries through his tunnels to the control-room bulb. “What is Adrian air, question?”

I read the results off the screen. “Looks like it’s…91 percent carbon dioxide, 7 percent methane, 1 percent argon, and the rest are tiny amounts of other gases. It’s a pretty thick atmosphere too. Those are all clear gases, but I can’t see the planet’s surface.”

“You can usually see a planet's surface from space, right?”

“If the atmosphere lets light pass through, yes.”

“Human eyes are amazing. I’m jealous.”

“Well, not amazing enough. I can’t see Adrian’s surface. When the air gets really dense, light can't pass through it. But that's not important. The methane—that’s strange.”

“Explain.”

“Methane doesn’t last long. Sunlight breaks it down quickly. So why is there methane?”

“Geology makes methane. Carbon dioxide plus minerals plus water plus heat equals methane.”

“Yes. Possible,” I say. “But there’s a lot of methane. Eight percent of a very dense atmosphere. Can geology create that much?”

“Do you have another idea?”

I rub the back of my my neck. “No. Not really. But it is strange.”

“Difference is science. You think about difference. Make theory. You is science human.”

“Yes. I’ll think about it.”

“How long until orbit, question?”

I switch to the Navigation console. We’re right on course, and the orbital-insertion burn is scheduled for twenty-two hours from now. “Just under one day,” I say.

“Excitement,” he says. “Then we sample Astrophage at Adrian. You ship sampler working well, question?”

“Yes,” I say, with no way to know if I’m telling the truth. There’s no reason for Rocky to know I only partly understand how my own ship works.

I flip through the science instruments until I land on the controls for the External Collection Unit. I look at the diagram on the screen. It’s simple enough. The sampler is a rectangular box. When activated, it will pivot up to be at a right angle to the hull. Then, doors on both sides of the rectangle will open up. Inside, there’s a bunch of sticky resin—ready to catch anything that flies in.

That’s it. Flypaper. Fancy space flypaper, but just flypaper.

“After collection, how sample enter ship, question?”

Simple doesn’t mean easy. As far as I can tell, there’s no automated system to do anything with the sample. “I have to go get it.”

“Humans are amazing. You leave ship.”

“Yeah, I guess.”

Eridians never bothered to invent spacesuits. Why would they? Space has no sensory input for them. It would be like a human with scuba gear diving into an ocean of black paint. There’s just no reason to do it. Eridians use hull robots for EVA work. The Hail Mary doesn’t have one of those, so any EVA work has to be done by me.

“Amazing is wrong word,” he says. “Amazing is a compliment. Better word is ♫♪♫♪ .”

“What’s that mean?”

“It is when person does not act normal. Danger to self.”

“Ah,” I say, adding the new chord into my language database. “Crazy. My word for that is ‘crazy.’”

“Crazy. Humans are crazy.”

I shrug.

“Darn it!” I said.

“Watch your language!” came the voice over the radio. “Seriously, though, what happened?”

The sample vial fell slowly from my hand to the bottom of the pool. It took several seconds to fall 3 feet but, wearing this EVA suit at the bottom of the world’s largest swimming pool, I had no chance of grabbing it.

“I dropped vial number three.”

“Okay,” said Forrester. “That’s three vials so far. We’re going to have to work on the clamp tool.”

“Might not be the tool. Might just be me.”

The tool in my clumsy gloved hand was not perfect, but still pretty clever. It turned the awkward movements of an EVA suit glove into precise control at the other end. All I had to do was squeeze a trigger with my index finger and the clamp tightened by 2 millimeters. If I squeezed a different trigger with my middle finger, it would rotate up to 90 degrees clockwise. My ring and pinkie fingers made it tilt forward up to 90 degrees.

“Stand by, I’m checking the video,” said Forrester.

NASA’s Neutral Buoyancy Lab at Johnson Space Center was an amazing feat of engineering. The gigantic swimming pool was big enough to fit a full-size model of the International Space Station inside. They used it to train astronauts on zero-g movement while in EVA suits.

After many meetings (that I had to go to), the microbiology people convinced Stratt that the mission needed tools made just for it. She agreed, but only if none of them were crucial to the mission. She insisted that all the important things be regular products with millions of hours of testing by customers.

And, because I was her science helper, I had to test the IVME kit.

IVME was an acronym that stood for four words that shouldn't be together: "in vacuum microbiology equipment." Astrophage lives in space. We could study them on Earth in our atmosphere, but we wouldn't fully understand how they worked until we studied them in vacuum and zero gravity. The crew of the Hail Mary would need these tools.

I stood in a corner of the NBL, with the huge ISS behind me. Two scuba divers floated nearby, ready to save me if something went wrong.

NASA had put a metal lab table in the pool for me. The biggest challenge wasn't making equipment that worked in vacuum—though they had to completely change how pipettes worked because there's no suction in space. The problem was the clumsy EVA gloves the user had to wear. Astrophage might like vacuum, but human bodies definitely don't.

But hey, at least I was learning a lot about how Russian EVA suits worked.

Yes, Russian. Not American. Stratt listened to several experts, and they all agreed that the Russian Orlan EVA suit was the safest and most reliable. So that's what the mission would use.

"Okay, I see what happened," Forrester said through the headset. "You told the clamp to tilt, but it released instead. The wires inside must be tangled. I'll be right there. Can you come up and bring the clamp with you?"

"Sure." I waved to the two divers and pointed up. They nodded and helped me to the surface.

I was lifted out of the pool by a crane and placed on the deck nearby. Several techs came and helped me out of the suit. It was pretty easy—I just stepped out of the back. I love these suits that open like a cocoon.

Forrester came from the control room next door and picked up the tool. “I’ll make some adjustments and we can try again in a couple of hours. I got a call while you were in the pool; you’re needed in Building 30. Shapiro and DuBois have a couple-hour break while they reset the flight-control simulators. No rest for the wicked. Stratt wants you over there training them on Astrophage.”

“Copy that, Houston,” I said. The world might have been ending, but being at NASA’s main campus was too cool for me not to be excited.

I left the NBL and walked to Building 30. They would have sent a car if I’d asked, but I didn’t want one. It was only a ten-minute walk. Besides, I loved walking around in my country’s space history.

I walked in, through security, and onward to a small conference room they’d set up. Martin DuBois, in his blue flight uniform, stood and shook my hand. “Dr. Grace. Good to see you again.”

His careful paperwork and notes were laid out in front of him. Annie Shapiro’s messy notes and crumpled papers were scattered on the table next to him, but her seat was empty.

“Where’s Annie?” I asked.

He sat back down. Even while seated, he kept a stiff, perfect posture. “She had to use the restroom. She should be back shortly.”

I sat down and opened my backpack. “You know, you can call me Ryland. We’re all PhDs here. I think first names are fine.”

“I’m sorry, Dr. Grace. That is not how I was raised. However, you may call me Martin if you wish.”

“Thanks.” I pulled out my laptop and turned it on. “How have you been lately?”

“I’m fine, thank you. Dr. Shapiro and I are now having sex.”

I paused. “Um. Okay.”

“I thought I should tell you.” He opened his notebook and put a pen down. “We shouldn’t keep secrets in the main team.”

“Right, right,” I said. “I mean. It shouldn’t matter. You’re the main scientist and Annie’s the backup. There’s no way you’d both be on the mission. But…I mean…your relationship…”

“Yes, you’re right,” DuBois said. “I’m going on a suicide mission in less than a year. And if I can’t go, she will. We know this, and we know this relationship will end in death.”

“These are sad times,” I said.

He put his hands together. “Dr. Shapiro and I don’t see it that way. We’re having a lot of sex.”

“Yeah, okay, I don’t need to know—”

“We don’t need condoms either. She’s on birth control and we’ve both had complete medical checkups for the program.”

I typed on my computer, hoping he’d talk about something else.

“It’s nice.”

“I’m sure it is.”

“Anyway, I thought you should know.”

“Yeah, no, sure.”

The door opened, and Annie trotted in.

“Sorry! Sorry! I had to pee. Like…really bad,” said the world’s smartest and most accomplished microbiologist. “I really had to go!”

“Welcome back, Dr. Shapiro. I’ve told Dr. Grace about our sexual relationship.”

I put my head in my hands.

“Cool,” said Annie. “Yeah, we’ve got nothing to hide.”

“Anyway,” said DuBois, “if I remember the last lesson correctly, we were working on the cellular biology within Astrophage mitochondria.”

I cleared my throat. “Yes. Today, I’ll be discussing the Astrophage’s Krebs cycle. It’s the same as what we find in Earth mitochondria, but with one extra step—”

Annie raised her hand. “Oh, sorry. One more thing—” She turned to DuBois. “Martin, we have about fifteen minutes of free time after this lesson and before our next training. Want to meet in the bathroom down the hall and have sex?”

“I agree to that,” said DuBois. “Thank you, Dr. Shapiro.”

“Okay, cool.”

They both looked at me, ready for their lesson. I waited a few seconds to make sure they were done sharing, but they seemed ready. “Okay, so the Krebs cycle in Astrophage has a different—wait. Do you call her Dr. Shapiro while having sex?”

“Of course. That’s her name.”

“I kind of like it,” she said.

“I regret asking,” I said. “Now, the Krebs cycle…”

Rocky’s data about Planet Adrian was spot on. It’s 3.93 times Earth’s mass and has a radius of 10,318 kilometers (almost double Earth’s). It’s moving along around Tau Ceti with an average orbital velocity of 35.9 kilometers per second. Plus, he had the planet's position correct to within 0.00001 percent. That data was all I needed to figure out the needed thrust.

It’s a good thing those numbers were right. If they hadn’t been, there would have been some serious problems when the orbital insertion went wrong. Maybe even some dying.

Of course, to use the spin drives at all, I had to take us out of centrifuge mode.

Rocky and I float in the control room, he in his ceiling bulb and me in the pilot’s seat. I watch the camera-feed screen with a stupid grin on my face.

I’m at another planet! I shouldn’t be this excited. I’ve been at another star for the past several weeks. But that’s kind of complex. Tau Ceti is pretty much like the sun. It’s bright, you can’t get too close to it, and it even emits the same general range of frequencies. For some reason, being at a new planet is much more exciting.

The wispy clouds of Adrian drift by beneath us. Or, more accurately, the wispy clouds barely move at all and we zoom by overhead. Adrian has a higher gravity than Earth, so our orbital velocity is just over 12 kilometers per second—far more than what’s needed to orbit Earth.

The pale-green planet that I’ve been watching for eleven days has a lot more detail now that we’re on top of it. It’s not just green. There are dark and light bands of green wrapping around it. Just like Jupiter and Saturn. But unlike those two gas-giant leviathans, Adrian is a rocky world. Thanks to Rocky’s notes, we know the radius and mass, which means we know its density. And it’s far too dense to be just gas. There’s a surface down there, I just can’t see it.

Man, what I wouldn’t give for a lander!

Realistically, it wouldn’t do me any good. Even if I had some way of landing on Adrian, the atmosphere would crush me dead. It’d be like landing on Venus. Or Erid, for that matter. Heck, in that case, I wish Rocky had a lander. The pressure down there might not be too much for an Eridian.

Speaking of Erid, Rocky’s calibrating some kind of device in his control-room bubble. It looks almost like a gun. I don’t think we’ve started a space war, so I assume it’s something else.

He holds the device in one hand, taps it with another, and uses two more to hold a flat panel connected to the device by a short cable. He uses his last hand to hold onto a grip.

He tweaks the device with something like a screwdriver, and suddenly the panel lights up. It was completely flat, but now it has texture. He waves the gun part left and right, and the patterns on the screen move with him.

“Success! It works!”

I lean over the pilot’s seat to get a better view. “What is it?”

“Wait.” He points the gun part at my external camera screen. He adjusts a few controls, and the pattern on the rectangle forms a circle. Looking closer, I see some parts of the circle are higher than others. It looks like a raised map.

“This device hear light. Like human eye.”

“Oh. It’s a camera.”

“♫♪♫,” he says quickly. Now we know the word "camera."

“It analyze light and show as texture.”

“Oh, and you can feel that texture?” I say. “Cool.”

“Thank you.” He attaches the camera to the wall of lights and adjusts it to face my main screen. “What wavelengths of light can humans see?”

“All wavelengths between 380 nanometers and 740 nanometers.” Most people don’t know that right away. But most people aren’t junior high teachers with huge charts of the visible spectrum on their classroom walls.

“I understand,” he says. He adjusts a few knobs on his device. “Now I ‘see’ what you see.”

“You’re an amazing engineer.”

He waves a claw, brushing it off. “No. Camera is old. Display is old. Both were on my ship for science. I only changed them to use inside.”

I think Eridians are very modest. Or, Rocky is someone who can’t accept a compliment.

He points to the circle on his display. “This is Adrian?”

I check the exact area of Adrian he’s pointing at, then look at my screen. “Yes, and that part is ‘green.’ ”

“I have no word for this.”

Of course the Eridian language has no words for colors. Why would it? I never thought of colors as strange. But if you’ve never heard of them, I guess they’re pretty weird. We have names for frequency ranges in the electromagnetic spectrum. Then again, my students all have eyes and they were still amazed when I told them “x-rays,” “microwaves,” “Wi-Fi,” and “purple” were all just wavelengths of light.

“Okay, then,” I say.

“Yes, yes. I call this color: middle-rough. My display is smooth for high-frequency light. Rough for low-frequency light. This color is middle-rough.”

“Got it,” I say. “And yes, green is right in the middle of the wavelengths humans can see.”

“Good, good,” he says. “Is sample ready?”

We’ve been in orbit for about a day now, and I turned on the sampler as soon as we got here. I switch to the External Collection Unit screen. It says it's working perfectly and even shows how long it’s been open: 21 hours and 17 minutes.

“Yeah, I guess.”

“You get.”

“Ugh,” I groan. “EVAs are so much work!”

“Lazy human. Go get!”

I laugh. He sounds a little different when he’s joking. It took me a while to figure it out. It’s like…it’s in the timing between words. They don’t have the same rhythm. I can’t really explain it, but I know it when I hear it.

From the External Collection Unit screen, I tell the sampler to close its doors and go back to its flat shape. The panel says it’s done, and I check with the hull cameras.

I climb into the Orlan EVA suit, go into the airlock, and cycle it.

Adrian is even more gorgeous in person. I stay out on the hull staring at the huge world for several minutes. Stripes of dark and light green cover the orb, and the reflected light from Tau Ceti is simply breathtaking. I could stare at it for hours.

I probably got to do this with Earth too. I wish I could remember. Man, I really wish I could remember that. It must have been just as beautiful.

“You out long time,” Rocky’s voice comes through the headset. “You are safe, right?”

I set up the EVA panel to always play my radio feed over speakers in the control room. Plus, I taped a headset microphone to Rocky’s control-room bulb and made it voice-activated. All he has to do is talk and it broadcasts.

“I’m looking at Adrian. It’s pretty.”

“Look later. Get sample now.”

“You’re pushy.”

“Yes.”

I climb along the hull, lit by the Adrian sun. Everything has a light-green tint. I find the sample collector right where it should be.

It’s smaller than I thought. About half a meter square. There’s a lever next to it with red and yellow stripes. The lever reads PULL LEVER TO RELEASE ECU—ПОТЯНУТЬ РЫЧАГ ЧТОБЫ ОСВОБОДИТЬ ECU—拉杆释放 ECU.

I clip a tether to a hole on the unit (probably for this), and pull the lever to the open position.

The sampler floats away from the hull.

I make my way back to the airlock, pulling the sampler. I cycle back in and take off the suit.

“All good, question?” Rocky asks.

“Yes.”

“Good!” Rocky says. “You check with science gear, question?”

“Yes. Now.” I bring up the Centrifuge panel. “Prepare for gravity.”

“Yes, gravity.” He grips handholds with three claws. “For science gear.”

Once the centrifuge spins up, I start working in the lab.

Rocky hurries into his tunnel in the lab ceiling and watches closely. Well, not "watches." Listens closely, I guess.

I put the sampler on the lab table and open one of the panels. This is the side that faced Tau Ceti. I smile at what I see.

I tilt my head back to look at Rocky. "This panel was white when we started; now it's black."

“Not understand.”

"The sampler's color changed to the color of Astrophage. We got a lot of Astrophage."

“Good good!”

Over the next two hours, I scrape everything off both halves of the sampler, putting each group in their own containers. Then I rinse each sample well with water and let the Astrophage settle to the bottom. I'm sure a lot of that sticky stuff came with the Astrophage when I scraped it off, and I want it gone.

I do a series of tests. First, I run some Astrophage through DNA-marker testing to see if they are the same as the Astrophage found on Earth. They are—at least, the markers I checked are the same.

Then I check the total amount of each sample.

“Interesting,” I say.

Rocky perks up. “What’s interesting?”

“Both sides had about the same number of creatures.”

“Not expected,” he says.

“Not expected,” I agree.

One side of the sample pointed toward Tau Ceti, and the other pointed toward Adrian. Astrophage travel to reproduce. For every eager Astrophage that goes to Adrian, two should come back. So, generally, twice as many Astrophage should go from Adrian to Tau Ceti as go the other way. But that’s not happening. The number going out is the same as the number coming in.

Rocky climbs along the tunnel on the lab roof to get a better view. “Mistake in counting? How do you count?”

“I measure the total heat given off by both samples.” It’s a certain way to know how much Astrophage there is. Each one is always 96.415 degrees Celsius. The more there are, the more heat the metal plate I put them on will absorb.

He taps two claws together. “That is good way. Number must be same. How?”

“I don’t know.” I smear some of the “returning” Astrophage (the ones going from Adrian to Tau Ceti) onto a slide. I take it to a microscope.

Rocky hurries through his tunnel to keep up. “That is what, question?”

“Microscope,” I say. “It helps me see very small things. I can see Astrophage with this.”

“Amaze.”

I look at the sample and gasp. There’s more than just Astrophage in there!

The familiar black dots of Astrophage are all over the sample. But so are clear cells, smaller bacteria-looking things, and larger amoeba-like things. There are thin things, fat things, spiral things…too many to count. Too many different kinds of things to count. It’s like looking at all the life in a drop of lake water!

“Wow!” I say. “Life! There’s a lot of life in here! Not just Astrophage. A lot of different species!”

Rocky literally bounces off the tunnel walls. “Amaze! Amaze amaze amaze!”

“Adrian isn’t just a planet,” I say. “Adrian is a planet with life, like Earth or Erid! That explains where the methane comes from. Life makes methane!”

Rocky freezes. Then he shoots straight up. I’ve never seen him raise his shell so high. “Life is also reason for population discrepancy! Life is reason!”

“What?” I say. He’s more excited than I’ve ever seen him. “How? I don’t understand.”

He taps the tunnel wall with his claw, pointing at my microscope. “Life on Adrian EATS Astrophage! Population balanced. Natural order. This explains everything!”

“Oh my God!” I gasp. My heart pounds in my chest. “Astrophage has a predator!”

There’s a whole biosphere on Adrian. Not just Astrophage. There’s even a living biosphere within the Petrova line.

This is where it all began. It has to be. How else can we explain so many different life-forms that all evolved to travel in space? They all came from the same genetic origin.

Astrophage was just one of many life-forms that evolved here. And with all life, there is variety and predation.

Adrian isn’t just a planet that Astrophage infected. It’s the Astrophage home! And it’s the home of Astrophage’s predators.

“This is amazing!” I yell. “If we find a predator…”

“We take it home!” Rocky says, much higher than usual. “It eats Astrophage, breeds, eats more Astrophage, breeds, eats more and more! Stars saved!”

“Yes!” I press my knuckles against the tunnel wall. “Fist-bump!”

“What, question?”

I knock on the tunnel again. “This. Do this.”

He copies my action against the wall across from my hand.

“Celebration!” I say.

“Celebration!”

Chapter 18

The crew of the Hail Mary sat on the couch in the break room, each with their chosen drink.

Commander Yáo had a German beer, Engineer Ilyukhina had a shockingly large glass of vodka, and Science Specialist DuBois had a glass of 2003 Cabernet Sauvignon that he had poured ten minutes earlier to let it breathe.

The break room had been hard to arrange. Stratt didn’t like anything not directly related to the mission, and an aircraft carrier didn’t have much extra space. But with over a hundred scientists from all over the world wanting a place to relax, she gave in. A small room in the corner of the hangar deck was built for this "luxury."

Dozens of people crowded into the room and watched the TV feed on the wall-mounted monitor. Without a word, the crew got to sit on the couch. The crew got every possible benefit. They were giving their lives for humanity. The least we could do was give them the best seats.

“And we’re just minutes from lift-off,” said the BBC reporter. We could have watched American, Chinese, or Russian news; it would have been the same. The wide shot of Baikonur Cosmodrome mixed with shots of the huge rocket on the launchpad.

The reporter stood in the observation room overlooking Moscow’s Mission Control Center. “Today’s launch is the ninth of sixteen for Project Hail Mary, but it may be the most important. This payload contains the cockpit, lab, and sleeping modules. Astronauts on the ISS are ready to receive the modules and will spend the next two weeks putting them on the Hail Mary ’s frame, which was built over the last several trips…”

Ilyukhina raised her vodka. “Don’t mess up my house, Roscosmos jerks!”

“Aren’t they your friends?” I asked.

“They can be both!” She roared with laughter.

The countdown appeared on the screen. Less than a minute left.

Yáo leaned forward, watching closely. It must have been difficult—a soldier used to action forced to simply watch something so important happen.

DuBois noticed Yáo’s face. “I’m sure the launch will be fine, Commander Yáo.”

“Mm,” said Yáo.

“Thirty seconds to launch,” said Ilyukhina. “I can’t wait that long.” She finished her vodka and immediately poured another.

The scientists moved closer as the countdown continued. I was pushed against the back of the couch, but I was too focused on the screen to notice.

DuBois turned to look at me. “Ms. Stratt isn’t joining us?”

“I don’t think so,” I said. “She doesn’t care about things like launches. She’s probably looking at spreadsheets in her office.”

He nodded. “Then it’s good you’re here. To represent her, in a way.”

“Me? Represent her? What makes you say that?”

Ilyukhina turned quickly to face me. “You are number two, yes? You are second in command of Project Hail Mary?”

“What? No! I’m just one of the scientists. Like all these people.” I pointed to the men and women behind me.

Ilyukhina and DuBois looked at each other and then back to me. “You really believe that?” she said.

Bob Redell spoke up behind me. “You’re not like the rest of us, Grace.”

I shrugged at him. “Of course I am. Why wouldn’t I be?”

“The thing is,” DuBois said, “you are, for some reason, special to Ms. Stratt. I thought you two were sleeping together.”

My mouth dropped open. “What?! Are you crazy?! No! No way!”

“Huh,” said Ilyukhina. “Maybe you should? She is tense. She could use a good romp.”

“Oh my God. Is that what people think?” I turned to face the scientists. Most of them looked away. “Nothing like that is happening! And I’m not her second in command! I’m just a scientist—forced into this project like the rest of you!”

Yáo turned around and stared at me for a moment. The room went silent. He didn’t talk much, so when he did, people listened.

“You are the number two,” he said. Then he turned back to the screen.

The BBC announcer counted down the last few seconds along with the timer on the screen. “Three…two…one…and we have lift-off!”

Flames and smoke were all around the rocket on the screen, and it went up into the sky. Slowly at first, then getting faster and faster.

Ilyukhina held up her glass for a few seconds and then cheered loudly. “Tower is clear! Launch is good!” She drank her vodka quickly.

“It’s only a hundred feet off the ground,” I said. “Maybe wait until it gets to orbit?”

DuBois took a sip of his wine. “Astronauts celebrate when the tower is clear.”

Yáo took a sip of his beer without saying anything.

—

“Why. Doesn’t. This. Work?!” I hit my forehead with both hands at each word.

I flop into the lab chair, feeling drained.

Rocky watches from his tunnel above. “No predator, question?”

“No predator.” I sigh.

The experiment is pretty straightforward. It’s a glass bulb filled with Adrian’s air. The air didn’t really come from Adrian, but the gas mix matches the spectrograph of its atmosphere. The pressure is very low—one-tenth of an atmosphere, like Adrian’s upper atmosphere must be.

Also in the bulb are our collected Adrian life-forms and some fresh Astrophage. I hoped that giving them a bunch of nice, juicy Astrophage would make the predator population boom, so I could separate it from the sample once it was the main cell type there.

Didn’t work.

“You are certain, question?”

I check my simple heat sensor. It’s just a thermocouple with one part stuck in ice water and the other part on the bulb. Heat comes from the Astrophage and is used up by the ice. The thermocouple's temperature tells me how much total heat the Astrophage is giving off. If the temperature drops, it means the Astrophage population shrank. But that’s not happening.

“Yeah, I’m sure,” I say. “No change in Astrophage population.”

“Maybe temperature of bulb no good. Too hot. Adrian upper atmosphere is probably much colder than you room temperature.”

I shake my head. “The temperature on Adrian shouldn’t matter. The predator needs to survive the temperature of Astrophage.”

“Ah. Yes. You are right.”

“Maybe the predator idea is wrong,” I say.

He clicks across the tunnel to the other side of the lab. He paces when he’s thinking. It’s interesting that humans and Eridians both do that. “Predators are the only answer. Maybe predators don’t live in the Petrova line. Maybe predators live lower in the atmosphere.”

I get excited. “Maybe.”

I look at the lab screen. It’s showing the view from the outside camera on Adrian. Not for any scientific reason—just because it looks cool. Right now, we’re about to cross into the day side of the planet. The light of dawn shines along a curve.

“Okay, let’s say the predator lives in the atmosphere. How high up?”

“What height is best, question? If you are predator, where do you go, question? You go to Astrophage.”

“Okay, so how high up are the Astrophage?” The question answers itself. “Ah! They breed at a certain height. Where there’s enough carbon dioxide for them to breed.”

“Yes!” He hurries back up his tunnel and stands above me. “We can find it. Easy. Use Petrovascope.”

I slam my fist into my palm. “Yes! Of course!”

Astrophage have to breed somewhere. Some level of carbon dioxide will be essential. But we don’t have to figure that out or make guesses. When an Astrophage divides, it and its offspring go back to Tau Ceti. And they use IR-light emission to do it. That means there will be a glow of Petrova-frequency light coming from all over the planet at that specific height.

“To the control room!” I say.

“Control room!” He hurries across the lab ceiling tunnel and disappears through his personal control-room entrance. I follow beside him, but I’m not quite as fast.
