I climb up the ladder, take the pilot’s seat, and turn on the Petrovascope. Rocky is already in his bulb and points his camera at my main screen.

The entire screen glows red.

“What is this? No data.”

“Wait,” I say. I bring up the controls and options and start moving sliders. “We’re inside the Petrova line. There’s Astrophage all around us. Let me just change the setting to only show the brightest sources….”

It takes a lot of adjusting, but I finally get the brightness range set. What I’m left with are irregular, blotchy areas of IR light coming from Adrian.

“I think this is our answer,” I say.

Rocky leans closer to his grainy screen to "see" what I'm looking at.

"Not what I expected," I say.

I thought it would just be a simple layer of IR light at a certain height. But it’s not like that at all. The clumps are basically clouds. And they don’t line up with the thin white clouds I can see with regular light. These are, for lack of a better word, IR clouds.

Or, more accurately, clouds of Astrophage that are giving off IR light. For some reason, Astrophage multiply much more in some areas than others.

“Strange pattern,” says Rocky, agreeing with me.

“Yes. Maybe the weather affects how they multiply?”

“Maybe. Can you figure out the height, question?”

“Yes. Wait.”

I zoom and move the Petrovascope until I’m looking at an Astrophage cloud right on the edge of Adrian. The readouts show the camera’s current angle compared to the ship's axes. I write those angles down and switch to the navigation console. It tells me the ship's angle compared to the center of our orbit. With that information, and a lot of trigonometry, I can figure out the height of the Astrophage clouds.

“They’re multiplying at a height of 91.2 kilometers from the surface. The width is less than 200 meters.”

Rocky folds one of his claws over the other. I know that body language. He’s thinking. “If there are predators, they’re there.”

“Agreed,” I say. “But how do we get a sample?”

“How close can we orbit, question?”

“One hundred kilometers from the planet. Any closer and the ship will burn up in the atmosphere.”

“This is too bad,” Rocky says. “Eight point eight kilometers from where they breed. We can’t get closer, question?”

“If we hit the atmosphere at orbital speed, we die. But what if we slow down?”

“Slowing down means we can’t orbit. We fall into the air. We die.”

I lean over the armrest to look at him. “We can use the engines to keep from falling into the atmosphere. Just keep thrusting away from the planet. Lower ourselves into the atmosphere, get a sample, and then leave.”

“Won’t work. We’ll die.”

“Why won’t it work?”

Engines give off huge IR light. If used in air, air becomes ions. Explosion. Destroy ship.

I wince. “Right, of course.”

When Dimitri first tested a spin drive, it was only on for 100 microseconds, and it melted a metric ton of metallic silicon behind it. And that test drive was one-thousandth the power of the Hail Mary’s engines. Everything works fine when I’m in a vacuum. But using the engines in air would create a fireball that makes a nuclear bomb look like a firecracker.

We sit in frustrated silence for a while. The salvation of both our worlds might be just 10 kilometers below us, and we can’t get to it. There has to be a way. But how? We don’t even need to be there. We just need to get a sample of the air there. Anything, no matter how small.

Wait a minute.

“How do you make xenonite again? You mix two liquids?”

Rocky is caught off guard by the question, but he answers. “Yes. Have liquid and liquid. Mix. They become xenonite.”

“How much can you make? How much of those liquids did you bring?”

“I bring much. I use to make my zone.”

I bring up a spreadsheet and start typing in numbers. “We need 0.4 cubic meters of xenonite. Can you make that much?”

“Yes,” he says. “We need enough liquid to fill 0.61 cubic meters.”

“Okay. Then I have…an idea.” I press my fingertips together.

—

It’s a simple idea, but also dumb. But when dumb ideas work, they become genius ideas. We’ll see what happens with this one.

The Astrophage breeding grounds are 10 kilometers into Adrian’s atmosphere. I can’t fly the Hail Mary that low because the air is too thick and I’d burn up. I can’t use the engines in the atmosphere because that’ll cause a disaster and everything will blow up.

So, it’s time to go fishing. We’re going to make a 10-kilometer-long chain, put a sampling device on the end (Rocky will make that), and drag it through the atmosphere. Sounds easy, right?

Wrong.

The Hail Mary has to stay at 12.6 kilometers per second to stay in orbit. Any slower and we’ll fall and burn up. But if we drag a chain through the air at that speed—even a xenonite chain—it’ll be torn apart and vaporized.

So we have to go slower. But going slower means falling toward the planet. Unless I use the engines to keep us at the same height. But if I do that, I’d be blasting directly away from the chain and sample device. The engine exhaust will vaporize all of it.

So we’ll push at an angle. Simple as that.

It’ll look completely absurd. The Hail Mary will be angled 30 degrees from straight up, pushing upward at that angle. Below it, the chain will hang 10 kilometers straight down. The air behind the thrusters will constantly be on fire. It should be quite a sight. But it’ll be behind us, and the chain will be passing through normal air.

In the end, our sideways speed will be just over 100 meters per second. The chain can handle that speed in the thin high-altitude air, no problem. I figured it’ll only bend about 2 degrees from straight down.

Once we think we have a sample, we get out of there. What could possibly go wrong!

I’m being sarcastic.

I’m not the best at 3-D modeling, but I can make a chain link in CAD pretty well. It’s not a regular oval link, though. It’s mostly oval, but with a small opening for another link to fit into. Easy to put the links together, but very unlikely for them to come apart on their own. Especially when they’re pulled tight.

I grab a block of aluminum and put it in the milling machine.

“This will work, right?” Rocky asks from his ceiling tunnel.

“It should,” I say.

I start the milling machine, and it gets right to work. It drills out the mold for a chain link exactly as I’d planned.

I pull the piece out, dust off the aluminum bits, and hold it up to the tunnel. “How’s this?”

“Very good!” Rocky says. “We will need many many many chain links. More molds mean I can make more at once. You can make many molds, yes?”

“Well.” I look in the supply cabinet. “I don’t have much aluminum.”

“You have many things in ship you don’t use. Two beds in dormitory, for instance. Melt them, make blocks, make more molds.”

“Wow. You really go all out, don’t you?”

“No understand.”

“I’m not going to melt a bunch of stuff. How would I even do that?”

“Astrophage. Melt anything.”

“You got me there,” I say. “But no. The heat would be too much for my life support to handle. That reminds me. Why do you have so much extra Astrophage?”

He pauses. “Strange story.”

I perk up. Always ready for a strange story. He clicks along his tunnel and sits in a slightly wider spot. “Science Eridians do lots of math. Figure out trip. More fuel means faster trip. So we make lots and lots of Astrophage.”

“How’d you make so much? Earth had a very hard time making it.”

“Was easy. Put in metal balls with carbon dioxide. Put in ocean. Wait. Astrophage double, double, double. Lots of Astrophage.”

“Riiight. Because your oceans are hotter than Astrophage.”

“Yes. Earth oceans are not. Sad.”

When it comes to making Astrophage, Erid had a huge advantage. The whole planet is like a pressure cooker. Twenty-nine atmospheres at 210 degrees Celsius means water is liquid on the surface. And their oceans are far, far hotter than the temperature Astrophage needs. They just put Astrophage in the water, let it absorb heat, and grow.

I’m jealous. We had to cover the Sahara Desert to grow our Astrophage. All they had to do was throw it in the water. The stored heat energy of Erid’s oceans is amazing. A huge amount of water—much more than Earth’s total oceans—holding a temperature around 200 degrees Celsius or more. That’s a lot of energy.

And that’s why they can take a century or so to solve the problem while Earth is going to freeze in a few decades. It’s not just their air storing heat. Their oceans store even more. Huge advantage. Again.

“Science Eridians design ship and fuel needs. Journey to take 6.64 years.”

That confuses me for a moment. 40 Eridani is ten light-years away from Tau Ceti, so you can’t get from one to the other in less than ten years from Erid’s view. He must mean 6.64 years of time felt by his ship because of time slowing down.

“Strange things happen on trip. Crew sick. Die.” His voice lowers. “Now I know was radiation.”

I look down and give him a moment.

“Everyone sick. I alone to run ship. More strange things happen. Engines not work right. I am engine expert. I cannot figure out problem.”

“Your engines failed?”

“No. Not fail. Thrust normal. But speed…not increase. No can explain.”

“Huh.”

He clatters back and forth as he talks. “Then more strange: Reach halfway point earlier than should. Much earlier. I turn ship around. Thrust to slow down. But Tau get farther away. How? Still moving toward Tau but Tau moving away. Much confusion.”

“Uh-oh,” I say. A thought creeps into my head. A very disturbing thought.

“I speed up. Slow down. Much confuse. But get here. Even with all mistakes and confusion, I get here in three years. Half of time science Eridian say should be. So much confuse.”

“Oh…oh my…” I mumble.

“Much, much, much fuel left. Much more than needed. Not complaining. But confused.”

“Yeah…” I say. “Tell me this: Is time on Erid the same as time on your ship?”

He cocks his carapace. “Question makes no sense. Of course, time is the same. Time is the same everywhere.”

I put my head in my hands. “Oh boy.”

Eridians don’t know about relativistic physics.

They calculated their entire journey with Newtonian physics. They figured it all out by assuming they could just accelerate faster and faster and the speed of light wasn’t a problem.

They don’t know about time dilation. Rocky doesn’t realize that Erid experienced much more time than he did on that trip. They don’t know about length dilation. The distance to Tau Ceti will actually increase as you slow down relative to it—even if you’re still going toward it.

An entire planet of intelligent people put together a ship based on wrong scientific assumptions, and by some miracle, the sole survivor of the crew was clever enough at trial-and-error problem solving to actually get it to its destination.

And out of that major screw-up comes my salvation. They thought they’d need a whole lot more fuel. So Rocky has tons to spare.

“Okay, Rocky,” I say. “Get comfortable. I have a lot of science to explain.”

He knocked twice and peeked into my office. “Dr. Grace? Are you Dr. Grace?”

It wasn’t a large office, but any personal space was precious on an aircraft carrier. Before it became my office, the room was a storage locker for bathroom supplies. The crew had three thousand people who needed toilet paper daily. I got to use the room as my office until the next time we were in port, then they’d fill it up with more supplies.

I was about as important as toilet paper.

I looked up from my laptop. The short, slightly messy man at the door waved awkwardly.

“Yeah,” I said. “I’m Grace. You are…?”

“Hatch. Steve Hatch. University of British Columbia. Nice to meet you.”

I pointed to the folding chair in front of the folding table I used as a desk.

He shuffled in, carrying a round metal object. I’d never seen anything like it. He plopped it on my table.

I looked at the object. It was like someone had flattened a basketball, then stuck a triangle on one end and a four-sided shape on the other.

He sat in the chair and stretched his arms. “Man, that was weird. I’ve never been on a helicopter before. Have you? Well, of course you have. How else would you get here? I mean, I guess you could have used a boat, but probably not. I hear they keep the carrier far away from land in case something goes wrong during the Astrophage experiments. A boat would have been nicer, honestly, that helicopter ride almost made me puke. But I’m not complaining. I’m just happy to be here.”

“Um”—I pointed to the object on my desk—“what is this thing?”

He seemed to get even more excited. “Ah, right! That’s a beetle! Well, a practice version, anyway. My team and I think we’ve worked out most of the problems. Well, you never get all the problems worked out, but we’re ready to test the engines for real. And the university said we had to do that here on the carrier. Also the government of British Columbia said it. Oh, and the national government of Canada said it too. I’m Canadian, by the way. But don’t worry! I’m not one of those Canadians who hate America. I think you guys are all right.”

“Beetle?”

“Yeah!” He picked it up and turned the four-sided shape toward me. “This is how the Hail Mary crew will send us back the information. It’s a little spacecraft that can fly itself back to Earth from Tau Ceti. Well, from anywhere, really. That’s what my team and I have been working on for the past year.”

I peek into the four-sided shape and see a shiny, glasslike surface. “Is that a spin drive?” I asked.

“Sure is! Man, those Russians know their stuff. We just used their designs and everything worked out great. At least, I think it did. We haven’t tested the spin drive yet. The hard part is getting it to navigate and steer.”

He turned the device around and pointed the triangular head toward me. “This is where the cameras and computer are. No complicated navigation systems. It uses regular light to see the stars. It finds constellations and figures out where it is from that.” He tapped the middle of the rounded shell. “There’s a little DC generator in here. As long as we have Astrophage, we have power.”

“What can it carry?” I ask.

“Data. It’s got a backup system with more storage than anyone could use.” He knocked on the dome. It echoed slightly. “Most of this thing is fuel. It’ll need about 125 kilos of Astrophage to make the trip. Sounds like a lot but…man…twelve light-years!”

I lifted the device and held it in my hands a couple of times. “How does it turn?”

“Reaction wheels inside,” he said. “It spins them one way, the ship turns the other. Easy-peasy.”

“Interstellar navigation is ‘easy-peasy’?” I smiled.

He chuckled. “Well, for what we have to do, yeah. It has a receiver that’s always listening for a signal from Earth. When it hears that signal, it’ll send out its location and wait for instructions from the Deep Space Network. We don’t have to be super exact with the navigation. We just need it to show up within radio range of Earth. Anywhere within the orbit of Saturn or so will do.”

I nod. “And then scientists can tell it exactly how to get back. Clever.”

He shrugged. “They’ll probably do that, yeah. But they don’t have to. They’ll have it radio over all the data first thing. The information gets across. Then they can pick it up later if they want. Oh, and we’re making four of these. All we need is for one of them to survive the trip.”

I turned the beetle this way and that. It was surprisingly light. A few pounds at most. “Okay, so there are four of these. How likely is each one to survive the trip? Is there at least a little backup aboard?”

He shrugged. “Not much, no. But it doesn’t have to travel for nearly as long as the Hail Mary does. So things don’t have to last as long.”

“It’s going the same route, right?” I asked. “Why doesn’t it take the same time?”

“Because the Hail Mary can’t speed up as fast, since it has fragile humans inside. The beetle doesn’t have that problem. Everything on it is made of tough, military-grade parts that can handle a lot of force. So it gets to near-light speed much faster.”

“Oh, interesting…” I wondered if this would be a good question for my students. I quickly decided against it. The math was way too hard for any eighth grader.

“Yeah,” Hatch said. “They speed up at five hundred g’s until they’re cruising at 0.93 c. It’ll take over twelve years to get back to Earth, but for them, it’ll only feel like twenty months. Do you believe in God? Sorry for asking. I do. And I think He was pretty amazing to make relativity a thing, don’t you? The faster you go, the less time you feel. It’s like He’s asking us to explore the universe, you know?”

He stopped talking and looked at me.

“Well,” I said. “This is really amazing. Good job.”

“Thanks!” he said. “So can I have some Astrophage to test it?”

“Sure,” I said. “How much do you want?”

“How about a hundred milligrams?”

I hesitated. “Hold on. That’s a lot of energy.”

“Okay, okay. Can’t blame me for asking. How about one milligram?”

“Yeah, I can do that.”

He clapped. “Hell yeah! Astrophage headed my way!” He leaned towards me. “Isn’t it amazing? Astrophage, I mean? It’s like…the coolest thing ever! Again, God’s just giving us the future!”

“Cool?” I said. “It’s a disaster that could wipe us all out. If anything, God’s handing us the end of the world.”

He shrugged. “I mean, maybe a little. But man. Perfect for storing energy! Imagine a house powered by a battery. Like—you have a double-A battery, but full of Astrophage. That’d power your house for about a hundred thousand years. Imagine buying a car and never needing to charge it? The whole idea of power grids will disappear. And it’ll all be clean, green energy once we start growing the stuff on the moon or something. All it needs is sunlight!”

“Clean? Renewable?” I said. “Are you saying Astrophage will be… good for the environment? Because it won’t be. Even if Hail Mary finds a fix, we’re facing a mass extinction. Twenty years from now, many species on Earth will be gone. And we’re working hard to make sure humans aren’t one of them.”

He waved off my comment. “Earth’s had five mass extinctions before. And humans are smart. We’ll make it through.”

“We’ll starve!” I said. “Billions of people are going to starve.”

“Naaaah,” he said. “We’re already saving up food. We’ve got a lot of methane in the air to trap the sunlight. It’ll be all right. As long as Hail Mary works.”

I just stared at him for a moment. “You are, without a doubt, the most positive person I’ve ever met.”

He gave me a double thumbs-up. “Thanks!”

He picked up the beetle and turned to leave. “Come on, Pete, let’s get you some Astrophage!”

“Pete?” I asked.

He looked over his shoulder. “Yeah. I’m naming them after the Beatles. The British rock group.”

“You’re a fan, I guess?”

He turned back to face me. “Fan? Oh, yes. I don’t want to overstate it, but Sgt. Pepper’s Lonely Hearts Club Band is the best music ever made. I know, I know. Some people wouldn’t agree. But they’re wrong.”

“Fair enough,” I said. “But why Pete? Aren’t the Beatles named John, Paul, George, and Ringo?”

“Right. And that’s what we’ll call the ones on the Hail Mary. But this guy is for testing near Earth. I get a whole SpaceX launch just for me! Isn’t that amazing! Anyway, I named him after Pete Best—he played drums for the Beatles before Ringo.”

“Okay, I didn’t know that,” I said.

“Now you do. I’m gonna get that Astrophage now. I’ve got to make sure these beetles will be able to…‘Get Back . ’ ”

“Okay.”

He frowned. “ ‘Get Back.’ It’s a song. By the Beatles.”

“Sure. Okay.”

He turned and left. “Some people just don’t appreciate the classics.”

I was left confused. Pretty sure I wasn’t the first.

Chapter 19

Rocky was stunned by relativity. At first, he just didn't believe me. But as I explained more about how it made sense of his trip, he started to accept it. He doesn't like it, but he understands that the universe follows rules that are more complex than we realize.

And since then, we’ve spent forever making chain.

I made molds as fast as I could, and Rocky made links as fast as the xenonite hardened. It was a good system—one where the results grew quickly. Each new mold I made added one to the number of links Rocky could make at a time.

Chain, chain, chain.

If I never see another chain, it will be too soon. Ten kilometers of chain—each link only 5 centimeters long. That’s two hundred thousand links. Each one connected by hand or claw. We each worked eight hours a day for two weeks just connecting links.

I saw chain whenever I closed my eyes. I dreamed about chain every night. One time I had spaghetti for dinner, and all I could see were smooth, white chains instead of noodles.

But we finished it.

Once we had all the links, we put them together side by side. We each made ten-meter lengths that we linked into twenties, and so on. At least we could be efficient that way. The hard part was finding a place to put it all. Ten kilometers is a lot of chain.

The lab became a storage area. But even then, it wasn’t big enough. Rocky—always a talented engineer—made large spools that barely fit through the airlock. After a lot of spacewalks, I attached them to the hull. Then I stored the chain on them in 500-meter sections. But to do spacewalks, I had to stop the centrifuge. So everything from then on was in zero g.

Ever put together a chain in zero gravity? It’s not easy.

The final assembly of those 500-meter pieces was tough, to say the least. I had to connect all twenty of them while in my spacesuit. Luckily, I had the manipulator tool from the IVME. NASA didn’t plan for it to make chains, but that’s what I used it for.

Now Rocky and I float in the control room. He’s in his pod and I’m in my pilot’s seat.

“Probe status?” I say.

Rocky checks his readouts. “Device is working.”

Rocky did a good job on the sampler probe. At least, I think he did. Engineering isn’t my strong suit.

The sampler is a steel ball, 20 centimeters across. It has a good, thick ring on top that connects to the chain. Small holes go through the ball around its middle. They lead to an empty space inside. There’s a pressure sensor in there and a few switches. The pressure sensor knows when the probe is at the right height, and will tell the switch to seal off the space. It’s easy to do: just turn the inner shell a little to move the holes out of line with the holes in the outer ball. That, with some good seals, will trap the local air in the space.

He also added a thermometer and heater in there. Once the sampler seals, the heater will keep the air inside at the same temperature it started at. Simple stuff, really, but I hadn’t thought of it. Life can be pretty sensitive to temperature changes.

The only thing left is a small radio transmitter that sends out a strange signal I couldn’t read or figure out with my gear. Apparently it’s a very common Eridian data connection. But he has the receiver for it and that’s what matters.

Just like that, with very little trouble, Rocky had made a life-support system for Adrian life-forms—a system that didn’t need to know what conditions to provide ahead of time. It just keeps things as they are.

He really is a genius. I wonder if all Eridians are like that, or if he’s special.

“I guess…we’re ready?” I say. I’m not exactly full of confidence.

“Yes,” he quavers.

I strap myself into the pilot’s seat. He uses three of his hands to grip handholds in his bulb.

I bring up the Attitude Control panel and start a roll. Once I have the ship pointed backward to where we're going and parallel to the ground below, I stop the rotation. Now we’re hurtling along, butt-first, at 12 kilometers per second. I need that to be almost zero.

“Orientation is good,” I say. “Starting thrust.”

“Yes,” says Rocky. He watches his readout screen intently. It shows him the textured version of my own screen, thanks to that camera he set up earlier.

“Here goes…” I fire up the spin drives. We go from zero g to 1.5 g’s in under a second. I am pressed back in my chair and Rocky grabs a support with a fourth hand to stay steady.

As the Hail Mary slows down, our speed can no longer keep us in orbit. I glance at the Radar panel and it confirms that we are losing altitude. I adjust the ship’s angle so we are pointing very slightly upward from horizontal. Just a fraction of a degree.

Even that small amount is too much! The radar shows us gaining altitude rapidly. I bring the angle back down. This is a sloppy, nasty, horrible way to fly a spacecraft, but it’s all I have. There was no point to calculating this maneuver in advance. There are so many variables and ways to mess up the math I’d be flying on manual almost immediately anyway.

After a few more tries, I get the hang of it. I increase the angle little by little as the ship slows down compared to the planet.

“You say when to drop probe,” Rocky says. His claw hangs over the button that will shoot out the spools and let the chain drop. We just have to hope it doesn’t get stuck.

“Not yet,” I say.

The angle screen shows we’re at 9 degrees from the flat. I need to get us to 60. Something catches my eye to the right. It’s the outside camera view. The planet below is…shining.

No. Not the whole planet. Just the part right behind us. It’s the air reacting to the IR blast from the engines. The Hail Mary is putting out hundreds of thousands of times more energy into that spot than Tau Ceti does.

The IR heats the air so much it breaks apart and it’s glowing red. The brightness gets stronger as our angle gets sharper. Then the glowing area starts to grow. I knew it would be a lot, but I didn't know it would be this much. We’re leaving a red line across the sky, destroying everything in the air. The carbon dioxide is probably being broken apart by the heat into carbon bits and free oxygen. The oxygen might not even be forming O2. That’s a lot of heat.

“The engines are heating up Adrian’s air a lot,” I say.

“How you know, question?”

“Sometimes I can see heat.”

“What, question?! Why you no tell me this, question?”

“It’s about sight…there’s no time to explain. Just trust me: We are making the air very hot.”

“Danger, question?”

“I don’t know.”

“I don’t like that answer.”

We go up and up and up. The light behind us gets brighter and brighter. Finally, we reach the right angle.

“Angle achieved,” I say.

“Happy! Release, question?”

“Stand by. Speed…”—I check the navigation screen—“127.5 meters per second! Just what I figured! Wow, it worked!”

I feel Adrian pull me back into my seat.

This is something I often have to explain to my students. Gravity doesn’t just “disappear” when you’re in orbit. Actually, the gravity you feel in orbit is almost the same as on the ground. The weightlessness astronauts feel in orbit is from constantly falling. But the curve of the Earth makes the ground move away at the same rate you fall. So you just fall forever.

The Hail Mary isn’t failing. The engines keep us in the sky, and our angle makes us move forward at 127 meters per second—about 285 miles per hour. Fast for a car, but surprisingly slow for a spaceship.

The air behind us glows so brightly that the outside camera shuts down to protect itself.

The Life Support panel appears on my main screen by itself. EXTERNAL TEMPERATURE EXTREME, it warns.

“The air is hot,” I say. “The ship is hot.”

“Ship no touch air,” Rocky says. “Why ship hot, question?”

“It’s reflecting our IR back at us. And it’s so hot now it’s creating its own IR. We’re getting baked.”

“Your ship Astrophage-cooled, question?”

“Yes. Astrophage cools the ship.”

Astrophage pipes run all along the hull for times like this. Well, not for “blasting a planet’s atmosphere with so much IR light it can melt steel,” but for the usual situations where heat builds up. Mostly from the sun or Tau Ceti heating the ship, and the heat having nowhere to go.

“Astrophage absorb heat. We safe.”

“Okay. We’re safe. And ready. Drop probe!”

“Drop probe!” He slams his claw on the Drop button.

I hear the scrape and clink of the spools sliding off the hull one by one and falling toward the planet. Twenty spools in total, each one drops and unwinds before the next is released. Our best shot at keeping the chain from tangling.

“Spool Six away…” Rocky says.

The Life Support panel blinks its warning again. I mute it again. Astrophage lives on stars. I’m sure a little reflected IR light won’t be too hot for it.

“Spool Twelve away…” Rocky says. “Sampler signal good. Sampler detecting air now.”

“Good!” I say.

“Good good,” he says. “Spool Eighteen away…air density increasing…”

With the external cameras offline, I can’t see what’s happening. But Rocky’s readings match our plan. Right now, the chain is unfurling as it falls. Our angled engines keep us in the sky, but nothing stops the chain from falling straight down.

“Spool Twenty away. All spools released. Air density of sampler is almost like an Astrophage breeding ground…”

I watch Rocky with bated breath.

“Sampler has closed! Seal is airtight, heater is on! Success success success!”

“Success!” I yell.

It’s working! It’s actually working! We have a sample of Adrian air from where the Astrophage breed! If there are any predators, they have to be there, right? I hope so.

“Step two now.” I sigh. This is not going to be fun.

I unhitch my restraints and climb out of the chair. Adrian’s gravity, 1.4 times Earth's, pulls me down at an angle. The whole room feels tilted because it is tilted. This isn’t engine thrust I’m feeling. It’s gravity.

One point four g’s isn’t too bad. Everything’s a bit harder, but not too much. I climb into the Orlan EVA suit. This is going to be difficult, to say the least. I have to go outside and do an EVA while completely under the effects of gravity.

Of course, no part of the EVA suit, the airlock, or my training was designed for this. Who would have thought I would have to walk around on the ship in full gravity? More than full, in fact?

Yet however much gravity there may be, there’s still no air. The worst possible situation. But there’s no other way. I have to get the sample.

Right now, the sampler hangs at the end of a 10-kilometer chain, just dangling in the air. There’s no easy way for us to get it back to the ship.

When planning this all out, my first thought was to blast off from the planet, then grab the sampler when we’re back to zero g. Problem is, there’s just no way to do that without destroying the sampler. Any way I try to get the ship out of Adrian’s gravity—or even into a stable orbit—will mean using the spin drives. They’d push the ship along, which would make the chain and sample fall behind us into the IR blast from the ship. And then the sampler, everything in it, and the chain all become individual, very hot atoms.

The next idea I had was to make a huge spool that could winch up the chain. But Rocky told me he’d never be able to make a spool big enough and strong enough to bring up the entire 10-kilometer length.

Rocky had a pretty smart idea: The sampler could climb the chain when it was done. But after some testing he dropped the idea. He said the risks just weren’t worth it.

So we have…this other plan.

I grab a special winch Rocky designed and attach it to my suit’s tool belt.

“Be careful,” says Rocky. “You are friend now.”

“Thanks,” I say. “You are friend also.”

“Thank.”

I cycle the airlock and look outside.

This is a strange experience. Space is black. The planet below me is grand. Everything looks right for being in orbit. But there's gravity.

A red glow from the planet shows around the edges of the Hail Mary. I'm not dumb—I turned the ship so it would protect me from the deadly heat coming off the atmosphere.

The airlock door is "up." I have to pull myself—and a hundred pounds of gear—up and through that opening. And I have to do it in 1.4 g.

It takes me five whole minutes. I grunt. I say some things that aren't quite swear words, but I get it done. Soon I’m standing on top of my ship. One wrong step and I’ll fall to my death. It wouldn't take long, either. If I fell below the ship, the engines would end me fast.

I attach a tether to the handrail at my feet. Will a zero-g tether save me if I fall? It’s not climbing gear. It wasn't made for this. But it’s better than nothing, I guess.

I walk along the hull toward the chain anchor. It’s a big xenonite square that Rocky made. He explained in detail how to stick it to the hull. Looks like it worked. The chain is still attached.

I reach it and get on my hands and knees. The gravity is really hard in this EVA suit. None of this is how it should be.

I hook my (maybe useless) tether to the closest handrail and pull the winch from my tool belt.

The chain hangs at a 30-degree angle and goes into the planet below. It goes so far that it's too thin to see after a kilometer or so. But I know from Rocky’s readings it’s the full 10 kilometers down, with a sample container full of what could save two whole planets of people.

I fit the winch between the chain and the anchor plate. The chain doesn’t move—not even a millimeter. But that was expected. There’s just no way human strength could move something that heavy.

I hook the winch to the anchor plate. The winch casing is xenonite, so the xenonite connection should be strong enough for what comes next.

I tap the winch a couple of times just to make sure it’s in place. It is.

Then I press the start button.

A gear comes out from the center of the winch, one tooth catching a chain link in the middle. The gear turns and pulls the chain into the winch. Inside, it turns the link around and slides it across its neighbor to release it.

When we made the chain, we used “trap” links that connect without us having to seal each one. It’s very unlikely the links would come apart on their own. But the winch is made to do just that.

Once the link is free, the winch pushes it out the side and does the same for the next link.

“The winch works,” I say into my radio.

“Happy,” Rocky says.

It’s simple, clear, neat, and solves all the problems. The winch is strong enough to lift the chain. It separates the links and lets them fall to the planet below. Having a long chain hanging down next to the one we’re pulling up would be a disaster. Imagine tangled earbud wires, but 10 kilometers long.

No, each link will fall away on its own, and the rising chain will keep going.

“When the winch reaches link two hundred sixteen, speed up.”

“Yes.”

I don’t know how many links have gone through. But it’s moving steadily. Maybe two links every second. A careful, slow start. I watch for two minutes. That seems right. “It’s working. At least two hundred sixteen links are done.”

“Speed up.”

Two links a second might sound fast, but it would take about thirty hours to raise the chain at that rate. I don’t want to be out here that long, and we don’t want to stay in this risky, constant-thrust position for that long. I push the control lever forward. The winch speeds up. Everything looks okay, so I put it in the final spot.

Now the links come out of the winch too fast to count, and the chain rises quickly.

“The winch is at top speed. Everything’s fine.”

“Good.”

I keep my hand on the control lever and watch the chain. If that sampler reaches the winch, everything will go wrong. The sample container will break, all the samples will die, and we’d have to make another chain.

I don’t want to do that. God, I can't say how much I don’t want to do that.

I squint into the distance, always watching. Boredom is a real problem here. I know it will take a while to pull up this whole chain, but I have to be ready for the sampler.

“Sampler device radio signal strong,” Rocky says. “Getting closer. Be ready.”

“I’m ready.”

“Be very ready.”

“I am very ready. Calm down.”

“Am calm. You calm down.”

“No, you calm dow—wait. I see the sampler!”

The end of the chain, with the sampler attached, rushes toward me from the planet below. I grab the control lever and slow the winch. The sampler climbs slower and slower until it’s barely moving. All but the last few links of the chain fall and the sampler is finally within reach. I stop the winch.

Instead of risking dropping the big orb, I grab the top link of the chain and unhook it from the winch. Now I have a ball and chain. I hold on to the chain tightly and clip it to my belt. I still don’t let go. I’m not taking any chances with this.

“Status, question?”

“I have the sample. Returning.”

“Great! Happy happy happy!”

“Don’t celebrate until I’m inside!”

“Understood.”

I take two steps and the ship shakes. I fall to the floor and grab two handrails.

“What the heck was that?!”

“I don't know. Ship moved. Suddenly.”

The ship shakes again, this time it’s a steady pull. “We’re going the wrong way!”

“Get inside fast fast fast!”

The horizon comes into view. The Hail Mary isn’t holding its position anymore. It’s tipping forward. That's definitely not right.

I scramble from grip to grip. I don’t have time to clip the tether each time. I just have to hope I don’t fall.

Another sudden jolt and the hull slides sideways under me. I fall on my back but keep holding tight to the sampler chain. What is happening?! No time to think. I have to get inside before the ship flips and kills me.

I hold onto the grips for dear life and crawl to the airlock. Thank God it’s still mostly facing up. I hold the sampler to my chest and fall inside. I land headfirst. Lucky for me the Orlan helmet is so strong.

I struggle to my feet as best I can in the bulky spacesuit. I reach up, grab the outer hatch, and slam it shut. I cycle the airlock and get out of the suit as fast as I can. I’ll leave the sampler in the airlock for now. I need to know what is wrong with the ship.

I half climb, half fall into the control room. Rocky is in his bulb.

“Screens flash many colors!” he yells over the noise. He points his camera around, watching the feed on his textured screen.

A metallic groan screams from somewhere below. Something is bending and doesn’t want to. I think it’s the hull.

I get in the control seat. No time to strap in. “Where’s that noise coming from?”

“All around,” he says. “But loudest at starboard dormitory wall. It bending inward.”

“Something’s tearing the ship apart! It has to be the gravity.”

“Agree.”

But that bothers me. This ship was made for speeding up. It lasted four years at 1.5 g’s. It should handle this same force, right? Something’s wrong.

Rocky grabs his handholds to stay put. “We have sample. We leave now.”

“Yeah, let’s get out of here!” I crank the spin-drive controls all the way up. The ship can hit 2 g’s if it has to. And it definitely has to now.

The ship jumps forward. This isn't a smooth, planned takeoff. This is just running away in a panic.

The smart way to escape a gravity well is sideways, using the Oberth effect. I try to keep us mostly level with the ground. I'm not trying to get away from Adrian. I just want a stable orbit that doesn't need engines to stay up. I need speed, not distance.

I need to keep the drives at full power for ten minutes. That should give us the 12 kilometers per second we need to stay in orbit. I just need to point a little above the horizon and go.

That’s what I want, anyway. But it’s not working. The ship keeps turning forward and drifting sideways. What’s happening?!

“Something wrong,” I say. “It’s fighting me.”

Rocky holds on easily. He's way stronger than me. "Engine problem? Adrian causing lots of heat."

"Maybe." I look at the Nav console. We're speeding up. That's good, at least.

"Ship bending in large space under sleeping area," Rocky says.

"What? There's no space below—oh." He can feel the whole ship with his echolocation. Not just where we live. So, "large space under the sleeping area" means the fuel tanks.

Oh no.

"Turn off engines?"

"We're too slow. We'll fall into the atmosphere."

"Okay. Hope."

"Hope." Yes, hope. That's all we have now. Hope that the ship doesn't break apart before we get into a stable orbit.

The next few minutes are the most nerve-wracking of my life. And, I've had some pretty nerve-wracking moments lately. The ship keeps making awful noises, but we're still alive, so I guess it didn't break. Finally, after what feels like way more than ten minutes, we're fast enough to stay in orbit.

“Speed good. Engines off.” I slide the spin-drive power sliders to zero. I let my head fall back to the headrest in relief. Now we can take our time and figure out what went wrong. No need to use the engines to…

Wait.

My head fell back into the headrest. It fell back into the headrest.

I hold my arms out in front of me, then relax them. They fall down and to the left.

“Uh…”

“Gravity still here,” says Rocky, echoing my own observations.

I check the Nav console. Our speed is good. We’re in a stable orbit around Adrian. Well, actually it’s ugly as heck—the highest point is 2,000 kilometers farther from the planet than the lowest point. But it’s an orbit, darn it. And it’s stable.

I check the Spin Drive panel again. All three drives are at zero. No thrust at all. I go into the diagnostics screen and confirm that each of the 1,009 revolver triangles spread throughout the three drives is stationary. They are.

I let my arm fall again. It does the same strange movement. Down and to the left.

Rocky does a similar motion with one of his arms. “Adrian gravity, maybe?”

“No. We’re in orbit.” I scratch my head.

“Spin drive, question?”

“No. It’s off. There’s no thrust.”

I let my arm fall again. This time it hits the seat's armrest.

“Ow!” I shake my hand. That really hurt.

I let it fall again as a test. It fell faster this time. That’s why it hurt.

Rocky pulls several tools from his jumpsuit and drops them one by one. “Gravity increasing.”

“This doesn’t make any sense!” I say.

I check the Nav panel again. Our speed has increased a lot since I last looked. “Our speed is increasing!”

“Engines on. Only explanation.”

“No way. The spin drives are off. We shouldn't be speeding up!”

“Force increasing,” he says.

“Yes,” I say. I’m struggling to breathe. Whatever force we're experiencing, it’s way more than a g or two. This is getting out of control.

Using all my strength, I reach for the screen and flip through the displays. Navigation, Petrovascope, External View, Life Support…everything looks fine. Until I get to “Structure.”

I'd never really looked at the Structure panel. It's just a gray outline of the ship. But now, for the first time, it shows something.

There’s an odd red spot on the left fuel tank. Is that a hole? It could be. The fuel tanks are outside the part that holds air. They could have a huge hole and we wouldn’t lose air.

“There’s a hole in the ship…” I say. I try to switch back to the external cameras.

Rocky watches my screen with his camera and texture pad. He’s doing great—no problems from the huge forces.

I move the cameras to show the damaged part of the ship.

And there it is. A huge hole in the left side of the ship. It must be 20 meters long and half as wide. The edges of the hole show what happened—the hull melted.

It was the backlash from Adrian’s atmosphere. Not a real explosion, but just infrared light bouncing off the air. The ship tried to warn me the hull was too hot. I should have listened.

I thought the hull couldn’t melt. Astrophage cooled it! But it can melt. Even if Astrophage perfectly absorbs heat (and maybe it does), the heat must pass through the metal to be absorbed. If the hull's outer layer melts before the heat can pass through it, the Astrophage can’t help.

“Confirmed. Hull breach. Port fuel tank.”

“Why thrust, question?”

It clicks. “Oh crap! The Astrophage in the fuel bay! It’s exposed to space! It can see Adrian! My fuel is going to Adrian to breed!”

“Bad bad bad!”

That’s the thrust source. Trillions of horny Astrophages, ready to breed. They see Adrian all at once. Not just carbon dioxide, but their homeland. The planet they evolved to find over billions of years.

As each new Astrophage layer rushes toward Adrian, the next layer gets exposed. The ship is pushed by the departing Astrophage's IR thrust. Luckily, the Astrophage behind them absorb the energy. But they also absorb the momentum.

It’s not perfect. It’s a chaotic, sputtering explosion. Any second, this could become a larger, less controlled IR plume, and we’ll be vaporized. I must stop this.

I can jettison fuel bays! I saw that the first day in the control room! Where was it…?

It takes all my strength to lift my arm to the screen, but I manage to pull up the Astrophage panel. It shows a map of the ship, and the fuel-bay area is divided into nine rectangles. I don’t have time to compare these rectangles to the damaged part of the hull. I grunt, force my arm forward, and tap one that I think is in the right spot.

“Getting…rid…of…bad…fuel bay…” I say through gritted teeth.

“Yes yes yes!” Rocky says, cheering me on.

The Fuel Pod screen appears: ASTROPHAGE 112.079 KG. Next to that, a button labeled “Jettison.” I hit it. A confirmation window appears. I confirm.

A sudden jerk of acceleration throws me to the side. Even Rocky can’t stay still. He slams into the side of his bulb but quickly recovers and grabs his handholds with all five hands.

The hull groans louder than before. The acceleration hasn’t stopped, and my vision gets blurry. The pilot’s seat starts to bend. I’m about to pass out, so we’re probably at 6 g or more.

“Thrust continues,” Rocky says, trembling.

I can’t reply. I can’t make any sound at all.

I know the fuel bay I got rid of was in the damaged area. There must be more than one broken bay. No time to be careful. In a few seconds, the force will be too strong for me to reach the screen at all. If there’s a second broken bay, it’ll be next to the one I just ditched. But there are two bays next to it. I pick one at random. Fifty-fifty chance. With a huge effort, I tap its icon, the Jettison button, and confirm.

A jolt shakes the ship, and I’m thrown around like a rag doll. In my darkening vision, I see Rocky curled up in a ball, bouncing against the walls, leaving silver blood splatters wherever he hits.

If anything, the force is worse than before. But wait…now it’s the other direction.

Instead of being pulled back into my seat, I’m now being pulled away from it, my body pressing into the restraints.

The Centrifuge screen, of all things, comes to the foreground. EXCESSIVE CENTRIFUGAL FORCE WARNING, it blinks.

“Nnnng,” I say. I meant to say Oh God, but I can’t breathe anymore.

All that fuel blasting out into space…it didn’t just leave along the ship’s long side. It blew out at an angle, spinning us like a top. And the exploding fuel bays probably made things even worse.

Well, I stopped the fuel leak, at least. There are no new forces pushing the ship. Now I just have to deal with the spin. I manage to get a breath in. The centrifugal force is less than the uncontrolled thrust force, but it’s still huge. But hey, at least it pulls my arms toward the screen instead of away from it.

If I can get the spin drives back online, maybe I can stop the—

My seat finally gives out. I hear the pops as the anchor points break. I fall forward, into the screen, still strapped to the metal seat, which crushes me from behind.

The chair probably doesn’t weigh much in normal gravity. Maybe 20 kilograms. But with this much centripetal force, it’s like having a cement block on my back. I can’t breathe.

This is it. The weight of the chair is so much I can’t fill my lungs. I get dizzy.

It’s called mechanical suffocation. That’s how boa constrictors kill. Funny thing to think about as I die.

Sorry, Earth, I think. Better last thought.

My lungs, now full of carbon dioxide, are freaking out. But the adrenaline doesn’t give me the strength to get away. It just keeps me awake to feel death better.

Thanks, adrenal glands.

The ship isn’t groaning anymore. I guess everything that could break has broken, and what’s left can handle it.

My eyes water. They sting. Why? Am I crying? I screwed up for everyone, and we’re all going to die because of it. Good reason to cry. But this isn’t feelings. It’s pain. My nose hurts too. Not from pressure. Something burns inside my nose.

Something probably broke in the lab. Some bad chemical. Good thing I can’t breathe. I probably wouldn’t like the smell.

Then, suddenly, I can breathe again! I don’t know how, but I gasp for air. Then I cough like crazy. Ammonia. It’s everywhere. Too strong. My lungs scream, and my eyes water more. Then there’s a new smell.

Fire.

I roll over and see Rocky floating above me. Not in his pod. He’s in the control room!

He has cut my restraints and pulled the chair free. He shoves it aside.

He stands over me, unsteady. I can feel the heat coming from his body just inches away. Smoke pours out of the radiator slits on his shell.

His knees give way, and he falls onto the screen next to me, breaking it. The LCD screen goes black, and the plastic frame melts.

I see a trail of smoke leading up the tunnel to the lab and beyond.

“Rocky! What have you done!”

The crazy guy must have used the big airlock in the dormitory! He came into my space to save me. And he’ll die because of it!

He shivers and pulls his legs under himself.

“Save…Earth…Save…Erid…” he trembles. Then he slumps down.

“Rocky!” I grab his shell without thinking. It’s like putting my hands on a hot stove. I pull back quickly. “Rocky…no…”

But he doesn't move.

Chapter 20

Rocky’s body warms the whole room.

I can barely move because the centrifuge is so strong.

“Nnnn!” I groan, pushing myself up from the broken monitor. I pull myself across the shards to the next monitor. I try not to lift too much of my body at once—I need to save my strength.

I slide my finger onto the monitor from the edge and tap the screen-select buttons. This is my only chance.

I remember the navigation controls. The manual-control section has a button to stop all rotation. That’s very tempting, but I can’t risk it. The fuel bay is wide open, I’ve dropped a couple of pods, and I don’t know what other damage there is. I don’t want to start any spin drives—even the small ones that control the ship's position.

I bring up the Centrifuge screen. It flashes red and white, still upset about the ship's spinning. I dismiss the warning and go into manual mode. There are many “don’t do this” messages, but I dismiss them all. Soon I can directly control the cable spools. I set them spinning at top speed.

The room spins and tilts oddly. My inner ears and my eyes don’t like the difference. I know it’s because the two halves of the ship are separating, which affects what I feel in the control room. But logic doesn’t help. I turn my head and throw up on the wall.

After a few seconds, the force drops a lot. It’s much easier to handle now. Less than 1 g, actually. All thanks to the magic of centrifuge math.

The force in a centrifuge is the opposite of the square of the radius. By spooling out the cables, I made the radius go from 20 meters (half the ship's length) to 75 meters (distance from the control room to the center with full cable extension). I don’t know how much force there was before, but now it’s one-fourteenth of what it was.

I’m still stuck against the monitor, but not as hard. I guess it’s about half a g. I can breathe again.

Everything feels backwards. I used the centrifuge in manual, so it did exactly what I told it: It extended the cables. It did not turn the crew area to face inward. The centrifuge pushes everything toward the front of the crew area. The lab is “up” from me now, and the dormitory is even farther “up.”

I don’t even know where the manual controls to turn the crew area are, and I don’t have time to find them. For now, I’ll have to work in upside-down land.

I float to the airlock and open it. Everything is a mess inside, but I don’t care. I untangle the crumpled spacesuit and take off the gloves. I put them on.

Back in the control room, I stand on the consoles (the control panels are “down” now). I hope I’m not hurting things too much. I get over Rocky’s body, grab both sides of his shell with my gloved hands, and lift.

Oh. God.

I put him back down. If I try to move him like that, I’ll hurt my back. But I did lift him, for a second. It felt like 200 pounds. Thank god we’re in one-half gravity. He’d weigh 400 pounds at full gravity.

I need more than my hands to lift him.

I take off the gloves, float back to the airlock, and throw things aside until I find the safety ropes. I wrap two ropes under Rocky’s shell and loop them over my shoulders. I burn my arms in a few places doing this, but I’ll deal with that later.

I clip each rope to itself under my armpits. This won’t be comfortable, and it won’t look cool, but my hands will be free, and I’ll be lifting with my legs.

I reach through the hatch to the lab with both hands and grab the closest rung of the ladder. It’s slow going at first. There’s no ladder in the control room. Why would there be? No one thought it would be upside down.

My shoulders scream in pain. This is not a well-designed backpack with a properly balanced load. It’s 200 pounds of alien held up by two thin straps digging into my collarbones. And I just have to hope the nylon straps don't melt from Rocky’s body heat.

I grunt and grimace, one rung at a time, until I get my feet into the lab. I use the edge of the hatch to brace my feet and pull Rocky up with the straps.

The lab is a mess. Everything is in piles all over the ceiling. Only the table and chairs are still on the floor above me—they’re bolted down. And, thankfully, most of the delicate equipment is bolted to them. However, that delicate, store-bought lab equipment wasn’t designed to be shaken like popcorn and subjected to 6 or 7 g’s. I wonder how much is completely broken.

The gravity is less up here. I’m closer to the center of the centrifuge. The higher I get the easier things will be.

I kick lab supplies and equipment out of my way and drag Rocky to the dormitory hatch. I repeat the painful process I just did a moment ago. The force is less, but it still hurts. Again, I use the hatch as a bracing point to pull Rocky into the room.

My little section of the dormitory barely fits us both. Rocky’s section is a mess, just like the lab. His workbench wasn’t bolted in place, so it’s on the ceiling now.

I drag him across the ceiling and I get up on my bunk. It has turned completely around, thanks to its rocking pivot mounting. It’s a handy platform for reaching the airlock between my zone and Rocky’s.

The airlock door sits open on my side. He used it to come save me.

“Man, why did you do that?!” I complain.

He could have let me die. He really should have. He could handle the pull of the spin easily. He could have taken his time, made some gadget, and used it to regain control of the ship. Yeah, I know, he’s a good guy and he saved my life, but this isn’t about us. He has a planet to save. Why risk his life and his whole mission for me?

The airlock door doesn’t go all the way to the ceiling, so I’ll have to play “The Floor Is Lava” to get in.

I jump into the airlock from my bunk, then use the straps to pull Rocky in with me. I start to climb back out, and that’s when I see the airlock control panel.

Or, rather, I see the smashed box that used to be the airlock control panel.

“Oh, come on!” I yell.

Both sides of the airlock had control panels, so either Rocky or I could use it as needed. But now mine are ruined—probably hit by some debris flying around during the chaos.

I have to get him back into his environment, but how? I have an idea. It’s not a good idea. There’s an emergency valve in the airlock itself that can let air in from Rocky’s side.

It’s there for a very specific situation. There’s no way I can ever go into Rocky’s area of the ship. I definitely can’t handle his environment, and my spacesuit would be crushed like a grape. But Rocky can come into my area with his homemade ball-spacesuit thing. So, just to be extra safe—in case there was an emergency while Rocky was in his ball in the airlock—there’s a valve that will let the air from his side vent in. It’s a large iron lever, so it can be moved with the magnets Rocky carries with him while in the ball.

I look at the lever in the airlock. I glance at the airlock’s door to my compartment and its spinning-wheel lock. I look back to the lever, then back to the door.

I tense my muscles and mentally count to three.

I pull the lever and jump toward my room.

Burning-hot ammonia fills the airlock and dormitory. I slam the airlock door behind me and spin the wheel lock. I hear hissing on the other side but I don’t see anything. I might never see anything again.

My eyes burn like they’re on fire. My lungs feel like a hundred knives are dancing. My skin is numb all along my left side. And my nose—forget it. The smell is so strong my sense of smell just quits.

My throat completely closes. My body wants nothing to do with the ammonia.

“Com…” I gasp. “Com…pu…ter…”

I want to die. Pain is everywhere. I climb into my bunk.

“Help!” I gasp.

“Multiple injuries,” says the computer. “Too much eye mucus. Blood around the mouth, second-degree burns. Trouble breathing. Triage result: insert breathing tube.”

The mechanical arms, which thankfully don’t seem to mind being upside down, grab me and something is shoved hard down my throat. I feel a poke on my good arm.

“IV fluids and sedation,” the computer reports.

And then I'm out cold.

—

I wake up covered in medical stuff and pain.

There's an oxygen mask on my face. My right arm has an IV, and my left arm is bandaged from wrist to shoulder. It hurts a lot.

Everything else hurts too. Especially my eyes.

But at least I can see. That's good.

"Computer," I say with a rough voice. "How long have I been asleep?"

"You were unconscious for six hours, seventeen minutes."

I take a deep breath. My lungs feel like they're coated in tar. Probably phlegm or some other gunk. I look over to Rocky's area. He's right where I left him in the airlock.

How can I tell if an Eridian is dead? When Rocky sleeps, he doesn't move at all. But that's probably also what happens when an Eridian dies.

I see a pulse-ox monitor on my right index finger.

“Compu—” I cough. “Computer: What is my blood oxygen level?”

“Ninety-one percent.”

“That’s good enough.” I take off the mask and sit up in bed. My bandaged arm hurts with every move. I pull the various things off my body.

I open and close my left hand. It’s working. The muscles are only a little sore.

I got hit with a quick burst of very hot, very high-pressure ammonia. I probably have chemical burns in my lungs and on my eyes. And probably a physical burn on my arm. My left side took the worst of the blast.

Twenty-nine atmospheres of pressure at 210 degrees Celsius (over 400 degrees Fahrenheit!). That must be what a grenade feels like. On another note: It’s lucky we didn’t crash into the planet with no one steering.

The ship is either in a stable orbit or we escaped Adrian’s gravity completely. I shake my head. It’s ridiculous how much power I have in the fuel bay. To not even know if I’m still near a planet …wow.

I’m lucky to be alive. There’s no other way to say it. Anything I do after this is a gift from the universe. I step off the bed and stand in front of the airlock. Gravity is still at one-half g and everything is still upside down.

What can I do for Rocky?

I sit on the floor facing him. I touch the airlock wall. That seems too dramatic, so I pull my hand back. Okay, I know some basic Eridian biology. But that doesn’t make me a doctor.

I grab a tablet and look through my notes. I don’t remember everything he said, but at least I wrote a lot down.

When badly hurt, an Eridian body will shut down to try to heal itself. I hope Rocky’s cells are doing their job in there. And I hope they can fix damage from: (1) dropping the air pressure way down, (2) suddenly being exposed to oxygen, and (3) being almost 200 degrees colder than normal.

I stop worrying and go back to my notes.

“Ah, here!” I say.

Here’s what I need: The capillaries in his shell radiator are made of metal alloys without oxygen. His mercury blood flows through these tubes, and air passes over them. In Erid’s air, which has no oxygen, this makes sense. In our air, it’s a fire hazard.

Oxygen just went over very hot metal pipes as thin as hair. They burned. That’s the smoke I saw from Rocky’s vents. His radiator was on fire.

Jesus.

The whole thing must be full of soot and ash. And the capillaries will be covered in oxides, which stop heat from flowing. Oxides don’t conduct electricity. The worst thing that could happen.

Okay. If he’s dead, he’s dead. I can’t hurt him anymore. But if he’s alive, I have to help. There’s no reason not to try.

But what do I do?

—

So many pressures. So many temperatures. So many air mixtures. I have to keep track of them all. My own space, Rocky’s space, and now the Adrian Astrophage breeding space too.

But first: gravity. I’m tired of living in The Poseidon Adventure. Time to fix this ship.

I go back “down” to the control room. The center panel is wrecked, but the others work fine. And they can be swapped out anyway. I’ll put a new one in the middle when I have time.

I pull up the Centrifuge screen and look around at the controls. I finally find the manual controls for the crew-compartment rotation. They were hidden pretty deep in the options; I’m glad I didn’t try to find them during the crisis.

I tell the crew compartment to rotate. Very, very slowly. I set the speed at 1 degree per second. It takes three minutes to turn around. And I hear a lot of thumps, clangs, and crashes from the lab. I don’t care about any of that. I just want to make sure Rocky doesn’t get hurt more. This slow speed should make his body slide along the airlock ceiling, then along the wall, and finally to the floor. That’s the plan, anyway.

Once the rotation is done, things feel normal again, but at half a g. I go back down to the dormitory to check on Rocky. He’s now on the airlock floor, and still right-side up. Good. He slid instead of tumbled.

I really want to work on Rocky, but I need to be sure the adventure that might have killed him wasn’t pointless. I grab the sample container from the ship’s airlock. I’m almost glad I left it there. The EVA suit it was packed with cushioned it from the crazy, sudden speed changes.

Rocky was smart enough to put readouts on the sampler to tell us the temperature and pressure inside. They’re analog dials with numbers in Eridian base-six. But I’ve seen enough of those to be able to understand them. The inside of the ball is minus 51 degrees Celsius with a pressure of 0.02 atmospheres. And I know from my earlier spectrometry test what the air is made of.

Okay, that’s the environment I need to recreate.

I sort through what’s left of the lab. It’s slow because I can barely use my left arm. But I can use it to help move things, at least. Just no heavy lifting for now.

I find a vacuum container that’s only a little broken. It’s a drum-shaped glass cylinder about a foot across. I patch up the crack with epoxy and test it. It can pump the air out and keep a vacuum. If it can keep a vacuum, it can keep 0.02 atmospheres.

I put the sample container inside.

The chemical cabinet is still firmly attached to the wall. I open it. Everything is jumbled inside, of course, but most containers look undamaged. I grab the small vial of Earth Astrophage.

There’s about a gram in there, part of the supplies for testing. I can always get more if I need it. All I have to do is cut any of the Astrophage coolant lines in the hull. But there’s no need for that now.

The sample is an oily sludge at the bottom of the vial. I open the vial and scoop it up with a cotton swab. (That gram of Astrophage has 100 trillion Joules of energy. Best not to think about it.)

I smear the Astrophage along the inside of the vacuum chamber and drop the cotton swab in next to the sample probe.

I remove all the air from the vacuum chamber.

The chemistry supplies include several small tanks of gases. Luckily, steel tanks are strong, so they survived the rough ride we just had. I add gases into the vacuum chamber, one at a time, through the input valve. I want to copy Adrian’s atmosphere. I add carbon dioxide, methane, and even argon. I don’t think the argon will matter—it’s a noble gas, so it shouldn’t react with anything. But that’s what I used to think about xenon, and that turned out to be wrong.

I can’t chill the air in there to minus 50 degrees, so I’ll just have to hope the life inside can handle Earth room temperature.

I hear a click just as I finish adding the argon. It’s the sampler. Just like Rocky designed them, the small valves opened when the outside pressure matched the pressure where Astrophage breed on Adrian. Good old Rocky. Best engineer I’ve ever known.

Okay. I’ve made the sample as safe as possible. The air is as close to its natural environment as I could make it, and there’s plenty of Astrophage to eat. If there are any tiny predators in there, they should be doing well.

I wipe my forehead with my bandaged arm, and immediately regret it. I wince in pain.

“How hard is it, Ryland?!” I snap to myself. “Stop using your burned arm!”

I climb back down the ladder to the dormitory.

“Computer: painkillers.”

The arms reach up and hand me a paper cup with two pills in it and a cup of water. I take the pills without even checking what they are.

I look back at my friend and try to think of a plan….

—

It’s been over a day since I pushed Rocky into that airlock, and he still hasn’t moved. But I haven’t been doing nothing. I’ve been busy making inventions in the lab. Making gadgets like this is usually Rocky’s thing, but I’m trying my best.

I thought about a lot of different ways to help. But I think I should let Rocky’s body heal itself as much as it can. I don’t want to operate on a human, especially not an Eridian. His body should know what to do. I just have to let it.

That doesn’t mean I’m going to do nothing, though. I have an idea about what’s happening. And if I’m wrong, what I want to do won’t hurt him.

Right now, there’s a lot of soot and other stuff from the explosion in his radiator organ. So it probably isn’t working well. If he’s alive, it’ll take his body a long time to clean it out. Maybe too long.

So maybe I can help?

I hold the box in my hand. It’s closed on five sides, with one side open. The walls are 4-inch-thick steel. It took me all day to fix the mill and get it working, but once I did, making this box was easy.

Inside is a strong air pump. That’s all. It can shoot air out really hard. I tested it in the lab, and it blew a hole in a thin sheet of aluminum from a foot away. It really works. I wish I could say I made it all myself, but I only made the box. The pump is from a high-pressure tank.

Also in the box are a battery, a camera, some stepper motors, and a drill. I need all these things for my plan to work.

I've cleaned up the lab, a bit. Most of the equipment is broken, but some might be repairable. I go to the other side of the table, where I have another experiment.

I have a small piece of xenonite—some scraps from when we made two hundred thousand chain links. I used a lot of epoxy to glue it to the end of a rough drill bit. It's been drying for over an hour. It should be ready.

I pick up the bit, and the xenonite stays attached. I try as hard as I can to pull them apart. I can't.

I nod and smile. This might work.

I do a few more tests with the box. My remote control for the motors works okay. It's not really remote control. It's a set of switches attached to a plastic container lid. Wires from the switches go through a small hole in the steel, which is then filled with resin. I can turn the power on or off to any of the parts inside. That's my "remote control." I just hope the motors can handle high heat or ammonia.

I take everything to the dormitory and prepare the epoxy. I mix it and put a lot of it on the edges of the open side of the steel box. I press the box against the airlock wall and hold it there. Then I just stand there for ten minutes, holding the box in place. I could have taped it to the wall while the epoxy dried, but I need a really good seal and don't want to risk it. My hands are better clamps than any tool I have in the lab.

I carefully let go of the box and wait for it to fall. It doesn't. I tap it a few times, and it seems pretty secure.

It's five-minute epoxy, but I'll wait an hour for it to fully dry.

I go back to the lab. Why not, right? Let's see what's happening in my little alien terrarium.

Not much, really. I don’t know what I thought would happen. Maybe little flying saucers zipping around in the room?

But the cylinder looks just like it did before. The sampler is where I left it. The Astrophage smear hasn't changed. The cotton swab is…

Wait…

I crouch down and sit. I peer into the chamber. The cotton swab is different. Just a bit. It’s…fuzzier.

Cool! Maybe there’s something on it I can check out. I just need to put it under a microscope to—

Oh.

It hits me. I have no way to take samples out. I totally forgot about that part.

“Idiot!” I hit my forehead.

I rub my eyes. Between the burn pain and the druggy feeling from the painkillers, it’s hard to focus. And I’m tired. I learned this in grad school: When you’re super tired, know you’re super tired. Don’t try to figure things out then. I have a sealed container that I need to open at some point. I’ll figure out how later.

I get out my tablet and take pictures of the container. Science rule number 1: If something is changing on its own, record it.

To be more scientific, I point a webcam at the experiment and set the computer to take a time-lapse at one frame per second. If anything is happening slowly, I want to know.

I go back to the control room. Where are we, anyway?

After working with the Nav console, I learn we’re still in orbit. It’s somewhat stable. The orbit will likely decay over time. But there's no immediate worry.

I check all the ship’s systems and run as many diagnostics as I can. The ship did well, even though it wasn't designed for this.

The two fuel bays I got rid of are gone, but the other seven seem fine. The diagnostics test shows some cracks in the hull here and there. But they all seem to be inside. None are on the outside, which is good. I don’t want my Astrophage to see Adrian again.

One of the tiny leaks is marked in red. I look closer. The computer is worried about where the leak is. It’s in the wall between the fuel area and the edge of the pressure vessel. I understand why it's a concern.

The wall is between the storage bay below the dormitory and Fuel Bay 4. I go to take a look.

Rocky still hasn’t moved. Not surprising. My steel box is still where I left it. I could use it now, but I'm going to wait the full hour.

I open the storage panels and take out a bunch of boxes. I climb into the storage area with a flashlight and toolkit. It’s cramped—less than 3 feet tall. I have to crawl around in there for about twenty minutes before I find the leak. I only see it because there’s a little frost around the edges. Air escaping into a vacuum gets very cold quickly. The ice probably helped slow the leak.

But it didn't really matter. The leak is so small it would take weeks to cause a problem. And the ship probably has extra air in tanks anyway. Still, there’s no reason to let it leak. I put a lot of epoxy on a small metal patch and seal the leak. I have to hold it for more than five minutes before it hardens. Epoxy takes a long time to harden when it’s cold, and the wall is below freezing there because of the leak. I thought about getting a heat gun from the lab but…that’s too much effort. I just hold the patch longer. It takes about fifteen minutes.

I climb back down and wince the whole time. My arm hurts constantly now. It’s a steady sting. It’s been less than an hour, but the painkillers aren’t working anymore.

“Computer! Painkillers!”

“Another dose available in three hours and four minutes.”

I frown. “Computer: What time is it?”

“Seven-fifteen p.m., Moscow Standard Time.”

“Computer: Set time to eleven p.m. Moscow Standard Time.”

“Clock set complete.”

“Computer: Painkillers.”

The arms hand me a package of pills and a bag of water. I swallow them down. What a dumb system. Astronauts trusted to save the world but not to manage their own painkiller doses? Dumb.

Okay. It’s been long enough. I turn back to the box.

First, I need to drill a hole in the xenonite. If something goes wrong, that's when things will get really bad. The plan is for the drill inside the box to make a hole in the xenonite, and for the box to hold in the pressure that bursts out. But you never know. The box might not be attached well enough.

I put on a medical breathing mask and eye protection. If superheated, high-pressure ammonia shoots into this room, I need to survive.

Earlier, I filed down a metal rod to make a spike. Its size is a bit bigger than the drill bit I have ready in the steel box. I hold the spike and hammer, ready to use them. If the pressure blows the box off, I'll hammer the spike into the hole, hoping it seals it.

Of course, the pressure might not blow the box off completely. It might just leak out around the edges of the glue. If that happens, I'll have to hit the box with the hammer until it comes off, then push in the spike.

Yes, it’s incredibly dangerous. But I don’t know if Rocky will survive without help. Maybe I’m being emotional instead of thinking clearly. But who cares?

I grip the hammer and spike. Then I turn on the drill.

It takes so long for the drill to get through the xenonite that I actually calm down from being bored. It’s only 1 centimeter, but it’s like trying to grind down diamond. I’m lucky the drill bit is strong enough to do anything at all. The camera inside shows slow but steady progress. Instead of drilling like wood or metal, this is more like glass. It breaks off in small pieces.

Finally, the drill bit breaks through to the other side. The pressure immediately shoots it back into the box, bending it sideways. There’s a whump as Eridian air rushes into the small box. I squint. After a few seconds, I open my eyes again.

If the box was going to blow off, it would have happened then. The seal held. For now, at least. I sigh in relief.

But I don’t take off the mask or goggles. You never know when the seal might break.

I check the camera screen. This will need careful aim, so I was very smart to make sure a camera could—

The camera feed is dead.

My wrist hurts, and I pull it away.

Oh. Right. Webcams aren’t made to work at 210 degrees Celsius and 29 atmospheres. And my solid steel box is, well, solid steel. Steel is a great heat conductor. I can’t even touch it now, it’s so hot.

I’m still stupid. First the Adrian sample container, and now this. I want to sleep, but Rocky is more important. At least being stupid isn’t forever. I’ll keep going. I know I shouldn’t, but I’m too stupid to care.

Okay, the camera is dead. I can’t see into the box. But I can still see Rocky in the airlock because the xenonite is clear. I’ll have to work with what I have.

I start the high-pressure pump. It still works—at least, it’s making noise. It should be shooting a very high-pressure jet of air toward Rocky. At 29 atmospheres, air acts almost like water. You can really push things around with it. But ammonia is clear. So I have no idea where it’s going.

I change the angle of the jet with the servo controls. Are they working? I don’t know. The pump is too loud for me to hear if the servos are doing anything. I sweep left and right, moving down and up a little bit at a time.

Finally, I see something. One of the levers in the airlock wiggles a bit. I focus on it. It gets pushed back several inches.

“Gotcha!” I say.

Now I know where it’s aimed. I guess a bit and aim for Rocky’s shell vents. Nothing happens, so I search in a grid pattern, back and forth, up and down, until I get one.

And wow, what a result!

I hit the right spot. Suddenly, Rocky’s shell vents puff out black smoke. The gross dust and bits that gathered when he was on fire. It feels really good. Like when you blow an air duster into an old computer.

I sweep back and forth, trying to hit each vent. The later ones don’t make as much of a show as the first one. I think they all go to the same place—like a person’s mouth and nose. Several openings for backup and safety.

After a few minutes, no more dirty dust comes out. I turn off the pump.

“Well, buddy,” I say. “I’ve done what I can. I just hope you can do the rest.”

I spend the rest of the day working on a second and third box to hold everything. I glue them over my device. The Eridian air will have to get through three seals to get into my space now. That’s all I can do.

I hope Rocky wakes up.

Chapter 21

“We can talk about this alone,” I said. “I can meet with you one at a time.”

The three astronauts sat on a couch in front of me. I’d taken over the breakroom and locked the door for this meeting. Yáo sat in the middle, looking serious as always. DuBois was to his left, sitting up straight. Ilyukhina slouched to Yáo’s left, drinking a beer.

“No need for separate meetings,” said Yáo. “There’s no room on this mission for secrets.”

I moved in my chair. Why did Stratt send me to do this? I’m not good with people and I don’t know how to handle sensitive issues. She said something about the crew liking me more than anyone else. Why? Maybe I just seemed nice because I was usually standing next to Stratt.

Anyway, launch was just a month away and I had to get this information.

“Okay,” I said. “Who wants to start?”

DuBois raised his hand. “I can go first if that’s okay with everyone.”

“Sure.” I scribbled with my pen to make sure it worked. “So…how would you like to die?”

Yeah. Uncomfortable topic. But one we had to cover. These three were going to give their lives so the rest of us could have a chance to fight. The least we could do was help them die the way they wanted to.

DuBois handed me a crisp piece of paper. “I’ve written down my request here. I think you’ll find everything is in order.”

I took the paper. It had bullet points, charts, and some notes at the bottom. “What is all this?”

DuBois pointed to the middle of the page. “I want to die by breathing in nitrogen. All my research shows it’s one of the least painful ways to die.”

I nodded and jotted down some notes.

“That paper lists the equipment I’ll need to make sure I die. It’s well within my personal-item weight limit.”

I frowned, mostly to hide that I didn’t know what to say.

He folded his hands on his lap. “It’s just a nitrogen tank and a connector for the EVA suit. I can wear the suit and have it pump in nitrogen instead of oxygen. The feeling of suffocation comes from too much carbon dioxide in the lungs, not lack of oxygen. The suit will keep removing the carbon dioxide I breathe out, leaving only nitrogen. I’ll just get tired and maybe a little dizzy. Then I’ll pass out.”

“All right.” I tried to stay calm. “What if the EVA suit isn’t available?”

“Section four has the backup plan. If I can’t use the EVA suit, I’ll use the ship’s airlock. The airlock is big enough that carbon dioxide won’t build up enough to be unpleasant.”

“Okay.” I wrote down a few more notes. Though I didn’t really need to. His paper was very detailed. “We’ll make sure there’s a tank with plenty of nitrogen, and a backup tank just in case the first one leaks.”

“Excellent. Thank you.”

I set the paper aside. “Ilyukhina? What about you?”

She set her beer down. “I want heroin.”

Everyone looked at her. Even Yáo paled a little.

“Sorry, what?” I said.
