“Heroin.” She shrugged. “I’ve been a good girl all my life. No drugs. Not much sex. I want to feel intense pleasure before I die. People die from heroin all the time. It must be really nice.”

I rubbed my temples. “You want to die…from a heroin overdose?”

“Not right away,” she said. “I want to enjoy it. Start with a normal dose that works. Get high. Addicts all say the first few times are the best. Then it gets worse. I want to feel those first few times. Then overdose when the time is right.”

“I guess…we can do that,” I said. “Dying from an overdose can be really awful, though.”

She dismissed my worry. “Have doctors figure out the best way to take it for me. The right amount to make the early times as good as possible. Then the fatal dose can have other drugs in it to make sure I die without pain.”

I wrote down her request. “Okay. Heroin. I don’t know where we’ll get it, but we’ll figure it out.”

“You have the whole world helping you,” she said. “Get a drug company to make me heroin. It can’t be that hard.”

“Right. I’m sure Stratt can call someone or something.”

I sighed. Two down, one to go. “All right. Commander Yáo? What about you?”

“I want a gun, please,” he said. “A Type-92 handgun. The usual Chinese military one. Keep the bullets in a dry, sealed plastic box for the trip.”

At least that made some sense. Quick and painless. “A gun. Got it. That’s easy.”

He looked at his crewmates. “I’ll be the last to die. If anything goes wrong with either of your plans, I’ll have the gun ready. Just in case.”

“Very thoughtful,” said DuBois. “Thank you.”

“Don’t shoot me if I look like I’m enjoying myself,” Ilyukhina said.

“Understood,” said Yáo. He turned back to me. “Is that all?”

“Yeah,” I said, already standing. “This has been really awkward, thanks. I’m going to…go somewhere else now.”

—

I toss and turn in bed. The burns on my arm hurt more than ever. The painkillers barely help. I’m starting to wonder if I can find Ilyukhina’s heroin.

I won’t, I won’t. But I definitely would if this were still a mission to die.

Focus on that. This isn’t a mission to die anymore. If I do things right, I save the world and go home.

The pain gets a little better. It comes and goes. When I can, I’ll look at whatever books I have on burns. I’d at least like to know when it’ll stop hurting.

Tap.

“Huh?” I mumble.

Tap.

I look toward the noise. It’s Rocky tapping on the airlock wall.

“Rocky!” I fall out of my bunk, rolling onto my right side as I land. I scramble along the floor to the airlock wall. “Rocky, buddy! Are you okay?!”

I hear a low thrum from inside him.

“I don’t understand. Speak louder.”

“Sick…” he mumbles.

“Yeah, you’re sick. You came into my air. Of course you’re sick! You almost died!”

He tries to lift himself from the floor, then falls back down. “How did I get back here, question?”

“I moved you.”

He taps the ground with a claw, annoyed. “You touch my air, question?”

“A little, yeah.”

He points to my left arm. “Skin on arm not smooth. Hurt, maybe?”

I guess he can see right through the bandages with his sonar. Must be pretty bad under there. I kind of thought it would be, but now I know. “Yeah. But I’ll be okay.”

“You hurt self to save me. Thanks.”

“You did the same thing. Is your radiator okay? You were on fire and full of soot.”

“It getting better.” He points to the soot all along the wall and floor. “This from inside me, maybe?”

“Yes.”

“How it leave me, maybe?”

I feel proud for a moment. Why shouldn’t I? It was hard, and I did it. I point to the steel box on the airlock wall, now covered three times over. “I made something to blow air at you. I aimed at your radiator things, and all that bad stuff came out.”

He’s quiet for a moment. Then, still a little unsteady, he says, “How long that stuff inside me, maybe?”

I think back on the day. “About…two days.”

“You almost kill me.”

“What?! How?! I blew all the soot out of your radiator!”

He shifts a little. “Black stuff not soot. My body makes it. It covers damage while body fixes itself.”

“Oh…” I say. “Oh no…”

I didn’t blow soot out of his radiator. I blew the scabs off his wounds! “I’m so sorry! I was trying to help.”

“It’s okay. If you did it sooner I’d die. But I healed enough before you did it. Taking it off hurt a little. Thanks.”

I put my head in my hands. “Sorry,” I say again.

“Don’t say sorry. You saved me when you put me here. Thank thank thank.” He tries to stand again, but only gets up for a second before falling. “I am weak. I will heal.”

I step back and sit on my bunk. “Would you be more comfortable in zero g? I can turn off the centrifuge.”

“No. Gravity helps heal.” He moves his legs into a sort of bed for his shell to rest on. Probably a comfy sleeping position. “Sample container safe, right?”

“Yes. It’s in the lab now. I created an Adrian-like space in a sealed container and put some Astrophage in with the sample. I'll check on it later.”

“Good,” he says. “Human eyes very useful.”

“Thanks,” I say. “But my human brain wasn’t so useful. I can’t get the sample out of the container.”

He tilts his shell slightly. “You sealed the sample and can’t open it, right?”

“Yes.”

“You’re usually not dumb. Why dumb now?”

“Humans are dumb when we need sleep and take pain medicine. I’m tired and medicated right now.”

“You should sleep.”

I stand up. “I will soon. But first, I have to fix our orbit. It’s not very good right now.”

“Fix orbit while dumb. Good plan.”

I laugh. “New word: ‘sarcasm.’ You say the opposite of what you mean to make a point. Sarcasm.”

He says the word for “sarcasm” in his language.

—

Between exhaustion and drugs, I sleep like a baby. I wake up feeling a million times better, but my burns feel a million times worse. I look at the bandages. They’re new.

Rocky is at his workbench, messing with his tools. He’s cleaned up his area. It looks brand new. “You are awake, yes?”

“Yeah,” I say. “How are you feeling? Are you healing?”

He wiggles a claw . “Need much more healing. But some healing complete. Cannot move much.”

I drop my head back on the pillow. “Same.”

“Robot arms did things to you arm while you sleep.”

I point to the bandages. “It changed the cloth. Changing the cloth helps people heal.”

He pokes at his latest invention with various tools.

“What’s that?”

“I go to lab to see device that store Adrian life. I made device now to collect sample from inside and not let you air in.” He holds up a large box. “Put you vacuum chamber in this. Close this. This make Adrian air inside.”

He opens the top and points to a couple of hinged rods. “Control these from outside. Gather sample. Seal you device. Open my device. Have sample. Do human science with sample.”

“Smart,” I say. “Thanks.”

He gets back to work.

I lie in my bunk. There are a lot of things I want to do, but I need to take it slow. I can’t risk another “stupid day” like yesterday. I almost ruined the sample and killed Rocky. I’m smart enough now to know I’m stupid. That’s progress.

“Computer: coffee!”

After a minute, the arms hand me a cup of coffee.

“Hey,” I say, sipping my coffee. “How come we hear the same sounds?”

He keeps working on the parts inside his device. “Useful thing. Both change over time. Not surprising.”

“Yeah, but why the same frequencies? Why don’t you hear much higher sounds than I can? Or much lower?”

“I do hear much higher and much lower sounds.”

Didn’t know that. But I should have guessed. It’s how an Eridian mostly senses things. Of course he’ll hear more than I do. That still leaves one question, though.

“Okay, but why is there overlap? Why don’t we hear completely different sound ranges?”

He puts the tool down, but keeps working on his device with two hands. With the free hand, he scrapes his workbench. “You hear this, question?”

“Yes.”

“That is sound of predator coming for you. That is sound of prey running away. Sound of things touching things very important. Changed over time to hear.”

“Ah! Yes.”

It’s clear now that he mentions it. Sounds—voices, instruments, birds—can all be very different. But the sound of things hitting each other won’t change much from planet to planet. Two rocks clanging on Earth will sound the same as on Erid. So we’re all made to hear it.

“Better question,” he says. “Why we think same speed, question?”

I turn onto my side. “We don’t think at the same speed. You do math way faster than I can. And you remember things perfectly. Humans can’t do that. Eridians are smarter.”

He grabs another tool and starts working again. “Math is not thinking. Math is steps. Memory is not thinking. Memory is storage. Thinking is thinking. Problem, answer. You and me think same speed. Why, question?”

“Hmm.”

I think about it. It’s a good question. Why isn’t Rocky a thousand times smarter than me? Or a thousand times dumber?

“Well…I have an idea why we’re about the same intelligence. Maybe.”

“Explain.”

“Intelligence helps us beat the other animals on our planet. But evolution is lazy. Once a problem is solved, the trait stops changing. So you and me, we’re both just smart enough to be smarter than our planet’s animals.”

“We are much much smarter than animals.”

“We’re as smart as evolution made us. So, we’re just smart enough to be able to control our planets.”

He thinks it over. “I agree. But why did Earth intelligence evolve to be the same as Erid intelligence?”

“Our intelligence comes from animal intelligence. So, what does animal intelligence come from? How smart do animals need to be?”

“Smart enough to spot danger or food in time to react.”

“Yes, exactly!” I say. “But how long is that time? How long does an animal have to react? How long will it take the danger to kill the animal, or the prey to escape? I think it’s about gravity.”

“Gravity, you ask?” He puts the device down completely. He’s paying full attention now.

“Yeah! Think about it. Gravity decides how fast an animal can run. Higher gravity means more time on the ground, so faster movement. I think animal intelligence has to be faster than gravity.”

“Interesting idea,” Rocky says. “But Erid has twice Earth’s gravity. You and I are equally smart.”

I sit up on my bed. “I bet our gravities are so close, in space terms, that the intelligence needed is almost the same. If we met a creature from a planet with one-hundredth of Earth’s gravity, I bet it would seem pretty dumb to us.”

“Maybe,” he says. He goes back to his gadget. “Another thing we have in common: You and I would both die for our people. Why? Evolution doesn’t like death.”

“It’s good for the species,” I say. “If people are willing to sacrifice themselves, the species is more likely to survive.”

“Not all Eridians willing to die for others.”

I chuckle. “Not all humans either.”

“You and me are good people,” Rocky says.

“Yeah.” I smile. “I suppose we are.”

—

Nine days until launch.

I paced around my room. It was pretty empty, but I didn’t mind. The portable unit was like a small mobile home with a kitchenette. Better than most people got. The Russians were busy setting up dozens of temporary shelters a few miles from Baikonur Cosmodrome. But then, I guess we were all busy lately.

Anyway, I’d barely used my bed since we’d arrived. There just always seemed to be some new issue or problem. Nothing big. Just…issues.

The Hail Mary was finished. Over 2 million kilograms of spacecraft and fuel in a good, steady orbit—four times the mass of the International Space Station, and built in one-twentieth the time. The press used to track the total cost, but around $10 trillion, they stopped. It just didn’t matter. It wasn’t about using resources wisely anymore. It was Earth versus Astrophage, and no cost was too great.

ESA astronauts had been on the ship for the last few weeks, testing it out. The test crew reported about five hundred problems that we’d been fixing for the last few weeks. None of them were critical.

It was really happening. The Hail Mary was going to launch in nine days.

I sat at the table that was my desk and looked through papers. I signed some and set others aside for Stratt to see tomorrow. How did I become an administrator? We all have to accept changes in our lives, I guess. If this was my role, then so be it.

I put the papers down and looked out the window. The Kazakhstani steppes were flat and bare. People usually don’t build launch sites near anything important. For obvious reasons.

I missed my kids.

Dozens of them. Hundreds, really, over a school year.

They didn’t curse at me or wake me up at night. Their fights were usually over quickly, either by a teacher's handshake or detention. And, a little selfish, but here it is: They admired me. I missed being respected like that.

I sighed.

My kids would have a hard time even if the mission worked. It would take thirteen years for the Hail Mary to reach Tau Ceti, and (if the crew found a solution) another thirteen years for the beetles to return to us. That’s over twenty-five years before we would even know what to do. My kids wouldn’t be kids anymore when it was over.

“Onward,” I mumbled, and grabbed the next problem report. Why was it on paper instead of just an email? Because Russians do things a certain way and it’s easier to work with them than to complain about it.

The report was from the ESA crew about problems in Slurry Pump Fourteen of the medical feeding transport system. Pump Fourteen was only part of the backup system and it was still 95 percent effective. But there was no reason to put up with that. We still had 83 kilograms of unused launch mass. I made a note to include a spare slurry pump—it was only 250 grams. The crew could install it before leaving orbit.

I set the paper aside and saw a brief flash out my window. Probably a jeep driving on the dirt road that led to the temporary shelters. I got headlights through my window from time to time. I ignored it.

The next paper in my stack was all about potential balance issues. The Hail Mary kept its center of mass along its long axis by pumping Astrophage around as needed. But we still wanted to keep things as balanced as possible anyway. The ESA crew had rearranged several supply bags in the storage compartment to better balance—

The window shattered as a deafening explosion shook the room. Glass shards nicked my face as a shockwave knocked me clean out of my chair.

After that: silence.

And then: sirens in the distance.

I got to my knees, and then to my feet. I opened and closed my mouth a few times to pop my ears.

I stumbled to the door and opened it. The first thing I noticed was that the small triplet of steps that once led to my door were several feet away. Then I saw the freshly disturbed earth between the steps and my door and I understood what happened.

The steps are anchored into the ground with four-by-fours sunk deep like fence posts. My portable has no such support.

My whole house moved, but the stairs didn't.

“Grace?! Are you okay?!” It was Stratt’s voice. Her portable radio was next to mine.

“Yeah!” I say. “What the heck was that?!”

“I don’t know,” she said. “Hold on.”

Soon, I saw a flashlight moving around. She came to me in a bathrobe and boots, already talking on her walkie-talkie. “Eto Stratt. Chto sluchylos’?” she demanded.

“Vzryv v issledovatel’skom tsentre,” came the reply.

“The research center blew up,” she said.

Baikonur was a place where rockets launched, but it also had research buildings. They weren’t labs, more like classrooms. Astronauts usually spent a week at Baikonur before a launch, wanting to study and get ready until the last day.

“Oh God,” I said. “Who was there? Who was there?!”

She pulled some papers from her robe pocket. “Hold on, hold on…” She flipped through them, dropping each one as she looked at the next. I knew what they were—I’d seen them every day for a year. Schedule charts, showing where everyone was and what they were doing at all times.

She stopped at the page she needed. She actually gasped. “DuBois and Shapiro. They’re supposed to be there doing Astrophage experiments.”

I put my hands on my head. “No! Please, no! The research center is five kilometers away. If the blast did this much damage here—”

“I know, I know!” She turned on her walkie-talkie again. “Prime crew—I need your locations. Report in.”

“Yáo here,” came the first reply. “In my bunk.”

“Ilyukhina here. At the officers’ bar. What was that explosion?”

Stratt and I waited for the reply we hoped for.

“DuBois,” she said. “DuBois! Report in!”

Silence.

“Shapiro. Dr. Annie Shapiro. Report in!”

More silence.

She took a deep breath and let it out. She clicked the walkie-talkie on one more time. “Stratt to transport—I need a jeep to take me to Ground Control.”

“Copy,” came the reply.

The next few hours were, honestly, chaos. The entire base was locked down for a while and everyone’s ID was checked. For all we knew, some doomsday cult wanted to wreck the mission. But nothing seemed wrong.

Stratt, Dimitri, and I sat in the bunker. Why were we in a bunker? The Russians weren’t taking any chances. It didn’t seem like a terrorist attack, but they were protecting the important people just in case. Yáo and Ilyukhina were off in another bunker. The other science leaders were in other bunkers too. Spread everyone out so there’s no single attack that would work. There was a dark reason for it. Baikonur was built during the Cold War, after all.

“The research buildings are a crater,” said Stratt. “And there’s still no sign of DuBois or Shapiro. Or the fourteen other staff that worked there.”

She pulled up pictures on her phone and showed them to us.

The photos showed total destruction. The area was lit up with bright lights the Russians had put up and the place was full of rescue workers. Though there was nothing for them to do.

Almost nothing was left. No debris, little wreckage. Stratt swiped through photo after photo. Some were close-ups of the ground. Round, shiny beads were all over the place. “What’s up with the beads?” she said.

“Metal condensate,” Dimitri said. “It means metals were turned into gas, then became liquid like raindrops.”

“Jesus,” she said.

I sighed. “The only thing in those labs that could make enough heat to vaporize metal is Astrophage.”

“I agree,” said Dimitri. “But Astrophage doesn't just 'explode.' How could this happen?”

Stratt looked at her wrinkled schedule. “According to this, DuBois wanted more practice with Astrophage-powered electrical generators. Shapiro was there to watch and help.”

“That doesn't make sense,” I said. “Those generators use a very small amount of Astrophage to make electricity. Not nearly enough to blow up a building.”

She put her phone down. “We’ve lost our main and backup science experts.”

“This is a nightmare,” said Dimitri.

“Dr. Grace, I want a short list of possible replacements.”

I stared, shocked. “Are you made of stone?! Our friends just died!”

“Yes, and everyone else will die if we don’t make this mission happen. We have nine days to find a replacement science expert.”

I teared up. “DuBois…Shapiro…” I sniffled and wiped my eyes. “They’re dead. They’re dead…oh God…”

Stratt slapped me. “Wake up!”

“Hey!”

“Cry later! Mission first! Do you still have that list of candidates who can resist comas from last year? Start looking through it. We need a new science specialist. And we need them now!”

—

“Collecting sample now…” I say.

Rocky watches me from his tunnel in the lab ceiling. His device works perfectly. The clear xenonite box has valves and pumps that let me control the environment inside. The vacuum chamber is inside with its lid open. The box even controls the temperature, keeping it a chilly minus 51 degrees Celsius.

Rocky scolded me for leaving the sample at (human) room temperature for so long. He had a lot to say about it. We had to add “reckless,” “idiot,” “foolish,” and “irresponsible” to our shared vocabulary so he could fully express his opinion.

There was another word he used a lot, but he wouldn’t tell me what it meant.

Three days off the painkillers, and I’m a lot smarter than I was. At least he understands that much—I wasn’t just some stupid human. I was a human with enhanced stupidity.

Rocky wouldn't give me the box I needed until I slept three times without using drugs. My arm really hurts, but he has a point.

Rocky got better in that time, too. I don't know what's happening inside him. He looks the same, but he's moving much better. Not full-speed, though. Me neither. We're both hurting, really.

We agreed to keep the gravity at one-half g.

I open and close the claws in the box a few times. "Look at me. I'm an Eridian now."

“Yes. Very Eridian. Hurry and get sample.”

"You're no fun." I grab the cotton swab and bring it to a glass slide. I rub it on the slide, leaving a smear, then put it back in the vacuum chamber. I seal the chamber, put the slide in a clear container, and seal the box.

"Okay. That should do it." I turn the valves to let my air in, then open the box. The slide is safe in its container. The galaxy's smallest spaceship. At least, for any Adrian life that might be there.

I walk to the microscope station.

Rocky follows in the tunnel above. “You sure you can see light that small?”

"Yes. It's old technology. Very old." I put the container on the tray and adjust the lenses. The container is clear enough to see through.

“Okay, Adrian, what do you have for me?” I put my face to the eyepieces.

The most obvious thing is the Astrophage. As usual, they’re jet-black, absorbing all light. That’s expected. I adjust the backlight and focus. And I see microbes everywhere.

One of my favorite experiments with the kids is to have them look at a drop of water. A drop of water, ideally one from a puddle outside, will be full of life. It always goes over well, except for the occasional kid who then refuses to drink water for a while.

“Lots of life in here,” I say. “Different kinds.”

“Good. Expected.”

Of course there would be. Any planet with life will have it everywhere. That’s my theory, at least. Evolution is extremely good at filling every corner of the ecosystem.

Right now I’m looking at hundreds of unique life-forms, never before seen by humans. Each one an alien race. I can’t help but smile. Still, I have work to do.

I pan around until I find a good clump of Astrophage. If there’s a predator to be found, it’ll be where the Astrophage is. Otherwise it’d be a pretty bad predator.

I flick on the microscope’s internal camera. The image appears on a little LCD screen. I adjust the screen and set it recording.

“This could take time,” I say. “Need to see interaction between— whoa! ”

I push my face back to the microscope for a better look. It only took seconds before the Astrophage was attacked. Am I incredibly lucky, or is this life-form just that aggressive?

Rocky scurried back and forth above me. “What, question? What happen, question?”

The monster lunges toward the clump of Astrophage. It’s a shapeless blob, like an amoeba. It presses against its much smaller prey and starts to surround the entire clump by oozing around both sides.

The Astrophage wriggle. They know something is wrong. They try to escape, but it’s too late. They can only sputter a short distance before they stop. Normally, Astrophage can accelerate to near light speed in seconds, but these can’t. Maybe the monster excreted a chemical that disables them somehow?

The encirclement is complete, and the Astrophage are surrounded. A few seconds later, the Astrophage become cell-like in appearance. No longer featureless black, their organelles and membranes are clearly visible in the microscope’s light. They have lost their ability to absorb heat and light energy.

They’re dead.

“Got it!” I say. “I found the predator! It ate Astrophage right in front of me!”

“Found!” Rocky cheers. “Isolate.”

“Yes, I’ll isolate it!” I say.

“Happy happy happy!” he says. “Now you name.”

I grab a nano-pipette from the supply. “I don’t get it.”

“Earth culture. You find. You name. What is the predator’s name, question?”

“Oh,” I say. I’m not feeling creative right now. This is too exciting to distract me. It’s an amoeba from Tau Ceti. “Taumoeba, I guess.”

Taumoeba. The savior of Earth and Erid.

Hopefully.

—

I should have a bolo tie. Maybe a cowboy hat. Because I’m a rancher now. And I’m running about 50 million Taumoeba on my ranch.

Once I got a few Taumoeba from the Adrian air sample, Rocky built a breeder tank and we let them get to work. It’s just a xenonite box full of Adrian air and a few hundred grams of Astrophage.

As far as we can tell, Taumoeba is very tough when it comes to temperature changes. Good thing, too, because I let it sit at room temperature that one day.

Drugs are bad.

Looking back, it’s clear why they handle temperature well. They live in a negative-51-degree-Celsius place and eat Astrophage, which is always 96.415 degrees Celsius. Everyone loves a hot meal, right?

And they breed like crazy! I gave them a ton of Astrophage to eat. It’s like adding yeast to sugar water. But instead of making alcohol, we’re making more Taumoebas. Now that we have enough to test, I can get started.

If you put a goat on Mars, what happens? It dies right away (and badly). Goats didn’t grow up to live on Mars. So, what happens if you put a Taumoeba on a planet other than Adrian?

That’s what I want to know.

Rocky watches from his tunnel above the main worktable as I make a new atmosphere in my vacuum chamber.

“No have oxygen, question?” he asked.

“No oxygen.”

“Oxygen dangerous.” He’s been a little nervous since his insides caught fire.

“I breathe oxygen. It’s okay.”

“Can explode.”

I pull my goggles off and look up at him. “There’s no oxygen in this experiment. Calm down.”

“Yes. Calm.”

I get back to work. I turn a valve to let a little gas into the vacuum chamber. I check the pressure gauge to make sure that—

“Confirm again: No oxygen, right?”

I jerk my head up to glare at him. “It’s just carbon dioxide and nitrogen! Only carbon dioxide and nitrogen! Nothing else! Don’t ask me again!”

“Yes. Won't ask again. Sorry.”

Can’t blame him, I guess. Being on fire sucks.

We have two planets to deal with here. No, not Earth and Erid. Those are just the planets we live on. The planets we care about right now are Venus and Threeworld. That’s where Astrophage is breeding out of control.

Venus, of course, is the second planet in my solar system. It’s about Earth’s size with a thick carbon-dioxide atmosphere.

Threeworld is the third planet in Rocky’s home system. At least, that’s what I call it. The Eridians don’t have a name for it, not even in their language. They just call it “Planet Three.” They didn’t have ancient people looking up at stars and naming them after gods. They only found the other planets in their system a few hundred years ago. But I don’t want to keep saying “Planet Three,” so I named it Threeworld.

The hardest part about working with aliens and saving humanity is constantly having to name things.

Threeworld is a very small planet—only about the size of Earth’s moon. But unlike our moon, Threeworld has an atmosphere. How? I have no idea. The gravity on the surface is only 0.2 g’s, which shouldn’t be enough. But somehow, Threeworld holds on to a thin atmosphere. According to Rocky, it’s 84 percent carbon dioxide, 8 percent nitrogen, 4 percent sulfur dioxide, and some other gases. The air pressure is less than 1 percent of Earth’s.

I check the readings and nod. I look closely at the experiment. I’m proud of this idea.

There’s a thin layer of Astrophage on a glass plate. I put it there by shining IR light through the glass and drawing the Astrophage from the other side. It’s the same way the spin drive works. The result is a smooth layer of Astrophage that’s only one cell thick.

Then I put Taumoeba on the slide. As they eat the Astrophage, the slide will become more transparent. It’s much easier to measure light than count tiny organisms.

“Okay…the chamber has the same atmosphere as Venus’s upper atmosphere. As close as I can get it.”

I think the Astrophage breeding zone depends on the air pressure. They have to slow down from almost light speed when they enter the planet’s atmosphere. But they’re so small that it doesn’t take long, and they absorb all the heat that’s created.

So, Astrophage stop moving when the air is 0.02 atmospheres thick. That’s what we’ll use as our standard pressure. The atmosphere on Venus is 0.02 atmospheres thick at about 70 kilometers up, and the temperature there is about minus 100 degrees Celsius (thanks to all the information I have!). So that’s the temperature I set the Venus experiment to. Rocky’s temperature control works perfectly, even at very low temperatures.

“Good. Now Threeworld.”

“What’s the temperature of Threeworld’s air at 0.02 atmosphere altitude?”

“Minus eighty-two degrees Celsius.”

“Okay, thanks,” I say. I go to the next chamber. It has the same setup of Astrophage and Taumoeba. I add the right gases to copy Threeworld’s atmosphere and temperature at the 0.02-atmosphere pressure. I get the information from Rocky’s perfect memory. It’s like Venus or Adrian. Mostly carbon dioxide with some other gases mixed in. No surprise—Astrophage go for the most CO2 they can find.

It’s lucky these planets aren’t full of helium or something. I don’t have any of that. But carbon dioxide? That’s easy. My body makes it. And nitrogen? Thanks to DuBois and how he died, there’s plenty aboard.

Threeworld does have some sulfur dioxide, though. Four percent of the atmosphere. It’s enough that I had to include it, so I made some. The lab has many chemicals, but no sulfur dioxide gas. But, it has sulfuric acid in liquid form. I got some copper tubing from a broken freezer coil and used it to help make the sulfur dioxide I needed. It worked great.

“Okay, Threeworld’s done,” I say. “We’ll wait an hour and check the results.”

“We have hope,” says Rocky.

“Yes, we have hope,” I say. “Taumoeba are very tough. They can live in a near vacuum, and they seem fine in extreme cold. Maybe Venus and Threeworld are livable for them. They’re good enough for Taumoeba’s prey, so why not for Taumoeba?”

“Yes. Things are good. All is good!”

“Yeah. For once, everything’s going great.”

Then the lights go out.

Chapter 22

Total darkness.

No lights. No monitor glow. Not even the LEDs on the lab equipment.

“Okay, stay calm,” I say. “Stay calm.”

“Why not be calm, question?” Rocky asks.

Well, of course he didn’t notice the lights go out. He doesn’t have eyes. “The ship just shut down. Everything stopped working.”

Rocky scuttles a bit in his tunnel. “Your equipment quiet now. My equipment still working.”

“Your equipment gets electricity from your generator. Mine’s powered by my ship. All the lights are off. Nothing is working at all!”

“This is bad, question?”

“Yes, it’s bad! For one thing, I can’t see!”

“Why ship turn off, question?”

“I don’t know,” I say. “Do you have a light? Something you can shine through the xenonite to see me?”

“No. Why would I have light, question?”

I fumble in the darkness, feeling around the lab. “Where’s the ladder to the control room?”

“Left. More left. Keep going…yes…reach forward…”

I grab a rung. “Thanks.”

“Amaze. Humans helpless without light.”

“Yes,” I say. “Come to the control room.”

“Yes.” I hear him scurry through his tunnel.

I climb up and it’s just as dark. The whole control room is dead. The monitors are off. Even the airlock window doesn't help—that part of the ship is facing away from Tau Ceti right now.

“Control room also have no light, question?” Rocky’s voice says—probably from his bulb in the ceiling.

“Nothing—wait…I see something….”

In one corner of one panel, there’s a small red LED. It’s glowing, but not very brightly. I sit in the pilot’s seat and squint at the control. The seat wobbles a bit. I didn’t do a great job repairing it, but it’s anchored to the floor again, at least.

Instead of the usual flat-panel displays all over the control room, this one little section has real buttons and an LCD display nearby. The light is coming from a button.

Of course, I push it. What else would I do?

The LCD display lights up. Some very pixelated text appears, saying: PRIMARY GENERATOR: OFFLINE. SECONDARY GENERATOR: OFFLINE. EMERGENCY BATTERIES: 100%.

“Okay, how do I use the batteries…?” I mumble.

“Progress, question?”

“Hang on.” I look all around the LCD panel until I finally see it. A little switch, covered by a plastic safety shield. It’s labeled “Batt.” It’ll have to do. I lift the shield and flip it.

Dim LEDs light up the control room—not nearly as well as the normal lights do. The smallest control screen—and only that screen—lights up. The Hail Mary mission patch is on the center of the screen and the words “Loading Operating System…” appear at the bottom.

“It’s working a bit,” I say. “My backup battery is on. But the generators are off.”

“Why not work, question?”

“I don’t know.”

“Your air okay, question? No power, no air to breathe. Humans breathe out carbon dioxide. You use all air and get hurt, question?”

“It’s okay,” I say. “The ship is big. It will take a long time for the air to go bad. I need to find out why this happened first.”

“Machines break. Show me. I fix.”

That’s a good idea. Rocky can do almost anything. Maybe he’s special, or all Eridians are like that. Either way, I’m very lucky. But can he fix human tech?

“Maybe. But first I need to know why both generators stopped at the same time.”

“Good question. More important: Can you fly ship without power, question?”

“No. I need power to do anything.”

“Then, most important: How long until orbit falls apart, question?”

I blink a couple of times. “I…don’t know.”

“Work fast.”

“Yeah.” I point at the screen. “First I have to wait for my computer to start up.”

“Hurry.”

“Okay, I’ll wait faster.”

“Sarcasm.”

The computer finishes starting up and shows a screen I’ve never seen. I can tell it’s bad news, because the word “TROUBLE” is in big letters at the top.

The nice buttons and tools from before the blackout are gone. This screen is just three rows of white text on a black background. The left is all Chinese, the middle is Russian, and the right is English.

I guess when things are normal, the ship changes the language based on who’s reading the screen. And this “safe boot” screen doesn’t know who will read it, so it’s in all our languages.

“What's going on?”

“This screen showed up with some information.”

“What’s wrong?”

“Let me read!”

Rocky can be a real pain when he’s worried. I read the status report.

EMERGENCY POWER: ONLINE

BATTERY: 100%

ESTIMATED TIME REMAINING: 04D, 16H, 17M

SABATIER LIFE SUPPORT: OFFLINE

CHEMICAL ABSORPTION LIFE SUPPORT: ONLINE. !!!LIMITED DURATION, NON-RENEWABLE!!!

TEMPERATURE CONTROL: OFFLINE

TEMPERATURE: 22°C

PRESSURE: 40,071 PA

“The ship’s keeping me alive, but not doing anything else.”

“Give me generator. I fix.”

“First I need to find it,” I say.

Rocky slumps. “You don't know where ship parts are?!”

“The computer has all that! I can’t remember everything!”

“Human brain useless!”

“Oh, shut up!”

I climb down the ladder to the lab. The emergency lights are on in here too. Rocky follows along in his tunnel.

I reach down, grab my tool bag, and keep going to the next ladder. He keeps following me.

“Where you go, question?”

“The storage area. It’s the only place I haven’t completely searched. And it’s the very bottom of the crew area. If the generator is easy for the crew to get to, that’s where it’ll be.”

Once in the dormitory, I crawl into the storage space. My arm hurts. I climb around to check the wall with the fuel bay. My arm hurts more.

By now, my arm just always hurts, so I try to ignore it. But no more painkillers. They just make me too dumb. I lie back in the storage area and let the pain go down a bit. There must be access panels in here, right? I can’t remember exactly how the ship is laid out, but important equipment is probably inside the pressurized area. For this very reason. Right?

How do I find it, though? I’d need x-ray vision to know where—oh, hey!

“Rocky! Are there any doors in here?”

He is silent for a moment. He taps on the wall a few times. “Six small doors.”

“Six?! Ugh. Tell me where the first one is.” I put my hand on the compartment ceiling.

“Move hand toward your feet and left…”

I follow his directions to the first door. Man, they’re hard to see. The emergency lights in the dormitory are weak to begin with, and very little light gets into the compartment. It's dim.

The panel is held by a simple flat-head screw that controls a latch. I turn it with a short screwdriver from my toolkit. The panel opens to show a pipe with a valve on it. The label reads PRIMARY OXYGEN SHUTOFF. Definitely don’t want to touch that. I close the cabinet.

“Next door.”

One by one, he leads me to each door and I check what’s behind it. I know he can sense the shapes behind the doors with sonar, but that’s no good. I’d rather just see what’s there than have him describe what he senses in our limited way of talking.

Behind the fourth door, I find it.

It’s a lot smaller than I expect. The whole space is about 1 cubic foot. The generator is in a black case with an odd shape, and I only know it’s a generator because of the label. I see two thick pipes with shutoff valves, and some normal-looking electrical wires.

“Found it,” I say.

“Good,” Rocky’s voice comes from the dormitory. “Take it out and give it to me.”

“I want to look at it first.”

“You not good at this. I fix.”

“The generator might not work in your environment!”

“Mmm,” he grumbles.

“If I can’t fix it, you can tell me how.”

“Mmm.”

The two pipes with shutoff valves must be the Astrophage supply lines. I look closer in the cubby and see labels. One says “fuel” and the other says “waste.” Easy enough.

I use a wrench to unscrew the hose on the “waste” line. Right when it comes loose, a dark liquid drips out. Not much—just what was between the shutoff valve and my end of the hose. It must be the fluid we use to carry away dead Astrophage. I got some on my hand—it feels slimy. Maybe it’s oil. That’s smart, actually. Any liquid will work, oil is lighter than water, and it won’t rust the pipes.

Next I unscrew the “fuel” line. It also sloshes out brown liquid. But this time, it smells terrible.

I wince and hide my face in my arm. “Ugh! God!”

“What is problem?” Rocky asks from below.

“The fuel smells bad,” I say. Eridians can't smell. But while explaining sight to Rocky took forever, smell was easy. Eridians can taste. Smell is just tasting from far away.

“Is it a natural or chemical smell?”

I sniff again, slowly. “It smells like rotten food. Astrophage usually doesn’t smell bad. It usually doesn't smell at all.”

“Astrophage is alive. Maybe Astrophage can rot.”

“Astrophage can’t rot,” I say. “How could it rot—OH NO! OH GOD NO!”

I wipe my hand across the stinky goo and climb out of the compartment. Then, keeping my gooey hand in the air and not touching anything, I climb the ladder to the lab.

Rocky clatters through his tunnel. “What’s wrong?”

“No, no, no, no…” I say, my voice cracking. My heart is pounding. I think I’m going to throw up.

I smear some goo on a glass slide and shove the slide into the microscope. There's no power for the light, so I grab a flashlight from the drawer and shine it on the plate. It’ll have to work.

I look through the lenses and my worst fears come true. “Oh God.”

“What is problem, question?!” Rocky’s voice is much higher than usual.

I grab my head, getting gross stuff on myself, but I don’t care. “Taumoeba. There are Taumoeba in the generator.”

“They hurt generator, question?” Rocky says. “Give me generator. I fix.”

“The generator works,” I say. “If there are Taumoeba in the generator, they’re also in the fuel. Taumoeba ate all the Astrophage. We have no power because we have no fuel.”

Rocky lifts his shell so fast it hits the tunnel roof. “How Taumoeba get into fuel, question?!”

“There are Taumoeba in my lab. I didn’t keep them closed off. I didn’t think about it. Some probably got out. The ship has cracks and holes since we almost died at Adrian. A small hole in a fuel line must have let Taumoeba in. It only takes one.”

“Bad! Very bad!”

I start breathing too fast. “We’re dead in space. We’re stuck here forever.”

“Not forever,” Rocky says.

I look up. “Really?”

“No. Orbit failing soon. Then we die.”

—

I spend the whole next day checking the fuel lines I can reach. It’s the same everywhere. Instead of Astrophage in oil, it’s Taumoeba and (let’s be honest) a lot of Taumoeba waste. Mostly methane with some other stuff mixed in. I guess that’s why there’s methane in Adrian’s air. Life goes on and all that.

There’s a little live Astrophage here and there, but with so much Taumoeba in the fuel, it won’t last. It’s no use trying to save it. It’d be like trying to get good meat from something rotten with botulism.

“Hopeless,” I say, dropping the latest fuel sample hard on the lab table. “The Taumoeba is everywhere.”

“I have Astrophage on my side of wall,” Rocky says. “About two hundred sixteen grams left.”

“That wouldn’t power my spin drive for long. Maybe thirty seconds. And it probably wouldn’t last. There’s Taumoeba everywhere on my side of the wall. Keep your Astrophage safe on your side.”

“I build new engine,” Rocky says. “Taumoeba change Astrophage to methane. Burn with oxygen. Make fire. Make push. Go to my ship. Lots of Astrophage there.”

“That’s…not a bad idea.” I rub my chin. “Use Taumoeba farts to move through space.”

“No understand word after Taumoeba.”

“It’s not important. Hang on, let me do the math….”

I pull up a tablet—the computer screen in the lab is still offline. I don’t remember how strong methane’s push is, but I do know that a hydrogen-oxygen reaction is about 450 seconds. Let’s say that’s the best it could be. I had 20,000 kilograms of Astrophage, so let’s pretend it’s all methane now. The ship weighs about 100,000 kilograms without fuel. I don’t even know if I have enough oxygen for this, but let’s ignore that for now….

It’s hard to concentrate. I’m tired and I know it.

I type away on the calculator app, then shake my head. “It’s no good. The ship would only get less than 800 meters per second of speed. We can’t escape Adrian’s gravity with that, much less cross 150 million kilometers of the Tau Ceti system.”

“Bad.”

I drop the tablet on the table and rub my eyes. “Yes. Bad.”

He clicks along his tunnel to hover above me. “Give me generator.”

I slump my shoulders. “Why? What good would it do?”

“I clean and sterilize. Remove all Taumoeba. I make tiny fuel tank with my Astrophage. Seal generator airtight. Give back to you. You hook up to ship. Power restored.”

I rub my aching arm. “Yeah. It’s a good idea. If the generator doesn’t melt in your air.”

“If melt, I fix.”

A few hundred grams of Astrophage isn’t enough to fly around the galaxy, but it’s more than enough to power the ship’s electrical system for…I don’t know…at least the rest of my life.

“Okay. Yeah. That’s a good idea. At least we’ll have the ship back online.”

“Yes.”

I trudge to the hatch. “I’ll get the generator.”

I really shouldn’t be using tools feeling like this, but I keep going. I go back to the dormitory, get into the crawlspace, and detach the generator. Or maybe it’s the backup generator. I don’t know. Either way, it turns Astrophage into electricity and that’s what matters.

I get back into the dormitory and put the generator in our airlock there. Rocky cycles the airlock and brings the generator to his workbench. Two claws get to work on it right away. A third points to my bunk. “I work on this now. You sleep.”

“Make sure you don’t get Taumoeba in your Astrophage over there!”

“My Astrophage in sealed xenonite container. Is safe. You sleep now.”

Everything aches, especially my bandaged arm. “I can’t sleep.”

He points more firmly. “You tell me humans need to sleep eight hours every sixteen hours. You have not slept for thirty-one hours. You sleep now.”

I sit on my bunk and sigh. “You’re right. I should at least try. It’s been a hard day. Night. Whatever. A hard day’s night.” I lie back in the bunk and pull the blanket over me.

“That sentence makes no sense.”

“It’s an Earth saying. From a song.” I close my eyes and mumble. “…and I’ve been working like a dog…”

A moment passes while I drift off…

“Whoa!” I shoot straight up. “The beetles!”

Rocky is surprised enough to drop the generator. “What is the problem?”

“Not a problem! A solution!” I leap to my feet. “The beetles! My ship has four smaller ships aboard called beetles! They’re made to take information back to Earth!”

“You told me this before,” Rocky says. “But they use the same fuel, correct? All the astrophage are dead now.”

I shake my head. “They use Astrophage, yeah, but each beetle is its own sealed unit. They don’t share air, fuel, or anything with Hail Mary. And each beetle has 120 kilograms of fuel! We have plenty of Astrophage!”

Rocky waves his arms in the air. “Enough to get us to my ship! Good news! Good good good!”

I wave my arms in the air too. “Maybe we won’t die here after all! I need to go outside to get beetles. I’ll be right back.” I hop off the bunk and head to the ladder.

“No!” Rocky says. He skitters over to the partition and taps the divider. “You sleep. Humans don't work well when tired. Going outside is dangerous. Sleep first. Outside later.”

I roll my eyes. “All right, all right.”

He points back to my bunk. “Sleep.”

“Yes, Mom.”

“Sarcasm. You sleep. I watch.”

“This doesn’t seem like a good idea anymore,” I say into my radio.

“Do task,” Rocky replies mercilessly.

I slept well and woke up ready to face the day. I had a nice breakfast. I stretched. Rocky gave me a sealed, fully functional generator that will last basically forever. I installed it and got the ship’s power back on without a problem.

Rocky and I talked about the best way to use the beetles to get back to the Blip-A. Everything seemed like a good idea until just now.

I stand in the airlock, all suited up for a spacewalk, looking out onto the vast emptiness of space. Planet Adrian reflects its pale-green light at me, lighting up the ship. Then it drifts out of view. I’m in darkness. But not for long. Because the planet shows up again at the top of my vision twelve seconds later.

The Hail Mary is still spinning. That’s a problem.

The ship has little Astrophage-powered thrusters on the sides to spin up and spin down for the artificial gravity. They don’t work, of course. They’re full of Taumoeba poop just like everything else. So here I am on another spacewalk that has to deal with gravity. But instead of Adrian’s gravity, it’s centripetal force threatening to throw me off into space.

One death is as good as another. So why is this worse than my little Adrian sample adventure? Because this time I have to balance on the nose of the ship. One wrong move could lead to death.

When I got the sampler, I stayed close to the hull, kept tethered, and had lots of handholds all around just in case I slipped.

But the beetles are stored in the nose of the ship.

The nose points toward the other side of the ship because of how the centrifuge works. This puts the beetles at the “top” of the crew area, as far as the artificial gravity is concerned. I need to go up there, open the nose, and get the small ships out. All while hoping I don’t slip. There are no places to attach a safety line at the nose. So, I’ll have to clip onto a spot lower down. This means if I fall, I’ll pick up speed before the line stops me. Will it hold? If not, the centrifuge will throw me into space, and I’ll become Adrian’s newest moon.

I check the safety lines four times. I used two of them, just to be safe. They’re firmly attached to a strong point in the airlock and to my suit. They should be able to handle the force if I fall.

“Should.”

I step out, grab the top of the airlock, and pull myself up. I could never do this with all my equipment on at full gravity.

The angle of the nose cone isn’t too steep, so I don’t slide off. I check the lines again, then crawl up the nose toward the top. The centrifuge pushes me to the side as I move. I have to stop every few feet and let friction with the ship’s surface stop my sideways movement.

“Status, question?”

“Making progress,” I say.

“Good.”

I reach the nose. The artificial gravity is weakest here, since it’s closest to the center of the spinning. That’s a nice little bonus.

The universe slowly spins around me every twenty-five seconds. For half the time, Adrian fills my entire view below. Then I see Tau Ceti’s bright light for a few seconds. Then nothing. It’s a little unsettling but not too bad. Just a bit annoying.

The beetle hatch is right where it should be. I’ll have to be careful here. I don’t want to break anything.

This was meant to be a suicide mission. They didn’t care if the Hail Mary made it back. The mechanism inside has explosives to blow off this hatch. Then the beetles can launch and find their way back to Earth. Good system, but I need this hatch to be undamaged for when I go home. It’s all about the aerodynamics.

Yes, aerodynamics.

The Hail Mary has always looked like something from a Heinlein novel. Shiny silver, smooth hull, sharp nose cone. Why bother with that for a ship that will never be in an atmosphere?

Because of the stuff between stars. There’s a very small amount of hydrogen and helium floating around in space. It’s about one atom per cubic centimeter, but when you’re traveling close to the speed of light, that adds up. Not only because you’re hitting many atoms, but also because those atoms, from your point of view, weigh more than normal. Relativistic physics is strange.

In short: I need the nose undamaged.

The whole panel and explosive device is attached to the hull with six hex bolts. I get a socket wrench from my tool belt and start working.

As soon as I unscrew the first bolt, it slides down the nose cone and falls into the endless distance.

“Um…” I say. “Rocky, you can make screws, right?”

“Yes. Easy. Why ask?”

“I dropped one.”

“Hold screws better.”

“How?”

“Use hand.”

“My hand’s busy with the wrench.”

“Use second hand.”

“My other hand’s on the hull to keep me steady.”

“Use third han—hmm. Get beetles. I’ll make new screws.”

“Okay.”

I start on the second bolt. This time I’m very careful. I stop using the wrench halfway and finish by hand. The thick fingers of the spacesuit are terrible for this. It takes ten minutes just for this one bolt. But I finish it and, most important, I don’t drop it.

I put it in a pouch on my suit. Now Rocky will know what I need him to copy.

I unscrew the next four bolts with the wrench and let them drop. I guess they’ll orbit Adrian for a while, but not forever. The slight drag up here will gradually slow them until they fall into Adrian’s atmosphere and burn up.

One bolt is left. But first, I lift the opposite corner of the assembly enough to create a small gap. I slip a tether through an empty bolt hole and clip it to itself. Then I clip the other end of the tether to my belt. Now I have four tethers attached to me. And I like it that way. I may look like space Spider-Man, but who cares?

I still have two more tethers coiled on my tool belt ready if needed. You can't have too many tethers.

I unscrew the last bolt and the assembly slides down the nose. I let it go past me and it stops at the end of the tether. It bounces a few times and hits the hull, then sways.

I look into the compartment. The beetles are where they should be, each in its own slot. The four little ships are identical except for a small name on each rounded fuel bay. They’re labeled “John,” “Paul,” “George,” and “Ringo,” of course.

“Status, question?”

“Recovering beetles.”

I start with John. A small clamp holds it in place, but I easily force it open. Behind the probe is a compressed air cylinder with a nozzle pointing out. That’s how they're launched. They need to be far from the ship before they start their spin drives. Even a cute little spin drive will vaporize anything behind it.

John comes out easily. The probe is bigger than I remember—almost the size of a suitcase. Of course, everything seems bigger when you’re doing an EVA with clumsy gloves.

Ol’ John is really heavy, too. I don’t know if I could even lift it in Earth’s gravity. I tie it to the backup tether, then reach in to get Paul.

—

Rocky can work fast when he needs to. And he needs to now.

We’re in a shaky orbit around Adrian. Now that the computers and guidance systems are back on, I can see the orbit. It’s not good. Our orbit is still very oval-shaped, and the closest part of it is way too close to the planet.

Every ninety minutes, we touch the very top of the atmosphere. It’s barely an atmosphere at that height. Just a few stray air molecules floating around. But it’s enough to slow us down just a little bit. That slowdown makes us go a little deeper into the atmosphere the next time around. You can see what’s going to happen.

We brush the atmosphere every ninety minutes. And I really don’t know how many times we can do that without problems. For some reason, the computer doesn’t have information for “oddly oval orbits around the planet Adrian.”

So yeah. Rocky is in a hurry.

It takes him just two hours to take Paul apart and understand most of how he works. This wasn't easy—before we moved Paul into Rocky’s area of the ship, we had to make a special “cooling box.” The beetles have plastic parts inside that would melt in Rocky’s air. A big piece of Astrophage took care of that. Astrophage may be too hot for humans to touch, but it’s cool enough that plastic won’t melt, and of course it easily absorbs the extra heat and keeps things at 96 degrees Celsius.

Paul has a lot of electronics and wires inside. Rocky doesn’t understand that too well—Eridian electronics aren’t nearly as advanced as Earth’s. They haven’t invented the transistor yet, much less IC chips. Working with Rocky is like having the world’s best engineer from 1950 on the ship with me.

It seems strange that a species could invent interstellar travel before the transistor, but Earth invented nuclear power, television, and even launched things into space several times before the transistor.

An hour later, he's gotten around all the computer controls. He doesn't have to understand them to do it—he just needs to know which wires to put power to directly. He rigged the spin drive to be turned on by a sound-based remote control. Where humans use radio for short-range digital communication, Eridians use sound.

He does the same for Ringo and John. This time it's much faster, because there's no research needed. That leaves George unchanged. The little beetles don't have much power, so the more we use the better, but I have to stop somewhere. I want to keep one safe, unchanged, ready to do what it was originally meant to do.

Because of Rocky, I might survive this mission, but it's not certain. The Hail Mary is in bad shape. Several fuel tanks are gone, and there's damage and leaks everywhere. There are Taumoeba waiting to eat any new fuel Rocky gives me. I can think of at least a hundred ways the trip home could fail. So, before I leave, I'm going to send George off with all my data and some Taumoeba. I'd rather have kept two, but we need three beetles to aim the thrust so we can angle the ship where we need it.

Rocky passes the three changed beetles through the dormitory airlock to my side.

“You put on hull,” he says. “Aim forty-five degrees out away from center of ship.”

“Okay.” I sigh. Another EVA on a spinning ship. Great.

But what else can I do? We can't stop the rotation without thrust.

I do the EVA. The only hard part is getting to the right place. The airlock is near the front, and I need to put the beetles on the back. And the ship is now in two halves connected by only five cables. But the designers of the Hail Mary thought of this. There are loops all along the cables to attach a tether to.

I'm getting better at the strange skill of doing EVAs in non-zero gravity. And unlike my risky move on the front of the ship, the back has lots of handles. Putting the beetles on is easy. I attach them to handles on the hull to hold them still while Rocky's xenonite glue dries and holds them in place.

In the end, I have John, Paul, and Ringo spaced equally around the hull, each angled so their engine points 45 degrees away from the ship's length.

“Beetles set,” I say into my radio. “Checking damaged area.”

“Good,” Rocky replies.

I head to where the fuel tank broke. There isn’t much to see—I got rid of the bad tanks right away. A missing rectangle of hull shows the space where the tanks used to be. The area around the hole shows damage. Black scorch marks stain the shiny hull. Two panels next to it are clearly bent.

“Some panels are bent. Some have burn marks. Not too bad.”

“Good news.”

“The burn marks are strange, don’t you think? Why burn marks?”

“Lots of heat.”

“Yeah, but no oxygen. This is space. How did it burn?”

“Idea: Many Astrophage in tanks. Some probably dead. Dead Astrophage have water. Dead Astrophage not protected from heat. Water and lots of heat become hydrogen and oxygen. Oxygen, heat, and hull become burn marks.”

“Yeah,” I say. “Good idea.”

“Thank.”

I get back across the cable bridge and into the airlock without any trouble. Rocky waits for me in his ceiling bulb in the control room.

“All is well, question?”

“Yes,” I say. “Controls for John, Paul, and Ringo are good?”

He holds identical control boxes in three of his hands. Each has a wire going to a speaker/microphone on the wall attached to the hull. He taps a readout box with a fourth hand. “Communication established. All beetles work and are ready.”

I strap myself into the command chair. This next part is going to be uncomfortable.

We put the beetles at 45-degree angles from the ship's middle line so we can angle the ship as needed. It also lets us control the ship’s spin. But we can only use the beetles when the ship is in one piece. So first I have to pull the halves together.

Because rotational inertia stays the same, that means the ship is going to spin really fast. In fact, it’ll spin exactly as fast as it was when Rocky had to save me last time. We haven’t gained or lost any inertia in that time.

I bring up the Centrifuge panel on the main control screen. Well, it’s just above the original main screen. That main screen got wrecked in the Adrian adventure. But this one’s good enough.

“You ready?”

“Yes.”

“The g-forces will be strong,” I say. “Easy for you, but hard for me. I might pass out.”

“Unhealthy for human, question?” There’s a slight tremble at the end.

“A little unhealthy. If I pass out, don’t worry. Just keep the ship stable. I’ll wake up when we stop spinning.”

“Understand.” Rocky holds the three controls, ready to go.

“Okay, here goes.” I put the centrifuge into manual mode and skip three warning messages. First, I turn the crew compartment 180 degrees. Just like last time, I go slow. But unlike last time, I have everything tied down. So as the world turns around and gravity changes directions, the lab and dormitory aren’t thrown into chaos.

Now I feel half a g pushing me toward the control panels. The nose is facing away from the rest of the ship again. I tell all four spools to pull in, no matter how fast the ship is spinning. The icons on the ship show them pulling in as ordered, and the force pushing me into the restraints gets stronger.

After just ten seconds, the forces are at 6 g’s and I can barely breathe. I gasp and struggle.

“You are not healthy!” Rocky squeaks. “Undo this. We make new plan.”

I can’t speak, so I shake my head. I feel the skin of my face pull tight. I must look like a monster right now. The edges of what I see fade to black. This must be the tunnel vision I’ve heard about. It’s a good name.

The tunnel gets darker and darker until it’s all black.

I wake up a few moments later. At least, I think it’s a few moments. My arms float freely, and only my straps keep me from floating out of my chair.

“Grace! You okay?”

“Uh.” I rub my eyes. My vision is blurry, and I’m still groggy. “Yeah. What’s happening?”

“Rotation stopped,” he says. “Beetles hard to control. Correction: Beetles easy to control. Ship powered by beetles hard to control.”

“You got it done, though. Good job.”

“Thank.”

I release my straps and stretch. Nothing seems broken or hurt except my burned arm from before. It feels great to be back in zero g. I’m always achy. Lots of hard work, and I’m still healing from injuries. Getting rid of gravity puts less stress on my body.

I check the screens on the monitor. “All systems are okay. At least, nothing’s damaged more than before.”

“Good. What's the next step, question?”

“Now I do math. A lot of math. I have to figure out how long and at what angle we need to fire the engines to get back to your ship, using the beetles as engines.”

“Good.”

Chapter 23

I arrived at the meeting on time, or so I thought. The email said 12:30, but when I got there, everyone was already seated, silent, and staring at me.

We were keeping the accident quiet for now. The world was watching this project—their only hope. The last thing we needed was for people to know our main and backup scientists were dead. Say what you will about the Russians, they know how to keep a secret. All of Baikonur was locked down.

The meeting room, a simple trailer provided by the Russians, had a great view of the launch pad. I could see the Soyuz through the window. It was old technology, but the most reliable launch system ever made.

Stratt and I hadn’t spoken since the explosion. She suddenly had to lead a quick investigation into the disaster. It couldn’t wait—if the accident was caused by something that would be used on the mission, we needed to know. I wanted to help, but she wouldn’t let me. Someone had to deal with the small, desperate issues reported by the ESA team.

Stratt stared right at me. Dimitri played with some papers—probably a design to improve the spin-drive. Dr. Lokken, the passionate Norwegian who designed the centrifuge, drummed her fingers on the table. Dr. Lamai wore her lab coat as always. Her team had perfected a fully automated medical robot, and she’d probably win a Nobel Prize someday, if Earth survived that long. Even Steve Hatch, the eccentric Canadian who invented the beetle probes, was there. He, at least, didn’t look uncomfortable. He just typed on a calculator. He had no papers, just the calculator.

Commander Yáo and Engineer Ilyukhina were also present. Yáo looked gloomy as usual, and Ilyukhina had no drink in her hand.

“Am I late?” I asked.

“No, you’re just in time,” Stratt said. “Have a seat.”

I sat in the only empty chair.

“We think we know what happened at the research center,” Stratt began. “The building is gone, but their records were all digital and saved on a server used by all of Baikonur. Luckily, that server is in the Ground Control Building. Also, DuBois—being DuBois—kept very careful notes.”

She held up a paper. “According to his online journal, yesterday he planned to test a very unusual problem that could happen in a generator powered by Astrophage.”

Ilyukhina shook her head. “I should have been the one testing this. I take care of ship maintenance. DuBois should have asked me.”

“What was he testing?” I asked.

Lokken cleared her throat. “A month ago, JAXA found a possible problem with the generator. It uses Astrophage to create heat, which powers a small turbine using a material that changes state. Old, reliable technology. It uses very little Astrophage—only twenty cells at a time.”

“That seems safe,” I said.

“It is. But if the system that controls the generator's pump fails, and there’s a very thick clump of Astrophage in the fuel line at the same time, up to one nanogram of Astrophage could enter the reaction chamber.”

“What would that do?”

“Nothing. The generator also controls how much IR light shines on the Astrophage. If the chamber gets too hot, the IR lights turn off to let the Astrophage cool down. Safe backup system. But there's a possible, very unlikely situation where a short in this system could make the IR lights turn on at full power and ignore the temperature safety. DuBois wanted to test this very, very unlikely situation.”

“So what did he do?”

Lokken paused and her lip trembled a bit. She braced herself and continued. “He got a fake generator—one of the ones we use for testing on the ground. He changed the pump and lights to make that weird thing happen on purpose. He wanted to set off a whole nanogram of Astrophage at once and see how it wrecked the generator.”

“Hold on,” I said. “One nanogram isn’t enough to destroy a building. At worst it could melt a little metal.”

“Yeah,” said Lokken. She breathed in deeply and then let it out. “You know how we keep tiny amounts of Astrophage, right?”

“Sure,” I said. “In little plastic containers floating in propylene glycol.”

She nodded. “When DuBois asked for one nanogram of Astrophage from the supply center, they gave him one milligram by mistake. And since the containers look the same and the amounts are so small, he and Shapiro couldn’t tell.”

“Oh God.” I rubbed my eyes. “That’s a million times more heat than they thought. It blew up the building and everyone inside. God.”

Stratt shuffled her papers. “The truth is: We don’t know how to handle Astrophage safely. If you asked for a firecracker and someone gave you a truckload of explosives, you’d know something was wrong. But the difference between a nanogram and a milligram? People just can’t tell.”

We were all quiet for a moment. She was right. We’d been playing with energy like an atomic bomb like it was nothing. In any other situation it would have been crazy. But we had no choice.

“So are we going to push back the launch?” I asked.

“No, we’ve discussed it and we all agree: We can’t delay the Hail Mary ’s launch. It’s put together, tested, fueled, and ready.”

“It's the orbit,” Dimitri said. “It's in a close orbit at a 51.6-degree angle, so Cape Canaveral and Baikonur can reach it easily. But it's also in a low orbit, which is getting lower. If it doesn't leave in the next three weeks, we'll have to send an entire mission just to raise it to a higher orbit.”

“The Hail Mary will leave on time,” said Stratt. “Five days from now. The crew will have two days of preflight checks, so the Soyuz has to launch in three days.”

“Okay,” I said. “What about the science expert? I'm sure we have hundreds of volunteers all over the world. We can give the chosen person a quick course in the science they need to know—”

“The decision's been made,” Stratt said. “Actually, it made itself. There's no time to train someone in everything they need to know. There's just too much information and research to learn. Even the smartest scientists couldn't learn it all in just three days. And remember, only about one in seven thousand people have the right genes to resist comas.”

Right then, I got a bad feeling. “I think I see where this is going.”

“As you probably know, your tests were positive. You're that one in seven thousand.”

“Welcome to the crew!” Ilyukhina said.

“Wait, wait. No.” I shook my head. “This is crazy. I know about Astrophage, but I don't know anything about being an astronaut.”

“We'll train you as we go.” Yáo spoke quietly, but confidently. “And we'll do the hard work. You'll only be used for science.”

“I just mean…come on! There has to be someone else!” I looked to Stratt. “What about Yáo's backup? Or Ilyukhina's?”

“They’re not biologists,” said Stratt. “They’re incredibly skilled at knowing the Hail Mary inside and out, how it works, and how to fix it. But we don’t have time to teach someone all the biology they need to know. It’s like asking a great building engineer to do brain surgery. It’s just not what they do.”

“What about other people on the list? The ones who weren’t first choices?”

“No one is as good as you. Honestly, we’re incredibly lucky you can resist comas. Do you think I kept you on the project this long because I needed a middle school teacher?”

“Oh…” I said.

“You know how the ship works,” Stratt continued. “You know the science behind Astrophage. You know how to use a spacesuit and all the special equipment. You’ve been at every important science or strategy meeting about the ship and its mission—I made sure of it. You have the right genes, so I made damn sure you had the right skills. I didn’t want this to happen, but here we are. You’ve been the backup science expert all along.”

“N-No, that can’t be true,” I said. “There must be other people. Scientists who are much better. And, you know, people who actually want to go. You must have made a list, right? Who’s the next person after me?”

Stratt picked up a paper in front of her. “Andrea Cáceres, who works at a distillery in Paraguay. She can resist comas and has a chemistry degree with some biology. And she offered to go on the mission when we first asked for astronauts.”

“Sounds great,” I said. “Let’s call her.”

“But you’ve had years of training. You know the ship and the mission completely. And you’re one of the world’s top experts on Astrophage. We’d only have a few days to train Cáceres. You know how I work, Dr. Grace. Better than anyone. I want to give Hail Mary every chance to succeed. And right now, that’s you.”

I looked down at the table. “But I…I don’t want to die….”

“Nobody does,” said Stratt.

“It must be your decision,” said Yáo. “I won’t have someone on my crew who doesn’t want to be here. You must come willingly. If you refuse, we’ll bring in Ms. Cáceres and try to train her. But I urge you to say yes. Billions of lives are at stake. Our lives don’t matter much compared to such a tragedy.”

I put my head in my hands. The tears started to flow. Why did this have to happen to me? “Can I think about it?”

“Yes,” Stratt said. “But not for long. If you say no, we have to get Cáceres here quickly. I want your answer by five p.m. tonight.”

I stood and shuffled out of the room. I don’t think I even said goodbye. It’s a dark and depressing feeling to have all your closest colleagues get together and decide you should die.

I checked my watch—12:38 p.m. I had four and a half hours to decide.

—

The Hail Mary’s spin drives are much too strong for its current weight. When we left Earth, the ship weighed 2.1 million kilograms—mostly fuel. Now the ship only weighs 120,000 kilograms, about one-twentieth of its starting weight.

Because the Hail Mary is so light now, the little beetles can give me 1.5 g’s of thrust together. But the ship wasn’t made to have thrust coming in at a 45-degree angle, pushing on random EVA handles on the hull. If we use the beetles at full power, they’ll just rip free of the handles and fly off into the Tauset.

Rocky remembered that when he stopped our spin. Now we have it under control, and I can do spacewalks in zero gravity as easily as breathing. I 3-D print a model of the Hail Mary’s insides and give it to Rocky to study. In less than an hour, he not only has a solution but has made the xenonite supports to put it in place.

So, I do another spacewalk. I add the xenonite supports to the beetles. For once, everything goes as planned. Rocky assures me that the ship can now handle full power from the beetles, and I believe him completely. The guy knows engineering.

I type a bunch of calculations into a complicated Excel spreadsheet that probably has errors in it somewhere. It takes me six hours to put together. I finally come up with what I think is the right answer. At least, it should put us close enough that we can see the Blip-A. Then we can adjust our course from there.

“Ready?” I say from the pilot seat.

“Ready,” Rocky says in his bulb. He holds the three control boxes in his hands.

“Okay…John and Paul to 4.5 percent.”

“John and Paul, 4.5 percent, confirmed,” he says.

Sure, Rocky could have made controls for me to use, but this is better. I have to watch the screen closely and pay attention to our course. Best to have someone give their full attention to the beetles. Besides, Rocky’s a ship engineer. Who better to run our makeshift engines?

“John and Paul to zero. Ringo to 1.1 percent,” I say.

“John and Paul zero. Ringo 1.1.”

We nudge the ship's direction a little at a time until it points roughly where I want. Finally, it seems right.

“Here goes nothing,” I say. “All ahead full!”

“John, Paul, Ringo 100 percent.”

I'm pushed back as the ship jumps forward, 1.5 g's of gravity pressing down as we speed straight (I hope) toward Blip-A.

“Keep this thrust for three hours,” I say.

“Three hours. I watch engines. You relax.”

“Thanks, but I can’t rest. I want to use gravity while I can.”

“I stay here. Tell me about the experiments.”

“Will do.”

I’m aiming for another eleven-day trip. It takes 130 kilograms of fuel to do that—about a quarter of what the beetles have (including George, who is on the lab table full of Astrophage). That should leave enough to fix any dumb errors in my calculations.

We’ll get up to full speed in three hours, then we’ll drift for almost eleven days. I don’t want to mess with starting or stopping the centrifuge. Yes, it can be done—Rocky showed it when he turned it off before. But it was tricky with lots of guessing and chances to lose control. Or worse—getting the cables tangled.

So, for the next three hours I have 1.5 times the normal force of gravity to deal with. After that it’ll be zero gravity for a while. Time to go to the lab.

I climb down the ladder. My arm hurts, but not as much as before. I’ve been changing the bandages every day—or rather, Dr. Lamai’s amazing medical machine has been doing it. There’s definitely scarring all over the skin. I’m going to have an ugly arm and shoulder forever. But I think the deeper layers of skin must have survived. If they hadn’t, I probably would have died of gangrene by now. Or Lamai’s machine would have cut off my arm when I wasn’t looking.

It’s been a while since I had to deal with 1.5 g’s. My legs don’t like it. But I’m used to this by now.

I walk to the main lab table, where the Taumoeba experiments are still going on. Every part of them is securely attached to the table, just in case we have more unexpected changes in speed. Of course, it’s not like I’m short on Taumoeba. I have a lot of them where my fuel used to be.

I check the Venus experiment first. The cooling system hums quietly, keeping the inside temperature right for Venus’s upper atmosphere. I originally planned to let the Taumoeba incubate in there for only an hour, but then the lights went out and we had other things to worry about. So now it’s been four days. If nothing else, they’ve had plenty of time to do their thing.

I gulp. This is important. The small glass slide inside had a thin layer of Astrophage, only one cell thick. If the Taumoeba are alive and eating Astrophage, light will be able to pass through. The more light I see through that slide, the fewer Astrophage are still alive on it.

I brace myself, take a deep breath, and look inside.

Pitch black.

My breathing quickens. I take a flashlight out of my pocket and shine it from behind. No light gets through at all. My heart drops.

I step over to the Threeworld Taumoeba experiment. I look at the slide and see the same thing. Completely black.

Taumoeba can’t live on Venus or Threeworld. Or, at least, they aren’t eating. My stomach feels like it’s going to melt.

So close! We were so close! We have the answer right here! Taumoeba! A natural enemy to the thing that’s ruining our worlds! And it’s strong too. It can live and grow in my fuel tanks, obviously. But not in Venus or Threeworld’s air. Why not?!

“What you see?” Rocky asks.

“Failure,” I say. “Both experiments. The Taumoeba are all dead.”

I hear Rocky punch the wall. “Anger!”

“All this work! All of it for nothing. Nothing!” I slam my fist on the table. “I gave up so much for this! I sacrificed so much!”

I hear Rocky’s shell fall to the ground in his bulb. A sign of deep sadness.

We’re both quiet for a while; Rocky slumped in his bulb and me with my face buried in my hands.

Finally, I hear a scrape. It’s Rocky picking his shell off the floor. “We work more,” he says. “We no give up. We work hard. We are brave.”

“Yeah, I guess so.”

I’m not the right person for this job. I’m a last-minute fill-in because the people who were really qualified messed up. But I’m here. I might not have all the answers, but I’m here. I must have signed up, thinking it was a hopeless task. It doesn’t help Earth much, but it’s something.

—

Stratt’s trailer was twice as big as mine. Perks of being in charge, I guess. Though to be fair, she needed the space. She sat at a large table full of papers. I could see at least six different languages in four different writing systems on the papers in front of her, but she seemed fine with all of them.

A Russian soldier stood in one corner of the room. Not exactly standing straight, but not relaxed either. There was a chair next to him, but he had chosen to stand.

“Hello, Dr. Grace,” Stratt said without looking up. She pointed to the soldier. “That’s Private Meknikov. Even though we know the explosion was an accident, the Russians aren’t taking any risks.”

I looked at the soldier. “So he’s here to make sure made-up terrorists don’t kill you?”

“Something like that.” She looked up. “So, it’s five o’clock. Have you decided? Are you going to be the Hail Mary ’s science expert?”

I sat across from her. I couldn’t look her in the eye. “No.”

She scowled at me. “I see.”

“It’s…you know…the kids. I should stay here for the kids.” I shifted in my seat. “Even if the Hail Mary finds the answer, we’re going to have almost thirty years of misery.”

“Uh-huh,” she said.

“And, um, well, I’m a teacher. I should teach. We need to raise a strong, solid generation of survivors. Right now we’re soft. You, me, the whole Western world. We’re the result of growing up in comfort and stability like never before. It’s the kids of today that’ll have to make the world of tomorrow work. And they’re going to inherit a mess. I can really do a lot more by preparing kids for the world that’s coming. I should stay here on Earth where I’m needed.”

“On Earth,” she repeated. “Where you’re needed.”

“Y-Yeah.”

“As opposed to on the Hail Mary, where you could be key to solving the whole problem because you’re completely trained for it.”

“It’s not like that,” I said. “I mean. It’s a little like that. But look, I’m no good on a crew. I’m not some fearless explorer.”

“Oh, I know,” she said. She clenched her fist and looked away for a moment. Then back to me with a burning look I’d never seen before. “Dr. Grace. You’re a coward and you’re full of it.”

I winced.

“If you really cared so much about the children, you’d get on that ship without hesitation. You could save billions of them from the apocalypse instead of preparing hundreds of them for it.”

I shook my head. “It’s not about that—”

“Do you think I don’t know you, Dr. Grace?!” she yelled. “You’re a coward and you always have been. You gave up a great science career because people didn’t like a paper you wrote. You ran to the safety of children who think you’re the best teacher ever. You don’t have a romantic partner because that would mean you might get hurt. You avoid risk like the plague.”

I stood up. “Okay, it’s true! I’m afraid! I don’t want to die! I worked so hard on this project and I deserve to live! I’m not going, and that’s final! Get the next person on the list—that chemist from Paraguay. She wants to go!”
