She slammed her fist on the table. “I don’t care who wants to go. I care who’s most qualified! Dr. Grace, I’m sorry, but you are going on that mission. I know you’re afraid. I know you don’t want to die. But you’re going.”

“You’re crazy. I’m leaving now.” I turned to the door.

“Meknikov!” she shouted.

The soldier quickly stepped between me and the door.

I turned back to her. “You have got to be kidding.”

“It would have been easier if you’d just said yes.”

“What’s your plan?” I pointed at the soldier. “Hold me at gunpoint for four years?”

“You’ll be in a coma during the trip.”

I tried to run past Meknikov, but he stopped me easily. He wasn’t rough. He was just much stronger than me. He held me by the shoulders and turned me to face Stratt.

“This is crazy!” I yelled. “Yáo will never agree to this! He said he doesn’t want anyone on his ship who doesn't want to be there!”

“Yeah, that was unexpected. He’s annoyingly honorable,” Stratt said.

She picked up a checklist written in Dutch. “First, you’ll be held in a cell for a few days until the launch. You can’t talk to anyone. Right before launch, you’ll get a strong sedative to knock you out, and we’ll put you in the Soyuz.”

“Don’t you think Yáo will suspect something?”

“I’ll tell Commander Yáo and Specialist Ilyukhina that because you don't have much astronaut training, you were worried you’d panic during the launch, so you chose to be unconscious. Once on the Hail Mary , Yáo and Ilyukhina will put you in your medical bed and start your coma. They’ll do all the pre-launch prep from there. You’ll wake up at Tau Ceti.”

I started to panic. This crazy plan might work. “No! You can’t do that! I won’t do it! This is insane!”

She rubbed her eyes. “Believe it or not, Dr. Grace, I kind of like you. I don’t respect you very much, but I think you’re a good person.”

“Easy for you to say when you’re not the one being murdered! You’re murdering me!” Tears rolled down my face. “I don’t want to die! Don’t send me off to die! Please!”

She looked sad. “I don’t like this any more than you do, Dr. Grace. If it’s any comfort, you’ll be seen as a hero. If Earth survives this, there’ll be statues of you everywhere.”

“I won’t do it!” I choked, tasting bile. “I’ll ruin the mission! You kill me?! Fine! I’ll kill your mission! I’ll destroy the ship!”

She shook her head. “No, you won’t. You’re just saying that. Like I said, you’re a good man at heart. When you wake up, you’ll be angry. I’m sure Yáo and Ilyukhina will be pretty mad about what I did to you too. But in the end, you three will be out there and you’ll do your job. Because humanity needs you to. I’m almost certain you’ll do the right thing.”

“Try me!” I screamed. “Go on! Try me! See what happens!”

“But I can’t just hope for the best, can I?” She glanced at her paper again. “I always thought the American CIA would have the best interrogation drugs. But did you know it’s actually the French? It’s true. Their DGSE has perfected a drug that makes you forget things for a long time. Not just hours or days, but weeks. They used it during anti-terror operations. It can be helpful for a suspect to forget he was ever questioned.”

I stared at her in horror. My throat hurt from yelling.

“Your med bed will give you a good dose of it before you wake up. You and your crewmates will just think it’s a side effect of the coma. Yáo and Ilyukhina will explain the mission to you and you’ll get right to work. The French say the drug doesn’t erase skills, language, or anything like that. By the time you remember everything, you guys might have already sent the beetles back. And if not, I bet you’ll be too involved in the project to quit.”

She nodded to Meknikov. He dragged me out the door and pulled me down the path.

I twisted my head back toward the door and screamed, “You can’t do this!”

"Just think about the kids, Grace," she said from the doorway. "All those kids you'll be helping. Think about them."

Chapter 24

Oh.

Okay.

I get it now.

I’m not some brave explorer who bravely gave his life to save Earth. I’m a scared man who had to be forced onto the mission.

I’m a coward.

It all hit me at once. I sit on the stool and stare at the lab table. I went from almost losing it to…this. This is worse. I’m empty.

I’m a coward.

I’ve known for some time that I’m not the best hope for saving mankind. I’m just a guy with the genes to survive a coma. I accepted that a while ago.

But I didn’t know I was a coward.

I remember the feelings. I remember that panic. I remember it all now. Pure terror. Not for Earth or people or the kids. For me. Total panic.

“God damn you, Stratt,” I mutter.

What bugs me the most is she was right. Her plan worked. My memory’s back, and now I’m so into the mission I’ll still give it my all. And, come on, of course I would. What else would I do? Let 7 billion people die to get back at Stratt?

At some point, Rocky came through his tunnel to the lab. I don’t know how long he’s been there. He didn’t have to—he could "see" everything from the control room with his sonar. But he’s here.

“You are very sad,” he says.

“Yeah.”

“I am sad too. But we not be sad for long. You are scientist. I am engineer. Together we solve.”

I throw my hands up, frustrated. “How?!”

He clicked along the tunnel to the closest spot above me. “Taumoeba eat all your fuel. So Taumoeba survive and breed in fuel-tank environment.”

“So?”

“Most life can’t live without its air. I’d die if I wasn’t in Erid’s air. You’d die if you weren’t in Earth’s air. But Taumoeba survives even when it’s not in Adrian air. Taumoeba is stronger than Erid life—stronger than Earth life.”

I crane my neck to look up at him. “True. And Astrophage are also pretty tough. They can live in a vacuum and on the surface of stars.”

He tapped two claws together. “Yes yes. Astrophage and Taumoeba are from the same biosphere. They probably evolved from a common ancestor. Adrian life is very strong.”

I sit up. “Yeah. Okay.”

“You already have an idea. This isn’t a question. I know you. You already have an idea. Tell me your idea.”

I sigh. “Well…Venus, Threeworld, and Adrian all have a lot of carbon dioxide. The Astrophage breeding zone in all three is where the pressure is 0.02 atmospheres. So maybe I’ll start with a chamber full of pure carbon dioxide at 0.02 atmospheres and see if Taumoeba survives that. Then add in more gases one at a time to see what the problem is.”

“I understand,” says Rocky.

I get to my feet and dust off my jumpsuit. “I need you to make me a test chamber. Clear xenonite with valves so I can let air in and out. Also, I need to be able to set the temperature to minus 100 degrees Celsius, minus 50 degrees Celsius, or minus 82 degrees Celsius.”

I could use my own equipment, but why not take advantage of superior material and craftsmanship?

“Yes yes. I’ll make it now. We’re a team. We’ll fix this. Don’t be sad.” He skitters down the tunnel toward the dormitory.

I check my watch. “The main burn ends in thirty-four minutes. After that, let’s use the beetles to spin the ship.”

Rocky pauses. “Dangerous.”

“Yeah, I know. But we need gravity for the lab and I don’t want to wait eleven days. I want to use our time well.”

“Beetles set up for thrust, not spinning.”

He’s right. Our engine system is very basic. We don’t have motors or swivels to direct our thrust. We’re like a ship from the 1500s, but we’re using beetles for sails. Actually, that’s not quite right. The ship could at least control the angle of its sails. We’re more like a paddlewheel boat with a broken rudder.

It’s not all bad, though. We have some control by choosing how much each engine pushes. That’s how Rocky stopped our spin before. “It’s worth the risk.”

He skitters back up the tunnel to face me. “Ship will spin unevenly. Can’t release centrifuge cables. They’ll tangle.”

“We’ll get the spin right first, then shut off the beetles, then release the cables.”

He draws back. “If ship not released, force is too strong for human.”

That’s a problem. I want 1 g of gravity for the lab when the ship is split in two. To get that much spin with the ship in one piece means spinning it very fast. Last time we did that, I passed out in the control room and Rocky almost died saving me.

“Okay…” I say. “How about this: I’ll lie down in the storage room under the dorm. That’s the closest I can get to the middle of the ship. The force will be weakest there. I’ll be okay.”

“How operate centrifuge from storage room, question?”

“I’ll…umm…I’ll bring the lab’s control screen down there with me. I’ll run data and power cables from the lab to the storage room. Yeah. That should work.”

“What if you unconscious and can’t operate controls, question?”

“Then you stop the rotation and I’ll wake up.”

He shimmies back and forth. “No like. Different plan: Wait eleven days. Get to my ship. Clean out your ship fuel tanks. Sterilize—make sure no Taumoeba. Fill with fuel from my ship. Then can use all of your ship functions again.”

I shake my head. “I don’t want to wait eleven days. I want to work now.”

“Why, question? Why not wait, question?”

He’s completely right, of course. I’m risking my life and maybe the ship itself. But I can’t just sit around for eleven days when there’s so much work to do. How do I explain “impatience” to someone who lives seven hundred years?

“Human thing,” I say.

“Understand. Not really understand, but…understand.”

—

The start-up went as planned. Rocky chose Ringo to do the spinning, leaving John and Paul off. George is still safely on the ship if I need him.

The g-forces during the start-up were tough—I won’t lie. But I stayed awake long enough to manually handle the centrifuge steps. I’m getting good at it now. Since then, it’s been a nice, steady 1 g.

Yeah, it was hasty and a little risky, but because of that, I’ve had seven days of serious science since then.

Rocky delivered the testing equipment as promised. As always, everything worked perfectly. Instead of a small, annoying glass vacuum chamber, I had something like a large fish tank. Xenonite doesn’t care about a lot of air pressure on a large, flat panel. “Bring it on,” says xenonite.

I have, let’s say, a never-ending supply of Taumoeba. The Hail Mary is now the Taumoeba party bus. All I have to do is open the fuel line that used to go to the generator when I want more.

“Hey, Rocky!” I call out from the lab. “Watch me pull a Taumoeba out of a hat!”

Rocky climbs up his tunnel from the control room. “I guess that's an Earth saying.”

“Yeah. Earth has entertainment called ‘television’ and—”

“Don't explain, please. You have results, question?”

Just as well. It would take a long time to explain cartoons to an alien. “I have some results.”

“Good good.” He hunkers down into a comfortable sitting position. “Tell results!” He tries to hide it, but his voice is just a bit higher than normal.

I gesture to the lab setup. “This works perfectly, by the way.”

“Thank. Tell about results.”

“My first experiment was Adrian’s environment. I added Taumoeba and a slide covered in Astrophage. The Taumoeba survived and ate it all. No surprise there.”

“No surprise. Is their normal environment. But proves equipment works.”

“Exactly. I did more tests to learn Taumoeba’s limits. In Adrian air, they can live from minus 180 degrees Celsius to 107 degrees Celsius. They die outside that range.”

“Impressive range.”

“Yes. Also, they can survive in almost a vacuum.”

“Like your fuel tanks.”

“Yeah, but not a total vacuum.” I frown. “They need carbon dioxide. At least a little. I made an Adrian environment but put argon in instead of carbon dioxide. The Taumoeba didn’t eat anything. They stayed still. Eventually they starved.”

“Expected,” he says. “Astrophage need carbon dioxide. Taumoeba from same place. Taumoeba also need carbon dioxide. How they get carbon dioxide in fuel tanks, question?”

“I had the same question!” I say. “So I did a spectrograph of my fuel-bay sludge. There’s a lot of CO2 gas dissolved in the liquid.”

“Astrophage probably have carbon dioxide inside. Or breaking down makes carbon dioxide. Some percentage died in fuel tanks over time. Not all cells are perfect. Flaws. Changes. Some die. Those dead Astrophage put carbon dioxide in tanks.”

“Agreed.”

“Good findings,” he says. He starts climbing back down.

“Wait. I have more. Much more.”

He stops. “More, you ask? Good.”

I lean against my lab table and pat the tank. “I made Venus in the tank. But not exactly Venus. Venus’s air is 96.5 percent carbon dioxide and 3.5 percent nitrogen. I started with just the carbon dioxide. The Taumoeba were fine. Then I added the nitrogen. And the Taumoeba all died.”

He raises his carapace. “All die, you ask? Fast, you ask?”

“Yes,” I say. “In seconds. All dead.”

“Nitrogen…surprising.”

“Yeah, very surprising!” I say. “I repeated the test with Threeworld’s air. Carbon dioxide only: The Taumoeba were fine. I added in the sulfur dioxide: The Taumoeba were fine. I added the nitrogen: Boom! All the Taumoeba died.”

He taps a claw absently on the tunnel wall. “Very very surprising. Nitrogen doesn't hurt Erid life. Many Erid life need nitrogen.”

“Same with Earth,” I say. “Earth’s air is seventy-eight percent nitrogen.”

“Confusing,” he says.

He’s not alone. I’m just as confused as he is. We’re both thinking the same thing: If all life came from one source, how can nitrogen be vital to two planets and deadly to a third?

Nitrogen is completely harmless and barely reactive as a gas. It’s usually happy as N2, which hardly reacts with anything. Our bodies ignore it, even though 78 percent of every breath is nitrogen. As for Erid, their air is mostly ammonia—a nitrogen mix. How could life from space ever seed Earth and Erid—two planets full of nitrogen—if a small bit of nitrogen kills that life?

Well, the answer is simple: Whatever life-form caused the panspermia, it was fine with nitrogen. Taumoeba, which came later, isn't.

Rocky’s shell sinks. “Situation bad. Threeworld air is eight percent nitrogen.”

I sit on the lab stool and cross my arms. “Venus’s air is 3.5 percent nitrogen. Same problem.”

He sinks more and his voice gets lower. “Hopeless. Cannot change Threeworld air. Cannot change Venus air. Cannot change Taumoeba. Hopeless.”

“Well,” I say. “We can’t change the air on Threeworld or Venus. But maybe we can change Taumoeba.”

“How, question?”

I grab my tablet from the workbench and scroll through my notes about Eridian bodies. “Do Eridians get diseases? Sicknesses inside you?”

“Some. Very, very bad.”

“How does your body kill diseases?”

“Eridian body is closed,” he explains. “It only opens when we eat or lay eggs. After it opens, the area inside gets very hot with hot blood for a long time. This kills any disease. Disease can only get into the body through a wound. Then it is very bad. The body shuts down the infected area and uses hot blood to kill the disease. If the disease is fast, the Eridian dies.”

No immune system at all. Just heat. Well, why not? The hot blood of an Eridian boils water to move their muscles. Why not use it to cook and clean food too? And with skin made of heavy rocks, they don’t get many cuts. Even their lungs don’t share material with the outside. If any germs get in, the body seals the area off and boils it. An Eridian body is like a strong fortress.

But a human body is more like a police state without borders.

“Humans are very different,” I say. “We get sick all the time. We have very strong immune systems. Also, we find cures for diseases in nature. We call them ‘antibiotics.’ ”

“No understand,” he says. “Cures for diseases in nature, question? How, question?”

“Other life on Earth created ways to fight the same diseases. They make chemicals that kill the disease without hurting other cells. Humans eat those chemicals, and they kill the disease but not our human cells.”

“Amaze. Erid no have this.”

“It’s not a perfect system, though,” I say. “Antibiotics work very well at first, but then they become less and less effective over time. Eventually, they barely work at all.”

“Why, question?”

Diseases change. Antibiotics kill almost all the disease in the body, but some live through it. By using antibiotics, humans are accidentally teaching diseases how to live through them.

“Ah!” Rocky says. He raises his shell a bit. “Disease learns to block the stuff that kills it.”

“Yes,” I say. I point at the tank. “Now think of Taumoeba as disease. Think of nitrogen as antibiotic.”

He pauses, then lowers his shell back down. “Got it! Make the area just a little deadly. Breed Taumoeba that live through it. Make it more deadly. Breed the ones that live through that. Do it again and again!”

“Yes,” I say. “We don’t need to know why or how nitrogen kills Taumoeba. We just need to breed Taumoeba that can handle nitrogen.”

“Yes!” he says.

“Good!” I slap the top of the tank. “Make me ten of these, but smaller. Also, make a way for me to grab Taumoeba samples without messing up the experiment. Make a really exact gas thing—I need to control the nitrogen in the tank just right.”

“Yes! I’ll make it! I’ll make it now!”

He hurries down to the dormitory.

I check the spectrograph results and shake my head. “No good. A total failure.”

“Too bad,” Rocky says.

I rest my chin in my hands. “Maybe I can filter out the toxins.”

“Maybe focus on the Taumoeba.” Rocky's voice has a special sarcastic tone. It's very clear right now.

“They’re doing well.” I look at the Taumoeba processing tanks along one side of the lab. “All we can do is wait. We’ve had good results. They’re already at 0.01 percent nitrogen and surviving. The next generation might handle 0.015.”

“This is a waste of time, and my food.”

“I need to know if I can eat your food.”

“Eat your own food.”

“I only have a few months of real food left. You have enough on your ship to feed twenty-three Eridians for years. Eridian and Earth life use the same proteins. Maybe I can eat your food.”

“Why you say ‘real food,’ question? What is not real food, question?”

I checked the readout again. Why does Eridian food have so many heavy metals in it? “Real food is food that tastes good. Food that’s fun to eat.”

“You have not-fun food, question?”

“Yeah. Coma slurry. The ship fed it to me during the trip here. I have enough to last me almost four years.”

“Eat that.”

“It tastes bad.”

“Food experience not that important.”

“Hey.” I point at him. “To humans, food experience is very important.”

“Humans strange.”

I point at the spectrometer readout screen. “Why does Eridian food have thallium in it?”

“Healthy.”

“Thallium kills humans!”

“Then eat human food.”

“Ugh.” I walk over to the Taumoeba tanks. Rocky had really done well. I can control the nitrogen content very precisely. And so far, things look promising. Sure, this generation can only handle a tiny bit of nitrogen, but it’s a bit more than the last one could.

The plan is working. Our Taumoeba are becoming resistant to nitrogen.

Will they ever be able to handle the 3.5 percent needed for Venus? Or the 8 percent for Threeworld? Who knows? We’ll just have to wait and see.

I’m using percentages here to track the nitrogen. I can do that because the air pressure where Astrophage breed is always the same. So, since the pressure is always the same in all experiments, I can just track the percent of nitrogen.

The correct way to do it would be to track “partial pressure.” But that’s annoying. I’d just end up dividing by 0.02 atmospheres and multiplying by it again later when using the data.

I pat the top of Tank Three. It’s been my lucky tank. Out of twenty-three generations of Taumoeba, Tank Three made the strongest strain nine times. Pretty good, considering she’s got nine other tanks to compete with.

Yes, Tank Three is a “she.” Don’t judge me.

“How long until we reach the Blip-A?”

“Seventeen hours until reverse-thrust maneuver.”

“Okay, let’s spin down the centrifuge now. Just in case we run into trouble and need more time to fix something.”

“Agree. I go to control room now. You go to storage locker and lie flat. Do not forget control panel with long extension cords.”

I glance around the lab. Everything is firmly secured. “Yeah, okay. Let’s do it.”

—

“John, Ringo, Paul off,” says Rocky. “Velocity is orbital.”

There is no “stationary” in a solar system. You’re always moving around something. In this case, Rocky reduced our speed to put us in a stable orbit about 1 AU from Tau Ceti. That’s where he left the Blip-A.

Rocky relaxes in his control-room bulb. He clamps the boxes to their wall mounts. Now that the engines are off we’re back to zero g, and the last thing we want is for the “make ship thrust” button to be floating around unattended.

He grabs a couple of handholds and centers his shell over the texture monitor. As always, it shows him my center monitor feed with colors shown as textures.

“You in control now.” He’s done his job. Now it’s my turn.

“How long until the flash?” I ask

Rocky pulls a clock off the wall. “Next flash is three minutes, seven seconds.”

“Okay.”

Rocky’s no fool. He left his ship set up to turn on its engines for a split second every twenty minutes or so, giving us a beacon we really need. It’s easy enough to figure out where the ship should be. But gravity from other planets, not knowing the last speeds exactly, not knowing Tau Ceti’s gravity exactly…they all add up to small errors. And a small error on the location of something orbiting a star is a pretty big distance.

So rather than hoping we can see Taulight reflect off the ship when we get to where it should be, he just set it up to flash the engines now and then. All I have to do is watch with the Petrovascope. It’ll be a very bright flash.

“What is current nitrogen tolerance, question?”

“Tank Three had some survivors at 0.6 percent nitrogen today. I’m breeding them now.”

“What spacing, question?”

It’s a conversation we’ve had many times. But it’s okay for him to be curious. His kind needs it.

The “spacing,” as we call it, is the difference in how much nitrogen each of the ten tanks gets. I don’t do the same thing in every tank. With each new group, I try ten new nitrogen amounts.

“I’m pushing it—0.05 percent more each time.”

“Good good,” he says.

All ten tanks are growing Taumoeba-06 (named for the nitrogen percent it can handle). Tank One is the control, like always. It has 0.6 percent nitrogen in the air. Taumoeba-06 should be fine in there. If it’s not, it means I messed up the last batch and have to go back to an older type.

Tank Two has 0.65 percent nitrogen. Tank Three has 0.7. And so on up to Tank Ten with 1.05 percent. The strongest ones will win and go on to the next round. I wait a few hours to make sure they can breed for at least two generations. Taumoeba doubles really fast. Fast enough to use up all my fuel in days, actually.

If we get to Venus or Threeworld nitrogen levels, I’ll test them more carefully.

“Flash is soon,” Rocky says.

“Copy.”

I put the Petrovascope on the main screen. Usually, it’s to the side, but the center is the only one Rocky can “see.” As expected, there’s just background light in the Petrova frequency from Tau Ceti. I move the camera around. We made sure we were closer to Tau Ceti than the Blip-A should be. So I’m looking almost directly away from the star. That should make the background IR less and give me a good view of the flash.

“Okay. I think I have it aimed about right at your ship.”

Rocky focuses on his screen. “Got it. Thirty-seven seconds until flash.”

“Hey. What’s your ship called, anyway?”

“Blip-A.”

“No, I mean, what do you call it besides that?”

“Ship.”

“Your ship doesn’t have a name?”

“Why would ship need name, question?”

I shrugged. “Ships have names.”

He points to my pilot’s seat. “What’s the name of your chair, question?”

“It doesn’t have a name.”

“Why does ship have name but chair no have name, question?”

“Never mind. Your ship is the Blip-A.”

“That is what I said. Flash in ten seconds.”

“Copy.”

Rocky and I fall silent and stare at our screens. It took me a while to notice the small details, but I can now tell when Rocky is paying attention to something. He angles his shell toward it and moves slightly back and forth. If I follow the line he’s moving on, that’s usually what he’s looking at.

“Three…two…one…now!”

Right on time, a few pixels on-screen blink white.

“Got it,” I say.

“I not notice.”

“It was dim. We must be far away. Hang on…” I switch to the Telescope screen and pan to where the flash came from. I sweep back and forth with small movements until I catch a slight change in the blackness. Taulight reflecting off the Blip-A. “Yeah, we’re pretty far away.”

“Beetles have much fuel left. It’s okay. Tell me the angle to change.”

I check the readouts at the bottom of the screen. All we have to do is line up the Hail Mary with the current telescope angle. “Rotate yaw plus 13.72 degrees. Rotate pitch minus 9.14 degrees.”

“Yaw plus one three point seven two. Pitch minus nine point one four.” He grabs the beetle controls from their holders and gets to work. By turning the beetles on and off in order, he angles the ship toward the Blip-A.

I zero the telescope and zoom in to confirm. The difference between space and the ship is so small it’s hard to see. But it’s there. “Angle correct.”

He focuses hard on his texture screen. “I don’t see anything on-screen.”

“Light difference is very, very small. Need human eyes to see it. Angle is good.”

“Understand. What is the range?”

I switch to the Radar screen. Nothing. “Too far for my radar to see. At least ten thousand kilometers.”

“Accelerate to what speed?”

“How about…three kilometers per second? We’ll get to the Blip-A in about an hour.”

“Three thousand meters per second. Is the standard acceleration rate okay?”

“Yes. Fifteen meters per second per second.”

“Thrust for two hundred seconds. Start now.”

I brace for gravity.

Chapter 25

We did it!

We actually did it!

I have Earth’s salvation in a little tank on the floor.

“Happy!” Rocky says. “Happy happy happy!”

I’m so giddy I might throw up. “Yes! But we’re not done yet.”

I strap myself into my bunk. A pillow tries to float away, but I grab it and wedge it under my head. I’m all wired up, but if I don’t go to sleep soon, Rocky will start hassling me. Sheesh—you almost mess up a mission one time and suddenly you have an alien-enforced bedtime.

“Taumoeba-35!” Rocky says. “Took many many generations but finally success!”

Scientific breakthroughs are a weird feeling. There’s no sudden flash of insight. Just a slow, steady progress toward a goal. But man, when you reach that goal it feels good.

We linked the ships back together weeks ago. Rocky was pretty excited to have access to his much larger ship again. First thing he did was set up a tunnel directly from his part of the Hail Mary to the Blip-A. It meant another hole in my hull, but at this point I trust Rocky to do any engineering task. Heck, if he wanted to do open-heart surgery on me, I’d probably let him. The guy is amazing at this stuff.

With the ships connected, I can’t run the Hail Mary’s centrifuge, so we’re back to zero g. But now that we’re just growing Taumoeba in tanks, I can do without my gravity-based lab equipment for now.

Over the weeks, we saw each generation of Taumoeba become more and more resistant to nitrogen. And now, today, we finally have Taumoeba-35: a type of Taumoeba that can survive with 3.5 percent nitrogen and a total air pressure of 0.02 atmospheres—like on Venus.

“You. Be happy now,” Rocky says from his workbench.

“I am, I am,” I say. “But we need to get to 8 percent so it can live on Threeworld. We’re not done yet.”

“Yes yes yes. But this is moment. Important moment.”

“Yeah.” I smile.

He’s playing with some new device. He’s always working on something. “Now you make exact Venus air in one tank and do careful tests on Taumoeba-35, question?”

“No,” I say. “We’ll keep going until we get to Taumoeba-80. It should work on Venus and Threeworld. I’ll test everything then.”

“Understand.”

I turn to look at his side of the room. The whole “watching me sleep” thing doesn’t bother me anymore. If anything, it’s nice. “What are you working on?”

The device is clamped to his workbench so it doesn't move. He works on it from many angles, using many tools. "This is Earth electricity thing."

"You're making a power converter?"

"Yes. Change Eridian electricity to Earth electricity, which is not as good."

"Prime sequence?"

"It would take too long to explain."

I remember to ask about it later. "Okay. What will you use it for?"

He puts down two tools and picks up three more. "If everything works, we make good Taumoeba. I give you fuel. You go to Earth, and I go to Erid. We say goodbye."

"Yeah, I guess," I mumble. I should be happier about surviving a suicide mission, coming home a hero, and saving my whole species. But saying goodbye to Rocky forever will be hard. I try not to think about it.

"You have many computers. I ask a favor: Can you give one to me as a gift?"

"A laptop? You want a laptop? Sure, I have a lot of them."

“Good good. And thinking machine has information, question? Science information from Earth, question?”

Ah, of course. I’m an advanced alien race with knowledge far beyond Eridian science. I think the laptops have terabyte drives. I could copy the entire contents of Wikipedia over to him.

“Yes. I can do that. But I don’t think a laptop will work in Eridian air. Too hot.”

He points to the device. “This is just one part of the thinking-machine life-support system. System will give power, keep Earth temperature, Earth air inside. Many backups. Make sure thinking machine doesn’t break. If it breaks, no Eridian can fix.”

“Ah, I see. How will you read the output?”

“Camera inside converts from Earth light readout to Eridian texture readout. Like camera in control room. Before we leave, you explain written language to me.”

He certainly knows enough English to look up any words he doesn’t know. “Yeah, sure. Our written language is easy. Kind of easy. There are only twenty-six letters, but many strange ways to say them. Well, I guess there are actually fifty-two symbols because capital letters look different even though they’re pronounced the same. Oh, and then there’s punctuation…”

“Our scholars will figure it out. You just get me started.”

“Yes. I’ll do that,” I say. “I want a gift from you too: xenonite. Solid form and liquid pre-xenonite form. Earth scientists will want that.”

“Yes, I give.”

I yawn. “I’m going to sleep soon.”

“I watch.”

“Good night, Rocky.”

“Good night, Grace.”

I fall asleep easier than I have in weeks. I have Taumoeba that can save Earth.

Changing an alien life-form. What could possibly go wrong?

—

When I was a kid, like most kids, I imagined being an astronaut. I imagined flying through space in a rocket ship, meeting aliens, and being awesome. I didn’t imagine cleaning out sewage tanks.

But that’s pretty much what I’m doing today. To be clear, it’s not my poop I’m cleaning. It’s Taumoeba poop. Thousands of kilograms of Taumoeba poop. Each of my seven remaining fuel bays has to be cleared of all that gunk before I can put new fuel in.

So, on one hand, I’m shoveling poop. On the other, at least I’m in an EVA suit while I do it. I’ve smelled this stuff before. It’s not great.

The gunky methane and rotting cells aren’t a problem. If that were all I had to deal with, I’d just ignore it. Twenty thousand kilograms of gunk in a two-million-kilogram tank? Barely worth paying attention to.

The problem is there’s probably surviving Taumoeba in there. The contamination ate all the available fuel several weeks ago, so they’ve mostly starved by now. At least, according to recent samples I checked. But some of the little bastards will probably still be alive. And the last thing I want to do is feed them 2 million kilograms of fresh Astrophage.

“Progress, question?” Rocky radios.

“Almost done with Fuel Bay Three.”

Fully inside the tank, I scrape black gunk off the walls with a homemade spatula and fling it out through a one-meter-wide hole in the side. Where’d the one-meter-wide hole come from? I made it.

The fuel tanks have no human-sized entry hatches. Why would they? Valves and piping lead in and out, but the largest of them is only a few inches wide. I don’t have anything to flush the tanks with—I left my “ten thousand gallons of water” collection back home. So for each tank I have to cut a hole, clean the gunk out, and then reseal it.

I have to say, though, the cutting torch Rocky made for me works like a charm. A little Astrophage, an IR light, some lenses, and I have a freakin’ death ray in my hands. The trick is keeping the output low. But Rocky put extra safeties in. He made sure the lenses had some impurities and they aren’t made of transparent xenonite. They’re IR-permeable glass. If the light output from the Astrophage inside gets too high, the lenses will melt. Then the beam will defocus and the cutter will be useless. I’d have to sheepishly ask Rocky to make me another one, but at least I wouldn’t cut my leg off.

So far, that hasn’t happened. But I wouldn’t put it past me.

I scrape a particularly stubborn crust of gunk off the wall. It floats away and I use the scraper to bat it out the hole. “Status on breeder tanks?” I ask.

“Tank Four still has live Taumoeba. Tank Five and higher all dead.”

I shuffle forward in the tank. It’s narrow enough that I can stay in place by putting both boots on one side of the cylinder and a hand on the other. This leaves my other hand free to scrape sludge. “Tank Four was 5.25 percent, right?”

“Not right. Five point two zero percent.”

“Okay. So we’re up to Taumoeba-52. Doin’ good.”

“How is progress, question?”

“Slow and steady,” I say.

I flick a clump of gunk off into space. I wish I could just flush the tanks with nitrogen and be done with it. After all, this Taumoeba can’t resist nitrogen at all. But it wouldn’t work. The gunk is several centimeters thick. No matter how much nitrogen I pumped in, there would be some Taumoeba it doesn’t reach—protected by a centimeter-thick wall of their kind.

All it takes is one survivor to start an infestation when I refill the tanks with Rocky’s spare Astrophage. So I have to clean the tanks out as best I can before doing the nitrogen cleanse.

“Your fuel tanks are big. Do you have enough nitrogen, question? I can give ammonia from Blip-A life support if you need.”

“Ammonia wouldn’t work,” I say. “Taumoeba doesn’t have a problem with nitrogen compounds. Just with pure N2. But don’t worry, I’m fine. I don’t need as much nitrogen as you think. We know 3.5 percent at 0.02 atmospheres will kill natural Taumoeba. That’s a partial pressure of less than 1 Pascal. These fuel bays are only 37 cubic meters each. All I need to do is squirt a few grams of nitrogen gas in here and it’ll kill everything. It’s amazingly deadly to Taumoeba.”

I put my hands on my hips. It was an awkward pose in the EVA suit, making me float away from the wall, but it felt right. “Okay. Fuel Bay Three is done.”

“Want to patch hole with xenonite now?”

I float out of the fuel bay and into space. I tug on my tether to pull me back to the hull. “No, I’ll clean everything first, then patch them all in one go.”

I use the handholds to get to Fuel Bay Four, secure myself, and turn on the Eridian AstroTorch.

—

Xenonite makes great containers for pressurized gas.

My fuel bays are all freshly cleaned and resealed. I filled them with about a hundred times more nitrogen than needed to kill any local Taumoeba. Then I just left it like that for a while. I’m taking no chances.

After a few days of sterilizing, it’s time for a test. Rocky gives me a few kilograms of Astrophage to use. I remember when “a few kilograms of Astrophage” would have been a blessing for everyone on Stratt’s Vat. But now it’s just, “Here’s a few quadrillion Joules of energy. Tell me if you need more.”

I divide the Astrophage into seven roughly equal blobs, release the nitrogen, and squirt one blob into each fuel bay. Then I wait a day.

During this time, Rocky is on his ship, working hard on a pump system to move Astrophage from his fuel tanks to mine. I offer to help, but he nicely says no. What could I even do on the Blip-A? My spacesuit can’t handle the conditions in there, so Rocky would have to build me a whole tunnel system…it’s not worth it.

I really want it to be worth it. It’s an alien spaceship! I want to see inside! But yeah, saving humanity is more important. That’s the priority.

I check the fuel bays. Any live Taumoeba will have found and eaten the Astrophage. So, if the Astrophage is still there, the bay is clean.

To make a long story short: Two of the seven bays weren’t clean.

“Hey, Rocky!” I yell from the control room.

He’s somewhere on the Blip-A, but I know he can hear me. He always hears me.

After a few seconds, the radio turns on. “What, question?”

“Two fuel bays still have Taumoeba.”

“Understood. Not good, but not bad. Other five are clean, question?”

I hold onto a handhold in the control room to stay in place. It’s easy to float away when you’re focused on talking. “Yeah, the other five seem good.”

“How did Taumoeba survive in the bad two bays, question?”

“I probably didn’t clean them well enough. Some gunk remained and shielded live Taumoeba from the nitrogen. That’s my guess.”

“Plan, question?”

“I’m going back into those two, scraping them down some more, and I’ll sterilize them again. I’ll leave the other five sealed for now.”

“Good plan. Do not forget to purge fuel lines.”

With all the tanks infected, it’s safe to assume the fuel lines (currently sealed off) will also be infected. “Yes. They’ll be easier than the tanks. I just need to blow high-pressure nitrogen through them. It’ll clean out the chunks and sterilize the rest. Then I’ll test them the same as the fuel bays.”

“Good good.” He says. “What is status of breeder tanks, question?”

“Still making good progress. We’re up to Taumoeba-62 now.”

“Someday we find out why nitrogen was problem.”

“Yeah, but that’s for other scientists. We just need Taumoeba-80.”

“Yes. Taumoeba-80. Maybe Taumoeba-86. Safety.”

When you think in base six, adding six to things randomly is normal.

“Agreed,” I say.

I enter the airlock and climb into the Orlan EVA suit. I grab the AstroTorch and attach it to my tool belt. I turn on the helmet radio and say, “Beginning EVA.”

“Understood. Radio if problem. My ship hull robot can help if you need.”

“I shouldn’t need it, but I’ll let you know.”

I seal the door behind me and start the airlock cycle.

—

“Screw it,” I say. I press the final confirmation button to drop Fuel Bay Five.

The explosives pop, and the empty tank drifts off into the void.

No amount of scrubbing, cleaning, nitrogen-flushing, or anything else could get the Taumoeba out of Fuel Bay Five. No matter what I did, they lived and devoured the test Astrophage I put in afterward.

At some point, you just have to give up.

I cross my arms and sink into my pilot’s seat. There’s no gravity to really sink with, so I have to consciously push myself into the seat. I’m pouting, damn it, and I intend to do it right. I’m missing three of my original nine fuel bays. Two from our adventure over Adrian, and another just now. That’s about 666,000 kilograms of fuel storage I no longer have.

Do I have enough fuel to get home? Sure. Any amount of fuel that can get me away from Tau Ceti’s gravity is enough to eventually get home. I could get home with just a few kilograms of Astrophage if I didn’t mind waiting a million years.

It’s not about getting there. It’s about how long it will take.

I do a lot of math, and I get answers I don’t like.

The trip from Earth to Tau Ceti took three years and nine months. And it was done by constantly accelerating at 1.5 g’s the entire time—which is what Dr. Lamai decided was the maximum g-force a human should be exposed to for almost four years. Earth experienced something like thirteen years during that time, but time dilation helped us out.

If I do the long trip home with just 1.33 million kilograms of fuel (which is all my remaining tanks can hold), the best way is a constant acceleration of 0.9 g’s. I’d be going slower, which means less time dilation, which means I experience more time. In total, I’ll experience five and a half years on that trip.

So what? It’s only an extra year and a half. What’s the big deal?

I don’t have much food.

This was a suicide mission. They gave us food to last several months, and that’s all. I’ve been using the food at a steady pace, but then I’ll have to use coma slurry. It won’t taste good, but it’s balanced, at least.

But again, this was a suicide mission. They didn’t give us enough coma slurry to get home either. The only reason I have any is because Commander Yáo and Specialist Ilyukhina died on the way.

In total, I have three months of real food left and about forty months of coma slurry. That’s just enough food to survive the trip home with full fuel and a little extra. But not enough to last the five and a half years of a slower trip.

Rocky’s food is useless. I’ve tested it many times. It’s full of heavy metals, some toxic and some highly toxic. There are good proteins and sugars that my body could use, but there’s no way to separate the poison from the food.

And there’s nothing here for me to grow. All my food is freeze-dried or dried. No seeds or plants or anything. I can eat what I have, and that’s it.

Rocky clicks along his tunnel to the control-room bulb. He goes in and out of the Blip-A so often that I don’t know what ship he’s on.

“You sound angry. Why?”

“I’m missing a third of my fuel bays. The trip home will take longer than I have food for.”

“How long since you last slept?”

“Huh? I’m talking about fuel here! Stay focused!”

“Grumpy. Angry. Stupid. How long since last sleep, question?”

I shrug. “I don’t know. I’ve been working on the breeder tanks and fuel bays…I forget when I last slept.”

“You sleep. I watch.”

I gesture wildly at the console. “I have a serious problem here! I don’t have enough fuel storage to survive the trip home! It’s 600,000 kilograms of fuel. It would take 135 cubic meters of storage! I don’t have that much space!”

“I make storage tank.”

“You don’t have enough xenonite for that!”

“Don’t need xenonite. Any strong material will do. Have much metal aboard my ship. Melt, shape, make tank for you.”

I blink a couple of times. “You can do that?”

“Obvious I can do that! You are stupid right now. You sleep. I watch and also design replacement tank. Agree, question?” He starts down the tube toward the dormitory.

“Huh…”

“Agree, question?!” he says, louder.

“Yeah…” I mumble. “Yeah, okay…”

—

I’ve done many EVAs by now. But none were as tiring as this one.

I’ve been out here for six hours. The Orlan is a tough, old suit and can handle it. I can’t say the same about myself.

“Installing final fuel bay now,” I pant. Almost there. Stay focused.

Rocky’s quickly made fuel bays are perfect, of course. All I had to do was take off one of my bays and give it to him to study. Well, I gave it to his robot. However he uses that robot to measure things, it does a good job. Every valve connection is in the right place and the right size. Every screw is perfectly spaced.

In total, he made three perfect copies of the fuel bay I gave him. The only difference is what they're made of. My original bays were made of aluminum. Someone on Stratt’s team had suggested a carbon-fiber hull, but she turned that down. Only well-tested technology. Humanity had over sixty years of testing aluminum spacecraft.

The new bays are made of…an alloy. What alloy? No idea. Rocky doesn’t even know. It’s a mix of metals from less important systems on the Blip-A. Mostly iron, he says. But there are at least twenty different elements all melted together. It’s basically “metal soup.”

But that’s okay. The fuel bays don’t need to hold pressure. They only have to keep the Astrophage on the ship—nothing else. They do need to be strong enough to not break apart from the weight of the fuel inside when the ship speeds up. But that’s not hard. They could literally be made out of wood and work just as well.

“You are slow,” he says.

“You are mean.” I tighten the large cylinder into place with straps.

“Sorry. I am excited. Breeder Tanks Nine and Ten!”

“Yeah!” I say. “Fingers crossed!”

We’re up to Taumoeba-78 as of the latest generation. That strain is breeding away in the tanks while I work on these fuel bays. The spacing is 0.25 percent, which means for the first time ever, some breeder tanks actually have 8 percent or more nitrogen inside.

As for installing the tanks…geez. I’ve learned that the first bolt is the hardest. The fuel bay has a lot of inertia and it’s hard to keep lined up with the hole. Also, the original mounting system for the bay is gone. The pyros took care of that. They never thought I’d be adding in new bays after getting rid of old ones. The pyros don’t just open a clamp. They shear the bolts clean off. And they don’t care about the damage to where it mounts.

I spend a lot of time un-dooming this suicide mission.

While the threaded mounting holes are in okay shape, every one of them has a sheared-off bolt to deal with. With no bolt head, they’re a real pain to unscrew. I’ve found that the best way is to bring extra steel rods and the AstroTorch. Melt the bolt a little, melt the rod a little, and weld them together. The result is ugly but it gives me something to grab onto with enough force to remove the bolt. Usually.

When I can’t get the bolt off, I just start melting things. Can’t be stuck if it’s liquid.

Three hours later, I finally have all the new fuel bays installed…sort of.

I cycle the airlock, climb out of the Orlan, and enter the control room. Rocky is in his bulb waiting for me.

“It went well, question?”

I wiggle my hand back and forth—a gesture both humans and Eridians do and with the same meaning. “Maybe. I’m not sure. A lot of the bolt holes were broken. So the bays aren’t connected as well as they should be.”

“Danger, question? Your ship accelerates at 15 meters per second per second. Will tanks hold, question?”

“I’m not sure. Earth engineers often build in extra safety. I hope they did this time. But I will test to be sure.”

“Good good. Enough talk. Check breeder tanks, please.”

“Yeah, yeah. Let me get some water first.”

He bounces and skitters down his tube to the lab. “Why humans need so much water, question? Inefficient life-forms!”

I gulp down a full liter of water I left in the control room before the EVA. It makes you thirsty. I wipe my mouth and let the bag float away. I push off the wall to float down the tunnel to the lab.

“Eridians need water, too, you know.”

“We stay inside. Closed system. Some waste inside, but we get all the water we need from food. Humans leak! Disgusting.”

I laugh as I float into the lab where Rocky is waiting. “On Earth, we have a scary spider that can kill you. You look like one of those. Just so you know.”

“Good. I’m happy. I am scary space monster. You are leaky space blob.” He points to the breeder tanks. “Check tanks!”

I kick off the wall and float over to the breeders. This is the crucial moment. I should check them one by one starting with Tank One, but forget that. I go straight to Tank Nine.

I shine a penlight into the tank and get a good look at the glass slide that was earlier covered in Astrophage. I check the tank numbers, then check the slide again.

I grin at Rocky. “Tank Nine’s slide is clear. We have Taumoeba-80!”

He makes a huge racket! His arms wave around, his hands hit the tunnel walls. It’s just random sounds with no pattern. After a few seconds he calms down. “Yes! Good! Good good good!”

“Ha-ha, wow. Okay. Calm down.” I check Tank Ten. “Hey, Tank Ten is also clear. We have Taumoeba-82.5!”

“Good good good!”

“Good good good, indeed!” I say.

“Now you test a lot. Venus air. Threeworld air.”

“Yes. Absolutely—”

He moves back and forth from one tunnel wall to the other. “Same gases in each test. Same pressure. Same temperature. Same death ‘radiation’ from space. Same light from nearby star. Same same same.”

“Yes. I’ll do that. I’ll do all of that.”

“Do now.”

“I need rest! I just did an eight-hour EVA!”

“Do now!”

“Ugh! No!” I float over to his tunnel and face him through the xenonite. “First I’m going to breed a lot more Taumoeba-82.5. Just to make sure we have enough for testing. And I’ll make several stable groups of it in sealed containers.”

“Yes! And some on my ship too!”

“Yes. More backups are better.”

He bounces back and forth some more. “ Erid will live! Earth will live! Everyone live! ” He curls the claws of one hand into a ball and presses it against the xenonite. “ Fist me! ”

I push my knuckles against the xenonite. “It’s ‘fist-bump,’ but yeah.”

—

There has to be alcohol somewhere. I can’t imagine Ilyukhina going on a suicide mission without having some booze. I can’t imagine her going across the street without some booze, honestly. And after looking through every box in the storage compartment, I finally find it—the personal kits.

The box has three zipped duffel bags. Each one is labeled with a crewman’s name. “Yáo,” “Ilyukhina,” and “DuBois.” I guess they never replaced DuBois’s personal kit, because I never got a chance to make mine.

Still a little mad about how that happened. But maybe I’ll get a chance to tell Stratt how I feel about it.

I pull the kits into the dormitory with me and Velcro them to the wall. Personal belongings of three people who are now dead. Friends who are now dead.

I may have a sad moment later and spend some time looking at all these bags. But for now, this is a time to celebrate. I want booze.

I open Ilyukhina’s bag. There are all sorts of random little things inside. A pendant with some Russian writing on it, a worn old teddy bear she probably had as a kid, a kilogram of heroin, some of her favorite books, and there we are! Five 1-liter bags of clear liquid labeled водка.

It’s Russian for “vodka.” How do I know that? Because I spent months on an aircraft carrier with a bunch of crazy Russian scientists. I saw that word a lot.

I zip up her bag and leave it Velcroed to the wall. I fly through to the lab where Rocky waits in his tunnel.

“Found it!” I say.

“ Good good! ” His usual jumpsuit and tool-belt is nowhere to be seen. He has an outfit on I’ve never seen before.

“Well, well, well! What have we here?” I say.

He sticks out his shell with pride. It’s covered with a smooth cloth that supports matching hard shapes here and there. Almost like armor, but not fully covering, and I don’t think they’re metal.

The top hole, where his vents are, is ringed with rough gems. Definitely jewelry of some kind. They’re cut, similar to how Earth jewelry might be cut, but the quality is horrible. They’re blotchy and discolored. But they’re really big and I bet they sound great to sonar.

The sleeves leading off the shirt stop about halfway down his arms and are also decorated at the cuffs. Each shoulder is connected to its neighbors by loose braided cords. And for the first time I’ve ever seen, he has gloves on. All five hands are covered in rough material like burlap.

This outfit would really stop Rocky from moving around, but hey, fashion isn’t about being comfy or easy.

“You look great!” I say.

“Thank! This is special clothing for celebration.”

I hold up a liter of vodka. “This is special liquid for celebration.”

“Humans…eat to celebrate?”

“Yeah. I know Eridians eat in private. I know you think it’s gross to watch. But this is how humans celebrate.”

“Is okay. Eat! We celebrate!”

I float over to the two experiments on the lab table. One has a copy of Venus’s air. The other has the air of Threeworld. I made them as exact as I could. I used the best info I have, which is a lot because I have every human reference book ever and Rocky knows his own system.

In both, Taumoeba have not only lived but done great. They breed fast, and even a tiny bit of Astrophage put into either one gets eaten right away.

I hold the bag of vodka up. “To Taumoeba-82.5! Savior of two worlds!”

“You will give that liquid to the Taumoeba, right?”

I unclip the straw. “No, it’s just something humans say. I am honoring Taumoeba-82.5.” I take a sip. It’s like fire in my mouth. Ilyukhina liked her vodka strong and rough.

“Yes. Much honoring!” he says. “Human and Eridian work together, save everyone!”

“Ah!” I say. “That reminds me: I need a life-support system for Taumoeba—something that feeds them just enough Astrophage to keep the colony alive. It has to be completely automatic, work on its own for several years, and weigh less than a kilogram. I need four of them.”

“Why so small?”

“I’m going to put one on each beetle. Just in case something happens to the Hail Mary on the way home.”

“Good plan! You are smart! I can make these for you. Also, today I finish fuel-transfer device. Can give you Astrophage now. Then we both go home!”

“Yeah.” My smile fades.

“This is happy! Your face looks sad. Why?”

“Going to be a long trip and I’ll be all alone.” I haven’t decided if I want to risk a coma on the way home. I may have to for my own sanity. Total solitude and nothing to eat but chalky, nasty coma slurry might just be too much. For the first part of the trip, at least, I definitely plan to stay awake.

“You’ll miss me, right? I’ll miss you. You are friend.”

“Yeah. I’m going to miss you.” I take another sip of vodka. “You’re my friend. Heck, you’re my best friend. And pretty soon we’re going to say goodbye forever.”

He tapped two gloved claws together. They made a muffled sound instead of the usual click that comes with the dismissive gesture. “Not forever. We save planets. Then we have Astrophage technology. Visit each other.”

I give a small smile. “Can we do all that within fifty Earth years?”

“Probably not. Why so soon, right?”

“I only have fifty years or so left to live. Humans don’t”—I hiccup—“don’t live long, remember?”

“Oh.” He’s quiet for a moment. “So we enjoy the time we have left, then go save planets. Then we are heroes!”

“Yeah!” I straighten up. I’m a little dizzy now. I’ve never been much of a drinker, and I’m drinking this vodka more than I should. “We’re the most important people in the galaxy! We’re awesome!”

He grabs a nearby wrench and raises it in one of his hands. “To us!”

I raise the vodka. “To ush!”

“Well. This is it,” I say from my side of the connector.

“Yes,” says Rocky on his side. His voice is low, even though he tries to keep it high.

The Hail Mary is fully fueled: 2.2 million kilograms of Astrophage. That's 200,000 kilograms more than when it left Earth. Rocky’s new tanks were, of course, better and bigger than my old ones.

I rub the back of my neck. “I think our people will meet again. I know humans will want to learn all about Erid.”

“Yes,” he says. “Thank you for laptop. Our scientists can learn about centuries of human technology. You have given greatest gift in history of my people.”

“You tested it in the life-support system you built for it, right?”

“Yes. That is a stupid question.” He grabs a handle on his side to stay in place.

Rocky had taken away his connecting tunnel and resealed the Hail Mary’s hull. He put the connector in place to finish packing.

At my request, he left the alien walls and tunnels in the Hail Mary as they were, but with a few meter-wide holes here and there so I can use the space. I think the more alien material Earth scientists have to study, the better.

The ship still smells a little like ammonia. I guess even alien material isn’t completely airtight. It’ll probably smell that way for a while.

“And your farms?” I say. “You double-checked them all?”

“Yes. Six backup Taumoeba-82.5 colonies, each in separate tanks with separate life-support systems. Each with a fake Earth atmosphere. Your farms are working, question?”

“Yeah,” I say. “Well, it’s just my ten breeder tanks. But now I have them all set up with Venus’s atmosphere. Oh, and thanks for the mini-farms, by the way. I’ll install them in the beetles during my trip. I won’t have much else to do.”

He glances at a notepad. “These numbers you gave me. Are you sure these are the times for me to turn around and the times for me to reach Erid, question? They are so soon. So fast.”

“Yeah, that’s time moving differently for you. Weird stuff. But those are the correct values. I checked them four times. You’ll reach Erid in under three Earth years.”

“But Earth is almost the same distance from Tau Ceti, and you will take four years, question?”

“I’ll feel four years, yes. Three years and nine months. Because time won’t be as compressed for me as it is for you.”

“You have explained before, but again…why, question?”

“Your ship speeds up quicker than mine. You’ll be getting closer to the speed of light.”

He wiggles his shell. “So complicated.”

I point to his ship. “All the info about relativity is on the laptop. Have your scientists check it out.”

“Yes. They will be very happy.”

“Not when they learn about quantum physics. Then they’ll be really annoyed.”

“Don’t get it.”

I laugh. “Don’t sweat it.”

We’re both quiet for a bit.

“Guess this is it,” I say.

“It is time,” he says. “We go save our homes now.”

“Yeah.”

“Your face is leaking.”

I wipe my eyes. “Human thing. Don’t worry about it.”

“I understand.” He pushes himself to his airlock door. He opens it and pauses there. “Goodbye, friend Grace.”

I wave weakly. “Goodbye, friend Rocky.”

He disappears into his ship and closes the airlock door. I return to the Hail Mary. After a few minutes, the Blip-A’s hull robot removes the tunnel.

We fly our ships almost side by side but with slightly different paths. This makes sure neither of us burns up the other with the exhaust from our Astrophage engines. Once we are a few thousand kilometers apart, we can aim in any direction we want.

Hours later, I sit in the cockpit with my spin drives off. I just want one last look. I watch the dot of IR light with the Petrovascope. That’s Rocky, going back to Erid.

“Godspeed, buddy,” I say.

I set course for Earth and start the spin drives.

I'm going home.

Chapter 26

I sat in my cell, staring at the wall.

It wasn’t a dirty jail cell or anything. If anything, it looked like a college dorm room. Painted brick walls, desk, chair, bed, private bathroom, and so on. But the door was steel and the windows had bars. I wasn’t going anywhere.

Why did the Baikonur launch site have a jail cell ready? I don’t know. Ask the Russians.

The launch was today. Soon, some strong guards would come through that door with a doctor. He’d inject me with something, and that would be the last time I saw Earth.

Almost as if planned, I heard the door being unlocked. Someone with more courage might have seen that as a chance. Rush the door and maybe get past the guards. But I had given up hope of escape long ago. What could I do? Run into the Kazakhstan desert and take my chances?

The door opened and Stratt walked in. The guards closed the door behind her.

“Hey,” she said.

I glared at her from my bed.

“The launch is on schedule,” she said. “You’ll be leaving soon.”

“Whoopee.”

She sat in the chair. “I know you won’t believe this, but it wasn’t easy for me to do this to you.”

“Yeah, you’re really sentimental.”

She ignored the jab. “Do you know what I studied in college? What my degree was in?”

I shrugged.

“History. I was a history major.” She tapped her fingers on the desk. “Most people think I studied science or business. Or communications. But no. It was history.”

“Doesn’t seem like you.” I sat up on my bunk. “You don’t spend much time looking back.”

“I was eighteen and had no idea what to do. I majored in history because I didn’t know what else to do.” She smirked. “Hard to picture me like that, huh?”

“Yeah.”

She looked out the barred window toward the launchpad in the distance. “But I learned a lot. I actually liked it. People today…they don’t know how good they have it. The past was constant misery for most people. And the further back you go, the worse it was.”

She stood and wandered around the room. “For fifty thousand years, until the industrial revolution, human life was about one thing: food. Every culture spent most of its time, energy, people, and resources on food. Hunting, gathering, farming, storing, distributing…it was all about food.

“Even the Roman Empire. Everyone knows about the emperors, the armies, and the conquests. But what the Romans really invented was a great system for getting farmland and moving food and water.”

She walked to the other side of the room. “The industrial revolution made farming mechanical. Since then, we’ve focused on other things. But that’s only been the last two hundred years. Before that, most people spent most of their lives directly involved in food production.”

“Thanks for the history lesson,” I said. “But I’d like my last moments on Earth to be a little more pleasant, if you don’t mind. So…could you leave?”

She ignored me. “Leclerc’s Antarctica nukes bought us some time. But not much. And we can only dump so many chunks of Antarctica into the ocean before the sea-level rise and ocean-biome death cause more problems than Astrophage. Remember what Leclerc told us: Half the world's population will die.”

“I know,” I muttered.

“No, you don’t know,” she said. “Because it gets much worse.”

“Worse than half of humanity dying?”

“Of course,” she said. “Leclerc’s estimate assumes all countries will work together to share resources and ration food. But do you think that will happen? Do you think the United States—the most powerful military force ever—will sit by while half their people starve? How about China, a nation of 1.3 billion people that’s always close to famine even when things are good? Do you think they’ll just leave their weaker neighbors alone?”

I shook my head. “There’ll be wars.”

“Yes. There’ll be wars. Fought for the same reason most wars were fought long ago: food. They’d use religion or honor or whatever as an excuse, but it was always about food. Land to grow it on and people to work that land.

“But that’s not all,” she said. “Because once the desperate, starving countries start invading each other for food, the food made will go down. Ever heard of the Tai Ping rebellion? It was a civil war in China in the 1800s. Four hundred thousand soldiers died fighting. And twenty million people died from the famine that followed. The war messed up farming, see? That’s how huge these things are.”

She wrapped her arms around herself. I’d never seen her look so weak. “Starvation. Chaos. Famine. Everything needed to make and get food, and everything needed for war. All of society will fall apart. There’ll be plagues too. Many of them. Everywhere. Because the hospitals and doctors will be overwhelmed. Diseases that were once easy to control will spread.”

She turned to face me. “War, famine, disease, and death. Astrophage is the end of the world. The Hail Mary is all we have now. I’ll do anything to give it even the smallest chance to work.”

I lay down on my bunk and faced away from her. “Whatever helps you sleep.”

She walked back to the door and knocked. A guard opened it. “Anyway. I just wanted you to know why I’m doing this. I had to tell you.”

“Go to hell.”

“Oh, I will, trust me. You three are going to Tau Ceti. The rest of us are going to hell. Or, more likely, hell is coming here.”

Yeah? Well, hell’s coming back to you, Stratt. In the form of me. I am hell.

I mean…I don’t know what I’ll say to her. But I definitely plan to say stuff. Mean stuff.

I’m eighteen days into my almost four-year journey. I’m just now reaching Tau Ceti’s heliopause—the edge of the star’s strong magnetic field. At least, the edge of where it’s strong enough to block fast-moving interstellar radiation. From now on, the radiation hitting the ship will be much higher.

Doesn’t matter to me. I’m surrounded by Astrophage. But it’s interesting to see the radiation sensors rise and rise. It’s progress, at least. But in the big picture, I’m on a long road trip and my current status is “just walking out the front door.”

I’m bored. I’m alone in a spaceship with not much to do.

I clean and organize the lab again. I might come up with some research ideas for either Astrophage or Taumoeba. Heck, I could write some papers while I’m traveling home. Oh, and there’s the intelligent alien I hung out with for a couple of months. I might want to write some things down about him too.

I do have a huge collection of video games. I have every piece of software that was available when they built the ship. I’m sure they can keep me busy for a while.

I check the Taumoeba farms. All ten are doing fine. I feed them Astrophage sometimes, just to keep them healthy and breeding. The farms copy Venus’s atmosphere, so as the Taumoeba generations pass, they’ll get even better at living on Venus. After four years of this, by the time I drop them off on the planet, they’ll be well suited for it.

And yes, I’ve already decided I’ll drop them off. Why not?

I have no idea what kind of world I’ll be returning to. Thirteen years have passed on Earth since I left, and they’ll experience another thirteen before I get back. Twenty-six years. All my students will be adults. I hope they all survive. But I have to admit…some probably won’t. I try not to think about it.

Anyway, once I get back to my solar system, I might as well stop by Venus and drop off the Taumoeba. I’m not sure how I’ll release it, but I have some ideas. The easiest is just to make a ball of Astrophage filled with Taumoeba and throw it at Venus. The Astrophage will protect the Taumoeba from the heat when entering the atmosphere, and then the Taumoeba will be free. They’ll have a feast. Venus must be full of Astrophage by now, and Taumoeba can get to work as soon as they find it.

I check my food supply. I’m still on track. I have three more months of real food packs left, and then it’s coma food from then on.

I don’t want to go back into a coma. I have the genes to survive it, but so did Yáo and Ilyukhina. Why risk dying if I don’t have to?

Also, I can’t be completely sure I set the course correctly. I think it’s right, and every time I check, I’m still heading home. But what if something goes wrong while I’m in a coma? What if I wake up and I’m a light-year away from the solar system?

But with the isolation, loneliness, and bad food, I might be willing to take those risks eventually. We’ll see.

Speaking of loneliness, I start thinking about Rocky. My only friend now. Really. He’s my only friend. I didn’t have much of a social life when things were normal. Sometimes I’d have dinner with other teachers and staff at the school. I’d have a beer with old friends from college on a Saturday night sometimes. But because of time moving differently in space, when I get home, they’ll all be much older than me.

I liked Dimitri. He was probably my favorite of the Hail Mary group. But who knows what he’s doing now? Russia and the United States might be at war. Or they might be allies in a war. I have no idea.

I climb the ladder to the control room. I sit in the pilot’s seat and open the navigation panel. I shouldn’t do this, but it’s become a habit. I turn off the spin drives and float. I don’t feel gravity anymore, but I barely notice. I’m used to it.

With the spin drives off, I can use the Petrovascope safely. I look around in space for a bit—I know where to look. I find it quickly. The small light of the Petrova frequency. The Blip-A’s engines. If I were close to that light, my ship would be vaporized.

I’m on one side of the system, and he’s on the other. Tau Ceti itself looks like a lightbulb in the distance. But I can still see the Blip-A’s engine flare clearly. Using light as fuel releases an incredible amount of power.

Maybe we could use that later. Maybe Earth and Erid can talk by sending out huge bursts of Petrova light, thanks to Astrophage. I wonder how much light it would take to make a flash that you could see from 40 Eridani. We could talk in Morse code or something. They have a copy of Wikipedia now. They'd figure out what we're doing when they saw the flashes.

Still, our "conversation" would be slow. 40 Eridani is sixteen light-years from Earth. So if we sent a message like "Hey, how's it going?" it would be thirty-two years before we got their answer.

I stare at the little dot of light on the screen and sigh. I’ll be able to track him for a while. I know where his ship will be at any time. He’ll use the exact flight plan I gave him. He trusts my science as much as I trust his engineering. But after a few months, the Petrovascope won’t be able to see the light anymore. Not because the light is too weak—it’s a very sensitive instrument. It won’t be able to see him because our speeds relative to each other will cause the light from his engines to shift to a redder color. It won’t be the Petrova wavelength anymore when it gets to me.

What? Would I do a crazy amount of math about relativity to figure out our speeds relative to each other at any time, as I see it, and then use those calculations to find out when the light from his engines will disappear from the Petrovascope’s view? Just so I know how much longer I can see my friend in the distance? Wouldn’t that be kind of sad?

Yeah.

Okay, my sad little daily routine is over. I turn off the Petrovascope and turn on the spin drives again.

—

I check my shrinking supply of real food. I’ve been traveling for thirty-two days now. According to my calculations, fifty-one days from now I’ll have to rely completely on coma slurry.

I go to the dormitory. “Computer, give me a sample of the coma food.”

The mechanical arms reach into their supply area, grab a bag of white powder, and drop it on the bunk.

I pick up the bag. Of course it’s a powder. Why would they include the liquid for long-term storage? The Hail Mary's water system is a closed loop. Water goes in, comes out, is purified, and reused.

I take the package to the lab, open it, and pour some powder into a beaker.

I add a little water, stir it, and it turns into a milky-white liquid. I sniff it. It doesn’t smell like anything. So I take a sip.

It’s hard, but I don't spit it out. It tastes like aspirin. That nasty pill taste. I’m going to have to eat this Bitter Pill Chow™ every meal for years.

Maybe a coma isn’t so bad.

I set the beaker aside. I’ll deal with that later. For now, I’ll work on the beetles.

I have four little Taumoeba farms, from Rocky. Each is a steel-like capsule, small enough to hold in my hand. I say “steel-like” because it’s an Eridian steel alloy humans haven't made yet. It’s much harder than our metal alloys, but not harder than diamond tools.

We debated the mini-farm casing. Xenonite seemed like the obvious choice. But how would Earth scientists open it? Our tools couldn’t cut it. The only option would be extreme heat, which could harm the Taumoeba inside.

I suggested a xenonite container with a lid, clamped down like a pressure door. I’d leave instructions on the USB stick for safe opening. Rocky rejected that. No matter how good the seal, it wouldn't be perfect. Over the two years, enough air could leak out and suffocate the Taumoeba. He insisted on a single, sealed container. Probably a good idea.

So we picked Eridian steel. It’s strong, doesn’t rust, and lasts a long time. Earth can cut it with a diamond saw. And hey, they'll probably study it to learn how to make it themselves. Everyone wins!

His plan for the farms was simple. Inside, there’s a living group of Taumoeba and a Venus-like air. Also, there’s a coil of very thin steel tubing filled with Astrophage. The Taumoeba can only reach the outside layer, so they have to eat their way down the tube, which is about 20 meters long. Some simple tests show that will feed the small Taumoeba group for several years. As for their waste—it'll just sit in their own waste. The container will gain methane and lose carbon dioxide over time, but it doesn’t matter. Though it’s small for humans, it’s a huge, gigantic space to the tiny microbes inside.

The beetles have been a priority for me. I want them ready to launch right away. Just in case something bad happens to the Hail Mary. But I don’t want to launch them unless it’s really important. The closer we are to Earth when they launch, the better chance they have of getting there safely.

Besides installing the mini-farms, I also have to refill the little guys. I’d used almost half their fuel when they were used as engines for the Hail Mary. But they only need 60 kilograms of Astrophage each to be full. That’s hardly anything compared to my supply of Astrophage made on Eridia.

The hardest part is opening the beetle’s fuel tank. Like everything else here, it wasn’t meant to be reused. It’s like refilling a disposable lighter. It’s just not made for that. It’s completely sealed. I have to clamp it in the mill and use a 6-millimeter drill bit to get in…it’s complicated. But I’m getting better at it.

I finished John and Paul yesterday. Today I’m working on Ringo and, if I have time, George. George will be the easiest. I don’t need to refuel him—I never used him as an engine. I just have to attach the mini-farm.

Figuring out where to put the mini-farm was another issue. Even though it’s small, it’s too big to fit inside the little probe. So I glue it to the bottom. Then I spot-weld a small weight to the top of the beetle. The computer inside cares a lot about where the probe’s center of mass is. It’s easier to add a weight than to completely change the guidance system.

Which brings us to the weight.

The extra weight of the farm makes the beetles weigh a kilogram more than they should. That’s okay. I remember many meetings with Steve Hatch discussing the design. He’s a strange guy, but he’s a great rocket scientist. The beetles know where they are in space by looking at the stars, and if they have less fuel than they should, they slow down their acceleration as needed.

In short: They’ll get home. It’ll just take a little longer. I did the math and it’s a small difference in Earth time. Though the beetles will experience several extra months during the trip than originally planned.

I go to the supply cabinet and pull out the BOCOA (big container of Astrophage). It’s a metal bin with wheels that blocks light. There are several hundred kilograms of Astrophage in there, and I’m in 1.5 g’s of gravity. That’s why I added the wheels. You’d be amazed what you can do with a machine shop and really wanting not to drag heavy stuff around.

I hold the handle with a towel because it’s so hot. I wheel it over to the lab table, sit in the chair, and get ready for the careful refueling. I get the plastic syringe ready. With it, I can squirt 100 milliliters of Astrophage into that 6-millimeter hole each time. That’s about 600 grams. In total, I have to do it about two hundred times per beetle.

I open the BOCOA and—

“Ugh!” I wince and pull away from the container. It smells awful.

“Uh…” I say. “Why does it smell like that?”

Then it hits me. I know that smell. It’s the smell of dead, rotting Astrophage.

The Taumoeba are loose again.

Chapter 27

I jump off the stool, but I don’t have a plan.

“Okay, don’t panic,” I tell myself. “Think clearly. Then act.”

The BOCOA is still hot. That means there’s a lot of live Astrophage still in it. I caught it early. That’s good. Not because of the BOCOA—it’s ruined. I’ll never be able to separate the Taumoeba from the Astrophage in there. But it means that however the Taumoeba got in there, it’s very recent and hopefully hasn’t reached the ship’s fuel.

Yes. That’s the top priority. Don’t let Taumoeba into the fuel bays. Last time they got in was because of tiny leaks in the system. But it had to have gotten there from the crew areas where I had brought it aboard. There isn’t much connection between the fuel system and the crew area. There’s just one likely place for transfer.

Life support.

If the ship is too cold, it runs air across coils filled with Astrophage to warm it up. A leak in one of those coils would do it. Luckily for me, I’ve had a big pile of 96°C Astrophage in the lab keeping the crew area so warm the ship has to use the air-conditioning system.

Okay, now I have a plan.

I hurry up the ladder to the control room. I pull up the Life Support panel and look at the logs. As I thought, the heater hasn’t been turned on in over a month. I turn off the heater completely. It shows as disabled, but I don’t trust it.

I go to the main breaker box. It’s under the pilot’s seat. I find the breaker for the heating system and switch it off.

“Okay,” I say.

I get back in the seat and check the Fuel panel. The fuel tanks all seem fine. The temperature is right. Taumoeba could quickly spread and eat everything in a fuel tank—I know that for sure. If they were affected, they’d be colder.

I pull up the spin-drive controls and shut off the engines. I feel weightless again. I probably don’t need to shut them off, but I don’t want the fuel doing anything right now. If there’s Taumoeba in a fuel line, I want it to stay there, not get pumped all over the ship.

“Okay…” I say again. “Okay…”

More thinking.

How did it get loose? I cleaned every part of this ship with nitrogen before getting any Astrophage from Rocky. The only Taumoeba on board are in the sealed mini-farms on the beetles and the sealed, xenonite breeder tanks.

No. No time for science questions. I can guess about the cause later. Right now I have an engineering problem. I wish Rocky were here.

I always wish Rocky were here.

“Nitrogen,” I say.

I don’t know how the Taumoeba got out, but I need them dead. Taumoeba-82.5 can handle 8.25 percent nitrogen at 0.02 atmospheres. Maybe a little higher. But it definitely can’t handle 100 percent nitrogen at the crew compartment’s 0.33 atmospheres. That's two hundred times the amount that would kill it.

I float to the breaker box and shut off everything for life support. Right away, the emergency alarm sounds and red lights turn on. I kick across the control room to the emergency system’s breaker box and shut those off too.

The master alarm is annoying, so I silence it on the main control panel.

I fly down to the lab and throw open my gas cylinder supply cupboard. I have about 10 kilograms of nitrogen gas in a single canister. Again, I owe my life to DuBois’s preferred way of suicide.

I don’t remember all the details about the life-support system, but I know it has manual overpressure valves. The ship simply won’t allow more than 0.33 atmospheres. If everything else fails (which it will, because I shut the emergency systems down), it’ll vent extra pressure into space.

I can’t just release the nitrogen and hope it works. I want to get rid of the oxygen first. I’m done messing around with this. I want 100 percent nitrogen in here. I want to make this ship so toxic to Taumoeba that it has no chance of living. Even if it’s hiding under some goop somewhere—I want the nitrogen to soak through it. Nitrogen everywhere. Everywhere!

I grab the nitrogen cylinder, kick off the floor, and float back up to the control room. I throw open the airlock inner door and get into the Orlan suit faster than ever before. I start everything up and don’t even bother with the self-check. No time.

I leave the inner airlock door open and turn the manual emergency valve on the outer door. The ship’s air hisses away into space. The main and emergency life-support systems are powered down. They can’t replace the lost gas.

Now I wait.
